Practice Exercise 42 Solving simple equations (answers on page 344)

Solve the following equations.
1. $2 x+5=7$
2. $8-3 t=2$
3. $\frac{2}{3} c-1=3$
4. $2 x-1=5 x+11$
5. $7-4 p=2 p-5$
6. $2.6 x-1.3=0.9 x+0.4$
7. $2 a+6-5 a=0$
8. $3 x-2-5 x=2 x-4$
9. $20 d-3+3 d=11 d+5-8$
10. $2(x-1)=4$
11. $16=4(t+2)$
12. $5(f-2)-3(2 f+5)+15=0$
13. $2 x=4(x-3)$
14. $6(2-3 y)-42=-2(y-1)$
15. $2(3 g-5)-5=0$
16. $4(3 x+1)=7(x+4)-2(x+5)$
17. $11+3(r-7)=16-(r+2)$
18. $8+4(x-1)-5(x-3)=2(5-2 x)$

Here are some further worked examples on solving simple equations.

Problem 9. Solve the equation $\frac{4}{x}=\frac{2}{5}$
The lowest common multiple (LCM) of the denominators, i.e. the lowest algebraic expression that both $x$ and 5 will divide into, is $5 x$.

Multiplying both sides by $5 x$ gives
$$\begin{array}{rlrl} 
& 5 x\left(\frac{4}{x}\right) & =5 x\left(\frac{2}{5}\right) \\
& \text { Cancelling gives } & 5(4) & =x(2) \\
\text { i.e. } & 20 & =2 x
\end{array}$$

Dividing both sides by 2 gives $\frac{20}{2}=\frac{2 x}{2}$
Cancelling gives $\quad \mathbf{1 0}=\boldsymbol{x}$ or $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{1 0}$
which is the solution of the equation $\frac{4}{x}=\frac{2}{5}$
When there is just one fraction on each side of the equation as in this example, there is a quick way to arrive at equation (1) without needing to find the LCM of the denominators.
We can move from $\frac{4}{x}=\frac{2}{5}$ to $4 \times 5=2 \times x$ by what is called 'cross-multiplication'.
In general, if $\frac{a}{b}=\frac{c}{d}$ then $a d=b c$
We can use cross-multiplication when there is one fraction only on each side of the equation.

Problem 10. Solve the equation $\frac{3}{t-2}=\frac{4}{3 t+4}$

Cross-multiplication gives $3(3 t+4)=4(t-2)$
Removing brackets gives $\quad 9 t+12=4 t-8$
Rearranging gives $\quad 9 t-4 t=-8-12$
i.e.
$$5 t=-20$$

Dividing both sides by 5 gives $\quad t=\frac{-20}{5}=-4$
which is the solution of the equation $\frac{3}{t-2}=\frac{4}{3 t+4}$
Problem 11. Solve the equation
$$\frac{2 y}{5}+\frac{3}{4}+5=\frac{1}{20}-\frac{3 y}{2}$$

The lowest common multiple (LCM) of the denominators is 20 ; i.e., the lowest number that $4,5,20$ and 2 will divide into.
Multiplying each term by 20 gives
$20\left(\frac{2 y}{5}\right)+20\left(\frac{3}{4}\right)+20(5)=20\left(\frac{1}{20}\right)-20\left(\frac{3 y}{2}\right)$
Cancelling gives $\quad 4(2 y)+5(3)+100=1-10(3 y)$ i.e.
$$8 y+15+100=1-30 y$$

Rearranging gives $\quad 8 y+30 y=1-15-100$
i.e.

Dividing both sides by 38 gives
Cancelling gives
$38 y=-114$
$\frac{38 y}{38}=\frac{-114}{38}$ $y=-3$
which is the solution of the equation
$$\frac{2 y}{5}+\frac{3}{4}+5=\frac{1}{20}-\frac{3 y}{2}$$

\section*{Problem 12. Solve the equation $\sqrt{x}=2$}

Whenever square root signs are involved in an equation, both sides of the equation must be squared.

Squaring both sides gives $(\sqrt{x})^{2}=(2)^{2}$
i.e.
$$x=4$$
which is the solution of the equation $\sqrt{x}=2$.
Problem 13. Solve the equation $2 \sqrt{d}=8$

Whenever square roots are involved in an equation, the square root term needs to be isolated on its own before squaring both sides.
Cross-multiplying gives
$$\begin{aligned}
\sqrt{d} & =\frac{8}{2} \\
\sqrt{d} & =4
\end{aligned}$$

Cancelling gives
Squaring both sides gives $(\sqrt{d})^{2}=(4)^{2}$
i.e.
$$d=16$$
which is the solution of the equation $2 \sqrt{d}=8$.
Problem 14. Solve the equation $\left(\frac{\sqrt{b}+3}{\sqrt{b}}\right)=2$
Cross-multiplying gives $\sqrt{b}+3=2 \sqrt{b}$
Rearranging gives
$$3=2 \sqrt{b}-\sqrt{b}$$
i.e.
$$3=\sqrt{b}$$

Squaring both sides gives
$$9=b$$
which is the solution of the equation $\left(\frac{\sqrt{b}+3}{\sqrt{b}}\right)=2$.

\section*{Problem 15. Solve the equation $x^{2}=25$}

Whenever a square term is involved, the square root of both sides of the equation must be taken.

Taking the square root of both sides gives $\sqrt{x^{2}}=\sqrt{25}$
i.e.
$$x= \pm 5$$
which is the solution of the equation $x^{2}=25$.
Problem 16. Solve the equation $\frac{15}{4 t^{2}}=\frac{2}{3}$
We need to rearrange the equation to get the $t^{2}$ term on its own.

Cross-multiplying gives
$$15(3)=2\left(4 t^{2}\right)$$
i.e.
$$45=8 t^{2}$$

Dividing both sides by 8 gives $\frac{45}{8}=\frac{8 t^{2}}{8}$
By cancelling
$$5.625=t^{2}$$
or
$$t^{2}=5.625$$

Taking the square root of both sides gives
$$\begin{aligned}
\sqrt{t^{2}} & =\sqrt{5.625} \\
\boldsymbol{t} & = \pm \mathbf{2 . 3 7 2}
\end{aligned}$$
i.e.
correct to 4 significant figures, which is the solution of the equation $\frac{15}{4 t^{2}}=\frac{2}{3}$

\subsection*{11.3 Practical problems involving simple equations}

There are many practical situations in engineering in which solving equations is needed. Here are some worked examples to demonstrate typical practical situations

Problem 17. Applying the principle of moments to a beam results in the equation
$$F \times 3=(7.5-F) \times 2$$
where $F$ is the force in newtons. Determine the value of $F$

Removing brackets gives $\quad 3 F=15-2 F$
Rearranging gives $\quad 3 F+2 F=15$
i.e.
$$5 F=15$$

Dividing both sides by 5 gives $\frac{5 F}{5}=\frac{15}{5}$
from which, force, $\boldsymbol{F}=\mathbf{3 N}$.
Problem 18. A copper wire has a length $L$ of 1.5 km , a resistance $R$ of $5 \Omega$ and a resistivity of $17.2 \times 10^{-6} \Omega \mathrm{~mm}$. Find the cross-sectional area, $a$, of the wire, given that $R=\frac{\rho L}{a}$

Since $R=\frac{\rho L}{a}$ then
$$5 \Omega=\frac{\left(17.2 \times 10^{-6} \Omega \mathrm{~mm}\right)\left(1500 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{~mm}\right)}{a} .$$

From the units given, $a$ is measured in $\mathrm{mm}^{2}$.
Thus, $5 a=17.2 \times 10^{-6} \times 1500 \times 10^{3}$
and
$$\begin{aligned}
a & =\frac{17.2 \times 10^{-6} \times 1500 \times 10^{3}}{5} \\
& =\frac{17.2 \times 1500 \times 10^{3}}{10^{6} \times 5}=\frac{17.2 \times 15}{10 \times 5}=5.16
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, the cross-sectional area of the wire is $\mathbf{5 . 1 6} \mathbf{~ m m}^{2}$.

Problem 19. $P V=m R T$ is the characteristic gas equation. Find the value of gas constant $R$ when pressure $P=3 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{~Pa}$, volume $V=0.90 \mathrm{~m}^{3}$, mass $m=2.81 \mathrm{~kg}$ and temperature $T=231 \mathrm{~K}$

Dividing both sides of $P V=m R T$ by $m T$ gives
$$\frac{P V}{m T}=\frac{m R T}{m T}$$

Cancelling gives $\quad \frac{P V}{m T}=R$
Substituting values gives $\quad R=\frac{\left(3 \times 10^{6}\right)(0.90)}{(2.81)(231)}$

Using a calculator, gas constant, $R=\mathbf{4 1 6 0} \mathbf{J} /(\mathrm{kg} \mathrm{K})$, correct to 4 significant figures.

Problem 20. A rectangular box with square ends has its length 15 cm greater than its breadth and the total length of its edges is 2.04 m . Find the width of the box and its volume

Let $x \mathrm{~cm}=$ width $=$ height of box. Then the length of the box is $(x+15) \mathrm{cm}$, as shown in Figure 11.1.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-091.jpg?height=356&width=427&top_left_y=755&top_left_x=443)

\section*{Figure 11.1}

The length of the edges of the box is $2(4 x)+4(x+15)$ cm , which equals 2.04 m or 204 cm .

Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
204 & =2(4 x)+4(x+15) \\
204 & =8 x+4 x+60 \\
204-60 & =12 x \\
144 & =12 x \\
x & =12 \mathrm{~cm}
\end{aligned}$$
i.e.
and
Hence, the width of the box is 12 cm .
Volume of box $=$ length $\times$ width $\times$ height
$$\begin{aligned}
& =(x+15)(x)(x)=(12+15)(12)(12) \\
& =(27)(12)(12) \\
& =\mathbf{3 8 8 8} \mathbf{c m}^{\mathbf{3}}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 21. The temperature coefficient of resistance $\alpha$ may be calculated from the formula $R_{t}=R_{0}(1+\alpha t)$. Find $\alpha$, given $R_{t}=0.928$, $R_{0}=0.80$ and $t=40$

Since $R_{t}=R_{0}(1+\alpha t)$, then
$$\begin{aligned}
0.928 & =0.80[1+\alpha(40)] \\
0.928 & =0.80+(0.8)(\alpha)(40) \\
0.928-0.80 & =32 \alpha \\
0.128 & =32 \alpha
\end{aligned}$$

Hence,
$$\alpha=\frac{0.128}{32}=\mathbf{0 . 0 0 4}$$

Problem 22. The distance $s$ metres travelled in time $t$ seconds is given by the formula $s=u t+\frac{1}{2} a t^{2}$, where $u$ is the initial velocity in $\mathrm{m} / \mathrm{s}$ and $a$ is the acceleration in $\mathrm{m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$. Find the acceleration of the body if it travels 168 m in 6 s , with an initial velocity of $10 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$
$$s=u t+\frac{1}{2} a t^{2}, \text { and } s=168, u=10 \text { and } t=6$$

Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
168 & =(10)(6)+\frac{1}{2} a(6)^{2} \\
168 & =60+18 a \\
168-60 & =18 a \\
108 & =18 a \\
a=\frac{108}{18} & =6
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, the acceleration of the body is $\mathbf{6} \mathbf{m} / \mathrm{s}^{\mathbf{2}}$.
Problem 23. When three resistors in an electrical circuit are connected in parallel the total resistance $R_{T}$ is given by $\frac{1}{R_{T}}=\frac{1}{R_{1}}+\frac{1}{R_{2}}+\frac{1}{R_{3}}$. Find the total resistance when $R_{1}=5 \Omega, R_{2}=10 \Omega$ and $R_{3}=30 \Omega$
$$\frac{1}{R_{T}}=\frac{1}{5}+\frac{1}{10}+\frac{1}{30}=\frac{6+3+1}{30}=\frac{10}{30}=\frac{1}{3}$$

Taking the reciprocal of both sides gives $\boldsymbol{R}_{\boldsymbol{T}}=\mathbf{3} \boldsymbol{\Omega}$ Alternatively, if $\frac{1}{R_{T}}=\frac{1}{5}+\frac{1}{10}+\frac{1}{30}$, the LCM of the denominators is $30 R_{T}$.

Hence, $30 R_{T}\left(\frac{1}{R_{T}}\right)=30 R_{T}\left(\frac{1}{5}\right)$
$$+30 R_{T}\left(\frac{1}{10}\right)+30 R_{T}\left(\frac{1}{30}\right) .$$

Cancelling gives $\quad 30=6 R_{T}+3 R_{T}+R_{T}$ i.e.
$$30=10 R_{T}$$
and
$$\boldsymbol{R}_{T}=\frac{30}{10}=\mathbf{3} \boldsymbol{\Omega}, \text { as above. }$$

1. A formula used for calculating resistance of a cable is $R=\frac{\rho L}{a}$. Given $R=1.25, L=2500$ and $a=2 \times 10^{-4}$, find the value of $\rho$.

4. When three resistors $R_{1}, R_{2}$ and $R_{3}$ are connected in parallel, the total resistance $R_{T}$ is determined from $\frac{1}{R_{T}}=\frac{1}{R_{1}}+\frac{1}{R_{2}}+\frac{1}{R_{3}}$
(a) Find the total resistance when $R_{1}=3 \Omega, R_{2}=6 \Omega$ and $R_{3}=18 \Omega$.
(b) Find the value of $R_{3}$ given that $R_{T}=3 \Omega, R_{1}=5 \Omega$ and $R_{2}=10 \Omega$.
5. Six digital camera batteries and 3 camcorder batteries cost $Â£ 96$. If a camcorder battery costs $Â£ 5$ more than a digital camera battery, find the cost of each.
6. Ohm's law may be represented by $I=V / R$, where $I$ is the current in amperes, $V$ is the voltage in volts and $R$ is the resistance in ohms. A soldering iron takes a current of 0.30 A from a 240 V supply. Find the resistance of the element.
7. The distance, $s$, travelled in time $t$ seconds is given by the formula $s=u t+\frac{1}{2} a t^{2}$ where $u$ is the initial velocity in $\mathrm{m} / \mathrm{s}$ and $a$ is the acceleration in $\mathrm{m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$. Calculate the acceleration of the body if it travels 165 m in 3 s , with an initial velocity of $10 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$.

Here are some further worked examples on solving simple equations in practical situations.

Problem 24. The extension $x \mathrm{~m}$ of an aluminium tie bar of length $l \mathrm{~m}$ and cross-sectional area $A \mathrm{~m}^{2}$ when carrying a load of $F$ Newtons is given by the modulus of elasticity $E=F l / A x$. Find the extension of the tie bar (in mm) if $E=70 \times 10^{9} \mathrm{~N} / \mathrm{m}^{2}, F=20 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{~N}, A=0.1 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$ and $l=1.4 \mathrm{~m}$
$E=F l / A x$, hence
$$70 \times 10^{9} \frac{\mathrm{~N}}{\mathrm{~m}^{2}}=\frac{\left(20 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{~N}\right)(1.4 \mathrm{~m})}{\left(0.1 \mathrm{~m}^{2}\right)(x)}$$
(the unit of $x$ is thus metres)
$$\begin{aligned}
70 \times 10^{9} \times 0.1 \times x & =20 \times 10^{6} \times 1.4 \\
x & =\frac{20 \times 10^{6} \times 1.4}{70 \times 10^{9} \times 0.1}
\end{aligned}$$

Cancelling gives
$$\begin{aligned}
x & =\frac{2 \times 1.4}{7 \times 100} \mathrm{~m} \\
& =\frac{2 \times 1.4}{7 \times 100} \times 1000 \mathrm{~mm} \\
& =4 \mathrm{~mm}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, the extension of the tie bar, $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{4} \mathbf{~ m m}$.
Problem 25. Power in a d.c. circuit is given by $P=\frac{V^{2}}{R}$ where $V$ is the supply voltage and $R$ is the circuit resistance. Find the supply voltage if the circuit resistance is $1.25 \Omega$ and the power measured is 320 W

Since $P=\frac{V^{2}}{R}, \quad$ then $\quad 320=\frac{V^{2}}{1.25}$
$$\begin{aligned}
(320)(1.25) & =V^{2} \\
V^{2} & =400
\end{aligned}$$
i.e.

Supply voltage,
$$V=\sqrt{400}= \pm 20 \mathrm{~V}$$

Problem 26. A painter is paid $Â£ 6.30$ per hour for a basic 36 hour week and overtime is paid at one and a third times this rate. Determine how many hours the painter has to work in a week to earn $Â£ 319.20$

Basic rate per hour $=Â£ 6.30$ and overtime rate per hour
$$=1 \frac{1}{3} \times Â£ 6.30=Â£ 8.40$$

Let the number of overtime hours worked $=x$
Then,
$$\begin{aligned}
(36)(6.30)+(x)(8.40) & =319.20 \\
226.80+8.40 x & =319.20
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
8.40 x & =319.20-226.80=92.40 \\
x & =\frac{92.40}{8.40}=11
\end{aligned}$$

Thus, 11 hours overtime would have to be worked to earn $Â£ 319.20$ per week. Hence, the total number of hours worked is $36+11$, i.e. $\mathbf{4 7}$ hours.

Problem 27. A formula relating initial and final states of pressures, $P_{1}$ and $P_{2}$, volumes, $V_{1}$ and $V_{2}$, and absolute temperatures, $T_{1}$ and $T_{2}$, of an ideal gas is $\frac{P_{1} V_{1}}{T_{1}}=\frac{P_{2} V_{2}}{T_{2}}$. Find the value of $P_{2}$ given $P_{1}=100 \times 10^{3}, V_{1}=1.0, V_{2}=0.266, T_{1}=423$ and $T_{2}=293$

Since $\frac{P_{1} V_{1}}{T_{1}}=\frac{P_{2} V_{2}}{T_{2}}$
then $\frac{\left(100 \times 10^{3}\right)(1.0)}{423}=\frac{P_{2}(0.266)}{293}$
Cross-multiplying gives
$$\begin{aligned}
\left(100 \times 10^{3}\right)(1.0)(293) & =P_{2}(0.266)(423) \\
& P_{2}
\end{aligned}=\frac{\left(100 \times 10^{3}\right)(1.0)(293)}{(0.266)(423)}, \mathbf{P}_{\mathbf{2}}=\mathbf{2 6 0 \times \mathbf { 1 0 } ^ { \mathbf { 3 } } \text { or } \mathbf { 2 . 6 } \times \mathbf { 1 0 } ^ { \mathbf { 5 } } .}$$

Problem 28. The stress, $f$, in a material of a thick cylinder can be obtained from $\frac{D}{d}=\sqrt{\left(\frac{f+p}{f-p}\right)}$. Calculate the stress, given that $D=21.5, d=10.75$ and $p=1800$

Since $\frac{D}{d}=\sqrt{\left(\frac{f+p}{f-p}\right)}$ then $\frac{21.5}{10.75}=\sqrt{\left(\frac{f+1800}{f-1800}\right)}$ i.e.

Squaring both sides gives
$$\begin{aligned}
2 & =\sqrt{\left(\frac{f+1800}{f-1800}\right)} \\
4 & =\frac{f+1800}{f-1800}
\end{aligned}$$

Cross-multiplying gives
$$\begin{aligned}
4(f-1800) & =f+1800 \\
4 f-7200 & =f+1800 \\
4 f-f & =1800+7200 \\
3 f & =9000 \\
f & =\frac{9000}{3}=3000
\end{aligned}$$

Hence,
stress, $f=3000$

Problem 29. 12 workmen employed on a building site earn between them a total of $Â£ 4035$ per week. Labourers are paid $Â£ 275$ per week and craftsmen are paid $Â£ 380$ per week. How many craftsmen and how many labourers are employed?

Let the number of craftsmen be $c$. The number of labourers is therefore $(12-c)$.

The wage bill equation is
$$\begin{aligned}
380 c+275(12-c) & =4035 \\
380 c+3300-275 c & =4035 \\
380 c-275 c & =4035-3300 \\
105 c & =735 \\
c & =\frac{735}{105}=7
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, there are 7 craftsmen and ( $12-7$ ), i.e. 5 labourers on the site.

\section*{Chapter 12}

\section*{Transposing formulae}

\subsection*{12.1 Introduction}

In the formula $I=\frac{V}{R}, I$ is called the subject of the formula.
Similarly, in the formula $y=m x+c, y$ is the subject of the formula.
When a symbol other than the subject is required to be the subject, the formula needs to be rearranged to make a new subject. This rearranging process is called transposing the formula or transposition.
For example, in the above formulae,
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { if } I & =\frac{V}{R} \text { then } V=I R \\
\text { and if } y & =m x+c \text { then } x=\frac{y-c}{m}
\end{aligned}$$

How did we arrive at these transpositions? This is the purpose of this chapter - to show how to transpose formulae. A great many equations occur in engineering and it is essential that we can transpose them when needed.

\subsection*{12.2 Transposing formulae}

There are no new rules for transposing formulae. The same rules as were used for simple equations in Chapter 11 are used; i.e., the balance of an equation must be maintained: whatever is done to one side of an equation must be done to the other.
It is best that you cover simple equations before trying this chapter.
Here are some worked examples to help understanding of transposing formulae.

\footnotetext{
Problem 1. Transpose $p=q+r+s$ to make $r$ the subject
}

The object is to obtain $r$ on its own on the LHS of the equation. Changing the equation around so that $r$ is on the LHS gives
$$q+r+s=p$$

From Chapter 11 on simple equations, a term can be moved from one side of an equation to the other side as long as the sign is changed.
Rearranging gives $r=\boldsymbol{p} \boldsymbol{q} \boldsymbol{q} \boldsymbol{s}$.
Mathematically, we have subtracted $q+s$ from both sides of equation (1).

Problem 2. If $a+b=w-x+y$, express $x$ as the subject

As stated in Problem 1, a term can be moved from one side of an equation to the other side but with a change of sign.
Hence, rearranging gives $x=w+y-a-b$

Problem 3. Transpose $v=f \lambda$ to make $\lambda$ the subject
$v=f \lambda$ relates velocity $v$, frequency $f$ and wavelength $\lambda$

Rearranging gives
$$f \lambda=v$$

Dividing both sides by $f$ gives $\frac{f \lambda}{f}=\frac{v}{f}$
Cancelling gives
$$\lambda=\frac{v}{f}$$

Problem 4. When a body falls freely through a height $h$, the velocity $v$ is given by $v^{2}=2 g h$. Express this formula with $h$ as the subject

Rearranging gives
$$2 g h=v^{2}$$

Dividing both sides by $2 g$ gives $\frac{2 g h}{2 g}=\frac{v^{2}}{2 g}$
Cancelling gives
$$h=\frac{v^{2}}{2 g}$$

Problem 5. If $I=\frac{V}{R}$, rearrange to make $V$ the subject
$I=\frac{V}{R}$ is Ohm's law, where $I$ is the current, $V$ is the voltage and $R$ is the resistance.

Rearranging gives
$$\frac{V}{R}=I$$

Multiplying both sides by $R$ gives $\quad R\left(\frac{V}{R}\right)=R(I)$
Cancelling gives
$$V=I R$$

Problem 6. Transpose $a=\frac{F}{m}$ for $m$
$a=\frac{F}{m}$ relates acceleration $a$, force $F$ and mass $m$.
Rearranging gives
$$\frac{F}{m}=a$$

Multiplying both sides by $m$ gives $m\left(\frac{F}{m}\right)=m(a)$

Cancelling gives
Rearranging gives
Dividing both sides by $a$ gives
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
F & =m a \\
m a & =F
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
\frac{m a}{a} & =\frac{F}{a} \\
\boldsymbol{m} & =\frac{\boldsymbol{F}}{\boldsymbol{a}}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 7. Rearrange the formula $R=\frac{\rho L}{A}$ to make (a) $A$ the subject and (b) $L$ the subject
$R=\frac{\rho L}{A}$ relates resistance $R$ of a conductor, resistivity $\rho$, conductor length $L$ and conductor cross-sectional area $A$.
(a) Rearranging gives
$$\frac{\rho L}{A}=R$$

Multiplying both sides by $A$ gives
$$A\left(\frac{\rho L}{A}\right)=A(R)$$

Cancelling gives
$$\rho L=A R$$

Rearranging gives
$$A R=\rho l$$

Dividing both sides by $R$ gives
$$\frac{A R}{R}=\frac{\rho L}{R}$$

Cancelling gives
$$A=\frac{\rho L}{R}$$
(b) Multiplying both sides of $\frac{\rho L}{A}=R$ by $A$ gives
$$\rho L=A R$$

Dividing both sides by $\rho$ gives $\frac{\rho L}{\rho}=\frac{A R}{\rho}$
Cancelling gives
$$L=\frac{A R}{\rho}$$

Problem 8. Transpose $y=m x+c$ to make $m$ the subject
$y=m x+c$ is the equation of a straight line graph, where $y$ is the vertical axis variable, $x$ is the horizontal axis variable, $m$ is the gradient of the graph and $c$ is the $y$-axis intercept.
Subtracting $c$ from both sides gives
$$y-c=m x$$
or
$$m x=y-c$$

Dividing both sides by $x$ gives
$$m=\frac{y-c}{x}$$

\subsection*{12.3 Further transposing of formulae}

Here are some more transposition examples to help us further understand how more difficult formulae are transposed.

Problem 9. Transpose the formula $v=u+\frac{F t}{m}$ to make $F$ the subject
$v=u+\frac{F t}{m}$ relates final velocity $v$, initial velocity $u$, force $F$, mass $m$ and time $t$. $\frac{F}{m}$ is acceleration $\left.a.\right)$
Rearranging gives $\quad u+\frac{F t}{m}=v$
and
$$\frac{F t}{m}=v-u$$

Multiplying each side by $m$ gives
$$m\left(\frac{F t}{m}\right)=m(v-u)$$

Cancelling gives
$$F t=m(v-u)$$

Dividing both sides by $t$ gives $\frac{F t}{t}=\frac{m(v-u)}{t}$
Cancelling gives $F=\frac{\boldsymbol{m}(\boldsymbol{v}-\boldsymbol{u})}{\boldsymbol{t}}$ or $\boldsymbol{F}=\frac{\boldsymbol{m}}{\boldsymbol{t}}(\boldsymbol{v}-\boldsymbol{u})$
This shows two ways of expressing the answer. There is often more than one way of expressing a transposed answer. In this case, these equations for $F$ are equivalent; neither one is more correct than the other.

Problem 10. The final length $L_{2}$ of a piece of wire heated through $\theta^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ is given by the formula $L_{2}=L_{1}(1+\alpha \theta)$ where $L_{1}$ is the original length. Make the coefficient of expansion $\alpha$ the subject

Rearranging gives
$$L_{1}(1+\alpha \theta)=L_{2}$$

Removing the bracket gives
$$L_{1}+L_{1} \alpha \theta=L_{2}$$

Rearranging gives
$$L_{1} \alpha \theta=L_{2}-L_{1}$$

Dividing both sides by $L_{1} \theta$ gives $\frac{L_{1} \alpha \theta}{L_{1} \theta}=\frac{L_{2}-L_{1}}{L_{1} \theta}$
Cancelling gives
$$\alpha=\frac{L_{2}-L_{1}}{L_{1} \theta}$$

An alternative method of transposing $L_{2}=L_{1}(1+\alpha \theta)$ for $\alpha$ is:

Dividing both sides by $L_{1}$ gives $\quad \frac{L_{2}}{L_{1}}=1+\alpha \theta$
Subtracting 1 from both sides gives $\frac{L_{2}}{L_{1}}-1=\alpha \theta$
or
$$\begin{aligned}
\alpha \theta & =\frac{L_{2}}{L_{1}}-1 \\
\alpha & =\frac{\frac{\boldsymbol{L}_{\mathbf{2}}}{\boldsymbol{L}_{\mathbf{1}}}-\mathbf{1}}{\boldsymbol{\theta}}
\end{aligned}$$

Dividing both sides by $\theta$ gives
$$L_{1}{ }_{2}$$

The two answers $\alpha=\frac{L_{2}-L_{1}}{L_{1} \theta}$ and $\alpha=\frac{\frac{L_{2}}{L_{1}}-1}{\theta}$ look quite different. They are, however, equivalent. The first answer looks tidier but is no more correct than the second answer.

Problem 11. A formula for the distance $s$ moved by a body is given by $s=\frac{1}{2}(v+u) t$. Rearrange the formula to make $u$ the subject

Rearranging gives
$$\frac{1}{2}(v+u) t=s$$

Multiplying both sides by 2 gives
$$(v+u) t=2 s$$

Dividing both sides by $t$ gives
$$\frac{(v+u) t}{t}=\frac{2 s}{t}$$

Cancelling gives
$$v+u=\frac{2 s}{t}$$

Rearranging gives $\quad u=\frac{2 s}{t}-v$ or $u=\frac{2 s-v t}{t}$

\section*{Problem 12. A formula for kinetic energy is} $k=\frac{1}{2} m v^{2}$. Transpose the formula to make $v$ the subject

Rearranging gives $\quad \frac{1}{2} m v^{2}=k$

Whenever the prospective new subject is a squared term, that term is isolated on the LHS and then the square root of both sides of the equation is taken.

Multiplying both sides by 2 gives
$$m v^{2}=2 k$$

Dividing both sides by $m$ gives
$\frac{m v^{2}}{m}=\frac{2 k}{m}$
Cancelling gives
$$v^{2}=\frac{2 k}{m}$$

Taking the square root of both sides gives
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
\sqrt{v^{2}} & =\sqrt{\left(\frac{2 k}{m}\right)} \\
v & =\sqrt{\left(\frac{2 k}{\boldsymbol{m}}\right)}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 13. In a right-angled triangle having sides $x, y$ and hypotenuse $z$, Pythagoras' theorem states $z^{2}=x^{2}+y^{2}$. Transpose the formula to find $x$

Rearranging gives
$$\begin{aligned}
x^{2}+y^{2} & =z^{2} \\
x^{2} & =z^{2}-y^{2}
\end{aligned}$$
and
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-099.jpg?height=23&width=26&top_left_y=1374&top_left_x=303)

Taking the square root of both sides gives
$$x=\sqrt{z^{2}-y^{2}}$$

Problem 14. Transpose $y=\frac{M L^{2}}{8 E I}$ to make $L$ the subject

Multiplying both sides by $8 E I$ gives
$$8 E I y=M L^{2}$$

Dividing both sides by $M$ gives
$$\begin{aligned}
\frac{8 E I y}{M} & =L^{2} \\
L^{2} & =\frac{8 E I y}{M}
\end{aligned}$$
or
or
Taking the square root of both sides gives
i.e.
$$\sqrt{L^{2}}=\sqrt{\frac{8 E I y}{M}}$$
$$L=\sqrt{\frac{8 E I y}{M}}$$

Problem 15. Given $t=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{l}{g}}$, find $g$ in terms of $t, l$ and $\pi$

Whenever the prospective new subject is within a square root sign, it is best to isolate that term on the LHS and then to square both sides of the equation.

Rearranging gives
$$2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{l}{g}}=t$$

Dividing both sides by $2 \pi$ gives
$$\sqrt{\frac{l}{g}}=\frac{t}{2 \pi}$$

Squaring both sides gives
$$\frac{l}{g}=\left(\frac{t}{2 \pi}\right)^{2}=\frac{t^{2}}{4 \pi^{2}}$$

Cross-multiplying, (i.e. multiplying each term by $4 \pi^{2} g$ ), gives
$$4 \pi^{2} l=g t^{2}$$
or
$$g t^{2}=4 \pi^{2} l$$

Dividing both sides by $t^{2}$ gives
$$\frac{g t^{2}}{t^{2}}=\frac{4 \pi^{2} l}{t^{2}}$$

Cancelling gives
$$g=\frac{4 \pi^{2} l}{t^{2}}$$

Problem 16. The impedance $Z$ of an a.c. circuit is given by $Z=\sqrt{R^{2}+X^{2}}$ where $R$ is the resistance. Make the reactance, $X$, the subject

Rearranging gives
$$\sqrt{R^{2}+X^{2}}=Z$$

Squaring both sides gives
$$R^{2}+X^{2}=Z^{2}$$

Rearranging gives
$$X^{2}=Z^{2}-R^{2}$$

Taking the square root of both sides gives
$$X=\sqrt{Z^{2}-R^{2}}$$

Problem 17. The volume $V$ of a hemisphere of radius $r$ is given by $V=\frac{2}{3} \pi r^{3}$. (a) Find $r$ in terms of $V$. (b) Evaluate the radius when $V=32 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$
(a) Rearranging gives
$$\frac{2}{3} \pi r^{3}=V$$

Multiplying both sides by 3 gives $2 \pi r^{3}=3 \mathrm{~V}$
Dividing both sides by $2 \pi$ gives $\quad \frac{2 \pi r^{3}}{2 \pi}=\frac{3 V}{2 \pi}$
Cancelling gives
$$r^{3}=\frac{3 V}{2 \pi}$$

Taking the cube root of both sides gives
$$\begin{aligned}
\sqrt[3]{r^{3}} & =\sqrt[3]{\left(\frac{3 V}{2 \pi}\right)} \\
\text { i.e. } \quad r & =\sqrt[3]{\left(\frac{\mathbf{3 V}}{\mathbf{2 \pi}}\right)}
\end{aligned}$$
(b) When $V=32 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$,
$$\text { radius } r=\sqrt[3]{\left(\frac{3 V}{2 \pi}\right)}=\sqrt[3]{\left(\frac{3 \times 32}{2 \pi}\right)}=2.48 \mathrm{~cm}$$

\subsection*{12.4 More difficult transposing of formulae}

Here are some more transposition examples to help us further understand how more difficult formulae are transposed.

Problem 18. (a) Transpose $S=\sqrt{\frac{3 d(L-d)}{8}}$ to make $l$ the subject. (b) Evaluate $L$ when $d=1.65$ and $S=0.82$

The formula $S=\sqrt{\frac{3 d(L-d)}{8}}$ represents the sag $S$ at the centre of a wire.
(a) Squaring both sides gives $\quad S^{2}=\frac{3 d(L-d)}{8}$

Multiplying both sides by 8 gives
$$8 S^{2}=3 d(L-d)$$

Dividing both sides by $3 d$ gives $\frac{8 S^{2}}{3 d}=L-d$
Rearranging gives
$$L=d+\frac{8 S^{2}}{3 d}$$
(b) When $d=1.65$ and $S=0.82$,
$$\boldsymbol{L}=d+\frac{8 S^{2}}{3 d}=1.65+\frac{8 \times 0.82^{2}}{3 \times 1.65}=\mathbf{2 . 7 3 7}$$

Problem 19. Transpose the formula $p=\frac{a^{2} x^{2}+a^{2} y}{r}$ to make $a$ the subject

Rearranging give
$$\frac{a^{2} x^{2}+a^{2} y}{r}=p$$

Multiplying both sides by $r$ gives
$$a^{2} x+a^{2} y=r p$$

Factorizing the LHS gives
$$a^{2}(x+y)=r p$$

Dividing both sides by $(x+y)$ gives
$$\frac{a^{2}(x+y)}{(x+y)}=\frac{r p}{(x+y)}$$

Cancelling gives
$$a^{2}=\frac{r p}{(x+y)}$$

Taking the square root of both sides gives
$$a=\sqrt{\left(\frac{r p}{x+y}\right)}$$

Whenever the letter required as the new subject occurs more than once in the original formula, after rearranging, factorizing will always be needed.

Problem 20. Make $b$ the subject of the formula $a=\frac{x-y}{\sqrt{b d+b e}}$

Rearranging gives
$$\frac{x-y}{\sqrt{b d+b e}}=a$$

Multiplying both sides by $\sqrt{b d+b e}$ gives
$$x-y=a \sqrt{b d+b e}$$
or
$$a \sqrt{b d+b e}=x-y$$

Dividing both sides by $a$ gives $\sqrt{b d+b e}=\frac{x-y}{a}$
Squaring both sides gives $\quad b d+b e=\left(\frac{x-y}{a}\right)^{2}$
Factorizing the LHS gives $\quad b(d+e)=\left(\frac{x-y}{a}\right)^{2}$
Dividing both sides by $(d+e)$ gives
$$b=\frac{\left(\frac{x-y}{a}\right)^{2}}{(d+e)} \quad \text { or } \quad b=\frac{(x-y)^{2}}{a^{2}(d+e)}$$

Problem 21. If $a=\frac{b}{1+b}$, make $b$ the subject of the formula

Rearranging gives
$$\frac{b}{1+b}=a$$

Multiplying both sides by $(1+b)$ gives
$$\begin{aligned}
& b=a(1+b) \\
& b=a+a b
\end{aligned}$$

Removing the bracket gives
Rearranging to obtain terms in $b$ on the LHS gives
$$b-a b=a$$

Factorizing the LHS gives
$$b(1-a)=a$$

Dividing both sides by $(1-a)$ gives $\boldsymbol{b}=\frac{\boldsymbol{a}}{\mathbf{1 - a}}$

Problem 22. Transpose the formula $V=\frac{E r}{R+r}$ to make $r$ the subject

Rearranging gives
$$\frac{E r}{R+r}=V$$

Multiplying both sides by $(R+r)$ gives
$$\begin{aligned}
& E r=V(R+r) \\
& E r=V R+V r
\end{aligned}$$

Removing the bracket gives
Rearranging to obtain terms in $r$ on the LHS gives
$$\begin{aligned}
E r-V r & =V R \\
r(E-V) & =V R
\end{aligned}$$

Factorizing gives
Dividing both sides by $(E-V)$ gives $\quad \boldsymbol{r}=\frac{\boldsymbol{V} \boldsymbol{R}}{\boldsymbol{E}-\boldsymbol{V}}$

Problem 23. Transpose the formula $y=\frac{p q^{2}}{r+q^{2}}-t$ to make $q$ the subject

Rearranging gives $\frac{p q^{2}}{r+q^{2}}-t=y$ and
$$\frac{p q^{2}}{r+q^{2}}=y+t$$

Multiplying both sides by $\left(r+q^{2}\right)$ gives
$$p q^{2}=\left(r+q^{2}\right)(y+t)$$

Removing brackets gives $p q^{2}=r y+r t+q^{2} y+q^{2} t$
Rearranging to obtain terms in $q$ on the LHS gives
$$p q^{2}-q^{2} y-q^{2} t=r y+r t$$

Factorizing gives $q^{2}(p-y-t)=r(y+t)$
Dividing both sides by $(p-y-t)$ gives
$$q^{2}=\frac{r(y+t)}{(p-y-t)}$$

Taking the square root of both sides gives
$$q=\sqrt{\left(\frac{r(y+t)}{p-y-t}\right)}$$

Problem 24. Given that $\frac{D}{d}=\sqrt{\left(\frac{f+p}{f-p}\right)}$ express $p$ in terms of $D, d$ and $f$

Rearranging gives $\quad \sqrt{\left(\frac{f+p}{f-p}\right)}=\frac{D}{d}$
Squaring both sides gives $\quad\left(\frac{f+p}{f-p}\right)=\frac{D^{2}}{d^{2}}$
Cross-multiplying, i.e. multiplying each term by $d^{2}(f-p)$, gives
$$d^{2}(f+p)=D^{2}(f-p)$$

Removing brackets gives $\quad d^{2} f+d^{2} p=D^{2} f-D^{2} p$
Rearranging, to obtain terms in $p$ on the LHS gives
$$d^{2} p+D^{2} p=D^{2} f-d^{2} f$$

Factorizing gives
$$p\left(d^{2}+D^{2}\right)=f\left(D^{2}-d^{2}\right)$$

Dividing both sides by $\left(d^{2}+D^{2}\right)$ gives
$$p=\frac{f\left(D^{2}-d^{2}\right)}{\left(d^{2}+D^{2}\right)}$$

Make the symbol indicated the subject of each of the formulae shown in problems 1 to 7 and express each in its simplest form.

\section*{Chapter 13}

\section*{Solving simultaneous equations}

\subsection*{13.1 Introduction}

Only one equation is necessary when finding the value of a single unknown quantity (as with simple equations in Chapter 11). However, when an equation contains two unknown quantities it has an infinite number of solutions. When two equations are available connecting the same two unknown values then a unique solution is possible. Similarly, for three unknown quantities it is necessary to have three equations in order to solve for a particular value of each of the unknown quantities, and so on.

Equations which have to be solved together to find the unique values of the unknown quantities, which are true for each of the equations, are called simultaneous equations.
Two methods of solving simultaneous equations analytically are:
(a) by substitution, and
(b) by elimination.
(A graphical solution of simultaneous equations is shown in Chapter 19.)

\subsection*{13.2 Solving simultaneous equations in two unknowns}

The method of solving simultaneous equations is demonstrated in the following worked problems.

Problem 1. Solve the following equations for $x$ and $y$, (a) by substitution and (b) by elimination
$$\begin{aligned}
x+2 y & =-1 \\
4 x-3 y & =18
\end{aligned}$$
(a) By substitution

From equation (1): $x=-1-2 y$
Substituting this expression for $x$ into equation (2) gives
$$4(-1-2 y)-3 y=18$$

This is now a simple equation in $y$. Removing the bracket gives
$$\begin{aligned}
-4-8 y-3 y & =18 \\
-11 y & =18+4=22 \\
y & =\frac{22}{-11}=-2
\end{aligned}$$

Substituting $y=-2$ into equation (1) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
x+2(-2) & =-1 \\
x-4 & =-1 \\
x & =-1+4=3
\end{aligned}$$

Thus, $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{3}$ and $\boldsymbol{y}=\mathbf{- 2}$ is the solution to the simultaneous equations.
Check: in equation (2), since $x=3$ and $y=-2$,
$$\mathrm{LHS}=4(3)-3(-2)=12+6=18=\text { RHS }$$
(b) By elimination
$$\begin{aligned}
x+2 y & =-1 \\
4 x-3 y & =18
\end{aligned}$$

If equation (1) is multiplied throughout by 4 , the coefficient of $x$ will be the same as in equation (2), giving
$$4 x+8 y=-4$$

Subtracting equation (3) from equation (2) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
& 4 x-3 y=18 \\
& 4 x+8 y=-4 \\
& \hline 0-11 y=22 \\
& \hline
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $\quad \boldsymbol{y}=\frac{22}{-11}=-\mathbf{2}$
(Note: in the above subtraction,
$$18--4=18+4=22 .)$$

Substituting $y=-2$ into either equation (1) or equation (2) will give $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{3}$ as in method (a). The solution $x=3, y=-2$ is the only pair of values that satisfies both of the original equations.

Problem 2. Solve, by a substitution method, the simultaneous equations
$$\begin{aligned}
3 x-2 y & =12 \\
x+3 y & =-7
\end{aligned}$$

From equation (2), $x=-7-3 y$
Substituting for $x$ in equation (1) gives
$$3(-7-3 y)-2 y=12$$
i.e.
$$\begin{array}{r}
-21-9 y-2 y=12 \\
-11 y=12+21=33
\end{array}$$

Hence, $y=\frac{33}{-11}=-3$
Substituting $y=-3$ in equation (2) gives
$$x+3(-3)=-7$$
i.e.
$$x-9=-7$$

Hence
$$x=-7+9=2$$

Thus, $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{2}, \boldsymbol{y}=\mathbf{- 3}$ is the solution of the simultaneous equations. (Such solutions should always be checked
by substituting values into each of the original two equations.)

Problem 3. Use an elimination method to solve the following simultaneous equations
$$\begin{aligned}
& 3 x+4 y=5 \\
& 2 x-5 y=-12
\end{aligned}$$

If equation (1) is multiplied throughout by 2 and equation (2) by 3 , the coefficient of $x$ will be the same in the newly formed equations. Thus,
$$\begin{array}{lc}
2 \times \text { equation (1) gives } & 6 x+8 y=10 \\
3 \times \text { equation (2) gives } & 6 x-15 y=-36
\end{array}$$

Equation (3) - equation (4) gives
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
& 0+23 y=46 \\
& \boldsymbol{y}=\frac{46}{23}=\mathbf{2}
\end{aligned}$$
$($ Note $+8 y--15 y=8 y+15 y=23 y$ and $10--36=$ $10+36=46$.)

Substituting $y=2$ in equation (1) gives
from which
$$3 x+4(2)=5$$
and
$$3 x=5-8=-3$$
$$x=-1$$

Checking, by substituting $x=-1$ and $y=2$ in equation (2), gives
$$\text { LHS }=2(-1)-5(2)=-2-10=-12=\text { RHS }$$

Hence, $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 1}$ and $\boldsymbol{y}=\mathbf{2}$ is the solution of the simultaneous equations.
The elimination method is the most common method of solving simultaneous equations.

Problem 4. Solve
$$\begin{aligned}
& 7 x-2 y=26 \\
& 6 x+5 y=29
\end{aligned}$$

When equation (1) is multiplied by 5 and equation (2) by 2 , the coefficients of $y$ in each equation are numerically the same, i.e. 10 , but are of opposite sign.
\begin{tabular}{lr}
$5 \times$ equation (1) gives & $35 x-10 y=130$ \\
$2 \times$ equation (2) gives & $12 x+10 y=58$ \\
\begin{tabular}{lr} 
Adding equations (3) \\
and (4) gives
\end{tabular} & $47 x+0=188$ \\
Hence, & $x=\frac{188}{47}=\mathbf{4}$
\end{tabular}

Note that when the signs of common coefficients are different the two equations are added and when the signs of common coefficients are the same the two equations are subtracted (as in Problems 1 and 3).

Substituting $x=4$ in equation (1) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
7(4)-2 y & =26 \\
28-2 y & =26 \\
28-26 & =2 y \\
2 & =2 y \\
y & =\mathbf{1}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence,

Checking, by substituting $x=4$ and $y=1$ in equation (2), gives
$$\mathrm{LHS}=6(4)+5(1)=24+5=29=\text { RHS }$$

Thus, the solution is $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{4}, \boldsymbol{y}=\mathbf{1}$.

\subsection*{13.3 Further solving of simultaneous equations}

Here are some further worked problems on solving simultaneous equations.

Problem 5. Solve
$$\begin{aligned}
& 3 p=2 q \\
& 4 p+q+11=0
\end{aligned}$$

Rearranging gives
$$\begin{aligned}
3 p-2 q & =0 \\
4 p+q & =-11
\end{aligned}$$

Multiplying equation (4) by 2 gives
$$8 p+2 q=-22$$

Adding equations (3) and (5) gives
$$\begin{gathered}
11 p+0=-22 \\
p=\frac{-22}{11}=-2
\end{gathered}$$

Substituting $p=-2$ into equation (1) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
3(-2) & =2 q \\
-6 & =2 q \\
q & =\frac{-6}{2}=-3
\end{aligned}$$

Checking, by substituting $p=-2$ and $q=-3$ into equation (2), gives

LHS $=4(-2)+(-3)+11=-8-3+11=0=$ RHS
Hence, the solution is $\boldsymbol{p}=\mathbf{- 2}, \boldsymbol{q}=\mathbf{- 3}$.

\section*{Problem 6. Solve}
$$\begin{gathered}
\frac{x}{8}+\frac{5}{2}=y \\
13-\frac{y}{3}=3 x
\end{gathered}$$

Whenever fractions are involved in simultaneous equations it is often easier to firstly remove them. Thus, multiplying equation (1) by 8 gives
$$\begin{aligned}
8\left(\frac{x}{8}\right)+8\left(\frac{5}{2}\right) & =8 y \\
\text { i.e. } \quad x+20 & =8 y
\end{aligned}$$

Multiplying equation (2) by 3 gives
$$39-y=9 x$$

Rearranging equations (3) and (4) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
& x-8 y=-20 \\
& 9 x+y=39
\end{aligned}$$

Multiplying equation (6) by 8 gives
$$72 x+8 y=312$$

Adding equations (5) and (7) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
& 73 x+0=292 \\
& x=\frac{292}{73}=4
\end{aligned}$$

Substituting $x=4$ into equation (5) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
4-8 y & =-20 \\
4+20 & =8 y \\
24 & =8 y \\
y=\frac{24}{8} & =\mathbf{3}
\end{aligned}$$

Checking, substituting $x=4$ and $y=3$ in the original equations, gives
(1): LHS $=\frac{4}{8}+\frac{5}{2}=\frac{1}{2}+2 \frac{1}{2}=3=y=$ RHS
(2): LHS $=13-\frac{3}{3}=13-1=12$
$$\text { RHS }=3 x=3(4)=12$$

Hence, the solution is $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{4}, \boldsymbol{y}=\mathbf{3}$.

Problem 7. Solve
$$\begin{aligned}
& 2.5 x+0.75-3 y=0 \\
& 1.6 x=1.08-1.2 y
\end{aligned}$$

It is often easier to remove decimal fractions. Thus, multiplying equations (1) and (2) by 100 gives
$$\begin{aligned}
& 250 x+75-300 y=0 \\
& 160 x=108-120 y
\end{aligned}$$

Rearranging gives
$$\begin{aligned}
& 250 x-300 y=-75 \\
& 160 x+120 y=108
\end{aligned}$$

Multiplying equation (3) by 2 gives
$$500 x-600 y=-150$$

Multiplying equation (4) by 5 gives
$$800 x+600 y=540$$

Adding equations (5) and (6) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
& 1300 x+0=390 \\
& \boldsymbol{x}=\frac{390}{1300}=\frac{39}{130}=\frac{3}{10}=\mathbf{0 . 3}
\end{aligned}$$

Substituting $x=0.3$ into equation (1) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
250(0.3)+75-300 y & =0 \\
75+75 & =300 y \\
150 & =300 y \\
y=\frac{150}{300} & =\mathbf{0 . 5}
\end{aligned}$$

Checking, by substituting $x=0.3$ and $y=0.5$ in equation (2), gives
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { LHS }=160(0.3)=48 \\
& \text { RHS }=108-120(0.5)=108-60=48
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, the solution is $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{0 . 3}, \boldsymbol{y}=\mathbf{0 . 5}$

\subsection*{13.4 Solving more difficult simultaneous equations}

Here are some further worked problems on solving more difficult simultaneous equations.

Problem 8. Solve
$$\begin{aligned}
& \frac{2}{x}+\frac{3}{y}=7 \\
& \frac{1}{x}-\frac{4}{y}=-2
\end{aligned}$$

In this type of equation the solution is easier if a substitution is initially made. Let $\frac{1}{x}=a$ and $\frac{1}{y}=b$
$$\begin{array}{lr}
\text { Thus equation (1) becomes } & 2 a+3 b=7 \\
\text { and equation (2) becomes } & a-4 b=-2
\end{array}$$

Multiplying equation (4) by 2 gives
$$2 a-8 b=-4$$

Subtracting equation (5) from equation (3) gives
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
0+11 b & =11 \\
\boldsymbol{b} & =\mathbf{1}
\end{aligned}$$

Substituting $b=1$ in equation (3) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
2 a+3 & =7 \\
2 a=7-3 & =4 \\
a & =\mathbf{2}
\end{aligned}$$
i.e.

Checking, substituting $a=2$ and $b=1$ in equation (4), gives
$$\mathrm{LHS}=2-4(1)=2-4=-2=\mathrm{RHS}$$

Hence, $\boldsymbol{a}=\mathbf{2}$ and $\boldsymbol{b}=\mathbf{1}$.
However, since $\quad \frac{1}{x}=a, \quad x=\frac{1}{a}=\frac{1}{2}$ or 0.5
and since $\quad \frac{1}{y}=b, \quad y=\frac{1}{b}=\frac{1}{1}=1$
Hence, the solution is $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{0 . 5}, \boldsymbol{y}=\mathbf{1}$.
Problem 9. Solve
$$\begin{aligned}
& \frac{1}{2 a}+\frac{3}{5 b}=4 \\
& \frac{4}{a}+\frac{1}{2 b}=10.5
\end{aligned}$$

Let $\frac{1}{a}=x \quad$ and $\quad \frac{1}{b}=y$
then
$$\begin{aligned}
& \frac{x}{2}+\frac{3}{5} y=4 \\
& 4 x+\frac{1}{2} y=10.5
\end{aligned}$$

To remove fractions, equation (3) is multiplied by 10 , giving
i.e.
$$10\left(\frac{x}{2}\right)+10\left(\frac{3}{5} y\right)=10(4)$$
$$5 x+6 y=40$$

Multiplying equation (4) by 2 gives
$$8 x+y=21$$

Multiplying equation (6) by 6 gives
$$48 x+6 y=126$$

Subtracting equation (5) from equation (7) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
43 x+0 & =86 \\
\boldsymbol{x} & =\frac{86}{43}=\mathbf{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Substituting $x=2$ into equation (3) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
\frac{2}{2}+\frac{3}{5} y & =4 \\
\frac{3}{5} y & =4-1=3 \\
y & =\frac{5}{3}(3)=\mathbf{5}
\end{aligned}$$

Since $\quad \frac{1}{a}=x, a=\frac{1}{x}=\frac{1}{2}$ or 0.5 and since $\frac{1}{b}=y, \quad b=\frac{1}{y}=\frac{1}{5}$ or 0.2

Hence, the solution is $\boldsymbol{a}=\mathbf{0 . 5}, \boldsymbol{b}=\mathbf{0 . 2}$, which may be checked in the original equations.

Problem 10. Solve
$$\begin{aligned}
& \frac{1}{x+y}=\frac{4}{27} \\
& \frac{1}{2 x-y}=\frac{4}{33}
\end{aligned}$$

To eliminate fractions, both sides of equation (1) are multiplied by 27 $(x+y)$, giving
$$27(x+y)\left(\frac{1}{x+y}\right)=27(x+y)\left(\frac{4}{27}\right)$$
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
27(1) & =4(x+y) \\
27 & =4 x+4 y
\end{aligned}$$

Similarly, in equation (2) $33=4(2 x-y)$
i.e.
$$33=8 x-4 y$$

Equation (3) + equation (4) gives
$$60=12 x \text { and } \boldsymbol{x}=\frac{60}{12}=\mathbf{5}$$

Substituting $x=5$ in equation (3) gives
$$27=4(5)+4 y$$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { from which } \quad 4 y=27-20=7 \\
& \text { and } \\
& \boldsymbol{y}=\frac{7}{4}=\mathbf{1} \frac{\mathbf{3}}{4} \text { or } \mathbf{1 . 7 5}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{5}, \boldsymbol{y}=\mathbf{1 . 7 5}$ is the required solution, which may be checked in the original equations.

Problem 11. Solve
$$\begin{aligned}
& \frac{x-1}{3}+\frac{y+2}{5}=\frac{2}{15} \\
& \frac{1-x}{6}+\frac{5+y}{2}=\frac{5}{6}
\end{aligned}$$

Before equations (1) and (2) can be simultaneously solved, the fractions need to be removed and the equations rearranged.

Multiplying equation (1) by 15 gives
$$15\left(\frac{x-1}{3}\right)+15\left(\frac{y+2}{5}\right)=15\left(\frac{2}{15}\right)$$
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
5(x-1)+3(y+2) & =2 \\
5 x-5+3 y+6 & =2 \\
5 x+3 y & =2+5-6 \\
5 x+3 y & =1
\end{aligned}$$

Hence,
$$0$$
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
(1-x)+3(5+y) & =5 \\
1-x+15+3 y & =5 \\
-x+3 y & =5-1-15 \\
-x+3 y & =-11
\end{aligned}$$

Hence,

Thus the initial problem containing fractions can be expressed as
$$\begin{aligned}
5 x+3 y & =1 \\
-x+3 y & =-11
\end{aligned}$$

Subtracting equation (4) from equation (3) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
& 6 x+0=12 \\
& x=\frac{12}{6}=2
\end{aligned}$$

Substituting $x=2$ into equation (3) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
5(2)+3 y & =1 \\
10+3 y & =1 \\
3 y=1-10 & =-9 \\
y=\frac{-9}{3} & =-3
\end{aligned}$$

Checking, substituting $x=2, y=-3$ in equation (4) gives
$$\text { LHS }=-2+3(-3)=-2-9=-11=\text { RHS }$$

Hence, the solution is $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{2}, \boldsymbol{y}=\mathbf{- 3}$.

$\frac{5}{x}-\frac{3}{y}=-2$
$\frac{2}{a}+\frac{5}{b}=-4$
3. $\frac{1}{2 p}+\frac{3}{5 q}=5$
4. $\frac{5}{x}+\frac{3}{y}=1.1$
$\frac{5}{p}-\frac{1}{2 q}=\frac{35}{2}$
$\frac{3}{x}-\frac{7}{y}=-1.1$

$\frac{1-c}{5}+\frac{3-d}{4}+\frac{13}{20}=0$

6. $\frac{3 r+2}{5}-\frac{2 s-1}{4}=\frac{11}{5}$ $\frac{4}{2 x-y}=\frac{16}{33}$
$\frac{3+2 r}{4}+\frac{5-s}{3}=\frac{15}{4}$
8. If $5 x-\frac{3}{y}=1$ and $x+\frac{4}{y}=\frac{5}{2}$, find the value of $\frac{x y+1}{y}$

\subsection*{13.5 Practical problems involving simultaneous equations}

There are a number of situations in engineering and science in which the solution of simultaneous equations is required. Some are demonstrated in the following worked problems.

Problem 12. The law connecting friction $F$ and load $L$ for an experiment is of the form $F=a L+b$ where $a$ and $b$ are constants. When $F=5.6 \mathrm{~N}$, $L=8.0 \mathrm{~N}$ and when $F=4.4 N, L=2.0 \mathrm{~N}$. Find the values of $a$ and $b$ and the value of $F$ when $L=6.5 \mathrm{~N}$

Substituting $F=5.6$ and $L=8.0$ into $F=a L+b$ gives
$$5.6=8.0 a+b$$

Substituting $F=4.4$ and $L=2.0$ into $F=a L+b$ gives
$$4.4=2.0 a+b$$

Subtracting equation (2) from equation (1) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
1.2 & =6.0 a \\
\boldsymbol{a} & =\frac{1.2}{6.0}=\frac{\mathbf{1}}{\mathbf{5}} \text { or } \mathbf{0 . 2}
\end{aligned}$$

Substituting $a=\frac{1}{5}$ into equation (1) gives
$$\begin{gathered}
5.6=8.0\left(\frac{1}{5}\right)+b \\
5.6=1.6+b \\
5.6-1.6=b \\
\quad \boldsymbol{b}=\mathbf{4}
\end{gathered}$$
i.e.

Checking, substituting $a=\frac{1}{5}$ and $b=4$ in equation (2), gives
$$\mathrm{RHS}=2.0\left(\frac{1}{5}\right)+4=0.4+4=4.4=\mathrm{LHS}$$

Hence, $\boldsymbol{a}=\frac{\mathbf{1}}{\mathbf{5}}$ and $\boldsymbol{b}=\mathbf{4}$
When $L=6.5, F=a L+b=\frac{1}{5}(6.5)+4=1.3+4$, i.e. $\boldsymbol{F}=\mathbf{5 . 3 0} \mathrm{N}$.

Problem 13. The equation of a straight line, of gradient $m$ and intercept on the $y$-axis $c$, is $y=m x+c$. If a straight line passes through the point where $x=1$ and $y=-2$, and also through the point where $x=3.5$ and $y=10.5$, find the values of the gradient and the $y$-axis intercept

Substituting $x=1$ and $y=-2$ into $y=m x+c$ gives
$$-2=m+c$$

Substituting $x=3.5$ and $y=10.5$ into $y=m x+c$ gives
$$10.5=3.5 m+c$$

Subtracting equation (1) from equation (2) gives
$$12.5=2.5 m, \quad \text { from which, } \quad \boldsymbol{m}=\frac{12.5}{2.5}=\mathbf{5}$$

Substituting $m=5$ into equation (1) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
-2 & =5+c \\
\boldsymbol{c} & =-2-5=-7
\end{aligned}$$

Checking, substituting $m=5$ and $c=-7$ in equation (2), gives
$$\text { RHS }=(3.5)(5)+(-7)=17.5-7=10.5=\text { LHS }$$

Hence, the gradient $\boldsymbol{m}=5$ and the $\boldsymbol{y}$-axis intercept $c=-7$.

Problem 14. When Kirchhoff's laws are applied to the electrical circuit shown in Figure 13.1, the currents $I_{1}$ and $I_{2}$ are connected by the equations
$$\begin{aligned}
27 & =1.5 I_{1}+8\left(I_{1}-I_{2}\right) \\
-26 & =2 I_{2}-8\left(I_{1}-I_{2}\right)
\end{aligned}$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-110.jpg?height=351&width=592&top_left_y=1929&top_left_x=336)

Figure 13.1
Solve the equations to find the values of currents $I_{1}$ and $I_{2}$

Removing the brackets from equation (1) gives
$$27=1.5 I_{1}+8 I_{1}-8 I_{2}$$

Rearranging gives
$$9.5 I_{1}-8 I_{2}=27$$

Removing the brackets from equation (2) gives
$$-26=2 I_{2}-8 I_{1}+8 I_{2}$$

Rearranging gives
$$-8 I_{1}+10 I_{2}=-26$$

Multiplying equation (3) by 5 gives
$$47.5 I_{1}-40 I_{2}=135$$

Multiplying equation (4) by 4 gives
$$-32 I_{1}+40 I_{2}=-104$$

Adding equations (5) and (6) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
& 15.5 I_{1}+0=31 \\
& \boldsymbol{I}_{\mathbf{1}}=\frac{31}{15.5}=\mathbf{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Substituting $I_{1}=2$ into equation (3) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
9.5(2)-8 I_{1} & =27 \\
19-8 I_{2} & =27 \\
19-27 & =8 I_{2} \\
-8 & =8 I_{2} \\
\boldsymbol{I}_{\mathbf{2}} & =\mathbf{1}
\end{aligned}$$
and

Hence, the solution is $\boldsymbol{I}_{\mathbf{1}}=\mathbf{2}$ and $\boldsymbol{I}_{\mathbf{2}}=\mathbf{- 1}$ (which may be checked in the original equations).

Problem 15. The distance $s$ metres from a fixed point of a vehicle travelling in a straight line with constant acceleration, $a \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$, is given by $s=u t+\frac{1}{2} a t^{2}$, where $u$ is the initial velocity in $\mathrm{m} / \mathrm{s}$ and $t$ the time in seconds. Determine the initial velocity and the acceleration given that $s=42 \mathrm{~m}$ when $t=2 \mathrm{~s}$, and $s=144 \mathrm{~m}$ when $t=4 \mathrm{~s}$. Also find the distance travelled after 3 s

Substituting $s=42$ and $t=2$ into $s=u t+\frac{1}{2} a t^{2}$ gives
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
& 42=2 u+\frac{1}{2} a(2)^{2} \\
& 42=2 u+2 a
\end{aligned}$$

Substituting $s=144$ and $t=4$ into $s=u t+\frac{1}{2} a t^{2}$ gives
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
144 & =4 u+\frac{1}{2} a(4)^{2} \\
144 & =4 u+8 a
\end{aligned}$$

Multiplying equation (1) by 2 gives
$$84=4 u+4 a$$

Subtracting equation (3) from equation (2) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
60 & =0+4 a \\
\boldsymbol{a} & =\frac{60}{4}=\mathbf{1 5}
\end{aligned}$$

Substituting $a=15$ into equation (1) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
42 & =2 u+2(15) \\
42-30 & =2 u \\
\boldsymbol{u} & =\frac{12}{2}=\mathbf{6}
\end{aligned}$$

Substituting $a=15$ and $u=6$ in equation (2) gives
$$\text { RHS }=4(6)+8(15)=24+120=144=\mathrm{LHS}$$

Hence, the initial velocity $u=\mathbf{6 m} / \mathrm{s}$ and the acceleration $a=15 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$.
Distance travelled after 3 s is given by $s=u t+\frac{1}{2} a t^{2}$ where $t=3, u=6$ and $a=15$.
Hence, $s=(6)(3)+\frac{1}{2}(15)(3)^{2}=18+67.5$
i.e. distance travelled after $\mathbf{3 s}=85.5 \mathrm{~m}$.

Problem 16. The resistance $R \Omega$ of a length of wire at $t^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ is given by $R=R_{0}(1+\alpha t)$, where $R_{0}$ is the resistance at $0^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ and $\alpha$ is the temperature coefficient of resistance in $/{ }^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$. Find the values of $\alpha$ and $R_{0}$ if $R=30 \Omega$ at $50^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ and $R=35 \Omega$ at $100^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$

Substituting $R=30$ and $t=50$ into $R=R_{0}(1+\alpha t)$ gives
$$30=R_{0}(1+50 \alpha)$$

Substituting $R=35$ and $t=100$ into $R=R_{0}(1+\alpha t)$ gives
$$35=R_{0}(1+100 \alpha)$$

Although these equations may be solved by the conventional substitution method, an easier way is to eliminate $R_{0}$ by division. Thus, dividing equation (1) by equation (2) gives
$$\frac{30}{35}=\frac{R_{0}(1+50 \alpha)}{R_{0}(1+100 \alpha)}=\frac{1+50 \alpha}{1+100 \alpha}$$

Cross-multiplying gives
$$\begin{aligned}
& \begin{aligned}
& 30(1+100 \alpha)=35(1+50 \alpha) \\
& 30+3000 \alpha=35+1750 \alpha \\
& 3000 \alpha-1750 \alpha=35-30 \\
& 1250 \alpha=5 \\
& \text { i.e. } \quad \begin{aligned}
\alpha & =\frac{5}{1250}=\frac{\mathbf{1}}{\mathbf{2 5 0}} \text { or } \mathbf{0 . 0 0 4}
\end{aligned} \\
& \text { Substituting } \alpha=\frac{1}{250} \text { into equation (1) gives } \\
& 30=R_{0}\left\{1+(50)\left(\frac{1}{250}\right)\right\} \\
& 30=R_{0}(1.2) \\
& \boldsymbol{R}_{\mathbf{0}}=\frac{30}{1.2}=\mathbf{2 5}
\end{aligned}
\end{aligned}$$

Checking, substituting $\alpha=\frac{1}{250}$ and $R_{0}=25$ in equation (2), gives
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { RHS } & =25\left\{1+(100)\left(\frac{1}{250}\right)\right\} \\
& =25(1.4)=35=\mathrm{LHS}
\end{aligned}$$

Thus, the solution is $\boldsymbol{\alpha}=\mathbf{0 . 0 0 4} /{ }^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ and $\boldsymbol{R}_{\mathbf{0}}=\mathbf{2 5} \boldsymbol{\Omega}$.

Problem 17. The molar heat capacity of a solid compound is given by the equation $c=a+b T$, where $a$ and $b$ are constants. When $c=52, T=100$ and when $c=172, T=400$. Determine the values of $a$ and $b$

When $c=52, \quad T=100$, hence
$$52=a+100 b$$

When $c=172, \quad T=400$, hence
$$172=a+400 b$$

Equation (2) - equation (1) gives
$$120=300 b$$
from which, $\quad \boldsymbol{b}=\frac{120}{300}=\mathbf{0 . 4}$
Substituting $b=0.4$ in equation (1) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
52 & =a+100(0.4) \\
\boldsymbol{a} & =52-40=\mathbf{1 2}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $\boldsymbol{a}=\mathbf{1 2}$ and $\boldsymbol{b}=\mathbf{0} .4$

\subsection*{13.6 Solving simultaneous equations in three unknowns}

Equations containing three unknowns may be solved using exactly the same procedures as those used with two equations and two unknowns, providing that there are three equations to work with. The method is demonstrated in the following worked problem.

Problem 18. Solve the simultaneous equations.
$$\begin{aligned}
x+y+z & =4 \\
2 x-3 y+4 z & =33 \\
3 x-2 y-2 z & =2
\end{aligned}$$

There are a number of ways of solving these equations. One method is shown below.
The initial object is to produce two equations with two unknowns. For example, multiplying equation (1) by 4 and then subtracting this new equation from equation (2) will produce an equation with only $x$ and $y$ involved.

Multiplying equation (1) by 4 gives
$$4 x+4 y+4 z=16$$

Equation (2) - equation (4) gives
$$-2 x-7 y=17$$

Similarly, multiplying equation (3) by 2 and then adding this new equation to equation (2) will produce another equation with only $x$ and $y$ involved.

Multiplying equation (3) by 2 gives
$$6 x-4 y-4 z=4$$

Equation (2) + equation (6) gives
$$8 x-7 y=37$$

Rewriting equation (5) gives
$$-2 x-7 y=17$$

Now we can use the previous method for solving simultaneous equations in two unknowns.

Equation (7) - equation (5) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
10 x & =20 \\
\boldsymbol{x} & =\mathbf{2}
\end{aligned}$$
from which,
(Note that $8 x--2 x=8 x+2 x=10 x$ )
Substituting $x=2$ into equation (5) gives
$$-4-7 y=17$$
from which, $\quad-7 y=17+4=21$
and
$$y=-3$$

Substituting $x=2$ and $y=-3$ into equation (1) gives
$$2-3+z=4$$
from which,
$$z=5$$

Hence, the solution of the simultaneous equations is $x=2, y=-3$ and $z=5$.

\section*{Chapter 14}

\section*{Solving quadratic equations}

\subsection*{14.1 Introduction}

As stated in Chapter 11, an equation is a statement that two quantities are equal and to 'solve an equation' means 'to find the value of the unknown'. The value of the unknown is called the root of the equation.
A quadratic equation is one in which the highest power of the unknown quantity is 2 . For example, $x^{2}-3 x+1=0$ is a quadratic equation.
There are four methods of solving quadratic equations. These are:
(a) by factorization (where possible),
(b) by 'completing the square',
(c) by using the 'quadratic formula', or
(d) graphically (see Chapter 19).

\subsection*{14.2 Solution of quadratic equations by factorization}

Multiplying out $(x+1)(x-3)$ gives $x^{2}-3 x+x-3$ i.e. $x^{2}-2 x-3$. The reverse process of moving from $x^{2}-2 x-3$ to $(x+1)(x-3)$ is called factorizing.
If the quadratic expression can be factorized this provides the simplest method of solving a quadratic equation.
For example, if $x^{2}-2 x-3=0$, then, by factorizing
$$(x+1)(x-3)=0$$

Hence, either $\quad(x+1)=0$, i.e. $x=-1$
or $\quad(x-3)=0$, i.e. $x=3$
Hence, $x=-1$ and $x=3$ are the roots of the quadratic equation $x^{2}-2 x-3=0$.
The technique of factorizing is often one of trial and error.

\section*{Problem 1. Solve the equation $x^{2}+x-6=0$ by factorization}

The factors of $x^{2}$ are $x$ and $x$. These are placed in brackets: $\quad(x \quad)(x \quad)$
The factors of -6 are +6 and -1 , or -6 and +1 , or +3 and -2 , or -3 and +2 .
The only combination to give a middle term of $+x$ is +3 and -2 ,
i.e.
$$x^{2}+x-6=(x+3)(x-2)$$

The quadratic equation $x^{2}+x-6=0$ thus becomes
$$(x+3)(x-2)=0$$

Since the only way that this can be true is for either the first or the second or both factors to be zero, then
$$\begin{array}{ll}
\text { either } & (x+3)=0, \text { i.e. } x=-3 \\
\text { or } & (x-2)=0, \text { i.e. } x=2
\end{array}$$

Hence, the roots of $x^{2}+x-6=0$ are $x=-3$ and $\boldsymbol{x}=2$.

Problem 2. Solve the equation $x^{2}+2 x-8=0$ by factorization

The factors of $x^{2}$ are $x$ and $x$. These are placed in brackets: $\quad(x \quad)(x \quad)$
The factors of -8 are +8 and -1 , or -8 and +1 , or +4 and -2 , or -4 and +2 .
The only combination to give a middle term of $+2 x$ is +4 and -2 ,
i.e.
$$x^{2}+2 x-8=(x+4)(x-2)$$
(Note that the product of the two inner terms ( $4 x$ ) added to the product of the two outer terms $(-2 x)$ must equal the middle term, $+2 x$ in this case.)

The quadratic equation $x^{2}+2 x-8=0$ thus becomes $(x+4)(x-2)=0$
Since the only way that this can be true is for either the first or the second or both factors to be zero,
either
$$\begin{array}{lll}
(x+4)=0, & \text { i.e. } & x=-4 \\
(x-2)=0, & \text { i.e. } & x=2
\end{array}$$
or
Hence, the roots of $x^{2}+2 x-8=0$ are $x=-4$ and $x=2$.

Problem 3. Determine the roots of $x^{2}-6 x+9=0$ by factorization
$x^{2}-6 x+9=(x-3)(x-3), \quad$ i.e. $(x-3)^{2}=0$
The LHS is known as a perfect square.
Hence, $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{3}$ is the only root of the equation $x^{2}-6 x+9=0$.

Problem 4. Solve the equation $x^{2}-4 x=0$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Factorizing gives } \quad x(x-4)=0 \\
& \text { If } \\
& x(x-4)=0, \\
& \text { either } \\
& x=0 \quad \text { or } \quad x-4=0 \\
& \text { i.e. } \\
& \boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{0} \text { or } \boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{4}
\end{aligned}$$

These are the two roots of the given equation. Answers can always be checked by substitution into the original equation.

Problem 5. Solve the equation $x^{2}+3 x-4=0$
Factorizing gives
$$(x-1)(x+4)=0$$

Hence, either
$$\begin{array}{rll}
x-1=0 & \text { or } & x+4=0 \\
\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{1} & \text { or } & \boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 4}
\end{array}$$
i.e.

Problem 6. Determine the roots of $4 x^{2}-25=0$ by factorization

The LHS of $4 x^{2}-25=0$ is the difference of two squares, $(2 x)^{2}$ and $(5)^{2}$.
By factorizing, $4 x^{2}-25=(2 x+5)(2 x-5)$, i.e. $(2 x+5)(2 x-5)=0$

Hence, either
$$(2 x+5)=0, \quad \text { i.e. } \quad x=-\frac{\mathbf{5}}{\mathbf{2}}=-\mathbf{2 . 5}$$
or
$$(2 x-5)=0, \quad \text { i.e. } \quad \boldsymbol{x}=\frac{\mathbf{5}}{\mathbf{2}}=\mathbf{2 . 5}$$

Problem 7. Solve the equation $x^{2}-5 x+6=0$

Factorizing gives
Hence, either i.e.
$$(x-3)(x-2)=0$$
$$x-3=0 \quad \text { or } x-2=0$$
$$x=\mathbf{3} \text { or } x=\mathbf{2}$$

Problem 8. Solve the equation $x^{2}=15-2 x$
Rearranging gives
$$x^{2}+2 x-15=0$$

Factorizing gives
$$(x+5)(x-3)=0$$

Hence, either
i.e.
$$x+5=0 \quad \text { or } \quad x-3=0$$
$$\boldsymbol{x}=-\mathbf{5} \text { or } \boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{3}$$

Problem 9. Solve the equation $3 x^{2}-11 x-4=0$ by factorization

The factors of $3 x^{2}$ are $3 x$ and $x$. These are placed in brackets: $(3 x \quad)(x \quad)$
The factors of -4 are -4 and +1 , or +4 and -1 , or -2 and 2.
Remembering that the product of the two inner terms added to the product of the two outer terms must equal $-11 x$, the only combination to give this is +1 and -4 ,
i.e.
$$3 x^{2}-11 x-4=(3 x+1)(x-4)$$

The quadratic equation $3 x^{2}-11 x-4=0$ thus becomes
$$(3 x+1)(x-4)=0$$

Hence, either $(3 x+1)=0$, i.e. $\boldsymbol{x}=-\frac{\mathbf{1}}{\mathbf{3}}$
or
$$(x-4)=0, \quad \text { i.e. } \quad x=4$$
and both solutions may be checked in the original equation.

Problem 10. Solve the quadratic equation $4 x^{2}+8 x+3=0$ by factorizing

The factors of $4 x^{2}$ are $4 x$ and $x$ or $2 x$ and $2 x$.
The factors of 3 are 3 and 1 , or -3 and -1 .
Remembering that the product of the inner terms added to the product of the two outer terms must equal $+8 x$, the only combination that is true (by trial and error) is
$$\left(4 x^{2}+8 x+3\right)=(2 x+3)(2 x+1)$$

Hence, $(2 x+3)(2 x+1)=0$, from which either $(2 x+3)=0$ or $(2 x+1)=0$.

Thus, $2 x=-3$, from which $\boldsymbol{x}=-\frac{\mathbf{3}}{\mathbf{2}}$ or $\mathbf{- 1 . 5}$ or $\quad 2 x=-1$, from which $\boldsymbol{x}=-\frac{\mathbf{1}}{\mathbf{2}}$ or $-\mathbf{0 . 5}$ which may be checked in the original equation.

Problem 11. Solve the quadratic equation $15 x^{2}+2 x-8=0$ by factorizing

The factors of $15 x^{2}$ are $15 x$ and $x$ or $5 x$ and $3 x$.
The factors of -8 are -4 are +2 , or 4 and -2 , or -8 and +1 , or 8 and -1 .
By trial and error the only combination that works is
$$15 x^{2}+2 x-8=(5 x+4)(3 x-2)$$

Hence, $(5 x+4)(3 x-2)=0$, from which either $5 x+$ $4=0$ or $3 x-2=0$.
Hence, $x=-\frac{\mathbf{4}}{\mathbf{5}} \quad$ or $\quad x=\frac{\mathbf{2}}{\mathbf{3}}$
which may be checked in the original equation.
Problem 12. The roots of a quadratic equation are $\frac{1}{3}$ and -2 . Determine the equation in $x$

If the roots of a quadratic equation are, say, $\alpha$ and $\beta$, then $(x-\alpha)(x-\beta)=0$.

Hence, if $\alpha=\frac{1}{3}$ and $\beta=-2$,
$$\begin{aligned}
\left(x-\frac{1}{3}\right)(x-(-2)) & =0 \\
\left(x-\frac{1}{3}\right)(x+2) & =0 \\
x^{2}-\frac{1}{3} x+2 x-\frac{2}{3} & =0 \\
x^{2}+\frac{\mathbf{5}}{\mathbf{3}} x-\frac{\mathbf{2}}{\mathbf{3}} & =0 \\
3 x^{2}+\mathbf{5}-\mathbf{2} & =0
\end{aligned}$$
or

Problem 13. Find the equation in $x$ whose roots are 5 and -5

If 5 and -5 are the roots of a quadratic equation then
$$(x-5)(x+5)=0$$
i.e.
i.e.
$$\begin{array}{r}
x^{2}-5 x+5 x-25=0 \\
\boldsymbol{x}^{\mathbf{2}}-\mathbf{2 5}=\mathbf{0}
\end{array}$$

Problem 14. Find the equation in $x$ whose roots are 1.2 and -0.4

If 1.2 and -0.4 are the roots of a quadratic equation then
i.e.
$$x^{2}-1.2 x+0.4 x-0.48=0$$
i.e.
$$x^{2}-0.8 x-0.48=0$$

\section*{Practice Exercise 54 Solving quadratic equations by factorization (answers on page 346)}

In problems 1 to 30, solve the given equations by factorization.
1. $x^{2}-16=0$
2. $x^{2}+4 x-32=0$
3. $(x+2)^{2}=16$
4. $4 x^{2}-9=0$
5. $3 x^{2}+4 x=0$
6. $8 x^{2}-32=0$
7. $x^{2}-8 x+16=0$
8. $x^{2}+10 x+25=0$

10. $x^{2}+5 x+6=0$

\subsection*{14.3 Solution of quadratic equations by 'completing the square'}

An expression such as $x^{2}$ or $(x+2)^{2}$ or $(x-3)^{2}$ is called a perfect square.
If $x^{2}=3$ then $x= \pm \sqrt{3}$
If $(x+2)^{2}=5$ then $x+2= \pm \sqrt{5}$ and $x=-2 \pm \sqrt{5}$
If $(x-3)^{2}=8$ then $x-3= \pm \sqrt{8}$ and $x=3 \pm \sqrt{8}$
Hence, if a quadratic equation can be rearranged so that one side of the equation is a perfect square and the other side of the equation is a number, then the solution of the equation is readily obtained by taking the square roots of each side as in the above examples. The process of rearranging one side of a quadratic equation into a perfect square before solving is called 'completing the square'.
$$(x+a)^{2}=x^{2}+2 a x+a^{2}$$

Thus, in order to make the quadratic expression $x^{2}+2 a x$ into a perfect square, it is necessary to add (half the coefficient of $x)^{2}$, i.e. $\left(\frac{2 a}{2}\right)^{2}$ or $a^{2}$
For example, $x^{2}+3 x$ becomes a perfect square by adding $\left(\frac{3}{2}\right)^{2}$, i.e.
$$x^{2}+3 x+\left(\frac{3}{2}\right)^{2}=\left(x+\frac{3}{2}\right)^{2}$$

The method of completing the square is demonstrated in the following worked problems.

Problem 15. Solve $2 x^{2}+5 x=3$ by completing the square

The procedure is as follows.
(i) Rearrange the equation so that all terms are on the same side of the equals sign (and the coefficient of the $x^{2}$ term is positive). Hence,
$$2 x^{2}+5 x-3=0$$
(ii) Make the coefficient of the $x^{2}$ term unity. In this case this is achieved by dividing throughout by 2 . Hence,
$$\begin{array}{ll}
\frac{2 x^{2}}{2}+\frac{5 x}{2}-\frac{3}{2} & =0 \\
\text { i.e. } \quad x^{2}+\frac{5}{2} x-\frac{3}{2} & =0
\end{array}$$
(iii) Rearrange the equations so that the $x^{2}$ and $x$ terms are on one side of the equals sign and the constant is on the other side. Hence,
$$x^{2}+\frac{5}{2} x=\frac{3}{2}$$
(iv) Add to both sides of the equation (half the coefficient of $x)^{2}$. In this case the coefficient of $x$ is $\frac{5}{2}$ Half the coefficient squared is therefore $\left(\frac{5}{4}\right)^{2}$ Thus,
$$x^{2}+\frac{5}{2} x+\left(\frac{5}{4}\right)^{2}=\frac{3}{2}+\left(\frac{5}{4}\right)^{2}$$

The LHS is now a perfect square, i.e.
$$\left(x+\frac{5}{4}\right)^{2}=\frac{3}{2}+\left(\frac{5}{4}\right)^{2}$$
(v) Evaluate the RHS. Thus,
$$\left(x+\frac{5}{4}\right)^{2}=\frac{3}{2}+\frac{25}{16}=\frac{24+25}{16}=\frac{49}{16}$$
(vi) Take the square root of both sides of the equation (remembering that the square root of a number gives a $\pm$ answer). Thus,
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
\sqrt{\left(x+\frac{5}{4}\right)^{2}} & =\sqrt{\left(\frac{49}{16}\right)} \\
x+\frac{5}{4} & = \pm \frac{7}{4}
\end{aligned}$$
(vii) Solve the simple equation. Thus,
$$\begin{aligned}
& x=-\frac{5}{4} \pm \frac{7}{4} \\
\text { i.e. } & x=-\frac{5}{4}+\frac{7}{4}=\frac{2}{4}=\frac{1}{2} \text { or } 0.5 \\
\text { and } \quad x & =-\frac{5}{4}-\frac{7}{4}=-\frac{12}{4}=-3
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{0 . 5}$ or $\boldsymbol{x}=-\mathbf{3}$; i.e., the roots of the equation $2 x^{2}+5 x=3$ are 0.5 and -3 .

Problem 16. Solve $2 x^{2}+9 x+8=0$, correct to 3 significant figures, by completing the square

Making the coefficient of $x^{2}$ unity gives
$$x^{2}+\frac{9}{2} x+4=0$$

Rearranging gives $\quad x^{2}+\frac{9}{2} x=-4$

Adding to both sides (half the coefficient of $x)^{2}$ gives
$$x^{2}+\frac{9}{2} x+\left(\frac{9}{4}\right)^{2}=\left(\frac{9}{4}\right)^{2}-4$$

The LHS is now a perfect square. Thus,
$$\left(x+\frac{9}{4}\right)^{2}=\frac{81}{16}-4=\frac{81}{16}-\frac{64}{16}=\frac{17}{16}$$

Taking the square root of both sides gives

Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
x+\frac{9}{4} & =\sqrt{\left(\frac{17}{16}\right)}= \pm 1.031 \\
x & =-\frac{9}{4} \pm 1.031
\end{aligned}$$
i.e. $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 1 . 2 2}$ or $\mathbf{- 3 . 2 8}$, correct to 3 significant figures.

Problem 17. By completing the square, solve the quadratic equation $4.6 y^{2}+3.5 y-1.75=0$, correct to 3 decimal places
$$4.6 y^{2}+3.5 y-1.75=0$$

Making the coefficient of $y^{2}$ unity gives
$$y^{2}+\frac{3.5}{4.6} y-\frac{1.75}{4.6}=0$$
and rearranging gives
$$y^{2}+\frac{3.5}{4.6} y=\frac{1.75}{4.6}$$

Adding to both sides (half the coefficient of $y)^{2}$ gives
$$y^{2}+\frac{3.5}{4.6} y+\left(\frac{3.5}{9.2}\right)^{2}=\frac{1.75}{4.6}+\left(\frac{3.5}{9.2}\right)^{2}$$

The LHS is now a perfect square. Thus,
$$\left(y+\frac{3.5}{9.2}\right)^{2}=0.5251654$$

Taking the square root of both sides gives
$$y+\frac{3.5}{9.2}=\sqrt{0.5251654}= \pm 0.7246830$$

Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
& y=-\frac{3.5}{9.2} \pm 0.7246830 \\
& \boldsymbol{y}=\mathbf{0 . 3 4 4} \text { or } \mathbf{- 1 . 1 0 5}
\end{aligned}$$
i.e.

\subsection*{14.4 Solution of quadratic equations by formula}

Let the general form of a quadratic equation be given by $a x^{2}+b x+c=0$, where $a, b$ and $c$ are constants.
Dividing $a x^{2}+b x+c=0$ by $a$ gives
$$x^{2}+\frac{b}{a} x+\frac{c}{a}=0$$

Rearranging gives $\quad x^{2}+\frac{b}{a} x=-\frac{c}{a}$
Adding to each side of the equation the square of half the coefficient of the term in $x$ to make the LHS a perfect square gives
$$x^{2}+\frac{b}{a} x+\left(\frac{b}{2 a}\right)^{2}=\left(\frac{b}{2 a}\right)^{2}-\frac{c}{a}$$

Rearranging gives $\left(x+\frac{b}{a}\right)^{2}=\frac{b^{2}}{4 a^{2}}-\frac{c}{a}=\frac{b^{2}-4 a c}{4 a^{2}}$
Taking the square root of both sides gives
$$x+\frac{b}{2 a}=\sqrt{\left(\frac{b^{2}-4 a c}{4 a^{2}}\right)}=\frac{ \pm \sqrt{b^{2}-4 a c}}{2 a}$$

Hence, $\quad x=-\frac{b}{2 a} \pm \frac{\sqrt{b^{2}-4 a c}}{2 a}$
i.e. the quadratic formula is $\quad x=\frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^{2}-4 a c}}{2 a}$
(This method of obtaining the formula is completing the square - as shown in the previous section.)
In summary,
if $a x^{2}+b x+c=0$ then $x=\frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^{2}-4 a c}}{2 a}$
This is known as the quadratic formula.

Problem 18. Solve $x^{2}+2 x-8=0$ by using the quadratic formula

Comparing $x^{2}+2 x-8=0$ with $a x^{2}+b x+c=0$ gives $a=1, b=2$ and $c=-8$.
Substituting these values into the quadratic formula
$$x=\frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^{2}-4 a c}}{2 a}$$
gives
$$\begin{aligned}
x=\frac{-2 \pm \sqrt{2^{2}-4(1)(-8)}}{2(1)} & =\frac{-2 \pm \sqrt{4+32}}{2} \\
& =\frac{-2 \pm \sqrt{36}}{2}=\frac{-2 \pm 6}{2} \\
& =\frac{-2+6}{2} \text { or } \frac{-2-6}{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $\boldsymbol{x}=\frac{4}{2}$ or $\frac{-8}{2}$, i.e. $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{2}$ or $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 4}$.
Problem 19. Solve $3 x^{2}-11 x-4=0$ by using the quadratic formula

Comparing $3 x^{2}-11 x-4=0$ with $a x^{2}+b x+c=0$ gives $a=3, b=-11$ and $c=-4$. Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
x & =\frac{-(-11) \pm \sqrt{(-11)^{2}-4(3)(-4)}}{2(3)} \\
& =\frac{+11 \pm \sqrt{121+48}}{6}=\frac{11 \pm \sqrt{169}}{6} \\
& =\frac{11 \pm 13}{6}=\frac{11+13}{6} \text { or } \frac{11-13}{6}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $x=\frac{24}{6}$ or $\frac{-2}{6}$, i.e. $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{4}$ or $\boldsymbol{x}=-\frac{\mathbf{1}}{\mathbf{3}}$
Problem 20. Solve $4 x^{2}+7 x+2=0$ giving the roots correct to 2 decimal places

Comparing $4 x^{2}+7 x+2=0$ with $a x^{2}+b x+c$ gives $a=4, b=7$ and $c=2$. Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
x & =\frac{-7 \pm \sqrt{7^{2}-4(4)(2)}}{2(4)} \\
& =\frac{-7 \pm \sqrt{17}}{8}=\frac{-7 \pm 4.123}{8} \\
& =\frac{-7+4.123}{8} \text { or } \frac{-7-4.123}{8}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 0 . 3 6}$ or $\mathbf{- 1 . 3 9}$, correct to 2 decimal places.

Problem 21. Use the quadratic formula to solve $\frac{x+2}{4}+\frac{3}{x-1}=7$ correct to 4 significant figures

Multiplying throughout by $4(x-1)$ gives
$$4(x-1) \frac{(x+2)}{4}+4(x-1) \frac{3}{(x-1)}=4(x-1)(7)$$

Cancelling gives $(x-1)(x+2)+(4)(3)=28(x-1)$
$$x^{2}+x-2+12=28 x-28$$

Hence,
$$x^{2}-27 x+38=0$$

Using the quadratic formula,
$$\begin{aligned}
x & =\frac{-(-27) \pm \sqrt{(-27)^{2}-4(1)(38)}}{2} \\
& =\frac{27 \pm \sqrt{577}}{2}=\frac{27 \pm 24.0208}{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $\quad x=\frac{27+24.0208}{2}=25.5104$
or
$$x=\frac{27-24.0208}{2}=1.4896$$

Hence, $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{2 5 . 5 1}$ or $\mathbf{1 . 4 9 0}$, correct to 4 significant figures.

\subsection*{14.5 Practical problems involving quadratic equations}

There are many practical problems in which a quadratic equation has first to be obtained, from given information, before it is solved.

Problem 22. The area of a rectangle is $23.6 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$ and its width is 3.10 cm shorter than its length. Determine the dimensions of the rectangle, correct to 3 significant figures

Let the length of the rectangle be $x \mathrm{~cm}$. Then the width is $(x-3.10) \mathrm{cm}$.
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Area }=\text { length } \times \text { width }=x(x-3.10)=23.6 \\
& \text { i.e. } \quad x^{2}-3.10 x-23.6=0
\end{aligned}$$

Using the quadratic formula,
$$\begin{aligned}
x= & \frac{-(-3.10) \pm \sqrt{(-3.10)^{2}-4(1)(-23.6)}}{2(1)} \\
=\frac{3.10 \pm \sqrt{9.61+94.4}}{2} & =\frac{3.10 \pm 10.20}{2} \\
& =\frac{13.30}{2} \text { or } \frac{-7.10}{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $x=6.65 \mathrm{~cm}$ or -3.55 cm . The latter solution is neglected since length cannot be negative.
Thus, length $x=6.65 \mathrm{~cm}$ and width $=x-3.10=$ $6.65-3.10=3.55 \mathrm{~cm}$, i.e. the dimensions of the rectangle are 6.65 cm by 3.55 cm .
(Check: Area $=6.65 \times 3.55=23.6 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$, correct to 3 significant figures.)

Problem 23. Calculate the diameter of a solid cylinder which has a height of 82.0 cm and a total surface area of $2.0 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$

Total surface area of a cylinder
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\text { curved surface area }+2 \text { circular ends } \\
& \left.=2 \pi r h+2 \pi r^{2} \text { (where } r=\text { radius and } h=\text { height }\right)
\end{aligned}$$

Since the total surface area $=2.0 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$ and the height $h=$ 82 cm or 0.82 m ,
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
& 2.0=2 \pi r(0.82)+2 \pi r^{2} \\
& 2 \pi r^{2}+2 \pi r(0.82)-2.0=0
\end{aligned}$$

Dividing throughout by $2 \pi$ gives $r^{2}+0.82 r-\frac{1}{\pi}=0$ Using the quadratic formula,
$$\begin{aligned}
r= & \frac{-0.82 \pm \sqrt{(0.82)^{2}-4(1)\left(-\frac{1}{\pi}\right)}}{2(1)} \\
=\frac{-0.82 \pm \sqrt{1.94564}}{2} & =\frac{-0.82 \pm 1.39486}{2} \\
& =0.2874 \text { or }-1.1074
\end{aligned}$$

Thus, the radius $r$ of the cylinder is 0.2874 m (the negative solution being neglected).

Hence, the diameter of the cylinder
$$\begin{aligned}
& =2 \times 0.2874 \\
& =\mathbf{0 . 5 7 4 8} \mathrm{m} \text { or } \mathbf{5 7 . 5} \mathbf{c m}
\end{aligned}$$
correct to 3 significant figures.

Problem 24. The height $s$ metres of a mass projected vertically upwards at time $t$ seconds is $s=u t-\frac{1}{2} g t^{2}$. Determine how long the mass will take after being projected to reach a height of 16 m (a) on the ascent and (b) on the descent, when $u=30 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ and $g=9.81 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$

When height $s=16 \mathrm{~m}, \quad 16=30 t-\frac{1}{2}(9.81) t^{2}$ i.e.
$$4.905 t^{2}-30 t+16=0$$

Using the quadratic formula,
$$\begin{aligned}
t & =\frac{-(-30) \pm \sqrt{(-30)^{2}-4(4.905)(16)}}{2(4.905)} \\
& =\frac{30 \pm \sqrt{586.1}}{9.81}=\frac{30 \pm 24.21}{9.81}=5.53 \text { or } 0.59
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, the mass will reach a height of 16 m after 0.59 s on the ascent and after 5.53 s on the descent.

Problem 25. A shed is 4.0 m long and 2.0 m wide. A concrete path of constant width is laid all the way around the shed. If the area of the path is $9.50 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$, calculate its width to the nearest centimetre

Figure 14.1 shows a plan view of the shed with its surrounding path of width $t$ metres.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-122.jpg?height=462&width=554&top_left_y=827&top_left_x=352)

Figure 14.1
$$\begin{array}{lc} 
& \text { Area of path }=2(2.0 \times t)+2 t(4.0+2 t) \\
\text { i.e. } & 9.50=4.0 t+8.0 t+4 t^{2} \\
\text { or } & 4 t^{2}+12.0 t-9.50=0
\end{array}$$

Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
t & =\frac{-(12.0) \pm \sqrt{(12.0)^{2}-4(4)(-9.50)}}{2(4)} \\
& =\frac{-12.0 \pm \sqrt{296.0}}{8}=\frac{-12.0 \pm 17.20465}{8} \\
\text { i.e. } \quad t & =0.6506 \mathrm{~m} \text { or }-3.65058 \mathrm{~m} .
\end{aligned}$$

Neglecting the negative result, which is meaningless, the width of the path, $\boldsymbol{t}=\mathbf{0 . 6 5 1} \mathrm{m}$ or $\mathbf{6 5} \mathbf{~} \mathrm{m}$ correct to the nearest centimetre.

Problem 26. If the total surface area of a solid cone is $486.2 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$ and its slant height is 15.3 cm , determine its base diameter.

From Chapter 27, page 245, the total surface area $A$ of a solid cone is given by $A=\pi r l+\pi r^{2}$, where $l$ is the slant height and $r$ the base radius.

If $A=482.2$ and $l=15.3$, then $482.2=\pi r(15.3)+\pi r^{2}$
i.e.
$$\pi r^{2}+15.3 \pi r-482.2=0$$
or
$$r^{2}+15.3 r-\frac{482.2}{\pi}=0$$

Using the quadratic formula,
$$\begin{aligned}
r & =\frac{-15.3 \pm \sqrt{\left[(15.3)^{2}-4\left(\frac{-482.2}{\pi}\right)\right]}}{2} \\
& =\frac{-15.3 \pm \sqrt{848.0461}}{2}=\frac{-15.3 \pm 29.12123}{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, radius $r=6.9106 \mathrm{~cm}$ (or -22.21 cm , which is meaningless and is thus ignored).
Thus, the diameter of the base $=2 r=2(6.9106)$
$$=13.82 \mathrm{~cm}$$

\subsection*{14.6 Solution of linear and quadratic equations simultaneously}

Sometimes a linear equation and a quadratic equation need to be solved simultaneously. An algebraic method of solution is shown in Problem 27; a graphical solution is shown in Chapter 19, page 160.

Problem 27. Determine the values of $x$ and $y$ which simultaneously satisfy the equations $y=5 x-4-2 x^{2}$ and $y=6 x-7$

For a simultaneous solution the values of $y$ must be equal, hence the RHS of each equation is equated.

Thus,
$$5 x-4-2 x^{2}=6 x-7$$

Rearranging gives
$$5 x-4-2 x^{2}-6 x+7=0$$
i.e.
$$-x+3-2 x^{2}=0$$
or
$$2 x^{2}+x-3=0$$

Factorizing gives
$$(2 x+3)(x-1)=0$$
i.e.
$$x=-\frac{3}{2} \text { or } x=1$$

In the equation $y=6 x-7$,
when $x=-\frac{3}{2}, \quad y=6\left(-\frac{3}{2}\right)-7=-16$
and when $x=1, \quad y=6-7=-1$
(Checking the result in $y=5 x-4-2 x^{2}$ :
when $x=-\frac{3}{2}, \quad y=5\left(-\frac{3}{2}\right)-4-2\left(-\frac{3}{2}\right)^{2}$
$$=-\frac{15}{2}-4-\frac{9}{2}=-16, \text { as above, }$$
and when $x=1, \quad y=5-4-2=-1$, as above.)

Hence, the simultaneous solutions occur when
$x=-\frac{3}{2}, y=-16$ and when $x=1, y=-1$.

\section*{Chapter 15}

\subsection*{15.1 Introduction to logarithms}

With the use of calculators firmly established, logarithmic tables are now rarely used for calculation. However, the theory of logarithms is important, for there are several scientific and engineering laws that involve the rules of logarithms.

From Chapter 7, we know that $\quad 16=2^{4}$.
The number 4 is called the power or the exponent or the index. In the expression $2^{4}$, the number 2 is called the base.

In another example, we know that $\quad 64=8^{2}$.
In this example, 2 is the power, or exponent, or index. The number 8 is the base.

\subsection*{15.1.1 What is a logarithm?}

Consider the expression $16=2^{4}$.
An alternative, yet equivalent, way of writing this expression is $\log _{2} 16=4$.
This is stated as 'log to the base 2 of 16 equals 4 '.
We see that the logarithm is the same as the power or index in the original expression. It is the base in the original expression that becomes the base of the logarithm.
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { The two statements } 16=2^{4} \text { and } \\
& \qquad \log _{2} 16=4 \text { are equivalent }
\end{aligned}$$

If we write either of them, we are automatically implying the other.
In general, if a number $y$ can be written in the form $a^{x}$, then the index $x$ is called the 'logarithm of $y$ to the base

\section*{Logarithms}
of $a^{\prime}$, i.e.
$$\text { if } y=a^{x} \text { then } x=\log _{a} y$$

In another example, if we write down that $64=8^{2}$ then the equivalent statement using logarithms is $\log _{8} 64=2$. In another example, if we write down that $\log _{3} 27=3$ then the equivalent statement using powers is $3^{3}=27$. So the two sets of statements, one involving powers and one involving logarithms, are equivalent.

\subsection*{15.1.2 Common logarithms}

From the above, if we write down that $1000=10^{3}$, then $3=\log _{10} 1000$. This may be checked using the 'log' button on your calculator.
Logarithms having a base of 10 are called common logarithms and $\log _{10}$ is usually abbreviated to lg. The following values may be checked using a calculator.
$$\begin{aligned}
\lg 27.5 & =1.4393 \ldots \\
\lg 378.1 & =2.5776 \ldots \\
\lg 0.0204 & =-1.6903 \ldots
\end{aligned}$$

\subsection*{15.1.3 Napierian logarithms}

Logarithms having a base of $e$ (where $e$ is a mathematical constant approximately equal to 2.7183 ) are called hyperbolic, Napierian or natural logarithms, and $\log _{e}$ is usually abbreviated to $\ln$. The following values may be checked using a calculator.
$$\begin{aligned}
\ln 3.65 & =1.2947 \ldots \\
\ln 417.3 & =6.0338 \ldots \\
\ln 0.182 & =-1.7037 \ldots
\end{aligned}$$

Napierian logarithms are explained further in Chapter 16, following.

Here are some worked problems to help understanding of logarithms.

\section*{Problem 1. Evaluate $\log _{3} 9$}

Let $x=\log _{3} 9 \quad$ then $3^{x}=9$ from the definition of a logarithm,
i.e.
$3^{x}=3^{2}$, from which $x=2$
Hence,
$\log _{3} 9=2$

\section*{Problem 2. Evaluate $\log _{10} 10$}

Let $x=\log _{10} 10$ then $10^{x}=10$ from the definition of a logarithm,
i.e.
$10^{x}=10^{1}$, from which $x=1$
Hence,
$\log _{10} 10=1$ (which may be checked using a calculator).

\section*{Problem 3. Evaluate $\log _{16} 8$}

Let $x=\log _{16} 8 \quad$ then $16^{x}=8$ from the definition of a logarithm,
i.e. $\left(2^{4}\right)^{x}=2^{3}$
i.e. $2^{4 x}=2^{3}$ from the laws of indices,
from which, $\quad 4 x=3$ and $x=\frac{\mathbf{3}}{\mathbf{4}}$
Hence, $\quad \log _{16} 8=\frac{3}{4}$

\section*{Problem 4. Evaluate $\lg 0.001$}

Let $x=\lg 0.001=\log _{10} 0.001$ then $10^{x}=0.001$ i.e.
$$10^{x}=10^{-3}$$
from which, $x=-3$
Hence,
$\lg 0.001=-3$ (which may be checked using a calculator)

\section*{Problem 5. Evaluate $\ln e$}

Let $x=\ln e=\log _{e} e$ then $e^{x}=e$
i.e.
$e^{x}=e^{1}$, from which
$$x=1$$

Hence,
$\ln \boldsymbol{e}=\mathbf{1}$ (which may be checked by a calculator)

Problem 6. Evaluate $\log _{3} \frac{1}{81}$
Let $x=\log _{3} \frac{1}{81} \quad$ then $\quad 3^{x}=\frac{1}{81}=\frac{1}{3^{4}}=3^{-4}$ from which $x=-4$

Hence, $\quad \log _{3} \frac{1}{81}=-4$

Problem 7. Solve the equation $\lg x=3$

If $\lg x=3$ then $\log _{10} x=3$
and
$$x=10^{3} \quad \text { i.e. } \boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{1 0 0 0}$$

Problem 8. Solve the equation $\log _{2} x=5$
If $\log _{2} x=5 \quad$ then $\quad \boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{2}^{\mathbf{5}}=\mathbf{3 2}$

Problem 9. Solve the equation $\log _{5} x=-2$
If $\log _{5} x=-2 \quad$ then $\quad x=5^{-2}=\frac{1}{5^{2}}=\frac{\mathbf{1}}{\mathbf{2 5}}$

13. $\lg x=5$
14. $\log _{3} x=2$
