\subsection*{18.3 Revision of laws of logarithms}

The laws of logarithms were stated in Chapter 15 as follows:
$$\begin{aligned}
& \log (\mathrm{A} \times \mathrm{B})=\log \mathrm{A}+\log \mathrm{B} \\
& \log \left(\frac{\mathrm{~A}}{\mathrm{~B}}\right)=\log \mathrm{A}-\log \mathrm{B} \\
& \log \mathrm{~A}^{n}=n \times \log \mathrm{A}
\end{aligned}$$

Also, $\quad \ln e=1$ and if, say, $\lg x=1.5$, then $x=10^{1.5}=31.62$

Further, if $3^{x}=7$ then $\lg 3^{x}=\lg 7$ and $x \lg 3=\lg 7$, from which $x=\frac{\lg 7}{\lg 3}=1.771$

These laws and techniques are used whenever non-linear laws of the form $y=a x^{n}, y=a b^{x}$ and $y=a e^{b x}$ are reduced to linear form with the values of $a$ and $b$ needing to be calculated. This is demonstrated in the following section.

\subsection*{18.4 Determination of laws involving logarithms}

Examples of the reduction of equations to linear form involving logarithms include
(a) $y=a x^{n}$

Taking logarithms to a base of $\mathbf{1 0}$ of both sides gives
$$\begin{aligned}
\lg y & =\lg \left(a x^{n}\right) \\
& =\lg a+\lg x^{n} \quad \text { by law (1) } \\
\text { i.e. } \quad \lg y & =n \lg x+\lg a \text { by law (3) }
\end{aligned}$$
which compares with $Y=m X+c$
and shows that $\lg y$ is plotted vertically against $\lg x$ horizontally to produce a straight line graph of gradient $\boldsymbol{n}$ and $\lg \boldsymbol{y}$-axis intercept $\lg$ a.
See worked Problems 4 and 5 to demonstrate how this law is determined.
(b) $y=a b^{x}$

Taking logarithms to a base of $\mathbf{1 0}$ of both sides gives
$$\begin{array}{lll} 
& \lg y & =\lg \left(a b^{x}\right) \\
\text { i.e. } & & \lg y \\
& =\lg a+\lg b^{x} \quad \text { by law (1) } \\
& & \lg y \\
& =\lg a+x \lg b \quad \text { by law (3) } \\
\text { i.e. } & & \lg y \\
\text { or } & =x \lg b+\lg a \\
& \lg y & =(\lg b) x+\lg a
\end{array}$$
which compares with
$$Y=m X+c$$
and shows that $\lg \boldsymbol{y}$ is plotted vertically against $\boldsymbol{x}$ horizontally to produce a straight line graph of gradient $\lg b$ and $\lg y$-axis intercept $\lg$ a.
See worked Problem 6 to demonstrate how this law is determined.
(c) $\boldsymbol{y}=\boldsymbol{a} \boldsymbol{e}^{\boldsymbol{b x}}$

Taking logarithms to a base of $\boldsymbol{e}$ of both sides
$$\begin{array}{lll}
\text { gives } & \ln y=\ln \left(a e^{b x}\right) & \\
\text { i.e. } & \ln y=\ln a+\ln e^{b x} & \text { by law (1) } \\
\text { i.e. } & \ln y=\ln a+b x \ln e & \text { by law (3) } \\
\text { i.e. } & \ln y=b x+\ln a & \text { since } \ln e=1
\end{array}$$
which compares with
$$Y=m X+c$$
and shows that $\ln \boldsymbol{y}$ is plotted vertically against $\boldsymbol{x}$ horizontally to produce a straight line graph of gradient $\boldsymbol{b}$ and $\ln \boldsymbol{y}$-axis intercept $\ln$ a.
See worked Problem 7 to demonstrate how this law is determined.

Problem 4. The current flowing in, and the power dissipated by, a resistor are measured experimentally for various values and the results are as shown below.
\begin{tabular}{llllll}
\hline Current, $I$ (amperes) & 2.2 & 3.6 & 4.1 & 5.6 & 6.8 \\
\hline Power, $P$ (watts) & 116 & 311 & 403 & 753 & 1110
\end{tabular}

Show that the law relating current and power is of the form $P=R I^{n}$, where $R$ and $n$ are constants, and determine the law

Taking logarithms to a base of 10 of both sides of $P=R I^{n}$ gives
$$\lg P=\lg \left(R I^{n}\right)=\lg R+\lg I^{n}=\lg R+n \lg I$$
by the laws of logarithms
i.e. $\quad \lg P=n \lg I+\lg R$
which is of the form $Y=m X+c$,
showing that $\lg P$ is to be plotted vertically against $\lg I$ horizontally.
A table of values for $\lg I$ and $\lg P$ is drawn up as shown below.
\begin{tabular}{|llllll|}
\hline$I$ & 2.2 & 3.6 & 4.1 & 5.6 & 6.8 \\
\hline $\lg I$ & 0.342 & 0.556 & 0.613 & 0.748 & 0.833 \\
\hline$P$ & 116 & 311 & 403 & 753 & 1110 \\
\hline $\lg P$ & 2.064 & 2.493 & 2.605 & 2.877 & 3.045 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

A graph of $\lg P$ against $\lg I$ is shown in Figure 18.4 and, since a straight line results, the law $P=R I^{\boldsymbol{n}}$ is verified.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-164.jpg?height=603&width=711&top_left_y=444&top_left_x=1114)

Figure 18.4
$$\text { Gradient of straight line, } \begin{aligned}
\boldsymbol{n} & =\frac{A B}{B C}=\frac{2.98-2.18}{0.8-0.4} \\
& =\frac{0.80}{0.4}=\mathbf{2}
\end{aligned}$$

It is not possible to determine the vertical axis intercept on sight since the horizontal axis scale does not start at zero. Selecting any point from the graph, say point $D$, where $\lg I=0.70$ and $\lg P=2.78$ and substituting values into
$$\lg P=n \lg I+\lg R$$
gives
$$2.78=(2)(0.70)+\lg R$$
from which,
$$\lg R=2.78-1.40=1.38$$

Hence,
$$\boldsymbol{R}=\operatorname{antilog} 1.38=10^{1.38}=\mathbf{2 4 . 0}$$

Hence, the law of the graph is $\boldsymbol{P}=\mathbf{2 4 . 0} \boldsymbol{I}^{\mathbf{2}}$
Problem 5. The periodic time, $T$, of oscillation of a pendulum is believed to be related to its length, $L$, by a law of the form $T=k L^{n}$, where $k$ and $n$ are constants. Values of $T$ were measured for various lengths of the pendulum and the results are as shown below.
\begin{tabular}{|llllllll|}
\hline Periodic time, $T(\mathrm{~s})$ & 1.0 & 1.3 & 1.5 & 1.8 & 2.0 & 2.3 \\
\hline Length, $L(\mathrm{~m})$ & 0.25 & 0.42 & 0.56 & 0.81 & 1.0 & 1.32 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

Show that the law is true and determine the approximate values of $k$ and $n$. Hence find the periodic time when the length of the pendulum is 0.75 m

From para (a), page 150, if $\quad T=k L^{n}$
then
and comparing with
$$\lg T=n \lg L+\lg k$$
$$Y=m X+c$$
shows that $\lg T$ is plotted vertically against $\lg L$ horizontally, with gradient $n$ and vertical-axis intercept $\lg k$.
A table of values for $\lg T$ and $\lg L$ is drawn up as shown below.
\begin{tabular}{lllllll|}
\hline$T$ & 1.0 & 1.3 & 1.5 & 1.8 & 2.0 & 2.3 \\
\hline $\lg T$ & 0 & 0.114 & 0.176 & 0.255 & 0.301 & 0.362 \\
\hline$L$ & 0.25 & 0.42 & 0.56 & 0.81 & 1.0 & 1.32 \\
$\lg L$ & -0.602 & -0.377 & -0.252 & -0.092 & 0 & 0.121 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

A graph of $\lg T$ against $\lg L$ is shown in Figure 18.5 and the law $T=k L^{n}$ is true since a straight line results.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-165.jpg?height=818&width=774&top_left_y=1142&top_left_x=269)

Figure 18.5
From the graph, gradient of straight line, $\boldsymbol{n}=\frac{A B}{B C}=\frac{0.25-0.05}{-0.10-(-0.50)}=\frac{0.20}{0.40}=\frac{\mathbf{1}}{\mathbf{2}}$
Vertical axis intercept, $\lg k=0.30$. Hence, $\boldsymbol{k}=\operatorname{antilog} 0.30=10^{0.30}=\mathbf{2 . 0}$

Hence, the law of the graph is $T=2.0 L^{1 / 2}$ or $T=2.0 \sqrt{L}$.
When length $L=0.75 \mathrm{~m}, \mathbf{T}=2.0 \sqrt{0.75}=\mathbf{1 . 7 3} \mathrm{s}$

Problem 6. Quantities $x$ and $y$ are believed to be related by a law of the form $y=a b^{x}$, where $a$ and $b$ are constants. The values of $x$ and corresponding values of $y$ are
\begin{tabular}{rllrrrr|}
$x$ & 0 & 0.6 & 1.2 & 1.8 & 2.4 & 3.0 \\
$y$ & 5.0 & 9.67 & 18.7 & 36.1 & 69.8 & 135.0
\end{tabular}

Verify the law and determine the approximate values of $a$ and $b$. Hence determine (a) the value of $y$ when $x$ is 2.1 and (b) the value of $x$ when $y$ is 100

From para (b), page 150, if $\quad y=a b^{x}$
then
$$\begin{aligned}
\lg y & =(\lg b) x+\lg a \\
Y & =m X+c
\end{aligned}$$
and comparing with
shows that $\lg y$ is plotted vertically and $x$ horizontally, with gradient $\lg b$ and vertical-axis intercept $\lg a$.
Another table is drawn up as shown below.
\begin{tabular}{|lllcccc|}
\hline$x$ & 0 & 0.6 & 1.2 & 1.8 & 2.4 & 3.0 \\
\hline$y$ & 5.0 & 9.67 & 18.7 & 36.1 & 69.8 & 135.0 \\
\hline $\lg y$ & 0.70 & 0.99 & 1.27 & 1.56 & 1.84 & 2.13 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

A graph of $\lg y$ against $x$ is shown in Figure 18.6 and, since a straight line results, the law $\boldsymbol{y}=\boldsymbol{a} \boldsymbol{b}^{\boldsymbol{x}}$ is verified.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-165.jpg?height=882&width=765&top_left_y=1528&top_left_x=1109)

Figure 18.6

Gradient of straight line,
$\lg \boldsymbol{b}=\frac{A B}{B C}=\frac{2.13-1.17}{3.0-1.0}=\frac{0.96}{2.0}=0.48$
Hence, $\boldsymbol{b}=\operatorname{antilog} 0.48=10^{0.48}=\mathbf{3 . 0}$, correct to 2 significant figures.

Vertical axis intercept, $\lg a=0.70$, from which
$$\begin{aligned}
\boldsymbol{a} & =\operatorname{antilog} 0.70 \\
& =10^{0.70}=\mathbf{5 . 0}, \text { correct to } \\
& 2 \text { significant figures. }
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, the law of the graph is $\boldsymbol{y}=\mathbf{5 . 0 ( 3 . 0})^{\boldsymbol{x}}$
(a) When $x=2.1, \boldsymbol{y}=5.0(3.0)^{2.1}=\mathbf{5 0 . 2}$
(b) When $y=100,100=5.0(3.0)^{x}$, from which $100 / 5.0=(3.0)^{x}$
i.e. $\quad 20=(3.0)^{x}$

Taking logarithms of both sides gives
$$\begin{aligned}
\lg 20 & =\lg (3.0)^{x}=x \lg 3.0 \\
\text { Hence, } \boldsymbol{x} & =\frac{\lg 20}{\lg 3.0}=\frac{1.3010}{0.4771}=\mathbf{2 . 7 3}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 7. The current $i \mathrm{~mA}$ flowing in a capacitor which is being discharged varies with time $t \mathrm{~ms}$, as shown below.
\begin{tabular}{lllllll}
$i(\mathrm{~mA})$ & 203 & 61.14 & 22.49 & 6.13 & 2.49 & 0.615 \\
$t(\mathrm{~ms})$ & 100 & 160 & 210 & 275 & 320 & 390
\end{tabular}

Show that these results are related by a law of the form $i=I e^{t / T}$, where $I$ and $T$ are constants. Determine the approximate values of $I$ and $T$

Taking Napierian logarithms of both sides of
$$\begin{aligned}
& \qquad \begin{aligned}
i & =I e^{t / T} \\
\text { gives } \quad \begin{array}{l}
\ln i
\end{array} & =\ln \left(I e^{t / T}\right)=\ln I+\ln e^{t / T} \\
& =\ln I+\frac{t}{T} \ln e
\end{aligned} \\
& \text { i.e. } \quad \begin{aligned}
& \ln i=\ln I+\frac{t}{T} \text { since } \ln e=1 \\
& \text { or } \\
& \text { anich compares with } \quad y=\left(\frac{1}{T}\right) t+\ln I
\end{aligned} \\
& \text { showing that } \ln i \text { is plotted vertically against } t \text { hor- } \\
& \text { izontally, with gradient } \frac{1}{T} \text { and vertical-axis intercept } \\
& \ln I .
\end{aligned}$$

Another table of values is drawn up as shown below.
\begin{tabular}{|lllllll|}
\hline$t$ & 100 & 160 & 210 & 275 & 320 & 390 \\
\hline$i$ & 203 & 61.14 & 22.49 & 6.13 & 2.49 & 0.615 \\
\hline $\ln i$ & 5.31 & 4.11 & 3.11 & 1.81 & 0.91 & -0.49 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

A graph of $\ln i$ against $t$ is shown in Figure 18.7 and, since a straight line results, the law $i=I \boldsymbol{e}^{\boldsymbol{t} / T}$ is verified.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-166.jpg?height=761&width=748&top_left_y=756&top_left_x=1093)

Figure 18.7

Gradient of straight line, $\quad \frac{1}{T}=\frac{A B}{B C}=\frac{5.30-1.30}{100-300}$

Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{4.0}{-200}=-0.02 \\
\boldsymbol{T} & =\frac{1}{-0.02}=\mathbf{- 5 0}
\end{aligned}$$

Selecting any point on the graph, say point $D$, where $t=200$ and $\ln i=3.31$, and substituting
into
$$\ln i=\left(\frac{1}{T}\right) t+\ln I$$
gives
$$3.31=-\frac{1}{50}(200)+\ln I$$
from which, $\quad \ln I=3.31+4.0=7.31$
and $\quad \boldsymbol{I}=\operatorname{antilog} 7.31=e^{7.31}=1495$ or $\mathbf{1 5 0 0}$
correct to 3 significant figures.
Hence, the law of the graph is $i=1500 e^{-t / 50}$

\section*{Chapter 19}

\section*{Graphical solution of equations}

\subsection*{19.1 Graphical solution of simultaneous equations}

Linear simultaneous equations in two unknowns may be solved graphically by
(a) plotting the two straight lines on the same axes, and
(b) noting their point of intersection.

The co-ordinates of the point of intersection give the required solution.
Here are some worked problems to demonstrate the graphical solution of simultaneous equations.

Problem 1. Solve graphically the simultaneous equations
$$\begin{array}{r}
2 x-y=4 \\
x+y=5
\end{array}$$

Rearranging each equation into $y=m x+c$ form gives
$$\begin{aligned}
& y=2 x-4 \\
& y=-x+5
\end{aligned}$$

Only three co-ordinates need be calculated for each graph since both are straight lines.
\begin{tabular}{|lrrr|}
\hline$x$ & 0 & 1 & 2 \\
\hline$y=2 x-4$ & -4 & -2 & 0 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\begin{tabular}{|l|lll|}
\hline$x$ & 0 & 1 & 2 \\
\hline$y=-x+5$ & 5 & 4 & 3 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

Each of the graphs is plotted as shown in Figure 19.1. The point of intersection is at $(3,2)$ and since this is the only point which lies simultaneously on both lines then $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{3}, \boldsymbol{y}=\mathbf{2}$ is the solution of the simultaneous equations.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-168.jpg?height=717&width=741&top_left_y=1469&top_left_x=1094)

Figure 19.1
Problem 2. Solve graphically the equations
$$\begin{array}{r}
1.20 x+y=1.80 \\
x-5.0 y=8.50
\end{array}$$

Rearranging each equation into $y=m x+c$ form gives
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
& y=-1.20 x+1.80 \\
& y=\frac{x}{5.0}-\frac{8.5}{5.0} \\
& y=0.20 x-1.70
\end{aligned}$$

Three co-ordinates are calculated for each equation as shown below.
\begin{tabular}{|lllc|}
\hline$x$ & 0 & 1 & 2 \\
\hline$y=-1.20 x+1.80$ & 1.80 & 0.60 & -0.60 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\begin{tabular}{|cccc|}
$x$ & 0 & 1 & 2 \\
$y=0.20 x-1.70$ & -1.70 & -1.50 & -1.30 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

The two sets of co-ordinates are plotted as shown in Figure 19.2. The point of intersection is $(2.50,-1.20)$. Hence, the solution of the simultaneous equations is $x=2.50, y=-1.20$
(It is sometimes useful to initially sketch the two straight lines to determine the region where the point of intersection is. Then, for greater accuracy, a graph having a smaller range of values can be drawn to 'magnify' the point of intersection.)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-169.jpg?height=549&width=633&top_left_y=1369&top_left_x=340)

Figure 19.2

\subsection*{19.2 Graphical solution of quadratic equations}

A general quadratic equation is of the form $\boldsymbol{y}=\boldsymbol{a} \boldsymbol{x}^{\mathbf{2}}+\boldsymbol{b} \boldsymbol{x}+\boldsymbol{c}$, where $a, b$ and $c$ are constants and $a$ is not equal to zero.
A graph of a quadratic equation always produces a shape called a parabola.
The gradients of the curves between 0 and $A$ and between $B$ and $C$ in Figure 19.3 are positive, whilst the gradient between $A$ and $B$ is negative. Points such as $A$ and $B$ are called turning points. At $A$ the gradient is zero and, as $x$ increases, the gradient of the curve changes from positive just before $A$ to negative just after. Such a point is called a maximum value. At $B$ the gradient is also zero and, as $x$ increases, the gradient of the curve changes from negative just before $B$ to positive just after. Such a point is called a minimum value.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-169.jpg?height=334&width=527&top_left_y=1750&top_left_x=1228)

Figure 19.3
Following are three examples of solutions using quadratic graphs.
(a) $y=a x^{2}$

Graphs of $y=x^{2}, y=3 x^{2}$ and $y=\frac{1}{2} x^{2}$ are shown in Figure 19.4. All have minimum values at the origin $(0,0)$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-170.jpg?height=253&width=221&top_left_y=307&top_left_x=264)
(a)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-170.jpg?height=253&width=212&top_left_y=307&top_left_x=488)
(b)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-170.jpg?height=253&width=280&top_left_y=307&top_left_x=717)
(c)

Figure 19.4
Graphs of $y=-x^{2}, y=-3 x^{2}$ and $y=-\frac{1}{2} x^{2}$ are shown in Figure 19.5. All have maximum values at the origin $(0,0)$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-170.jpg?height=251&width=226&top_left_y=848&top_left_x=245)
(a)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-170.jpg?height=253&width=218&top_left_y=850&top_left_x=493)
(b)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-170.jpg?height=251&width=275&top_left_y=848&top_left_x=736)
(c)

Figure 19.5
When $y=a x^{2}$,
(i) curves are symmetrical about the $y$-axis,
(ii) the magnitude of $a$ affects the gradient of the curve, and
(iii) the sign of $a$ determines whether it has a maximum or minimum value.
(b) $y=a x^{2}+c$

Graphs of $y=x^{2}+3, y=x^{2}-2, y=-x^{2}+2$ and $y=-2 x^{2}-1$ are shown in Figure 19.6.
When $y=a x^{2}+c$,
(i) curves are symmetrical about the $y$-axis,
(ii) the magnitude of $a$ affects the gradient of the curve, and
(iii) the constant $c$ is the $y$-axis intercept.
(c) $y=a x^{2}+b x+c$

Whenever $b$ has a value other than zero the curve is displaced to the right or left of the $y$-axis.
When $b / a$ is positive, the curve is displaced $b / 2 a$ to the left of the $y$-axis, as shown in Figure 19.7(a). When $b / a$ is negative, the curve is displaced $b / 2 a$ to the right of the $y$-axis, as shown in Figure 19.7(b).

Quadratic equations of the form $a x^{2}+b x+c=0$ may be solved graphically by
(a) plotting the graph $y=a x^{2}+b x+c$, and
(b) noting the points of intersection on the $x$-axis (i.e. where $y=0$ ).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-170.jpg?height=823&width=758&top_left_y=299&top_left_x=1080)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-170.jpg?height=481&width=742&top_left_y=1180&top_left_x=1088)

Figure 19.7
The $x$ values of the points of intersection give the required solutions since at these points both $y=0$ and $a x^{2}+b x+c=0$.
The number of solutions, or roots, of a quadratic equation depends on how many times the curve cuts the $x$-axis. There can be no real roots, as in Figure 19.7(a), one root, as in Figures 19.4 and 19.5, or two roots, as in Figure 19.7(b).
Here are some worked problems to demonstrate the graphical solution of quadratic equations.

Problem 3. Solve the quadratic equation $4 x^{2}+4 x-15=0$ graphically, given that the solutions lie in the range $x=-3$ to $x=2$. Determine also the co-ordinates and nature of the turning point of the curve

Let $y=4 x^{2}+4 x-15$. A table of values is drawn up as shown below.
\begin{tabular}{lrrrrrr}
$x$ \\
$y=4 x^{2}+4 x-15$ & 9 & -7 & -2 & -1 & 0 & 1 \\
2
\end{tabular}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-171.jpg?height=785&width=744&top_left_y=589&top_left_x=284)

Figure 19.8
A graph of $y=4 x^{2}+4 x-15$ is shown in Figure 19.8. The only points where $y=4 x^{2}+4 x-15$ and $y=0$ are the points marked $A$ and $B$. This occurs at $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 2 . 5}$ and $\boldsymbol{x}=1.5$ and these are the solutions of the quadratic equation $4 x^{2}+4 x-15=0$.
By substituting $x=-2.5$ and $x=1.5$ into the original equation the solutions may be checked.
The curve has a turning point at $(-0.5,-16)$ and the nature of the point is a minimum.
An alternative graphical method of solving $4 x^{2}+4 x-15=0$ is to rearrange the equation as $4 x^{2}=-4 x+15$ and then plot two separate graphs - in this case, $y=4 x^{2}$ and $y=-4 x+15$. Their points of intersection give the roots of the equation $4 x^{2}=-4 x+15$, i.e. $4 x^{2}+4 x-15=0$. This is shown in Figure 19.9, where the roots are $x=-2.5$ and $x=1.5$, as before.

Problem 4. Solve graphically the quadratic equation $-5 x^{2}+9 x+7.2=0$ given that the solutions lie between $x=-1$ and $x=3$. Determine also the co-ordinates of the turning point and state its nature
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-171.jpg?height=649&width=765&top_left_y=310&top_left_x=1109)

Figure 19.9
Let $y=-5 x^{2}+9 x+7.2$. A table of values is drawn up as shown below.
\begin{tabular}{|lcccc|}
\hline$x$ & -1 & -0.5 & 0 & 1 \\
\hline$y=-5 x^{2}+9 x+7.2$ & -6.8 & 1.45 & 7.2 & 11.2 \\
\hline$x$ & 2 & 2.5 & 3 \\
\hline$y=-5 x^{2}+9 x+7.2$ & 5.2 & -1.55 & -10.8 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

A graph of $y=-5 x^{2}+9 x+7.2$ is shown plotted in Figure 19.10. The graph crosses the $x$-axis (i.e. where $\boldsymbol{y}=0$ ) at $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 0 . 6}$ and $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{2} .4$ and these are the solutions of the quadratic equation $-5 x^{2}+9 x+7.2=0$. The turning point is a maximum, having co-ordinates (0.9, 11.25).

Problem 5. Plot a graph of $y=2 x^{2}$ and hence solve the equations
(a) $2 x^{2}-8=0$
(b) $2 x^{2}-x-3=0$

A graph of $y=2 x^{2}$ is shown in Figure 19.11.
(a) Rearranging $2 x^{2}-8=0$ gives $2 x^{2}=8$ and the solution of this equation is obtained from the points of intersection of $y=2 x^{2}$ and $y=8$; i.e., at coordinates $(-2,8)$ and $(2,8)$, shown as $A$ and $B$, respectively, in Figure 19.11. Hence, the solutions of $2 x^{2}-8=0$ are $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 2}$ and $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{+ 2}$.
(b) Rearranging $2 x^{2}-x-3=0$ gives $2 x^{2}=x+3$ and the solution of this equation is obtained from the points of intersection of $y=2 x^{2}$ and $y=x+3$; i.e., at $C$ and $D$ in Figure 19.11. Hence, the solutions of $2 x^{2}-x-3=0$ are $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 1}$ and $x=1.5$

Problem 6. Plot the graph of $y=-2 x^{2}+3 x+6$ for values of $x$ from $x=-2$ to $x=4$. Use the graph to find the roots of the following equations.
(a) $-2 x^{2}+3 x+6=0$
(b) $-2 x^{2}+3 x+2=0$
(c) $-2 x^{2}+3 x+9=0$
(d) $-2 x^{2}+x+5=0$

A table of values for $y=-2 x^{2}+3 x+6$ is drawn up as shown below.
\begin{tabular}{|rrrrrrrr|}
\hline$x$ & -2 & -1 & 0 & 1 & 2 & 3 & 4 \\
$y$ & -8 & 1 & 6 & 7 & 4 & -3 & -14 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

A graph of $y=-2 x^{2}+3 x+6$ is shown in Figure 19.12.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-172.jpg?height=993&width=755&top_left_y=420&top_left_x=1087)

Figure 19.12
(a) The parabola $y=-2 x^{2}+3 x+6$ and the straight line $y=0$ intersect at $A$ and $B$, where $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 1 . 1 3}$ and $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{2 . 6 3}$, and these are the roots of the equation $-2 x^{2}+3 x+6=0$.
(b) Comparing
$$\begin{aligned}
& y=-2 x^{2}+3 x+6 \\
& 0=-2 x^{2}+3 x+2
\end{aligned}$$
with
shows that, if 4 is added to both sides of equation (2), the RHS of both equations will be the same. Hence, $4=-2 x^{2}+3 x+6$. The solution of this equation is found from the points of intersection of the line $y=4$ and the parabola $y=-2 x^{2}+3 x+6$; i.e., points $C$ and $D$ in Figure 19.12. Hence, the roots of $-2 x^{2}+3 x+2=0$ are $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 0 . 5}$ and $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{2}$.
(c) $-2 x^{2}+3 x+9=0$ may be rearranged as $-2 x^{2}+3 x+6=-3$ and the solution of this equation is obtained from the points of intersection of the line $y=-3$ and the parabola $y=-2 x^{2}+3 x+6$; i.e., at points $E$ and $F$ in Figure 19.12. Hence, the roots of $-2 x^{2}+3 x+9=0$ are $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 1 . 5}$ and $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{3}$.
(d) Comparing
$$y=-2 x^{2}+3 x+6$$
with
$$0=-2 x^{2}+x+5$$
shows that, if $2 x+1$ is added to both sides of equation (4), the RHS of both equations will be the same. Hence, equation (4) may be written as $2 x+1=-2 x^{2}+3 x+6$. The solution of this equation is found from the points of intersection of the line $y=2 x+1$ and the parabola $y=-2 x^{2}+3 x+6$; i.e., points $G$ and $H$ in Figure 19.12. Hence, the roots of $-2 x^{2}+x+5=0$ are $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 1 . 3 5}$ and $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{1 . 8 5}$

\subsection*{19.3 Graphical solution of linear and quadratic equations simultaneously}

The solution of linear and quadratic equations simultaneously may be achieved graphically by
(a) plotting the straight line and parabola on the same axes, and
(b) noting the points of intersection.

The co-ordinates of the points of intersection give the required solutions.
Here is a worked problem to demonstrate the simultaneous solution of a linear and quadratic equation.

Problem 7. Determine graphically the values of $x$ and $y$ which simultaneously satisfy the equations $y=2 x^{2}-3 x-4$ and $y=2-4 x$
$y=2 x^{2}-3 x-4$ is a parabola and a table of values is drawn up as shown below.
\begin{tabular}{|rrrrrrr|}
\hline$x$ & -2 & -1 & 0 & 1 & 2 & 3 \\
$y$ & 10 & 1 & -4 & -5 & -2 & 5 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
$y=2-4 x$ is a straight line and only three co-ordinates need be calculated:
\begin{tabular}{|rrrr|}
$x$ & 0 & 1 & 2 \\
$y$ & 2 & -2 & -6
\end{tabular}

The two graphs are plotted in Figure 19.13 and the points of intersection, shown as $A$ and $B$, are at coordinates $(-2,10)$ and $(1.5,-4)$. Hence, the simultaneous solutions occur when $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 2}, \boldsymbol{y}=\mathbf{1 0}$ and when $x=1.5, y=-4$.
These solutions may be checked by substituting into each of the original equations.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-174.jpg?height=823&width=603&top_left_y=315&top_left_x=328)

Figure 19.13

\subsection*{19.4 Graphical solution of cubic equations}

A cubic equation of the form $a x^{3}+b x^{2}+c x+d=0$ may be solved graphically by
(a) plotting the graph $y=a x^{3}+b x^{2}+c x+d$, and
(b) noting the points of intersection on the $x$-axis (i.e. where $y=0$ ).

The $x$-values of the points of intersection give the required solution since at these points both $y=0$ and $a x^{3}+b x^{2}+c x+d=0$.
The number of solutions, or roots, of a cubic equation depends on how many times the curve cuts the $x$-axis and there can be one, two or three possible roots, as shown in Figure 19.14.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-174.jpg?height=221&width=215&top_left_y=665&top_left_x=1091)
(a)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-174.jpg?height=218&width=245&top_left_y=667&top_left_x=1328)
(b)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-174.jpg?height=218&width=264&top_left_y=667&top_left_x=1576)
(c)

Figure 19.14

Here are some worked problems to demonstrate the graphical solution of cubic equations.

Let $y=4 x^{3}-8 x^{2}-15 x+9$. A table of values is drawn up as shown below.
\begin{tabular}{rrrrrrr|}
$x$ & -2 & -1 & 0 & 1 & 2 & 3 \\
$y$ & -25 & 12 & 9 & -10 & -21 & 0
\end{tabular}

A graph of $y=4 x^{3}-8 x^{2}-15 x+9$ is shown in Figure 19.15.
The graph crosses the $x$-axis (where $y=0$ ) at $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{- 1 . 5}$, $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{0 . 5}$ and $\boldsymbol{x}=\mathbf{3}$ and these are the solutions to the cubic equation $4 x^{3}-8 x^{2}-15 x+9=0$.
The turning points occur at $(\mathbf{- 0 . 6}, \mathbf{1 4 . 2})$, which is a maximum, and (2, -21), which is a minimum.

\section*{Chapter 20}

\section*{Angles and triangles}

\subsection*{20.1 Introduction}

Trigonometry is a subject that involves the measurement of sides and angles of triangles and their relationship to each other. This chapter involves the measurement of angles and introduces types of triangle.

\subsection*{20.2 Angular measurement}

An angle is the amount of rotation between two straight lines. Angles may be measured either in degrees or in radians.
If a circle is divided into 360 equal parts, then each part is called $\mathbf{1}$ degree and is written as $\mathbf{1}^{\circ}$
$$\begin{array}{ll}
\text { i.e. } & \mathbf{1} \text { revolution }=360^{\circ} \\
\text { or } & \mathbf{1} \text { degree is } \frac{1}{360} \text { th of a revolution }
\end{array}$$

Some angles are given special names.
- Any angle between $0^{\circ}$ and $90^{\circ}$ is called an acute angle.
- An angle equal to $90^{\circ}$ is called a right angle.
- Any angle between $90^{\circ}$ and $180^{\circ}$ is called an obtuse angle.
- Any angle greater than $180^{\circ}$ and less than $360^{\circ}$ is called a reflex angle.
- An angle of $180^{\circ}$ lies on a straight line.
- If two angles add up to $90^{\circ}$ they are called complementary angles.
- If two angles add up to $180^{\circ}$ they are called supplementary angles.
- Parallel lines are straight lines which are in the same plane and never meet. Such lines are denoted by arrows, as in Figure 20.1.
- A straight line which crosses two parallel lines is called a transversal (see $M N$ in Figure 20.1).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-178.jpg?height=462&width=516&top_left_y=1046&top_left_x=1209)

Figure 20.1

With reference to Figure 20.1,
(a) $a=c, b=d, e=g$ and $f=h$. Such pairs of angles are called vertically opposite angles.
(b) $\quad a=e, b=f, c=g$ and $d=h$. Such pairs of angles are called corresponding angles.
(c) $c=e$ and $b=h$. Such pairs of angles are called alternate angles.
(d) $b+e=180^{\circ}$ and $c+h=180^{\circ}$. Such pairs of angles are called interior angles.

\subsection*{20.2.1 Minutes and seconds}

One degree may be sub-divided into 60 parts, called minutes.
i.e.
$$1 \text { degree = } 60 \text { minutes }$$
which is written as $\quad \mathbf{1}^{\circ}=\mathbf{6 0}^{\prime}$.

41 degrees and 29 minutes is written as $41^{\circ} 29^{\prime} .41^{\circ} 29^{\prime}$ is equivalent to $41 \frac{29^{\circ}}{60}=41.483^{\circ}$ as a decimal, correct to 3 decimal places by calculator.
1 minute further subdivides into 60 seconds,
i.e.
$$1 \text { minute }=60 \text { seconds }$$
which is written as
$$1^{\prime}=60^{\prime \prime} .$$
(Notice that for minutes, 1 dash is used and for seconds, 2 dashes are used.)
For example, 56 degrees, 36 minutes and 13 seconds is written as $56^{\circ} 36^{\prime} 13^{\prime \prime}$.

\subsection*{20.2.2 Radians and degrees}

One radian is defined as the angle subtended at the centre of a circle by an arc equal in length to the radius. (For more on circles, see Chapter 26.)
With reference to Figure 20.2, for arc length $s$, $\theta$ radians $=\frac{s}{r}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-179.jpg?height=465&width=462&top_left_y=1297&top_left_x=423)

Figure 20.2

When $s$ is the whole circumference, i.e. when $s=2 \pi r$,
$$\theta=\frac{s}{r}=\frac{2 \pi r}{r}=2 \pi$$

In one revolution, $\theta=360^{\circ}$. Hence, the relationship between degrees and radians is
$$360^{\circ}=2 \pi \text { radians or } \mathbf{1 8 0} \mathbf{0}^{\circ}=\boldsymbol{\pi} \mathbf{r a d}$$
i.e. $\quad 1 \mathbf{r a d}=\frac{180^{\circ}}{\pi} \approx 57.30^{\circ}$

Here are some worked examples on angular measurement.

Problem 1. Evaluate $43^{\circ} 29^{\prime}+27^{\circ} 43^{\prime}$
$$\begin{array}{r}
43^{\circ} 29^{\prime} \\
+\quad \frac{27^{\circ} 43^{\prime}}{} \begin{array}{c}
71^{\circ} 12^{\prime} \\
\hline 1^{\circ}
\end{array}
\end{array}$$
(i) $29^{\prime}+43^{\prime}=72^{\prime}$
(ii) Since $60^{\prime}=1^{\circ}, 72^{\prime}=1^{\circ} 12^{\prime}$
(iii) The $12^{\prime}$ is placed in the minutes column and $1^{\circ}$ is carried in the degrees column.
(iv) $43^{\circ}+27^{\circ}+1^{\circ}($ carried $)=71^{\circ}$. Place $71^{\circ}$ in the degrees column.

This answer can be obtained using the calculator as follows.
1. Enter 43
2. Press ${ }^{\circ}$,,
3. Enter 29
4. Press ${ }^{\circ}$, , ,
5. Press +
6. Enter 27
7. Press ${ }^{\circ}$, ,
8. Enter 43
9. Press ${ }^{\circ}$,, ,
10. Press $=\quad$ Answer $=71^{\circ} \mathbf{1 2}^{\prime}$

Thus, $\mathbf{4 3}^{\circ} \mathbf{2 9}^{\prime}+\mathbf{2 7}^{\circ} \mathbf{4 3}^{\prime}=\mathbf{7 1}^{\circ} 12^{\prime}$.
Problem 2. Evaluate $84^{\circ} 13^{\prime}-56^{\circ} 39^{\prime}$
$$\begin{array}{r}
84^{\circ} 13^{\prime} \\
-\quad 56^{\circ} 39^{\prime} \\
\hline 27^{\circ} 34^{\prime} \\
\hline
\end{array}$$
(i) $13^{\prime}-39^{\prime}$ cannot be done.
(ii) $1^{\circ}$ or $60^{\prime}$ is 'borrowed' from the degrees column, which leaves $83^{\circ}$ in that column.
(iii) $\left(60^{\prime}+13^{\prime}\right)-39^{\prime}=34^{\prime}$, which is placed in the minutes column.
(iv) $83^{\circ}-56^{\circ}=27^{\circ}$, which is placed in the degrees column.

This answer can be obtained using the calculator as follows.
1. Enter 84
2. Press ${ }^{\circ}$,,
3. Enter 13
4. Press ${ }^{\circ}$, , ,
5. Press -
6. Enter 56
7. Press ${ }^{\circ}$, ,
8. Enter 39
9. Press ${ }^{\circ}$,,
10. Press $=\quad$ Answer $=27^{\circ} 34^{\prime}$

Thus, $84^{\circ} 13^{\prime}-\mathbf{5 6}^{\circ} \mathbf{3 9} 9^{\prime}=\mathbf{2 7}^{\circ} 34^{\prime}$.
Problem 3. Evaluate $19^{\circ} 51^{\prime} 47^{\prime \prime}+63^{\circ} 27^{\prime} 34^{\prime \prime}$
$$\begin{array}{r}
19^{\circ} 51^{\prime} 47^{\prime \prime} \\
+\frac{63^{\circ} 27^{\prime} 34^{\prime \prime}}{} \frac{83^{\circ} 19^{\prime} 21^{\prime \prime}}{1^{\circ} 1^{\prime}}
\end{array}$$
(i) $47^{\prime \prime}+34^{\prime \prime}=81^{\prime \prime}$
(ii) Since $60^{\prime \prime}=1^{\prime}, 81^{\prime \prime}=1^{\prime} 21^{\prime \prime}$
(iii) The $21^{\prime \prime}$ is placed in the seconds column and $1^{\prime}$ is carried in the minutes column.
(iv) $51^{\prime}+27^{\prime}+1^{\prime}=79^{\prime}$
(v) Since $60^{\prime}=1^{\circ}, 79^{\prime}=1^{\circ} 19^{\prime}$
(vi) The $19^{\prime}$ is placed in the minutes column and $1^{\circ}$ is carried in the degrees column.
(vii) $19^{\circ}+63^{\circ}+1^{\circ}($ carried $)=83^{\circ}$. Place $83^{\circ}$ in the degrees column.

This answer can be obtained using the calculator as follows.
1. Enter 19
4. Press ${ }^{\circ}$,,
7. Press +
10. Enter 27
12. Enter 34
14. Press $=\quad$ Answer $=\mathbf{8 3}^{\circ} \mathbf{1 9}^{\prime} \mathbf{2 1}^{\prime \prime}$
2. Press ${ }^{\circ}$, , ,

$$\begin{aligned}

\frac{27^{\circ}}{60} & =0.45^{\circ} \quad \text { by calculator }
\end{aligned}$$

Here are some further worked examples on angular measurement.

Problem 7. State the general name given to the following angles: (a) $157^{\circ}$ (b) $49^{\circ}$ (c) $90^{\circ}$ (d) $245^{\circ}$
(a) Any angle between $90^{\circ}$ and $180^{\circ}$ is called an obtuse angle.
Thus, $157^{\circ}$ is an obtuse angle.
(b) Any angle between $0^{\circ}$ and $90^{\circ}$ is called an acute angle.

Thus, $49^{\circ}$ is an acute angle.
(c) An angle equal to $90^{\circ}$ is called a right angle.
(d) Any angle greater than $180^{\circ}$ and less than $360^{\circ}$ is called a reflex angle.
Thus, $245^{\circ}$ is a reflex angle.

\section*{Problem 8. Find the angle complementary to $48^{\circ} 39^{\prime}$}

If two angles add up to $90^{\circ}$ they are called complementary angles. Hence, the angle complementary to $48^{\circ} 39^{\prime}$ is
$$90^{\circ}-48^{\circ} 39^{\prime}=\mathbf{4 1}^{\circ} \mathbf{2 1} \mathbf{1}^{\prime}$$

Problem 9. Find the angle supplementary to $74^{\circ} 25^{\prime}$

If two angles add up to $180^{\circ}$ they are called supplementary angles. Hence, the angle supplementary to $74^{\circ} 25^{\prime}$ is
$$180^{\circ}-74^{\circ} 25^{\prime}=\mathbf{1 0 5}^{\circ} \mathbf{3 5}^{\prime}$$

Problem 10. Evaluate angle $\theta$ in each of the diagrams shown in Figure 20.3
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-181.jpg?height=280&width=744&top_left_y=1487&top_left_x=284)

Figure 20.3
(a) The symbol shown in Figure 20.4 is called a right angle and it equals $90^{\circ}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-181.jpg?height=226&width=232&top_left_y=1994&top_left_x=535)

Figure 20.4
Hence, from Figure 20.3(a),
$$\begin{aligned}
\theta+41^{\circ} & =90^{\circ} \\
\text { from which, } \quad \boldsymbol{\theta} & =90^{\circ}-41^{\circ}=\mathbf{4 9 ^ { \circ }}
\end{aligned}$$
(b) An angle of $180^{\circ}$ lies on a straight line. Hence, from Figure 20.3(b),
$$\begin{aligned}
180^{\circ} & =53^{\circ}+\theta+44^{\circ} \\
\text { from which, } \quad \boldsymbol{\theta} & =180^{\circ}-53^{\circ}-44^{\circ}=\mathbf{8 3}^{\circ}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 11. Evaluate angle $\theta$ in the diagram shown in Figure 20.5
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-181.jpg?height=486&width=470&top_left_y=747&top_left_x=1251)

Figure 20.5

There are $360^{\circ}$ in a complete revolution of a circle.
Thus, $360^{\circ}=58^{\circ}+108^{\circ}+64^{\circ}+39^{\circ}+\theta$
from which, $\theta=360^{\circ}-58^{\circ}-108^{\circ}-64^{\circ}-39^{\circ}=91^{\circ}$

Problem 12. Two straight lines $A B$ and $C D$ intersect at 0 . If $\angle A O C$ is $43^{\circ}$, find $\angle A O D$, $\angle D O B$ and $\angle B O C$

From Figure 20.6, $\angle A O D$ is supplementary to $\angle A O C$.
Hence, $\angle A O D=180^{\circ}-43^{\circ}=137^{\circ}$.
When two straight lines intersect, the vertically opposite angles are equal.
Hence, $\quad \angle D O B=43^{\circ}$ and $\angle B O C 137^{\circ}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-181.jpg?height=283&width=660&top_left_y=2044&top_left_x=1161)

Figure 20.6

Problem 13. Determine angle $\beta$ in Figure 20.7
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-182.jpg?height=384&width=367&top_left_y=399&top_left_x=451)

Figure 20.7
$\alpha=180^{\circ}-133^{\circ}=47^{\circ}$ (i.e. supplementary angles). $\alpha=\beta=47^{\circ}$ (corresponding angles between parallel lines).

Problem 14. Determine the value of angle $\theta$ in Figure 20.8
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-182.jpg?height=210&width=460&top_left_y=1213&top_left_x=399)

Figure 20.8

Let a straight line $F G$ be drawn through $E$ such that $F G$ is parallel to $A B$ and $C D$.
$\angle B A E=\angle A E F$ (alternate angles between parallel lines $A B$ and $F G$ ), hence $\angle A E F=23^{\circ} 37^{\prime}$.
$\angle E C D=\angle F E C$ (alternate angles between parallel lines $F G$ and $C D$ ), hence $\angle F E C=35^{\circ} 49^{\prime}$.
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Angle } \boldsymbol{\theta}=\angle A E F+\angle F E C & =23^{\circ} 37^{\prime}+35^{\circ} 49^{\prime} \\
& =\mathbf{5 9}^{\circ} \mathbf{2 6}^{\prime}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 15. Determine angles $c$ and $d$ in Figure 20.9
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-182.jpg?height=261&width=405&top_left_y=2120&top_left_x=427)

Figure 20.9
$a=b=46^{\circ}$ (corresponding angles between parallel lines).
Also, $b+c+90^{\circ}=180^{\circ}$ (angles on a straight line).
Hence, $46^{\circ}+c+90^{\circ}=180^{\circ}$, from which, $c=44^{\circ}$. $b$ and $d$ are supplementary, hence $\boldsymbol{d}=180^{\circ}-46^{\circ}$
$$=134^{\circ} .$$

Alternatively, $90^{\circ}+c=d$ (vertically opposite angles).

Problem 16. Convert the following angles to radians, correct to 3 decimal places.
(a) $73^{\circ}$ (b) $25^{\circ} 37^{\prime}$

Although we may be more familiar with degrees, radians is the SI unit of angular measurement in engineering ( 1 radian $\approx 57.3^{\circ}$ ).
(a) Since $180^{\circ}=\pi \mathrm{rad}$ then $1^{\circ}=\frac{\pi}{180} \mathrm{rad}$.

Hence, $\mathbf{7 3}^{\circ}=73 \times \frac{\pi}{180} \mathrm{rad}=\mathbf{1 . 2 7 4} \mathbf{r a d}$.
(b) $25^{\circ} 37^{\prime}=25 \frac{37^{\circ}}{60}=25.616666 \ldots$

Hence, $\quad \mathbf{2 5}^{\circ} \mathbf{3 7} \mathbf{7}^{\prime}=25.616666 \ldots{ }^{\circ}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =25.616666 \ldots \times \frac{\pi}{180} \mathrm{rad} \\
& =\mathbf{0 . 4 4 7} \mathbf{r a d} .
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 17. Convert 0.743 rad to degrees and minutes

Since $180^{\circ}=\pi \mathrm{rad}$ then $1 \mathrm{rad}=\frac{180^{\circ}}{\pi}$
Hence, $\mathbf{0 . 7 4 3} \mathbf{r a d}=0.743 \times \frac{180^{\circ}}{\pi}=42.57076 \ldots \circ$
$$=42^{\circ} 34^{\prime}$$

Since $\pi \mathrm{rad}=180^{\circ}$, then $\frac{\pi}{2} \mathrm{rad}=90^{\circ}, \frac{\pi}{4} \mathrm{rad}=45^{\circ}$, $\frac{\pi}{3} \mathrm{rad}=60^{\circ}$ and $\frac{\pi}{6} \mathrm{rad}=30^{\circ}$

\subsection*{20.3 Triangles}

A triangle is a figure enclosed by three straight lines. The sum of the three angles of a triangle is equal to $180^{\circ}$.

\subsection*{20.3.1 Types of triangle}

An acute-angled triangle is one in which all the angles are acute; i.e., all the angles are less than $90^{\circ}$. An example is shown in triangle $A B C$ in Figure 20.15(a). A right-angled triangle is one which contains a right angle; i.e., one in which one of the angles is $90^{\circ}$. An example is shown in triangle $D E F$ in Figure 20.15(b).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-184.jpg?height=416&width=771&top_left_y=923&top_left_x=244)

Figure 20.15
An obtuse-angled triangle is one which contains an obtuse angle; i.e., one angle which lies between $90^{\circ}$ and $180^{\circ}$. An example is shown in triangle $P Q R$ in Figure 20.16(a).
An equilateral triangle is one in which all the sides and all the angles are equal; i.e., each is $60^{\circ}$. An example is shown in triangle $A B C$ in Figure 20.16(b).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-184.jpg?height=353&width=788&top_left_y=1738&top_left_x=238)

Figure 20.16
An isosceles triangle is one in which two angles and two sides are equal. An example is shown in triangle $E F G$ in Figure 20.17(a).
A scalene triangle is one with unequal angles and therefore unequal sides. An example of an acute angled scalene triangle is shown in triangle $A B C$ in Figure 20.17(b).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-184.jpg?height=606&width=340&top_left_y=318&top_left_x=1094)
(a)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-184.jpg?height=468&width=348&top_left_y=460&top_left_x=1480)
(b)

Figure 20.17
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-184.jpg?height=372&width=538&top_left_y=1045&top_left_x=1195)

Figure 20.18
With reference to Figure 20.18,
(a) Angles $A, B$ and $C$ are called interior angles of the triangle.
(b) Angle $\theta$ is called an exterior angle of the triangle and is equal to the sum of the two opposite interior angles; i.e., $\theta=A+C$.
(c) $a+b+c$ is called the perimeter of the triangle.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-184.jpg?height=503&width=318&top_left_y=1891&top_left_x=1305)

Figure 20.19

A right-angled triangle $A B C$ is shown in Figure 20.19. The point of intersection of two lines is called a vertex (plural vertices); the three vertices of the triangle are labelled as $A, B$ and $C$, respectively. The right angle is angle $C$. The side opposite the right angle is given the special name of the hypotenuse. The hypotenuse, length $A B$ in Figure 20.19, is always the longest side of a right-angled triangle. With reference to angle $B, A C$ is the opposite side and $B C$ is called the adjacent side. With reference to angle $A, B C$ is the opposite side and $A C$ is the adjacent side.
Often sides of a triangle are labelled with lower case letters, $a$ being the side opposite angle $A, b$ being the side opposite angle $B$ and $c$ being the side opposite angle $C$. So, in the triangle $A B C$, length $A B=c$, length $B C=a$ and length $A C=b$. Thus, $c$ is the hypotenuse in the triangle $A B C$.
$\angle$ is the symbol used for 'angle'. For example, in the triangle shown, $\angle C=90^{\circ}$. Another way of indicating an angle is to use all three letters. For example, $\angle A B C$ actually means $\angle B$; i.e., we take the middle letter as the angle. Similarly, $\angle B A C$ means $\angle A$ and $\angle A C B$ means $\angle C$.
Here are some worked examples to help us understand more about triangles.

Problem 18. Name the types of triangle shown in Figure 20.20
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-185.jpg?height=451&width=720&top_left_y=1529&top_left_x=291)

Figure 20.20
(a) Equilateral triangle (since all three sides are equal).
(b) Acute-angled scalene triangle (since all the angles are less than $90^{\circ}$ ).
(c) Right-angled triangle $\left(39^{\circ}+51^{\circ}=90^{\circ}\right.$; hence, the third angle must be $90^{\circ}$, since there are $180^{\circ}$ in a triangle).
(d) Obtuse-angled scalene triangle (since one of the angles lies between $90^{\circ}$ and $180^{\circ}$ ).
(e) Isosceles triangle (since two sides are equal).

Problem 19. In the triangle $A B C$ shown in Figure 20.21, with reference to angle $\theta$, which side is the adjacent?

Figure 20.21

The triangle is right-angled; thus, side $A C$ is the hypotenuse. With reference to angle $\theta$, the opposite side is $B C$. The remaining side, $\boldsymbol{A B}$, is the adjacent side.

Problem 20. In the triangle shown in
Figure 20.22, determine angle $\theta$

Figure 20.22

The sum of the three angles of a triangle is equal to $180^{\circ}$.
The triangle is right-angled. Hence,
$90^{\circ}+56^{\circ}+\angle \theta=180^{\circ}$
from which, $\quad \angle \boldsymbol{\theta}=180^{\circ}-90^{\circ}-56^{\circ}=34^{\circ}$.

Problem 21. Determine the value of $\theta$ and $\alpha$ in Figure 20.23
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-186.jpg?height=308&width=540&top_left_y=334&top_left_x=362)

Figure 20.23

In triangle $A B C, \angle A+\angle B+\angle C=180^{\circ}$ (the angles in a triangle add up to $180^{\circ}$ ).
Hence, $\angle C=180^{\circ}-90^{\circ}-62^{\circ}=28^{\circ}$. Thus, $\angle D C E=28^{\circ}$ (vertically opposite angles).
$\theta=\angle D C E+\angle D E C$ (the exterior angle of a triangle is equal to the sum of the two opposite interior angles).
Hence, $\angle \boldsymbol{\theta}=28^{\circ}+15^{\circ}=\mathbf{4 3}^{\circ}$.
$\angle \alpha$ and $\angle D E C$ are supplementary; thus, $\alpha=180^{\circ}-15^{\circ}=165^{\circ}$.

Problem 22. $A B C$ is an isosceles triangle in which the unequal angle $B A C$ is $56^{\circ} . A B$ is extended to $D$ as shown in Figure 20.24. Find, for the triangle, $\angle A B C$ and $\angle A C B$. Also, calculate $\angle D B C$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-186.jpg?height=338&width=310&top_left_y=1374&top_left_x=477)

Figure 20.24

Since triangle $A B C$ is isosceles, two sides - i.e. $A B$ and $A C$ - are equal and two angles - i.e. $\angle A B C$ and $\angle A C B$ are equal.
The sum of the three angles of a triangle is equal to $180^{\circ}$. Hence, $\angle A B C+\angle A C B=180^{\circ}-56^{\circ}=124^{\circ}$.
Since $\angle A B C=\angle A C B$ then $\angle A B C=\angle A C B=\frac{124^{\circ}}{2}=62^{\circ}$.
An angle of $180^{\circ}$ lies on a straight line; hence, $\angle A B C+\angle D B C=180^{\circ}$ from which, $\angle D B C=180^{\circ}-\angle A B C=180^{\circ}-62^{\circ}=\mathbf{1 1 8}^{\circ}$.
Alternatively, $\angle D B C=\angle A+\angle C$ (exterior angle equals sum of two interior opposite angles), i.e. $\angle D B C=56^{\circ}+62^{\circ}=118^{\circ}$.

Problem 23. Find angles $a, b, c, d$ and $e$ in Figure 20.25
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-186.jpg?height=226&width=329&top_left_y=405&top_left_x=1305)

Figure 20.25
$\boldsymbol{a}=\mathbf{6} \mathbf{2}^{\circ}$ and $\boldsymbol{c}=\mathbf{5 5}^{\circ}$ (alternate angles between parallel lines).
$55^{\circ}+b+62^{\circ}=180^{\circ}$ (angles in a triangle add up to $180^{\circ}$ ); hence, $\boldsymbol{b}=180^{\circ}-55^{\circ}-62^{\circ}=63^{\circ}$.
$b=\boldsymbol{d}=\mathbf{6 3}{ }^{\circ}$ (alternate angles between parallel lines).
$e+55^{\circ}+63^{\circ}=180^{\circ}$ (angles in a triangle add up to $180^{\circ}$ ); hence, $\boldsymbol{e}=180^{\circ}-55^{\circ}-63^{\circ}=62^{\circ}$.
Check: $e=a=62^{\circ}$ (corresponding angles between parallel lines).

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-188.jpg?height=410&width=473&top_left_y=912&top_left_x=393)

Figure 20.33

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-188.jpg?height=312&width=422&top_left_y=1726&top_left_x=391)

Figure 20.34

\subsection*{20.4 Congruent triangles}

Two triangles are said to be congruent if they are equal in all respects; i.e., three angles and three sides in one triangle are equal to three angles and three sides in the other triangle. Two triangles are congruent if
(a) the three sides of one are equal to the three sides of the other (SSS),
(b) two sides of one are equal to two sides of the other and the angles included by these sides are equal (SAS),
(c) two angles of the one are equal to two angles of the other and any side of the first is equal to the corresponding side of the other (ASA), or
(d) their hypotenuses are equal and one other side of one is equal to the corresponding side of the other (RHS).

Problem 24. State which of the pairs of triangles shown in Figure 20.35 are congruent and name their sequence
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-188.jpg?height=677&width=752&top_left_y=1115&top_left_x=1088)

Figure 20.35
(a) Congruent $A B C, F D E$ (angle, side, angle; i.e., ASA).
(b) Congruent GIH, JLK (side, angle, side; i.e., SAS).
(c) Congruent $M N O, R Q P$ (right angle, hypotenuse, side; i.e., RHS).
(d) Not necessarily congruent. It is not indicated that any side coincides.
(e) Congruent $A B C, F E D$ (side, side, side; i.e., SSS).

Problem 25. In Figure 20.36, triangle $P Q R$ is isosceles with $Z$, the mid-point of $P Q$. Prove that triangles $P X Z$ and $Q Y Z$ are congruent and that triangles $R X Z$ and $R Y Z$ are congruent. Determine the values of angles $R P Z$ and $R X Z$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-189.jpg?height=237&width=757&top_left_y=597&top_left_x=283)

Figure 20.36

Since triangle $P Q R$ is isosceles, $P R=R Q$ and thus $\angle Q P R=\angle R Q P$.
$\angle R X Z=\angle Q P R+28^{\circ}$ and $\angle R Y Z=\angle R Q P+28^{\circ}$ (exterior angles of a triangle equal the sum of the two interior opposite angles). Hence, $\angle R X Z=\angle R Y Z$.
$\angle P X Z=180^{\circ}-\angle R X Z$ and $\angle Q Y Z=180^{\circ}-\angle R Y Z$. Thus, $\angle P X Z=\angle Q Y Z$.

Triangles $P X Z$ and $Q Y Z$ are congruent since $\angle X P Z=\angle Y Q Z, P Z=Z Q$ and $\angle X Z P=\angle Y Z Q$ (ASA). Hence, $X Z=Y Z$.

Triangles $P R Z$ and $Q R Z$ are congruent since $P R=R Q, \quad \angle R P Z=\angle R Q Z \quad$ and $\quad P Z=Z Q \quad$ (SAS). Hence, $\angle R Z X=\angle R Z Y$.

Triangles $R X Z$ and $R Y Z$ are congruent since $\angle R X Z=\angle R Y Z, X Z=Y Z$ and $\angle R Z X=\angle R Z Y$ (ASA). $\angle Q R Z=67^{\circ}$ and thus $\angle P R Q=67^{\circ}+67^{\circ}=134^{\circ}$.
Hence, $\angle \boldsymbol{R} \boldsymbol{P} Z=\angle R Q Z=\frac{180^{\circ}-134^{\circ}}{2}=\mathbf{2 3}^{\circ}$. $\angle \boldsymbol{R} \boldsymbol{X Z}=23^{\circ}+28^{\circ}=\mathbf{5 1}^{\circ}$ (external angle of a triangle equals the sum of the two interior opposite angles).

\subsection*{20.5 Similar triangles}

Two triangles are said to be similar if the angles of one triangle are equal to the angles of the other triangle. With reference to Figure 20.38, triangles $A B C$ and $P Q R$ are similar and the corresponding sides are in proportion to each other,
i.e.
$$\frac{p}{a}=\frac{q}{b}=\frac{r}{c}$$

Figure 20.38

Problem 26. In Figure 20.39, find the length of side $a$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-190.jpg?height=353&width=654&top_left_y=336&top_left_x=302)

Figure 20.39

In triangle $A B C, 50^{\circ}+70^{\circ}+\angle C=180^{\circ}$, from which $\angle C=60^{\circ}$.
In triangle $D E F, \angle E=180^{\circ}-50^{\circ}-60^{\circ}=70^{\circ}$. Hence, triangles $A B C$ and $D E F$ are similar, since their angles are the same. Since corresponding sides are in proportion to each other,
$$\frac{a}{d}=\frac{c}{f} \quad \text { i.e. } \quad \frac{a}{4.42}=\frac{12.0}{5.0}$$

Hence, side, $\boldsymbol{a}=\frac{12.0}{5.0}(4.42)=\mathbf{1 0 . 6 1} \mathbf{~ c m}$.
Problem 27. In Figure 20.40, find the dimensions marked $r$ and $p$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-190.jpg?height=346&width=728&top_left_y=1378&top_left_x=265)

Figure 20.40

In triangle $P Q R, \angle Q=180^{\circ}-90^{\circ}-35^{\circ}=55^{\circ}$.
In triangle $X Y Z, \angle X=180^{\circ}-90^{\circ}-55^{\circ}=35^{\circ}$.
Hence, triangles $P Q R$ and $Z Y X$ are similar since their angles are the same. The triangles may he redrawn as shown in Figure 20.41.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-190.jpg?height=321&width=740&top_left_y=2082&top_left_x=261)

Figure 20.41

By proportion: $\frac{p}{z}=\frac{r}{x}=\frac{q}{y}$
i.e. $\quad \frac{p}{12.97}=\frac{r}{7.44}=\frac{6.82}{10.63}$
from which, $\quad r=7.44\left(\frac{6.82}{10.63}\right)=4.77 \mathrm{~cm}$
By proportion: $\frac{p}{z}=\frac{q}{y} \quad$ i.e. $\quad \frac{p}{12.97}=\frac{6.82}{10.63}$
Hence, $\quad \boldsymbol{p}=12.97\left(\frac{6.82}{10.63}\right)=\mathbf{8 . 3 2} \mathbf{~ c m}$

Problem 28. In Figure 20.42, show that triangles $C B D$ and $C A E$ are similar and hence find the length of $C D$ and $B D$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-190.jpg?height=405&width=402&top_left_y=1110&top_left_x=1266)

Figure 20.42

Since $B D$ is parallel to $A E$ then $\angle C B D=\angle C A E$ and $\angle C D B=\angle C E A$ (corresponding angles between parallel lines). Also, $\angle C$ is common to triangles $C B D$ and CAE.
Since the angles in triangle $C B D$ are the same as in triangle $C A E$, the triangles are similar. Hence,
by proportion: $\frac{C B}{C A}=\frac{C D}{C E}\left(=\frac{B D}{A E}\right)$
i.e. $\quad \frac{9}{6+9}=\frac{C D}{12}$, from which
$$C D=12\left(\frac{9}{15}\right)=7.2 \mathrm{~cm}$$

Also,
$$\begin{aligned}
& \frac{9}{15}=\frac{B D}{10}, \text { from which } \\
& \qquad \boldsymbol{B D}=10\left(\frac{9}{15}\right)=\mathbf{6 c m}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 29. A rectangular shed 2 m wide and 3 m high stands against a perpendicular building of height 5.5 m . A ladder is used to gain access to the roof of the building. Determine the minimum distance between the bottom of the ladder and the shed

A side view is shown in Figure 20.43, where $A F$ is the minimum length of the ladder. Since $B D$ and $C F$ are parallel, $\angle A D B=\angle D F E$ (corresponding angles between parallel lines). Hence, triangles $B A D$ and $E D F$ are similar since their angles are the same.
$$A B=A C-B C=A C-D E=5.5-3=2.5 \mathrm{~m}$$

By proportion: $\quad \frac{A B}{D E}=\frac{B D}{E F} \quad$ i.e. $\quad \frac{2.5}{3}=\frac{2}{E F}$
Hence, $E F=2\left(\frac{3}{2.5}\right)=\mathbf{2 . 4} \mathbf{m}=$ minimum distance

\section*{from bottom of ladder to the shed.}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-191.jpg?height=565&width=527&top_left_y=1377&top_left_x=393)

\subsection*{20.6 Construction of triangles}

To construct any triangle, the following drawing instruments are needed:
(a) ruler and/or straight edge
(b) compass
(c) protractor
(d) pencil.

Here are some worked problems to demonstrate triangle construction.

Problem 30. Construct a triangle whose sides are $6 \mathrm{~cm}, 5 \mathrm{~cm}$ and 3 cm
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-192.jpg?height=310&width=481&top_left_y=1095&top_left_x=389)

Figure 20.47

With reference to Figure 20.47:
(i) Draw a straight line of any length and, with a pair of compasses, mark out 6 cm length and label it $A B$.
(ii) Set compass to 5 cm and with centre at $A$ describe $\operatorname{arc} D E$.
(iii) Set compass to 3 cm and with centre at $B$ describe $\operatorname{arc} F G$.
(iv) The intersection of the two curves at $C$ is the vertex of the required triangle. Join $A C$ and $B C$ by straight lines.

It may be proved by measurement that the ratio of the angles of a triangle is not equal to the ratio of the sides (i.e., in this problem, the angle opposite the 3 cm side is not equal to half the angle opposite the 6 cm side).

Problem 31. Construct a triangle $A B C$ such that $a=6 \mathrm{~cm}, b=3 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $\angle C=60^{\circ}$

With reference to Figure 20.48:
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-192.jpg?height=294&width=489&top_left_y=406&top_left_x=1220)

Figure 20.48
(i) Draw a line $B C, 6 \mathrm{~cm}$ long.
(ii) Using a protractor centred at $C$, make an angle of $60^{\circ}$ to $B C$.
(iii) From $C$ measure a length of 3 cm and label $A$.
(iv) Join $B$ to $A$ by a straight line.

Problem 32. Construct a triangle $P Q R$ given that $Q R=5 \mathrm{~cm}, \angle Q=70^{\circ}$ and $\angle R=44^{\circ}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-192.jpg?height=422&width=465&top_left_y=1324&top_left_x=1237)

(i) Draw a straight line 5 cm long and label it $Q R$.
(ii) Use a protractor centred at $Q$ and make an angle of $70^{\circ}$. Draw $Q Q^{\prime}$.
(iii) Use a protractor centred at $R$ and make an angle of $44^{\circ}$. Draw $R R^{\prime}$.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-193.jpg?height=318&width=484&top_left_y=302&top_left_x=414)

(i) Draw a straight line 5 cm long and label it $X Y$.

With compass centred at $A$ draw the arc $P Q$. With the same compass setting and centred at $A^{\prime}$, draw the arc $R S$. Join the intersection of the arcs, $C$ to $X$, and a right angle to $X Y$ is produced at $X$. (Alternatively, a protractor can be used to construct a $90^{\circ}$ angle.)

\section*{Chapter 21}

\section*{Introduction to trigonometry}

\subsection*{21.1 Introduction}

Trigonometry is a subject that involves the measurement of sides and angles of triangles and their relationship to each other.

The theorem of Pythagoras and trigonometric ratios are used with right-angled triangles only. However, there are many practical examples in engineering where knowledge of right-angled triangles is very important.

In this chapter, three trigonometric ratios - i.e. sine, cosine and tangent - are defined and then evaluated using a calculator. Finally, solving right-angled triangle problems using Pythagoras and trigonometric ratios is demonstrated, together with some practical examples involving angles of elevation and depression.

\subsection*{21.2 The theorem of Pythagoras}

The theorem of Pythagoras states:
In any right-angled triangle, the square of the hypotenuse is equal to the sum of the squares of the other two sides.
In the right-angled triangle $A B C$ shown in Figure 21.1, this means
$$b^{2}=a^{2}+c^{2}$$

Figure 21.1

If the lengths of any two sides of a right-angled triangle are known, the length of the third side may be calculated by Pythagoras' theorem.

From equation (1): $\quad b=\sqrt{a^{2}+c^{2}}$
Transposing equation (1) for $a$ gives $a^{2}=b^{2}-c^{2}$, from which $a=\sqrt{b^{2}-c^{2}}$
Transposing equation (1) for $c$ gives $c^{2}=b^{2}-a^{2}$, from which $c=\sqrt{b^{2}-a^{2}}$
Here are some worked problems to demonstrate the theorem of Pythagoras.

Problem 1. In Figure 21.2, find the length of $B C$

Figure 21.2
From Pythagoras, $a^{2}=b^{2}+c^{2}$
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
a^{2} & =4^{2}+3^{2} \\
& =16+9=25
\end{aligned}$$

Hence,
$$a=\sqrt{25}=5 \mathrm{~cm} .$$
$\sqrt{25}= \pm 5$ but in a practical example like this an answer of $a=-5 \mathrm{~cm}$ has no meaning, so we take only the positive answer.

Thus
$$a=B C=5 \mathrm{~cm} .$$
$P Q R$ is a $3,4,5$ triangle. There are not many rightangled triangles which have integer values (i.e. whole numbers) for all three sides.

Problem 2. In Figure 21.3, find the length of $E F$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-195.jpg?height=172&width=367&top_left_y=554&top_left_x=470)

Figure 21.3

By Pythagoras' theorem, $\quad e^{2}=d^{2}+f^{2}$
Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
13^{2} & =d^{2}+5^{2} \\
169 & =d^{2}+25 \\
d^{2} & =169-25=144
\end{aligned}$$

Thus,
$$d=\sqrt{144}=12 \mathrm{~cm}$$
i.e.
$$d=E F=12 \mathrm{~cm}$$

DEF is a 5, 12, 13 triangle, another right-angled triangle which has integer values for all three sides.

Problem 3. Two aircraft leave an airfield at the same time. One travels due north at an average speed of $300 \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{h}$ and the other due west at an average speed of $220 \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{h}$. Calculate their distance apart after 4 hours

After 4 hours, the first aircraft has travelled
$$4 \times 300=1200 \mathrm{~km} \text { due north }$$
and the second aircraft has travelled
$$4 \times 220=880 \mathrm{~km} \text { due west },$$
as shown in Figure 21.4. The distance apart after 4 hours $=B C$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-195.jpg?height=313&width=402&top_left_y=2089&top_left_x=455)

Figure 21.4

From Pythagoras' theorem,
and
$$\begin{aligned}
B C^{2} & =1200^{2}+880^{2} \\
& =1440000+774400=2214400 \\
B C & =\sqrt{2214400}=1488 \mathrm{~km} .
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, distance apart after 4 hours $=1488 \mathrm{~km}$.

\subsection*{21.3 Sines, cosines and tangents}

With reference to angle $\theta$ in the right-angled triangle $A B C$ shown in Figure 21.9,
$$\operatorname{sine} \theta=\frac{\text { opposite side }}{\text { hypotenuse }}$$
'Sine' is abbreviated to 'sin', thus $\sin \theta=\frac{B C}{A C}$

Figure 21.9
$$\text { Also, } \operatorname{cosine} \theta=\frac{\text { adjacent side }}{\text { hypotenuse }}$$
'Cosine' is abbreviated to 'cos', thus $\cos \boldsymbol{\theta}=\frac{\boldsymbol{A B}}{\boldsymbol{A C}}$ Finally, tangent $\theta=\frac{\text { opposite side }}{\text { adjacent side }}$
'Tangent' is abbreviated to 'tan', thus $\tan \theta=\frac{\boldsymbol{B C}}{\boldsymbol{A B}}$
These three trigonometric ratios only apply to rightangled triangles. Remembering these three equations is very important and the mnemonic 'SOH CAH TOA' is one way of remembering them.
$\mathbf{S O H}$ indicates $\underline{\sin }=\underline{\mathbf{o p p}}$ posite $\div \underline{\text { hypotenuse }}$
$\mathbf{C A H}$ indicates $\underline{\mathbf{c o s}}=\underline{\mathbf{a} d j a c e n t} \div \underline{\text { hypotenuse }}$
TOA indicates $\underline{\mathbf{t}} \mathbf{x}=\underline{\mathbf{o}}$ pposite $\div \underline{\text { addjacent }}$
Here are some worked problems to help familiarize ourselves with trigonometric ratios.

Problem 4. In triangle $P Q R$ shown in
Figure 21.10, determine $\sin \theta, \cos \theta$ and $\tan \theta$

Figure 21.10
$$\begin{aligned}
& \sin \theta=\frac{\text { opposite side }}{\text { hypotenuse }}=\frac{P Q}{P R}=\frac{5}{13}=\mathbf{0 . 3 8 4 6} \\
& \cos \boldsymbol{\theta}=\frac{\text { adjacent side }}{\text { hypotenuse }}=\frac{Q R}{P R}=\frac{12}{13}=\mathbf{0 . 9 2 3 1} \\
& \boldsymbol{\operatorname { t a n } \theta} \boldsymbol{\theta}=\frac{\text { opposite side }}{\text { adjacent side }}=\frac{P Q}{Q R}=\frac{5}{12}=\mathbf{0 . 4 1 6 7}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 5. In triangle $A B C$ of Figure 21.11, determine length $A C, \sin C, \cos C, \tan C, \sin A$, $\cos A$ and $\tan A$

Figure 21.11

By Pythagoras, $\quad A C^{2}=A B^{2}+B C^{2}$
i.e.
$$A C^{2}=3.47^{2}+4.62^{2}$$
from which
$$A C=\sqrt{3.47^{2}+4.62^{2}}=\mathbf{5 . 7 7 8} \mathrm{cm}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \sin C=\frac{\text { opposite side }}{\text { hypotenuse }}=\frac{A B}{A C}=\frac{3.47}{5.778}=\mathbf{0 . 6 0 0 6} \\
& \cos C=\frac{\text { adjacent side }}{\text { hypotenuse }}=\frac{B C}{A C}=\frac{4.62}{5.778}=\mathbf{0 . 7 9 9 6} \\
& \tan C=\frac{\text { opposite side }}{\text { adjacent side }}=\frac{A B}{B C}=\frac{3.47}{4.62}=\mathbf{0 . 7 5 1 1} \\
& \sin A=\frac{\text { opposite side }}{\text { hypotenuse }}=\frac{B C}{A C}=\frac{4.62}{5.778}=\mathbf{0 . 7 9 9 6} \\
& \cos A=\frac{\text { adjacent side }}{\text { hypotenuse }}=\frac{A B}{A C}=\frac{3.47}{5.778}=\mathbf{0 . 6 0 0 6} \\
& \tan A=\frac{\text { opposite side }}{\text { adjacent side }}=\frac{B C}{A B}=\frac{4.62}{3.47}=\mathbf{1 . 3 3 1 4}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 6. If $\tan B=\frac{8}{15}$, determine the value of $\sin B, \cos B, \sin A$ and $\tan A$

A right-angled triangle $A B C$ is shown in Figure 21.12. If $\tan B=\frac{8}{15}$, then $A C=8$ and $B C=15$.

Figure 21.12

By Pythagoras, $A B^{2}=A C^{2}+B C^{2}$
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
A B^{2} & =8^{2}+15^{2} \\
A B & =\sqrt{8^{2}+15^{2}}=17 \\
\sin \boldsymbol{B} & =\frac{A C}{A B}=\frac{\mathbf{8}}{\mathbf{1 7}} \text { or } \mathbf{0 . 4 7 0 6} \\
\cos \boldsymbol{B} & =\frac{B C}{A B}=\frac{\mathbf{1 5}}{\mathbf{1 7}} \text { or } \mathbf{0 . 8 8 2 4} \\
\sin \boldsymbol{A} & =\frac{B C}{A B}=\frac{\mathbf{1 5}}{\mathbf{1 7}} \text { or } \mathbf{0 . 8 8 2 4} \\
\tan \boldsymbol{A} & =\frac{B C}{A C}=\frac{\mathbf{1 5}}{\mathbf{8}} \text { or } \mathbf{1 . 8 7 5 0}
\end{aligned}$$
from which

Problem 7. Point $A$ lies at co-ordinate $(2,3)$ and point $B$ at $(8,7)$. Determine (a) the distance $A B$ and (b) the gradient of the straight line $A B$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-198.jpg?height=462&width=790&top_left_y=303&top_left_x=237)

Figure 21.13
(a) Points $A$ and $B$ are shown in Figure 21.13(a).

In Figure 21.13(b), the horizontal and vertical lines $A C$ and $B C$ are constructed. Since $A B C$ is a right-angled triangle, and $A C=(8-2)=6$ and $B C=(7-3)=4$, by Pythagoras' theorem,
$$\begin{aligned}
& A B^{2}=A C^{2}+B C^{2}=6^{2}+4^{2} \\
& \boldsymbol{A B}=\sqrt{6^{2}+4^{2}}=\sqrt{52} \\
&=\mathbf{7 . 2 1 1} \text { correct to } 3 \\
& \text { decimal places. }
\end{aligned}$$
(b) The gradient of AB is given by $\tan \theta$, i.e.
$$\text { gradient }=\tan \theta=\frac{B C}{A C}=\frac{4}{6}=\frac{\mathbf{2}}{\mathbf{3}}$$

\subsection*{21.4 Evaluating trigonometric ratios of acute angles}

The easiest way to evaluate trigonometric ratios of any angle is to use a calculator. Use a calculator to check the following (each correct to 4 decimal places).
$$\begin{array}{ll}
\sin 29^{\circ}=\mathbf{0 . 4 8 4 8} & \sin 53.62^{\circ}=\mathbf{0 . 8 0 5 1} \\
\cos 67^{\circ}=\mathbf{0 . 3 9 0 7} & \cos 83.57^{\circ}=\mathbf{0 . 1 1 2 0} \\
\tan 34^{\circ}=\mathbf{0 . 6 7 4 5} & \tan 67.83^{\circ}=\mathbf{2 . 4 5 4 1}
\end{array}$$
$\sin 67^{\circ} 43^{\prime}=\sin 67{\frac{43^{\circ}}{60}}_{60}=\sin 67.7166666 \ldots{ }^{\circ}=\mathbf{0 . 9 2 5 3}$
$\cos 13^{\circ} 28^{\prime}=\cos 13 \frac{28^{\circ}}{60}=\cos 13.466666 \ldots \circ=\mathbf{0 . 9 7 2 5}$
$\tan 56^{\circ} 54^{\prime}=\tan 56 \frac{54^{\circ}}{60}=\tan 56.90^{\circ}=\mathbf{1 . 5 3 4 0}$
If we know the value of a trigonometric ratio and need to find the angle we use the inverse function on our calculators. For example, using shift and $\sin$ on our calculator gives $\sin ^{-1}$ (
If, for example, we know the sine of an angle is 0.5 then the value of the angle is given by
$$\sin ^{-1} 0.5=\mathbf{3 0 ^ { \circ }}\left(\text { Check that } \sin 30^{\circ}=0.5\right)$$

Similarly, if
$$\cos \theta=0.4371 \text { then } \theta=\cos ^{-1} 0.4371=\mathbf{6 4 . 0 8}^{\circ}$$
and if
$$\tan A=3.5984 \text { then } A=\tan ^{-1} 3.5984=74.47^{\circ}$$
each correct to 2 decimal places.
Use your calculator to check the following worked problems.

Problem 8. Determine, correct to 4 decimal places, $\sin 43^{\circ} 39^{\prime}$
$$\sin 43^{\circ} 39^{\prime}=\sin 43 \frac{39^{\circ}}{60}=\sin 43.65^{\circ}=\mathbf{0 . 6 9 0 3}$$

This answer can be obtained using the calculator as follows:
1. Press sin
2. Enter 43
3. Press ${ }^{\circ} "$
4. Enter 39
5. Press ${ }^{\circ}$ "
6. Press )
7. Press $=$
Answer $=\mathbf{0 . 6 9 0 2 5 1 2} \ldots$

Problem 9. Determine, correct to 3 decimal places, $6 \cos 62^{\circ} 12^{\prime}$
$6 \cos 62^{\circ} 12^{\prime}=6 \cos 62 \frac{12}{60}^{\circ}=6 \cos 62.20^{\circ}=\mathbf{2 . 7 9 8}$
This answer can be obtained using the calculator as follows:
1. Enter 6
2. Press cos
3. Enter 62
4. Press ${ }^{\circ} "$
5. Enter 12
6. Press ${ }^{\circ} "$
7. Press )
8. Press $=$ Answer $=\mathbf{2 . 7 9 8 3 1 9} \ldots$

Problem 10. Evaluate sin 1.481, correct to 4 significant figures
$\sin 1.481$ means the sine of 1.481 radians. (If there is no degrees sign, i.e. ${ }^{\circ}$, then radians are assumed). Therefore a calculator needs to be on the radian function.
Hence, $\sin 1.481=\mathbf{0 . 9 9 6 0}$

Problem 11. Evaluate $\cos (3 \pi / 5)$, correct to 4 significant figures

As in Problem 10, $3 \pi / 5$ is in radians.
Hence, $\boldsymbol{\operatorname { c o s }}(\mathbf{3} \boldsymbol{\pi} / \mathbf{5})=\cos 1.884955 \ldots=\mathbf{- 0 . 3 0 9 0}$
Since, from page $166, \pi$ radians $=180^{\circ}$, $3 \pi / 5 \mathrm{rad}=\frac{3}{5} \times 180^{\circ}=108^{\circ}$.
i.e. $3 \pi / 5 \mathrm{rad}=108^{\circ}$. Check with your calculator that $\cos 108^{\circ}=\mathbf{- 0 . 3 0 9 0}$

Problem 12. Evaluate tan 2.93, correct to 4 significant figures

Again, since there is no degrees sign, 2.93 means 2.93 radians.
Hence, $\boldsymbol{\operatorname { t a n }} \mathbf{2 . 9 3}=\mathbf{- 0 . 2 1 4 8}$
It is important to know when to have your calculator on either degrees mode or radian mode. A lot of mistakes can arise from this if we are not careful.

Problem 17. Evaluate the following expression, correct to 3 significant figures
$$\frac{4.2 \tan 49^{\circ} 26^{\prime}-3.7 \sin 66^{\circ} 1^{\prime}}{7.1 \cos 29^{\circ} 34^{\prime}}$$

By calculator:
$$\begin{aligned}
\tan 49^{\circ} 26^{\prime} & =\tan \left(49 \frac{26}{60}\right)^{\circ}=1.1681 \\
\sin 66^{\circ} 1^{\prime} & =0.9137 \text { and } \cos 29^{\circ} 34^{\prime}=0.8698
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $\frac{4.2 \tan 49^{\circ} 26^{\prime}-3.7 \sin 66^{\circ} 1^{\prime}}{7.1 \cos 29^{\circ} 34^{\prime}}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{(4.2 \times 1.1681)-(3.7 \times 0.9137)}{(7.1 \times 0.8698)} \\
& =\frac{4.9060-3.3807}{6.1756}=\frac{1.5253}{6.1756} \\
& =0.2470=\mathbf{0 . 2 4 7}
\end{aligned}$$
correct to 3 significant figures.

\subsection*{21.5 Solving right-angled triangles}
'Solving a right-angled triangle' means 'finding the unknown sides and angles'. This is achieved using
(a) the theorem of Pythagoras and/or
(b) trigonometric ratios.

Six pieces of information describe a triangle completely; i.e., three sides and three angles. As long as at least three facts are known, the other three can usually be calculated.
Here are some worked problems to demonstrate the solution of right-angled triangles.

Problem 18. In the triangle $A B C$ shown in Figure 21.19, find the lengths $A C$ and $A B$

Figure 21.19

There is usually more than one way to solve such a triangle.

In triangle $A B C$,
$$\tan 42^{\circ}=\frac{A C}{B C}=\frac{A C}{6.2}$$
(Remember SOH CAH TOA)
Transposing gives
$$\boldsymbol{A C}=6.2 \tan 42^{\circ}=\mathbf{5 . 5 8 3} \mathbf{~ m m}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
\cos 42^{\circ}=\frac{B C}{A B} & =\frac{6.2}{A B}, \text { from which } \\
A B & =\frac{6.2}{\cos 42^{\circ}}=\mathbf{8 . 3 4 3} \mathbf{~ m m}
\end{aligned}$$

Alternatively, by Pythagoras, $A B^{2}=A C^{2}+B C^{2}$ from which $\boldsymbol{A B}=\sqrt{A C^{2}+B C^{2}}=\sqrt{5.583^{2}+6.2^{2}}$
$$=\sqrt{69.609889}=8.343 \mathrm{~mm}$$

Problem 19. Sketch a right-angled triangle $A B C$ such that $B=90^{\circ}, A B=5 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $B C=12 \mathrm{~cm}$. Determine the length of $A C$ and hence evaluate $\sin A, \cos C$ and $\tan A$

Triangle $A B C$ is shown in Figure 21.20.

Figure 21.20
By Pythagoras' theorem, $\quad A C=\sqrt{5^{2}+12^{2}}=13$
By definition: $\sin A=\frac{\text { opposite side }}{\text { hypotenuse }}=\frac{12}{13}$ or $\mathbf{0 . 9 2 3 1}$
and
$$\begin{array}{r}
\text { (Remember SOH CAH TOA) } \\
\cos C=\frac{\text { adjacent side }}{\text { hypotenuse }}=\frac{12}{13} \text { or } \mathbf{0 . 9 2 3 1} \\
\tan A=\frac{\text { opposite side }}{\text { adjacent side }}=\frac{12}{5} \text { or } \mathbf{2 . 4 0 0}
\end{array}$$

Problem 20. In triangle $P Q R$ shown in Figure 21.21, find the lengths of $P Q$ and $P R$

Figure 21.21
$\tan 38^{\circ}=\frac{P Q}{Q R}=\frac{P Q}{7.5}$, hence
$$P Q=7.5 \tan 38^{\circ}=7.5(0.7813)=\mathbf{5 . 8 6 0} \mathbf{~ c m}$$
$\cos 38^{\circ}=\frac{Q R}{P R}=\frac{7.5}{P R}$, hence
$$P R=\frac{7.5}{\cos 38^{\circ}}=\frac{7.5}{0.7880}=\mathbf{9 . 5 1 8} \mathbf{c m}$$

Check: using Pythagoras' theorem, $(7.5)^{2}+(5.860)^{2}=90.59=(9.518)^{2}$.

Problem 21. Solve the triangle $A B C$ shown in Figure 21.22
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-202.jpg?height=164&width=348&top_left_y=753&top_left_x=458)

Figure 21.22
To 'solve the triangle $A B C$ ' means 'to find the length $A C$ and angles $B$ and $C^{\prime}$.
$$\begin{aligned}
\sin C=\frac{35}{37} & =0.94595, \text { hence } \\
\boldsymbol{C} & =\sin ^{-1} 0.94595=\mathbf{7 1 . 0 8}^{\circ}
\end{aligned}$$
$\boldsymbol{B}=180^{\circ}-90^{\circ}-71.08^{\circ}=\mathbf{1 8 . 9 2}^{\circ}$ (since the angles in a triangle add up to $180^{\circ}$ )
$$\begin{aligned}
\sin B & =\frac{A C}{37}, \text { hence } \\
\boldsymbol{A C} & =37 \sin 18.92^{\circ}=37(0.3242)=\mathbf{1 2 . 0} \mathbf{~ m m}
\end{aligned}$$
or, using Pythagoras' theorem, $37^{2}=35^{2}+A C^{2}$, from which $A C=\sqrt{\left(37^{2}-35^{2}\right)}=\mathbf{1 2 . 0} \mathbf{m m}$.

Problem 22. Solve triangle $X Y Z$ given $\angle X=90^{\circ}, \angle Y=23^{\circ} 17^{\prime}$ and $Y Z=20.0 \mathrm{~mm}$

It is always advisable to make a reasonably accurate sketch so as to visualize the expected magnitudes of unknown sides and angles. Such a sketch is shown in Figure 21.23.
$$\angle Z=180^{\circ}-90^{\circ}-23^{\circ} 17^{\prime}=\mathbf{6 6}^{\circ} \mathbf{4 3}{ }^{\prime}$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-202.jpg?height=194&width=351&top_left_y=2208&top_left_x=454)

Figure 21.23
$$\begin{array}{r}
\sin 23^{\circ} 17^{\prime}=\frac{X Z}{20.0}, \text { hence } X Z=20.0 \sin 23^{\circ} 17^{\prime} \\
=20.0(0.3953)=\mathbf{7 . 9 0 6} \mathbf{m m} \\
\cos 23^{\circ} 17^{\prime}=\frac{X Y}{20.0}, \text { hence } X Y=20.0 \cos 23^{\circ} 17^{\prime} \\
=20.0(0.9186)=\mathbf{1 8 . 3 7} \mathbf{~ m m}
\end{array}$$

Check: using Pythagoras' theorem,
$(18.37)^{2}+(7.906)^{2}=400.0=(20.0)^{2}$,

\subsection*{21.6 Angles of elevation and depression}

If, in Figure 21.27, $B C$ represents horizontal ground and $A B$ a vertical flagpole, the angle of elevation of the top of the flagpole, $A$, from the point $C$ is the angle that the imaginary straight line $A C$ must be raised (or elevated) from the horizontal $C B$; i.e., angle $\theta$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-204.jpg?height=204&width=397&top_left_y=1449&top_left_x=433)

Figure 21.27
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-204.jpg?height=180&width=378&top_left_y=1746&top_left_x=440)

Figure 21.28
If, in Figure 21.28, $P Q$ represents a vertical cliff and $R$ a ship at sea, the angle of depression of the ship from point $P$ is the angle through which the imaginary straight line $P R$ must be lowered (or depressed) from the horizontal to the ship; i.e., angle $\phi$. (Note, $\angle P R Q$ is also $\phi$ - alternate angles between parallel lines.)

Problem 23. An electricity pylon stands on horizontal ground. At a point 80 m from the base of the pylon, the angle of elevation of the top of the
pylon is $23^{\circ}$. Calculate the height of the pylon to the nearest metre

Figure 21.29 shows the pylon $A B$ and the angle of elevation of $A$ from point $C$ is $23^{\circ}$.

Figure 21.29
$$\tan 23^{\circ}=\frac{A B}{B C}=\frac{A B}{80}$$

Hence, height of pylon $A B=80 \tan 23^{\circ}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =80(0.4245)=33.96 \mathrm{~m} \\
& =\mathbf{3 4} \mathbf{m} \text { to the nearest metre. }
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 24. A surveyor measures the angle of elevation of the top of a perpendicular building as $19^{\circ}$. He moves 120 m nearer to the building and finds the angle of elevation is now $47^{\circ}$. Determine the height of the building

The building $P Q$ and the angles of elevation are shown in Figure 21.30.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-204.jpg?height=275&width=563&top_left_y=1408&top_left_x=1183)

Figure 21.30
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { In triangle } P Q S, \tan 19^{\circ} & =\frac{h}{x+120} \\
& \text { Hence, } \quad \begin{aligned}
h & =\tan 19^{\circ}(x+120) \\
\text { i.e. } h & =0.3443(x+120)
\end{aligned}
\end{aligned}$$

In triangle $P Q R, \tan 47^{\circ}=\frac{h}{x}$
Hence, $\quad h=\tan 47^{\circ}(x)$ i.e. $h=1.0724 x$
Equating equations (1) and (2) gives
$$\begin{aligned}
0.3443(x+120) & =1.0724 x \\
0.3443 x+(0.3443)(120) & =1.0724 x \\
(0.3443)(120) & =(1.0724-0.3443) x \\
41.316 & =0.7281 x \\
x & =\frac{41.316}{0.7281}=56.74 \mathrm{~m}
\end{aligned}$$

From equation (2), height of building, $\boldsymbol{h}=1.0724 x=1.0724(56.74)=\mathbf{6 0 . 8 5} \mathbf{m}$.

Problem 25. The angle of depression of a ship viewed at a particular instant from the top of a 75 m vertical cliff is $30^{\circ}$. Find the distance of the ship from the base of the cliff at this instant. The ship is sailing away from the cliff at constant speed and 1 minute later its angle of depression from the top of the cliff is $20^{\circ}$. Determine the speed of the ship in km/h

Figure 21.31 shows the cliff $A B$, the initial position of the ship at $C$ and the final position at $D$. Since the angle of depression is initially $30^{\circ}, \angle A C B=30^{\circ}$ (alternate angles between parallel lines).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-205.jpg?height=283&width=587&top_left_y=1057&top_left_x=360)

Figure 21.31
$\tan 30^{\circ}=\frac{A B}{B C}=\frac{75}{B C}$ hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
B C & =\frac{75}{\tan 30^{\circ}} \\
& =\mathbf{1 2 9 . 9 \mathbf { m } = \text { initial position }} \quad \mathbf{o f ~ s h i p ~ f r o m ~ b a s e ~ o f ~ c l i f f ~}
\end{aligned}$$

In triangle $A B D$,
$$\tan 20^{\circ}=\frac{A B}{B D}=\frac{75}{B C+C D}=\frac{75}{129.9+x}$$

Hence,
$$129.9+x=\frac{75}{\tan 20^{\circ}}=206.06 \mathrm{~m}$$
from which
$$x=206.06-129.9=76.16 \mathrm{~m}$$

Thus, the ship sails 76.16 m in 1 minute; i.e., 60 s ,
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Hence, speed of ship }=\frac{\text { distance }}{\text { time }}=\frac{76.16}{60} \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s} \\
& =\frac{76.16 \times 60 \times 60}{60 \times 1000} \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{h}=4.57 \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{h}
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{Revision Test 8 : Angles, triangles and trigonometry}

This assignment covers the material contained in Chapters 20 and 21. The marks available are shown in brackets at the end of each question.
1. Determine $38^{\circ} 48^{\prime}+17^{\circ} 23^{\prime}$.
8. In Figure RT8.4, if triangle $A B C$ is equilateral, determine $\angle C D E$.
2. Determine $47^{\circ} 43^{\prime} 12^{\prime \prime}-58^{\circ} 35^{\prime} 53^{\prime \prime}+26^{\circ} 17^{\prime} 29^{\prime \prime}$.

\section*{Trigonometric waveforms}

\subsection*{22.1 Graphs of trigonometric functions}

By drawing up tables of values from $0^{\circ}$ to $360^{\circ}$, graphs of $y=\sin A, y=\cos A$ and $y=\tan A$ may be plotted. Values obtained with a calculator (correct to 3 decimal places - which is more than sufficient for plotting graphs), using $30^{\circ}$ intervals, are shown below, with the respective graphs shown in Figure 22.1.
(a) $y=\sin A$
\begin{tabular}{llllllll}

\end{tabular}

$A$ & $210^{\circ}$ & $240^{\circ}$ & $270^{\circ}$ & $300^{\circ}$ & $330^{\circ}$ & $360^{\circ}$ \\

\end{tabular}
