(c)

Figure 22.1
From Figure 22.1 it is seen that
(a) Sine and cosine graphs oscillate between peak values of $\pm 1$.
(b) The cosine curve is the same shape as the sine curve but displaced by $90^{\circ}$.
(c) The sine and cosine curves are continuous and they repeat at intervals of $360^{\circ}$ and the tangent curve appears to be discontinuous and repeats at intervals of $180^{\circ}$.

\subsection*{22.2 Angles of any magnitude}

Figure 22.2 shows rectangular axes $X X^{\prime}$ and $Y Y^{\prime}$ intersecting at origin 0 . As with graphical work, measurements made to the right and above 0 are positive, while those to the left and downwards are negative.

Let $0 A$ be free to rotate about 0 . By convention, when $0 A$ moves anticlockwise angular measurement is considered positive, and vice versa.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-209.jpg?height=500&width=590&top_left_y=789&top_left_x=356)

Figure 22.2

Let $0 A$ be rotated anticlockwise so that $\theta_{1}$ is any angle in the first quadrant and let perpendicular $A B$ be constructed to form the right-angled triangle $0 A B$ in Figure 22.3. Since all three sides of the triangle are positive, the trigonometric ratios sine, cosine and tangent will all be positive in the first quadrant. (Note: $O A$ is always positive since it is the radius of a circle.)

Let $0 A$ be further rotated so that $\theta_{2}$ is any angle in the second quadrant and let $A C$ be constructed to form the
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-209.jpg?height=495&width=641&top_left_y=1911&top_left_x=330)

Figure 22.3
right-angled triangle $0 A C$. Then,
$$\begin{aligned}
& \sin \theta_{2}=\frac{+}{+}=+\quad \cos \theta_{2}=\frac{-}{+}=- \\
& \tan \theta_{2}=\frac{+}{-}=-
\end{aligned}$$

Let $0 A$ be further rotated so that $\theta_{3}$ is any angle in the third quadrant and let $A D$ be constructed to form the right-angled triangle $0 A D$. Then,
$$\begin{aligned}
& \sin \theta_{3}=\frac{-}{+}=-\quad \cos \theta_{3}=\frac{-}{+}=- \\
& \tan \theta_{3}=\frac{-}{-}=+
\end{aligned}$$

Let $0 A$ be further rotated so that $\theta_{4}$ is any angle in the fourth quadrant and let $A E$ be constructed to form the right-angled triangle $0 A E$. Then,
$$\begin{aligned}
& \sin \theta_{4}=\frac{-}{+}=-\quad \cos \theta_{4}=\frac{+}{+}=+ \\
& \tan \theta_{4}=\frac{-}{+}=-
\end{aligned}$$

The above results are summarized in Figure 22.4, in which all three trigonometric ratios are positive in the first quadrant, only sine is positive in the second quadrant, only tangent is positive in the third quadrant and only cosine is positive in the fourth quadrant.
The underlined letters in Figure 22.4 spell the word CAST when starting in the fourth quadrant and moving in an anticlockwise direction.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-209.jpg?height=505&width=576&top_left_y=1627&top_left_x=1198)

Figure 22.4

It is seen that, in the first quadrant of Figure 22.1, all of the curves have positive values; in the second only sine is positive; in the third only tangent is positive; and in the fourth only cosine is positive - exactly as summarized in Figure 22.4.

A knowledge of angles of any magnitude is needed when finding, for example, all the angles between $0^{\circ}$ and $360^{\circ}$ whose sine is, say, 0.3261 . If 0.3261 is entered into a calculator and then the inverse sine key pressed (or $\sin ^{-1} \mathrm{key}$ ) the answer $19.03^{\circ}$ appears. However, there is a second angle between $0^{\circ}$ and $360^{\circ}$ which the calculator does not give. Sine is also positive in the second quadrant (either from CAST or from Figure 22.1(a)). The other angle is shown in Figure 22.5 as angle $\theta$, where $\theta=180^{\circ}-19.03^{\circ}=160.97^{\circ}$. Thus, $19.03^{\circ}$ and $160.97^{\circ}$ are the angles between $0^{\circ}$ and $360^{\circ}$ whose sine is 0.3261 (check that $\sin 160.97^{\circ}=0.3261$ on your calculator).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-210.jpg?height=443&width=517&top_left_y=855&top_left_x=368)

Figure 22.5
Be careful! Your calculator only gives you one of these answers. The second answer needs to be deduced from a knowledge of angles of any magnitude, as shown in the following worked problems.

Problem 1. Determine all of the angles between $0^{\circ}$ and $360^{\circ}$ whose sine is -0.4638

The angles whose sine is -0.4638 occur in the third and fourth quadrants since sine is negative in these quadrants - see Figure 22.6.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-210.jpg?height=354&width=601&top_left_y=1911&top_left_x=326)

Figure 22.6

From Figure 22.7, $\theta=\sin ^{-1} 0.4638=27.63^{\circ}$. Measured from $0^{\circ}$, the two angles between $0^{\circ}$ and $360^{\circ}$
whose sine is -0.4638 are $180^{\circ}+27.63^{\circ}$ i.e. $\mathbf{2 0 7 . 6 3}^{\circ}$ and $360^{\circ}-27.63^{\circ}$, i.e. $332.37^{\circ}$. (Note that if a calculator is used to determine $\sin ^{-1}(-0.4638)$ it only gives one answer: $-27.632588^{\circ}$.)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-210.jpg?height=326&width=399&top_left_y=515&top_left_x=1265)

Figure 22.7

Problem 2. Determine all of the angles between $0^{\circ}$ and $360^{\circ}$ whose tangent is 1.7629

A tangent is positive in the first and third quadrants see Figure 22.8.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-210.jpg?height=375&width=579&top_left_y=1198&top_left_x=1175)

Figure 22.8
From Figure 22.9, $\theta=\tan ^{-1} 1.7629=60.44^{\circ}$. Measured from $0^{\circ}$, the two angles between $0^{\circ}$ and $360^{\circ}$ whose tangent is 1.7629 are $\mathbf{6 0 . 4 4}{ }^{\circ}$ and $180^{\circ}+60.44^{\circ}$, i.e. $\mathbf{2 4 0 . 4 4}{ }^{\circ}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-210.jpg?height=329&width=416&top_left_y=1880&top_left_x=1256)

Figure 22.9

Problem 3. Solve the equation $\cos ^{-1}(-0.2348)=\alpha$ for angles of $\alpha$ between $0^{\circ}$ and $360^{\circ}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-211.jpg?height=481&width=546&top_left_y=307&top_left_x=378)

Figure 22.10

Cosine is positive in the first and fourth quadrants and thus negative in the second and third quadrants - see Figure 22.10 or from Figure 22.1(b).
In Figure 22.10, angle $\theta=\cos ^{-1}(0.2348)=76.42^{\circ}$. Measured from $0^{\circ}$, the two angles whose cosine is -0.2348 are $\alpha=180^{\circ}-76.42^{\circ}$, i.e. $103.58^{\circ}$ and $\alpha=180^{\circ}+76.42^{\circ}$, i.e. $\mathbf{2 5 6 . 4 2}{ }^{\circ}$

\subsection*{22.3 The production of sine and cosine waves}

In Figure 22.11, let $O R$ be a vector 1 unit long and free to rotate anticlockwise about 0 . In one revolution a circle is produced and is shown with $15^{\circ}$ sectors. Each radius arm has a vertical and a horizontal component. For example, at $30^{\circ}$, the vertical component is $T S$ and the horizontal component is $O S$.

From triangle $O S T$,
$$\sin 30^{\circ}=\frac{T S}{T O}=\frac{T S}{1} \quad \text { i.e. } \quad T S=\sin 30^{\circ}$$
and $\quad \cos 30^{\circ}=\frac{O S}{T O}=\frac{O S}{1}$ i.e. $O S=\cos 30^{\circ}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-211.jpg?height=522&width=1300&top_left_y=1887&top_left_x=424)

Figure 22.11
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-212.jpg?height=508&width=1305&top_left_y=310&top_left_x=394)

Figure 22.12

\subsection*{22.3.1 Sine waves}

The vertical component $T S$ may be projected across to $T^{\prime} S^{\prime}$, which is the corresponding value of $30^{\circ}$ on the graph of $y$ against angle $x^{\circ}$. If all such vertical components as $T S$ are projected on to the graph, a sine wave is produced as shown in Figure 22.11.

\subsection*{22.3.2 Cosine waves}

If all horizontal components such as $O S$ are projected on to a graph of $y$ against angle $x^{\circ}$, a cosine wave is produced. It is easier to visualize these projections by redrawing the circle with the radius arm $O R$ initially in a vertical position as shown in Figure 22.12.
It is seen from Figures 22.11 and 22.12 that a cosine curve is of the same form as the sine curve but is displaced by $90^{\circ}$ (or $\pi / 2$ radians). Both sine and cosine waves repeat every $360^{\circ}$.

\subsection*{22.4 Terminology involved with sine and cosine waves}

Sine waves are extremely important in engineering, with examples occurring with alternating currents and voltages - the mains supply is a sine wave - and with simple harmonic motion.

\subsection*{22.4.1 Cycle}

When a sine wave has passed through a complete series of values, both positive and negative, it is said to have completed one cycle. One cycle of a sine wave is shown in Figure 22.1(a) on page 195 and in Figure 22.11.

\subsection*{22.4.2 Amplitude}

The amplitude is the maximum value reached in a half cycle by a sine wave. Another name for amplitude is peak value or maximum value.

A sine wave $y=5 \sin x$ has an amplitude of 5 , a sine wave $v=200 \sin 314 t$ has an amplitude of 200 and the sine wave $y=\sin x$ shown in Figure 22.11 has an amplitude of 1 .

\subsection*{22.4.3 Period}

The waveforms $y=\sin x$ and $y=\cos x$ repeat themselves every $360^{\circ}$. Thus, for each, the period is $360^{\circ}$. A waveform of $y=\tan x$ has a period of $180^{\circ}$ (from Figure 22.1(c)).
A graph of $y=3 \sin 2 A$, as shown in Figure 22.13, has an amplitude of 3 and period $180^{\circ}$.
A graph of $y=\sin 3 A$, as shown in Figure 22.14, has an amplitude of $\mathbf{1}$ and period of $120^{\circ}$.
A graph of $y=4 \cos 2 x$, as shown in Figure 22.15, has an amplitude of 4 and a period of $180^{\circ}$.
In general, if $y=A \sin p x$ or $y=A \cos p x$, amplitude $=A$ and period $=\frac{360^{\circ}}{p}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-212.jpg?height=433&width=768&top_left_y=1980&top_left_x=1086)

Figure 22.13
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-213.jpg?height=419&width=714&top_left_y=311&top_left_x=305)

Figure 22.14
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-213.jpg?height=408&width=739&top_left_y=810&top_left_x=284)

Figure 22.15
Problem 4. Sketch $y=2 \sin \frac{3}{5} A$ over one cycle

Amplitude $=2 ;$ period $=\frac{360^{\circ}}{\frac{3}{5}}=\frac{360^{\circ} \times 5}{3}=600^{\circ}$
A sketch of $y=2 \sin \frac{3}{5} A$ is shown in Figure 22.16.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-213.jpg?height=413&width=758&top_left_y=1621&top_left_x=272)

Figure 22.16

\subsection*{22.4.4 Periodic time}

In practice, the horizontal axis of a sine wave will be time. The time taken for a sine wave to complete one cycle is called the periodic time, $\boldsymbol{T}$.
In the sine wave of voltage $v$ (volts) against time $t$ (milliseconds) shown in Figure 22.17, the amplitude is 10 V and the periodic time is 20 ms ; i.e., $T=20 \mathrm{~ms}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-213.jpg?height=421&width=769&top_left_y=299&top_left_x=1107)

Figure 22.17

\subsection*{22.4.5 Frequency}

The number of cycles completed in one second is called the frequency $f$ and is measured in hertz, Hz.
$$f=\frac{\mathbf{1}}{T} \text { or } T=\frac{\mathbf{1}}{f}$$

Problem 5. Determine the frequency of the sine wave shown in Figure 22.17

In the sine wave shown in Figure 22.17, $T=20 \mathrm{~ms}$, hence
frequency, $f=\frac{1}{T}=\frac{1}{20 \times 10^{-3}}=\mathbf{5 0 H z}$
Problem 6. If a waveform has a frequency of 200 kHz , determine the periodic time

If a waveform has a frequency of 200 kHz , the periodic time $T$ is given by
$$\text { periodic time, } \begin{aligned}
\boldsymbol{T}=\frac{1}{f} & =\frac{1}{200 \times 10^{3}} \\
& =5 \times 10^{-6} \mathrm{~s}=\mathbf{5} \mu \mathbf{s}
\end{aligned}$$

\subsection*{22.4.6 Lagging and leading angles}

A sine or cosine curve may not always start at $0^{\circ}$. To show this, a periodic function is represented by $y=A \sin (x \pm \alpha)$ where $\alpha$ is a phase displacement compared with $y=A \sin x$. For example, $y=\sin A$ is shown by the broken line in Figure 22.18 and, on the same axes, $y=\sin \left(A-60^{\circ}\right)$ is shown. The graph $y=\sin \left(A-60^{\circ}\right)$ is said to lag $y=\sin A$ by $60^{\circ}$.
In another example, $y=\cos A$ is shown by the broken line in Figure 22.19 and, on the same axes, $y=$ $\cos \left(A+45^{\circ}\right)$ is shown. The graph $\boldsymbol{y}=\boldsymbol{\operatorname { c o s }}\left(\boldsymbol{A}+\mathbf{4 5 ^ { \circ }}\right)$ is said to lead $y=\cos A$ by $45^{\circ}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-214.jpg?height=495&width=771&top_left_y=303&top_left_x=246)

Figure 22.18
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-214.jpg?height=508&width=768&top_left_y=907&top_left_x=245)

Figure 22.19

Problem 7. Sketch $y=5 \sin \left(A+30^{\circ}\right)$ from $A=0^{\circ}$ to $A=360^{\circ}$
$$\text { Amplitude }=\mathbf{5} \text { and period }=360^{\circ} / 1=\mathbf{3 6 0}{ }^{\circ}$$
$5 \sin \left(A+30^{\circ}\right)$ leads $5 \sin A$ by $30^{\circ}$ (i.e. starts $30^{\circ}$ earlier).
A sketch of $y=5 \sin \left(A+30^{\circ}\right)$ is shown in Figure 22.20.

Problem 8. Sketch $y=7 \sin (2 A-\pi / 3)$ in the range $0 \leq A \leq 360^{\circ}$
$$\text { Amplitude }=7 \text { and period }=2 \pi / 2=\pi \text { radians }$$

In general, $y=\sin (p t-\alpha)$ lags $y=\sin p t$ by $\alpha / p$, hence $7 \sin (2 A-\pi / 3)$ lags $7 \sin 2 A$ by $(\pi / 3) / 2$, i.e.
$$\pi / 6 \mathrm{rad} \text { or } 30^{\circ}$$

A sketch of $y=7 \sin (2 A-\pi / 3)$ is shown in Figure 22.21.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-214.jpg?height=454&width=768&top_left_y=296&top_left_x=1086)

Figure 22.20
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-214.jpg?height=429&width=690&top_left_y=889&top_left_x=1122)

Figure 22.21

Problem 9. Sketch $y=2 \cos (\omega t-3 \pi / 10)$ over one cycle

Amplitude $=2$ and period $=2 \pi / \omega \mathrm{rad}$
$2 \cos (\omega t-3 \pi / 10)$ lags $2 \cos \omega t$ by $3 \pi / 10 \omega$ seconds. A sketch of $y=2 \cos (\omega t-3 \pi / 10)$ is shown in Figure 22.22.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-214.jpg?height=457&width=698&top_left_y=1903&top_left_x=1121)

Figure 22.22

\subsection*{22.5 Sinusoidal form: $A \sin (\omega t \pm \alpha)$}

If a sine wave is expressed in the form $y=A \sin (\omega t \pm \alpha)$ then
(a) $\mathrm{A}=$ amplitude.
(b) $\quad \omega=$ angular velocity $=2 \pi f \mathrm{rad} / \mathrm{s}$.
(c) frequency, $f=\frac{\omega}{2 \pi}$ hertz.
(d) periodic time, $T=\frac{2 \pi}{\omega}$ seconds (i.e. $\left.T=\frac{1}{f}\right)$.
(e) $\alpha=$ angle of lead or lag (compared with $y=A \sin \omega t)$.

Here are some worked problems involving the sinusoidal form $A \sin (\omega t \pm \alpha)$.

Problem 10. An alternating current is given by $i=30 \sin (100 \pi t+0.35)$ amperes. Find the
(a) amplitude, (b) frequency, (c) periodic time and
(d) phase angle (in degrees and minutes)
(a) $i=30 \sin (100 \pi t+0.35) A$; hence, amplitude $=30 \mathrm{~A}$.
(b) Angular velocity, $\omega=100 \pi, \mathrm{rad} / \mathrm{s}$, hence
$$\text { frequency, } f=\frac{\omega}{2 \pi}=\frac{100 \pi}{2 \pi}=\mathbf{5 0 H z}$$
(c) Periodic time, $\boldsymbol{T}=\frac{1}{f}=\frac{1}{50}=\mathbf{0 . 0 2} \mathrm{s}$ or $\mathbf{2 0} \mathrm{ms}$.
(d) 0.35 is the angle in radians. The relationship between radians and degrees is
$$360^{\circ}=2 \pi \text { radians or } \mathbf{1 8 0}{ }^{\circ}=\pi \text { radians }$$
from which,
$$\mathbf{1}^{\circ}=\frac{\pi}{\mathbf{1 8 0}} \mathbf{r a d} \text { and } 1 \mathbf{r a d}=\frac{\mathbf{1 8 0}^{\circ}}{\pi}\left(\approx 57.30^{\circ}\right)$$

Hence, phase angle, $\boldsymbol{\alpha}=0.35 \mathrm{rad}$ $=\left(0.35 \times \frac{180}{\pi}\right)^{\circ}=\mathbf{2 0 . 0 5}{ }^{\circ}$ or $\mathbf{2 0}^{\circ} \mathbf{3}^{\prime}$ leading $i=30 \sin (100 \pi t)$.

Problem 11. An oscillating mechanism has a maximum displacement of 2.5 m and a frequency of 60 Hz . At time $t=0$ the displacement is 90 cm . Express the displacement in the general form $A \sin (\omega t \pm \alpha)$

Amplitude $=$ maximum displacement $=2.5 \mathrm{~m}$.
Angular velocity, $\omega=2 \pi f=2 \pi(60)=120 \pi \mathrm{rad} / \mathrm{s}$.
Hence, $\quad$ displacement $=2.5 \sin (120 \pi t+\alpha) \mathbf{m}$.
When $t=0$, displacement $=90 \mathrm{~cm}=0.90 \mathrm{~m}$.
hence,
$$0.90=2.5 \sin (0+\alpha)$$
i.e.

Hence,
$$\sin \alpha=\frac{0.90}{2.5}=0.36$$
$$\begin{aligned}
\alpha & =\sin ^{-1} 0.36=21.10^{\circ} \\
& =21^{\circ} 6^{\prime}=0.368 \mathrm{rad}
\end{aligned}$$

Thus, displacement $=2.5 \sin (120 \pi t+0.368) \mathrm{m}$.

Problem 12. The instantaneous value of voltage in an a.c. circuit at any time $t$ seconds is given by $v=340 \sin (50 \pi t-0.541)$ volts. Determine the (a) amplitude, frequency, periodic time and phase angle (in degrees), (b) value of the voltage when $t=0$, (c) value of the voltage when $t=10 \mathrm{~ms}$,
(d) time when the voltage first reaches 200 V and
(e) time when the voltage is a maximum. Also,
(f) sketch one cycle of the waveform
(a) Amplitude $=340 \mathrm{~V}$

Angular velocity, $\omega=50 \pi$
Frequency, $f=\frac{\omega}{2 \pi}=\frac{50 \pi}{2 \pi}=\mathbf{2 5} \mathbf{H z}$
Periodic time, $T=\frac{1}{f}=\frac{1}{25}=\mathbf{0 . 0 4} \mathrm{s}$ or 40 ms
Phase angle $=0.541 \mathrm{rad}=\left(0.541 \times \frac{180}{\pi}\right)^{\circ}$
$=31^{\circ}$ lagging $v=340 \sin (50 \pi t)$
(b) When $\boldsymbol{t}=\mathbf{0}$,
$$\begin{aligned}
\boldsymbol{v} & =340 \sin (0-0.541) \\
& =340 \sin \left(-31^{\circ}\right)=-\mathbf{1 7 5 . 1} \mathbf{V}
\end{aligned}$$
(c) When $t=10 \mathrm{~ms}$,
$$\begin{aligned}
v & =340 \sin \left(50 \pi \times 10 \times 10^{-3}-0.541\right) \\
& =340 \sin (1.0298) \\
& =340 \sin 59^{\circ}=\mathbf{2 9 1 . 4} \text { volts }
\end{aligned}$$
(d) When $v=200$ volts,
$$\begin{aligned}
& 200=340 \sin (50 \pi t-0.541) \\
& \frac{200}{340}=\sin (50 \pi t-0.541)
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $\quad(50 \pi t-0.541)=\sin ^{-1} \frac{200}{340}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =36.03^{\circ} \text { or } 0.628875 \mathrm{rad} \\
50 \pi t & =0.628875+0.541 \\
& =1.169875
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, when $v=200 \mathrm{~V}$,
time, $t=\frac{1.169875}{50 \pi}=7.448 \mathrm{~ms}$
(e) When the voltage is a maximum, $v=340 \mathrm{~V}$.

Hence $\quad 340=340 \sin (50 \pi t-0.541)$
$$\begin{aligned}
1 & =\sin (50 \pi t-0.541) \\
50 \pi t-0.541 & =\sin ^{-1} 1=90^{\circ} \text { or } 1.5708 \mathrm{rad} \\
50 \pi t & =1.5708+0.541=2.1118
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, time, $t=\frac{2.1118}{50 \pi}=\mathbf{1 3 . 4 4} \mathbf{m s}$
(f) A sketch of $v=340 \sin (50 \pi t-0.541)$ volts is shown in Figure 22.23.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-216.jpg?height=440&width=782&top_left_y=1326&top_left_x=1076)

Figure 22.23

In problems 1 to 3 find the (a) amplitude, (b) frequency, (c) periodic time and (d) phase angle (stating whether it is leading or lagging $\sin \omega t$ ) of the alternating quantities given.
1. $i=40 \sin (50 \pi t+0.29) \mathrm{mA}$
2. $y=75 \sin (40 t-0.54) \mathrm{cm}$
3. $v=300 \sin (200 \pi t-0.412) \mathrm{V}$
4. A sinusoidal voltage has a maximum value of 120 V and a frequency of 50 Hz . At time $t=0$, the voltage is (a) zero and (b) 50 V . Express the instantaneous voltage $v$ in the form $v=A \sin (\omega t \pm \alpha)$.
5. An alternating current has a periodic time of 25 ms and a maximum value of 20 A . When time $=0$, current $i=-10$ amperes. Express the current $i$ in the form $i=A \sin (\omega t \pm \alpha)$.

Determine the

(b) value of current at $t=0$,

\section*{Chapter 23}

\section*{Non-right-angled triangles and some practical applications}

\subsection*{23.1 The sine and cosine rules}

To 'solve a triangle' means 'to find the values of unknown sides and angles'. If a triangle is right-angled, trigonometric ratios and the theorem of Pythagoras may be used for its solution, as shown in Chapter 21. However, for a non-right-angled triangle, trigonometric ratios and Pythagoras' theorem cannot be used. Instead, two rules, called the sine rule and the cosine rule, are used.

\subsection*{23.1.1 The sine rule}

With reference to triangle $A B C$ of Figure 23.1, the sine rule states
$$\frac{a}{\sin A}=\frac{b}{\sin B}=\frac{c}{\sin C}$$

Figure 23.1

The rule may be used only when
(a) 1 side and any 2 angles are initially given, or
(b) 2 sides and an angle (not the included angle) are initially given.

\subsection*{23.1.2 The cosine rule}

With reference to triangle $A B C$ of Figure 23.1, the cosine rule states
or
$$\begin{aligned}
& a^{2}=b^{2}+c^{2}-2 b c \cos A \\
& b^{2}=a^{2}+c^{2}-2 a c \cos B \\
& c^{2}=a^{2}+b^{2}-2 a b \cos C
\end{aligned}$$

The rule may be used only when
(a) 2 sides and the included angle are initially given, or
(b) 3 sides are initially given.

\subsection*{23.2 Area of any triangle}

The area of any triangle such as $A B C$ of Figure 23.1 is given by
(a) $\frac{1}{2} \times$ base $\times$ perpendicular height
or (b) $\frac{1}{2} a b \sin C$ or $\frac{1}{2} a c \sin B$ or $\frac{1}{2} b c \sin A$
or (c) $\sqrt{[s(s-a)(s-\boldsymbol{b})(s-c)]}$ where $s=\frac{a+b+c}{2}$

\subsection*{23.3 Worked problems on the solution of triangles and their areas}

Problem 1. In a triangle $X Y Z, \angle X=51^{\circ}$, $\angle Y=67^{\circ}$ and $Y Z=15.2 \mathrm{~cm}$. Solve the triangle and find its area

The triangle $X Y Z$ is shown in Figure 23.2. Solving the triangle means finding $\angle Z$ and sides $X Z$ and $X Y$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-219.jpg?height=305&width=272&top_left_y=970&top_left_x=523)

Figure 23.2
Since the angles in a triangle add up to $180^{\circ}$, $Z=180^{\circ}-51^{\circ}-67^{\circ}=62^{\circ}$

Applying the sine rule,
$$\begin{aligned}
\frac{15.2}{\sin 51^{\circ}} & =\frac{y}{\sin 67^{\circ}} \\
& =\frac{z}{\sin 62^{\circ}} \\
\frac{15.2}{\sin 51^{\circ}} & =\frac{y}{\sin 67^{\circ}}
\end{aligned}$$

Using
and transposing gives
$$\begin{aligned}
\boldsymbol{y} & =\frac{15.2 \sin 67^{\circ}}{\sin 51^{\circ}} \\
& =\mathbf{1 8 . 0 0} \mathbf{c m}=X \mathbf{Z}
\end{aligned}$$

Using
$$\begin{aligned}
\frac{15.2}{\sin 51^{\circ}} & =\frac{z}{\sin 62^{\circ}} \\
z & =\frac{15.2 \sin 62^{\circ}}{\sin 51^{\circ}} \\
& =\mathbf{1 7 . 2 7} \mathbf{c m}=X Y
\end{aligned}$$
and transposing gives

Area of triangle $X Y Z=\frac{1}{2} x y \sin Z$
$$\begin{gathered}
\qquad \begin{aligned}
&=\frac{1}{2}(15.2)(18.00) \sin 62^{\circ}=\mathbf{1 2 0 . 8} \mathbf{c m}^{2} \\
&\left(\text { or area }=\frac{1}{2} x z \sin Y\right.=\frac{1}{2}(15.2)(17.27) \sin 67^{\circ} \\
&\left.=\mathbf{1 2 0 . 8} \mathbf{c m}^{2}\right)
\end{aligned}
\end{gathered}$$

It is always worth checking with triangle problems that the longest side is opposite the largest angle and viceversa. In this problem, $Y$ is the largest angle and $X Z$ is the longest of the three sides.

Problem 2. Solve the triangle $A B C$ given $B=78^{\circ} 51^{\prime}, A C=22.31 \mathrm{~mm}$ and $A B=17.92 \mathrm{~mm}$.
Also find its area

Triangle $A B C$ is shown in Figure 23.3. Solving the triangle means finding angles $A$ and $C$ and side $B C$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-219.jpg?height=324&width=359&top_left_y=806&top_left_x=1315)

Figure 23.3
Applying the sine rule,$\quad \frac{22.31}{\sin 78^{\circ} 51^{\prime}}=\frac{17.92}{\sin C}$
from which $\sin C=\frac{17.92 \sin 78^{\circ} 51^{\prime}}{22.31}=0.7881$
Hence, $\quad C=\sin ^{-1} 0.7881=52^{\circ} 0^{\prime}$ or $128^{\circ} 0^{\prime}$
Since $B=78^{\circ} 51^{\prime}$, $C$ cannot be $128^{\circ} 0^{\prime}$, since $128^{\circ} 0^{\prime}+$ $78^{\circ} 51^{\prime}$ is greater than $180^{\circ}$. Thus, only $\boldsymbol{C}=\mathbf{5 2}^{\circ} \mathbf{0}^{\prime}$ is valid.
Angle $\boldsymbol{A}=180^{\circ}-78^{\circ} 51^{\prime}-52^{\circ} 0^{\prime}=\mathbf{4 9}^{\circ} \mathbf{9}^{\prime}$.
Applying the sine rule, $\quad \frac{a}{\sin 49^{\circ} 9^{\prime}}=\frac{22.31}{\sin 78^{\circ} 51^{\prime}}$
from which $\quad \boldsymbol{a}=\frac{22.31 \sin 49^{\circ} 9^{\prime}}{\sin 78^{\circ} 51^{\prime}}=\mathbf{1 7 . 2 0} \mathbf{m m}$

Hence, $\boldsymbol{A}=\mathbf{4 9 ^ { \circ }} \mathbf{9}^{\prime}, \boldsymbol{C}=\mathbf{5} 2^{\circ} \mathbf{0}^{\prime}$ and $\boldsymbol{B C}=\mathbf{1 7 . 2 0} \mathrm{mm}$.
$$\text { Area of triangle } \begin{aligned}
\boldsymbol{A} \boldsymbol{B} \boldsymbol{C} & =\frac{1}{2} a c \sin B \\
& =\frac{1}{2}(17.20)(17.92) \sin 78^{\circ} 51^{\prime} \\
& =\mathbf{1 5 1 . 2} \mathbf{m m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 3. Solve the triangle $P Q R$ and find its area given that $Q R=36.5 \mathrm{~mm}, P R=29.6 \mathrm{~mm}$ and $\angle Q=36^{\circ}$

Triangle $P Q R$ is shown in Figure 23.4.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-220.jpg?height=253&width=424&top_left_y=362&top_left_x=417)

Figure 23.4
Applying the sine rule, $\frac{29.6}{\sin 36^{\circ}}=\frac{36.5}{\sin P}$ from which $\sin P=\frac{36.5 \sin 36^{\circ}}{29.6}=0.7248$
Hence, $\quad P=\sin ^{-1} 0.7248=46.45^{\circ}$ or $133.55^{\circ}$
When $P=46.45^{\circ}$ and $Q=36^{\circ}$ then
$R=180^{\circ}-46.45^{\circ}-36^{\circ}=97.55^{\circ}$
When $P=133.55^{\circ}$ and $Q=36^{\circ}$ then
$R=180^{\circ}-133.55^{\circ}-36^{\circ}=10.45^{\circ}$
Thus, in this problem, there are two separate sets of results and both are feasible solutions. Such a situation is called the ambiguous case.
Case 1. $P=46.45^{\circ}, Q=36^{\circ}, R=97.55^{\circ}$, $p=36.5 \mathrm{~mm}$ and $q=29.6 \mathrm{~mm}$
From the sine rule, $\quad \frac{r}{\sin 97.55^{\circ}}=\frac{29.6}{\sin 36^{\circ}}$
from which $\quad r=\frac{29.6 \sin 97.55^{\circ}}{\sin 36^{\circ}}=\mathbf{4 9 . 9 2} \mathrm{mm}=\boldsymbol{P Q}$
Area of $P Q R=\frac{1}{2} p q \sin R=\frac{1}{2}(36.5)(29.6) \sin 97.55^{\circ}$
$$=535.5 \mathrm{~mm}^{2}$$

Case 2. $P=133.55^{\circ}, Q=36^{\circ}, R=10.45^{\circ}$, $p=36.5 \mathrm{~mm}$ and $q=29.6 \mathrm{~mm}$
From the sine rule, $\quad \frac{r}{\sin 10.45^{\circ}}=\frac{29.6}{\sin 36^{\circ}}$
from which $\quad r=\frac{29.6 \sin 10.45^{\circ}}{\sin 36^{\circ}}=\mathbf{9 . 1 3 4} \mathbf{m m}=P Q$
Area of $P Q R=\frac{1}{2} p q \sin R=\frac{1}{2}(36.5)(29.6) \sin 10.45^{\circ}$
$$=97.98 \mathrm{~mm}^{2}$$

The triangle $P Q R$ for case 2 is shown in Figure 23.5.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-220.jpg?height=207&width=422&top_left_y=2172&top_left_x=418)

Figure 23.5

Practice Exercise 90 Solution of triangles and their areas (answers on page 350)

In problems 1 and 2, use the sine rule to solve the triangles $A B C$ and find their areas.
1. $A=29^{\circ}, B=68^{\circ}, b=27 \mathrm{~mm}$
2. $B=71^{\circ} 26^{\prime}, C=56^{\circ} 32^{\prime}, b=8.60 \mathrm{~cm}$

In problems 3 and 4, use the sine rule to solve the triangles $D E F$ and find their areas.
3. $d=17 \mathrm{~cm}, f=22 \mathrm{~cm}, F=26^{\circ}$
4. $d=32.6 \mathrm{~mm}, e=25.4 \mathrm{~mm}, D=104^{\circ} 22^{\prime}$

In problems 5 and 6, use the sine rule to solve the triangles $J K L$ and find their areas.
5. $j=3.85 \mathrm{~cm}, k=3.23 \mathrm{~cm}, K=36^{\circ}$
6. $k=46 \mathrm{~mm}, l=36 \mathrm{~mm}, L=35^{\circ}$
23.4 Further worked problems on the solution of triangles and their areas

Problem 4. Solve triangle $D E F$ and find its area given that $E F=35.0 \mathrm{~mm}, D E=25.0 \mathrm{~mm}$ and $\angle E=64^{\circ}$

Triangle $D E F$ is shown in Figure 23.6. Solving the triangle means finding angles $D$ and $F$ and side $D F$. Since two sides and the angle in between the two sides are given, the cosine needs to be used.

Figure 23.6
Applying the cosine rule, $\quad e^{2}=d^{2}+f^{2}-2 d f \cos E$ i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
e^{2} & =(35.0)^{2}+(25.0)^{2}-\left[2(35.0)(25.0) \cos 64^{\circ}\right] \\
& =1225+625-767.15 \\
& =1082.85
\end{aligned}$$
from which
$$\begin{aligned}
e & =\sqrt{1082.85} \\
& =\mathbf{3 2 . 9 1} \mathrm{mm}=\boldsymbol{D F}
\end{aligned}$$

Applying the sine rule, $\quad \frac{32.91}{\sin 64^{\circ}}=\frac{25.0}{\sin F}$ from which $\sin F=\frac{25.0 \sin 64^{\circ}}{32.91}=0.6828$

Thus,
$$\angle F=\sin ^{-1} 0.6828=43^{\circ} 4^{\prime} \text { or } 136^{\circ} 56^{\prime}$$
$F=136^{\circ} 56^{\prime}$ is not possible in this case since $136^{\circ} 56^{\prime}+$ $64^{\circ}$ is greater than $180^{\circ}$. Thus, only $\boldsymbol{F}=\mathbf{4 3}^{\circ} \mathbf{4}^{\prime}$ is valid. Then $\angle \boldsymbol{D}=180^{\circ}-64^{\circ}-43^{\circ} 4^{\prime}=\mathbf{7 2}^{\circ} 56^{\prime}$.
Area of triangle $\boldsymbol{D E F}=\frac{1}{2} d f \sin E$
$$=\frac{1}{2}(35.0)(25.0) \sin 64^{\circ}=\mathbf{3 9 3 . 2} \mathbf{~ m m}^{2}$$

Problem 5. A triangle $A B C$ has sides $a=9.0 \mathrm{~cm}, b=7.5 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $c=6.5 \mathrm{~cm}$. Determine its three angles and its area

Triangle $A B C$ is shown in Figure 23.7. It is usual first to calculate the largest angle to determine whether the triangle is acute or obtuse. In this case the largest angle is $A$ (i.e. opposite the longest side).

Figure 23.7
Applying the cosine rule, $a^{2}=b^{2}+c^{2}-2 b c \cos A$ from which
$$2 b c \cos A=b^{2}+c^{2}-a^{2}$$
and
$$\begin{aligned}
\cos A=\frac{b^{2}+c^{2}-a^{2}}{2 b c} & =\frac{7.5^{2}+6.5^{2}-9.0^{2}}{2(7.5)(6.5)} \\
& =0.1795
\end{aligned}$$

Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
& \boldsymbol{A}=\cos ^{-1} 0.1795=79.67^{\circ} \\
& \quad\left(\text { or } 280.33^{\circ},\right. \text { which is clearly impossible) }
\end{aligned}$$

The triangle is thus acute angled since $\cos A$ is positive. (If $\cos A$ had been negative, angle $A$ would be obtuse; i.e., would lie between $90^{\circ}$ and $180^{\circ}$.)

Applying the sine rule, $\quad \frac{9.0}{\sin 79.67^{\circ}}=\frac{7.5}{\sin B}$ from which $\quad \sin B=\frac{7.5 \sin 79.67^{\circ}}{9.0}=0.8198$

Hence,
$$\boldsymbol{B}=\sin ^{-1} 0.8198=\mathbf{5 5 . 0}^{\circ}$$
and
$$C=180^{\circ}-79.67^{\circ}-55.07^{\circ}=\mathbf{4 5 . 2 6}^{\circ}$$

Area $=\sqrt{[s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)]}$, where
$s=\frac{a+b+c}{2}=\frac{9.0+7.5+6.5}{2}=11.5 \mathrm{~cm}$
Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { area } & =\sqrt{[11.5(11.5-9.0)(11.5-7.5)(11.5-6.5)]} \\
& =\sqrt{[11.5(2.5)(4.0)(5.0)]}=\mathbf{2 3 . 9 8} \mathrm{cm}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Alternatively, area $=\frac{1}{2} a c \sin B$
$$=\frac{1}{2}(9.0)(6.5) \sin 55.07^{\circ}=\mathbf{2 3 . 9 8} \mathbf{c m}^{2}$$

Problem 6. Solve triangle $X Y Z$, shown in Figure 23.8, and find its area given that $Y=128^{\circ}, X Y=7.2 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $Y Z=4.5 \mathrm{~cm}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-221.jpg?height=232&width=349&top_left_y=1142&top_left_x=1317)

Figure 23.8

Applying the cosine rule,
$$\begin{aligned}
y^{2} & =x^{2}+z^{2}-2 x z \cos Y \\
& =4.5^{2}+7.2^{2}-\left[2(4.5)(7.2) \cos 128^{\circ}\right] \\
& =20.25+51.84-[-39.89] \\
& =20.25+51.84+39.89=112.0 \\
\boldsymbol{y} & =\sqrt{112.0}=\mathbf{1 0 . 5 8} \mathbf{c m}=X \mathbf{Z}
\end{aligned}$$

Applying the sine rule, $\frac{10.58}{\sin 128^{\circ}}=\frac{7.2}{\sin Z}$
from which $\sin Z=\frac{7.2 \sin 128^{\circ}}{10.58}=0.5363$
Hence,
$$Z=\sin ^{-1} 0.5363=\mathbf{3 2 . 4 3}^{\circ}$$
(or $147.57^{\circ}$ which is not possible)
Thus, $\boldsymbol{X}=180^{\circ}-128^{\circ}-32.43^{\circ}=\mathbf{1 9 . 5 7}^{\circ}$
Area of $X Y Z=\frac{1}{2} x z \sin Y=\frac{1}{2}(4.5)(7.2) \sin 128^{\circ}$
$$=12.77 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$$

\section*{Now try the following Practice Exercise}

\section*{Practice Exercise 91 Solution of triangles and their areas (answers on page 350)}

In problems 1 and 2, use the cosine and sine rules to solve the triangles $P Q R$ and find their areas.
1. $q=12 \mathrm{~cm}, r=16 \mathrm{~cm}, P=54^{\circ}$
2. $q=3.25 \mathrm{~m}, r=4.42 \mathrm{~m}, P=105^{\circ}$

In problems 3 and 4, use the cosine and sine rules to solve the triangles $X Y Z$ and find their areas.
3. $x=10.0 \mathrm{~cm}, y=8.0 \mathrm{~cm}, z=7.0 \mathrm{~cm}$
4. $x=21 \mathrm{~mm}, y=34 \mathrm{~mm}, z=42 \mathrm{~mm}$

\subsection*{23.5 Practical situations involving trigonometry}

There are a number of practical situations in which the use of trigonometry is needed to find unknown sides and angles of triangles. This is demonstrated in the following worked problems.

Problem 7. A room 8.0 m wide has a span roof which slopes at $33^{\circ}$ on one side and $40^{\circ}$ on the other. Find the length of the roof slopes, correct to the nearest centimetre

A section of the roof is shown in Figure 23.9.

Figure 23.9
Angle at ridge, $B=180^{\circ}-33^{\circ}-40^{\circ}=107^{\circ}$
From the sine rule, $\quad \frac{8.0}{\sin 107^{\circ}}=\frac{a}{\sin 33^{\circ}}$ from which $\quad \boldsymbol{a}=\frac{8.0 \sin 33^{\circ}}{\sin 107^{\circ}}=\mathbf{4 . 5 5 6} \mathrm{m}=\boldsymbol{B C}$
Also from the sine rule, $\frac{8.0}{\sin 107^{\circ}}=\frac{c}{\sin 40^{\circ}}$ from which $\quad \boldsymbol{c}=\frac{8.0 \sin 40^{\circ}}{\sin 107^{\circ}}=\mathbf{5 . 3 7 7} \mathbf{m}=\boldsymbol{A} \boldsymbol{B}$

Hence, the roof slopes are $\mathbf{4 . 5 6} \mathrm{m}$ and 5.38 m , correct to the nearest centimetre.

Problem 8. A man leaves a point walking at $6.5 \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{h}$ in a direction $\mathrm{E} 20^{\circ} \mathrm{N}$ (i.e. a bearing of $70^{\circ}$ ). A cyclist leaves the same point at the same time in a direction $\mathrm{E} 40^{\circ} \mathrm{S}$ (i.e. a bearing of $130^{\circ}$ ) travelling at a constant speed. Find the average speed of the cyclist if the walker and cyclist are 80 km apart after 5 hours

After 5 hours the walker has travelled $5 \times 6.5=32.5 \mathrm{~km}$ (shown as $A B$ in Figure 23.10). If $A C$ is the distance the cyclist travels in 5 hours then $B C=80 \mathrm{~km}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-222.jpg?height=375&width=383&top_left_y=962&top_left_x=1273)

Figure 23.10

Applying the sine rule, $\quad \frac{80}{\sin 60^{\circ}}=\frac{32.5}{\sin C}$
from which $\quad \sin C=\frac{32.5 \sin 60^{\circ}}{80}=0.3518$
Hence, $\quad C=\sin ^{-1} 0.3518=20.60^{\circ}$ (or $159.40^{\circ}$, which is not possible)
and
$$B=180^{\circ}-60^{\circ}-20.60^{\circ}=99.40^{\circ}$$

Applying the sine rule again, $\frac{80}{\sin 60^{\circ}}=\frac{b}{\sin 99.40^{\circ}}$
from which $\quad b=\frac{80 \sin 99.40^{\circ}}{\sin 60^{\circ}}=91.14 \mathrm{~km}$
Since the cyclist travels 91.14 km in 5 hours,
average speed $=\frac{\text { distance }}{\text { time }}=\frac{91.14}{5}=\mathbf{1 8 . 2 3} \mathbf{k m} / \mathbf{h}$
Problem 9. Two voltage phasors are shown in Figure 23.11. If $V_{1}=40 \mathrm{~V}$ and $V_{2}=100 \mathrm{~V}$, determine the value of their resultant (i.e. length $O A)$ and the angle the resultant makes with $V_{1}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-223.jpg?height=294&width=452&top_left_y=341&top_left_x=433)

Figure 23.11

Angle $O B A=180^{\circ}-45^{\circ}=135^{\circ}$
Applying the cosine rule,
$$\begin{aligned}
O A^{2} & =V_{1}^{2}+V_{2}^{2}-2 V_{1} V_{2} \cos O B A \\
& =40^{2}+100^{2}-\left\{2(40)(100) \cos 135^{\circ}\right\} \\
& =1600+10000-\{-5657\} \\
& =1600+10000+5657=17257
\end{aligned}$$

Thus, resultant, $\boldsymbol{O A}=\sqrt{17257}=131.4 \mathrm{~V}$
Applying the sine rule $\quad \frac{131.4}{\sin 135^{\circ}}=\frac{100}{\sin A O B}$
from which $\quad \sin A O B=\frac{100 \sin 135^{\circ}}{131.4}=0.5381$
Hence, angle $A O B=\sin ^{-1} 0.5381=32.55^{\circ}$ (or $147.45^{\circ}$, which is not possible)
Hence, the resultant voltage is $\mathbf{1 3 1 . 4}$ volts at $32.55^{\circ}$ to $V_{1}$

Problem 10. In Figure 23.12, $P R$ represents the inclined jib of a crane and is 10.0 m long. $P Q$ is 4.0 m long. Determine the inclination of the jib to the vertical and the length of tie $Q R$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-223.jpg?height=395&width=331&top_left_y=1717&top_left_x=488)

Figure 23.12

Applying the sine rule, $\quad \frac{P R}{\sin 120^{\circ}}=\frac{P Q}{\sin R}$
from which $\quad \sin R=\frac{P Q \sin 120^{\circ}}{P R}=\frac{(4.0) \sin 120^{\circ}}{10.0}$
$$=0.3464$$

Hence, $\angle R=\sin ^{-1} 0.3464=20.27^{\circ}$ (or $159.73^{\circ}$, which is not possible)
$\angle \boldsymbol{P}=180^{\circ}-120^{\circ}-20.27^{\circ}=39.73^{\circ}$, which is the inclination of the jib to the vertical

Applying the sine rule, $\frac{10.0}{\sin 120^{\circ}}=\frac{Q R}{\sin 39.73^{\circ}}$
from which
$$\text { length of tie, } \begin{aligned}
Q R & =\frac{10.0 \sin 39.73^{\circ}}{\sin 120^{\circ}} \\
& =\mathbf{7 . 3 8} \mathbf{~ m}
\end{aligned}$$

\subsection*{23.6 Further practical situations involving trigonometry}

Problem 11. A vertical aerial stands on horizontal ground. A surveyor positioned due east of the aerial measures the elevation of the top as $48^{\circ}$. He moves due south 30.0 m and measures the elevation as $44^{\circ}$. Determine the height of the aerial

In Figure 23.16, $D C$ represents the aerial, $A$ is the initial position of the surveyor and $B$ his final position.
From triangle $A C D, \tan 48^{\circ}=\frac{D C}{A C}$ from which
$$A C=\frac{D C}{\tan 48^{\circ}}$$

Similarly, from triangle $B C D, B C=\frac{D C}{\tan 44^{\circ}}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-224.jpg?height=467&width=373&top_left_y=309&top_left_x=1278)

Figure 23.16

For triangle $A B C$, using Pythagoras' theorem,
$$\begin{aligned}
B C^{2} & =A B^{2}+A C^{2} \\
\left(\frac{D C}{\tan 44^{\circ}}\right)^{2} & =(30.0)^{2}+\left(\frac{D C}{\tan 48^{\circ}}\right)^{2} \\
D C^{2}\left(\frac{1}{\tan ^{2} 44^{\circ}}-\frac{1}{\tan ^{2} 48^{\circ}}\right) & =30.0^{2} \\
D C^{2}(1.072323-0.810727) & =30.0^{2} \\
D C^{2} & =\frac{30.0^{2}}{0.261596}=3440.4
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, height of aerial, $\boldsymbol{D C}=\sqrt{3340.4}=\mathbf{5 8 . 6 5} \mathbf{~ m}$.

Problem 12. A crank mechanism of a petrol engine is shown in Figure 23.17. Arm $O A$ is 10.0 cm long and rotates clockwise about $O$. The connecting $\operatorname{rod} A B$ is 30.0 cm long and end $B$ is constrained to move horizontally.
(a) For the position shown in Figure 23.17, determine the angle between the connecting $\operatorname{rod} A B$ and the horizontal, and the length of $O B$.
(b) How far does $B$ move when angle $A O B$ changes from $50^{\circ}$ to $120^{\circ}$ ?
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-224.jpg?height=232&width=573&top_left_y=2151&top_left_x=1178)

Figure 23.17
(a) Applying the sine rule, $\frac{A B}{\sin 50^{\circ}}=\frac{A O}{\sin B}$
from which $\sin B=\frac{A O \sin 50^{\circ}}{A B}=\frac{10.0 \sin 50^{\circ}}{30.0}$
$$=0.2553$$

Hence, $B=\sin ^{-1} 0.2553=14.79^{\circ}$ (or $165.21^{\circ}$, which is not possible)
Hence, the connecting rod $A B$ makes an angle of $14.79^{\circ}$ with the horizontal.
Angle $O A B=180^{\circ}-50^{\circ}-14.79^{\circ}=115.21^{\circ}$
Applying the sine rule: $\frac{30.0}{\sin 50^{\circ}}=\frac{O B}{\sin 115.21^{\circ}}$
from which
$$\begin{aligned}
\boldsymbol{O B} & =\frac{30.0 \sin 115.21^{\circ}}{\sin 50^{\circ}} \\
& =\mathbf{3 5 . 4 3} \mathbf{~ c m}
\end{aligned}$$
(b) Figure 23.18 shows the initial and final positions of the crank mechanism.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-225.jpg?height=242&width=570&top_left_y=1170&top_left_x=374)

Figure 23.18
In triangle $O A^{\prime} B^{\prime}$, applying the sine rule,
$$\frac{30.0}{\sin 120^{\circ}}=\frac{10.0}{\sin A^{\prime} B^{\prime} O}$$
from which $\sin A^{\prime} B^{\prime} O=\frac{10.0 \sin 120^{\circ}}{30.0}=0.28868$
Hence, $\quad A^{\prime} B^{\prime} O=\sin ^{-1} 0.28868=16.78^{\circ} \quad$ (or $163.22^{\circ}$, which is not possible)
Angle $O A^{\prime} B^{\prime}=180^{\circ}-120^{\circ}-16.78^{\circ}=43.22^{\circ}$
Applying the sine rule, $\frac{30.0}{\sin 120^{\circ}}=\frac{O B^{\prime}}{\sin 43.22^{\circ}}$
from which $O B^{\prime}=\frac{30.0 \sin 43.22^{\circ}}{\sin 120^{\circ}}=23.72 \mathrm{~cm}$
Since $O B=35.43 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $O B^{\prime}=23.72 \mathrm{~cm}$, $B B^{\prime}=35.43-23.72=11.71 \mathrm{~cm}$
Hence, $B$ moves 11.71 cm when angle $A O B$ changes from $50^{\circ}$ to $120^{\circ}$.

Problem 13. The area of a field is in the form of a quadrilateral $A B C D$ as shown in Figure 23.19.
Determine its area
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-225.jpg?height=340&width=383&top_left_y=334&top_left_x=1300)

Figure 23.19
A diagonal drawn from $B$ to $D$ divides the quadrilateral into two triangles.
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Area of quadrilateral } \begin{array}{l}
A B C D
\end{array} \\
& \qquad \begin{array}{r}
= \\
\quad \\
\quad=\frac{1}{2}(39.8)(21.4) \sin 114^{\circ} \\
\quad \\
\quad+\frac{1}{2}(42.5)(62.3) \sin 56^{\circ} \\
\\
=389.04+1097.5 \\
=
\end{array} \mathbf{1 4 8 7}^{\mathbf{2}} \\
& \hline
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{Chapter 24}

\section*{Cartesian and polar co-ordinates}

\subsection*{24.1 Introduction}

There are two ways in which the position of a point in a plane can be represented. These are
(a) Cartesian co-ordinates, i.e. $(x, y)$.
(b) Polar co-ordinates, i.e. $(r, \theta)$, where $r$ is a radius from a fixed point and $\theta$ is an angle from a fixed point.

\subsection*{24.2 Changing from Cartesian to polar co-ordinates}

In Figure 24.1, if lengths $x$ and $y$ are known then the length of $r$ can be obtained from Pythagoras' theorem (see Chapter 21) since $O P Q$ is a right-angled triangle.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-227.jpg?height=313&width=435&top_left_y=1904&top_left_x=436)

Figure 24.1

Hence, $r^{2}=\left(x^{2}+y^{2}\right)$, from which $\quad \boldsymbol{r}=\sqrt{\boldsymbol{x}^{2}+\boldsymbol{y}^{\mathbf{2}}}$
From trigonometric ratios (see Chapter 21), $\tan \theta=\frac{y}{x}$
from which $\theta=\tan ^{-1} \frac{y}{x}$
$r=\sqrt{x^{2}+y^{2}}$ and $\theta=\tan ^{-1} \frac{y}{x}$ are the two formulae we need to change from Cartesian to polar co-ordinates. The angle $\theta$, which may be expressed in degrees or radians, must always be measured from the positive $x$-axis; i.e., measured from the line $O Q$ in Figure 24.1. It is suggested that when changing from Cartesian to polar co-ordinates a diagram should always be sketched.

Problem 1. Change the Cartesian co-ordinates $(3,4)$ into polar co-ordinates

A diagram representing the point $(3,4)$ is shown in Figure 24.2.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-227.jpg?height=327&width=381&top_left_y=1759&top_left_x=1301)

Figure 24.2

From Pythagoras' theorem, $r=\sqrt{3^{2}+4^{2}}=5$ (note that -5 has no meaning in this context).
By trigonometric ratios, $\theta=\tan ^{-1} \frac{4}{3}=53.13^{\circ}$ or 0.927 rad .

Note that $53.13^{\circ}=53.13 \times \frac{\pi}{180} \mathrm{rad}=0.927 \mathrm{rad}$.

Hence, (3, 4) in Cartesian co-ordinates corresponds to $\left(5,53.13^{\circ}\right)$ or ( $5,0.927 \mathrm{rad}$ ) in polar co-ordinates.

Problem 2. Express in polar co-ordinates the position (-4, 3)

A diagram representing the point using the Cartesian co-ordinates $(-4,3)$ is shown in Figure 24.3.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-228.jpg?height=283&width=430&top_left_y=775&top_left_x=414)

Figure 24.3
From Pythagoras' theorem, $r=\sqrt{4^{2}+3^{2}}=5$
By trigonometric ratios, $\quad \alpha=\tan ^{-1} \frac{3}{4}=36.87^{\circ}$ or 0.644 rad

Hence, $\theta=180^{\circ}-36.87^{\circ}=143.13^{\circ}$
or $\quad \theta=\pi-0.644=2.498 \mathrm{rad}$
Hence, the position of point $\boldsymbol{P}$ in polar co-ordinate form is $\left(5,143.13^{\circ}\right)$ or ( $5,2.498 \mathrm{rad}$ ).

Problem 3. Express $(-5,-12)$ in polar co-ordinates

A sketch showing the position $(-5,-12)$ is shown in Figure 24.4.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-228.jpg?height=410&width=375&top_left_y=1872&top_left_x=444)

Figure 24.4
$r=\sqrt{5^{2}+12^{2}}=13$ and $\alpha=\tan ^{-1} \frac{12}{5}=67.38^{\circ}$ or 1.176 rad

Hence, $\theta=180^{\circ}+67.38^{\circ}=247.38^{\circ}$ or $\theta=\pi+1.176=4.318 \mathrm{rad}$.
Thus, $(-5,-12)$ in Cartesian co-ordinates corresponds to $\left(13,247.38^{\circ}\right)$ or (13, 4.318 rad$)$ in polar co-ordinates.

Problem 4. Express $(2,-5)$ in polar co-ordinates

A sketch showing the position $(2,-5)$ is shown in Figure 24.5.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-228.jpg?height=405&width=361&top_left_y=795&top_left_x=1284)

Figure 24.5
$r=\sqrt{2^{2}+5^{2}}=\sqrt{29}=5.385$, correct to 3 decimal places $\alpha=\tan ^{-1} \frac{5}{2}=68.20^{\circ}$ or 1.190 rad
Hence, $\theta=360^{\circ}-68.20^{\circ}=291.80^{\circ}$ or $\theta=2 \pi-1.190=5.093 \mathrm{rad}$.

Thus, $(2,-5)$ in Cartesian co-ordinates corresponds to $\left(5.385,291.80^{\circ}\right)$ or $(5.385,5.093 \mathrm{rad})$ in polar coordinates.

\subsection*{24.3 Changing from polar to Cartesian co-ordinates}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-229.jpg?height=318&width=438&top_left_y=492&top_left_x=437)

Figure 24.6

From the right-angled triangle $O P Q$ in Figure 24.6, $\cos \theta=\frac{x}{r}$ and $\sin \theta=\frac{y}{r}$ from trigonometric ratios

Hence, $x=r \cos \theta$ and $y=r \sin \theta$.
If lengths $r$ and angle $\theta$ are known then $x=r \cos \theta$ and $y=r \sin \theta$ are the two formulae we need to change from polar to Cartesian co-ordinates.

Problem 5. Change $\left(4,32^{\circ}\right)$ into Cartesian co-ordinates

A sketch showing the position $\left(4,32^{\circ}\right)$ is shown in Figure 24.7.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-229.jpg?height=286&width=440&top_left_y=1598&top_left_x=436)

Figure 24.7
$$\begin{array}{ll}
\text { Now } & x=r \cos \theta=4 \cos 32^{\circ}=3.39 \\
\text { and } & y=r \sin \theta=4 \sin 32^{\circ}=2.12
\end{array}$$

Hence, $\left(4,32^{\circ}\right)$ in polar co-ordinates corresponds to (3.39, 2.12) in Cartesian co-ordinates.

\section*{Problem 6. Express $\left(6,137^{\circ}\right)$ in Cartesian co-ordinates}

A sketch showing the position $\left(6,137^{\circ}\right)$ is shown in Figure 24.8.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-229.jpg?height=291&width=449&top_left_y=305&top_left_x=1267)

Figure 24.8
$$x=r \cos \theta=6 \cos 137^{\circ}=-4.388$$
which corresponds to length $O A$ in Figure 24.8.
$$y=r \sin \theta=6 \sin 137^{\circ}=4.092$$
which corresponds to length $A B$ in Figure 24.8.
Thus, $\left(6,137^{\circ}\right)$ in polar co-ordinates corresponds to (-4.388, 4.092) in Cartesian co-ordinates.
(Note that when changing from polar to Cartesian coordinates it is not quite so essential to draw a sketch. Use of $x=r \cos \theta$ and $y=r \sin \theta$ automatically produces the correct values and signs.)

\section*{Problem 7. Express (4.5, 5.16 rad$)$ in Cartesian co-ordinates}

A sketch showing the position (4.5, 5.16 rad ) is shown in Figure 24.9.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-229.jpg?height=353&width=430&top_left_y=1589&top_left_x=1279)

Figure 24.9
$$x=r \cos \theta=4.5 \cos 5.16=1.948$$
which corresponds to length $O A$ in Figure 24.9.
$$y=r \sin \theta=4.5 \sin 5.16=-4.057$$
which corresponds to length $A B$ in Figure 24.9.
Thus, (1.948, -4.057) in Cartesian co-ordinates corresponds to $(4.5,5.16 \mathrm{rad})$ in polar co-ordinates.

\subsection*{24.4 Use of Pol/Rec functions on calculators}

Another name for Cartesian co-ordinates is rectangular co-ordinates. Many scientific notation calculators have Pol and Rec functions. 'Rec' is an abbreviation of 'rectangular' (i.e. Cartesian) and 'Pol' is an abbreviation of 'polar'. Check the operation manual for your particular calculator to determine how to use these two functions. They make changing from Cartesian to polar coordinates, and vice-versa, so much quicker and easier. For example, with the Casio fx-83ES calculator, or similar, to change the Cartesian number $(3,4)$ into polar form, the following procedure is adopted.
1. Press 'shift'
2. Press 'Pol'
3. Enter 3
4. Enter 'comma' (obtained by 'shift' then ) )
5. Enter 4
6. Press )
7. Press $=$

The answer is $\boldsymbol{r}=\mathbf{5}, \boldsymbol{\theta}=\mathbf{5 3 . 1 3}{ }^{\circ}$
Hence, (3, 4) in Cartesian form is the same as $\left(5,53.13^{\circ}\right)$ in polar form.
If the angle is required in radians, then before repeating the above procedure press 'shift', 'mode' and then 4 to change your calculator to radian mode.
Similarly, to change the polar form number ( $7,126^{\circ}$ ) into Cartesian or rectangular form, adopt the following procedure.
1. Press 'shift'
2. Press 'Rec'
3. Enter 7
4. Enter 'comma'
5. Enter 126 (assuming your calculator is in degrees mode)
6. Press )
7. Press $=$

The answer is $\boldsymbol{X}=\mathbf{- 4 . 1 1}$ and, scrolling across, $\boldsymbol{Y}=\mathbf{5 . 6 6}$, correct to 2 decimal places.
Hence, $\left(7,126^{\circ}\right)$ in polar form is the same as $(-4.11,5.66)$ in rectangular or Cartesian form.

Now return to Practice Exercises 94 and 95 in this chapter and use your calculator to determine the answers, and see how much more quickly they may be obtained.

\section*{Revision Test 9 : Trigonometric waveforms and practical trigonometry}

This assignment covers the material contained in Chapters 22-24. The marks available are shown in brackets at the end of each question.
1. A sine wave is given by $y=8 \sin 4 x$. State its peak value and its period, in degrees.
2. A periodic function is given by $y=15 \tan 2 x$. State its period in degrees.
3. The frequency of a sine wave is 800 Hz . Calculate the periodic time in milliseconds.
4. Calculate the frequency of a sine wave that has a periodic time of $40 \mu \mathrm{~s}$.
5. Calculate the periodic time for a sine wave having a frequency of 20 kHz .
6. An alternating current completes 12 cycles in 16 ms . What is its frequency?
7. A sinusoidal voltage is given by $e=150 \sin (500 \pi t-0.25)$ volts. Determine the
(a) amplitude,
(b) frequency,
(c) periodic time,
(d) phase angle (stating whether it is leading or lagging $150 \sin 500 \pi t$ ).
8. Determine the acute angles in degrees, degrees and minutes, and radians.
(a) $\sin ^{-1} 0.4721$
(b) $\cos ^{-1} 0.8457$
(c) $\tan ^{-1} 1.3472$
9. Sketch the following curves, labelling relevant points.
(a) $y=4 \cos \left(\theta+45^{\circ}\right)$
(b) $y=5 \sin \left(2 t-60^{\circ}\right)$
10. The current in an alternating current circuit at any time $t$ seconds is given by $i=120 \sin (100 \pi t+0.274)$ amperes. Determine
(a) the amplitude, frequency, periodic time and phase angle (with reference to $120 \sin 100 \pi t$ ),
(b) the value of current when $t=0$,
(c) the value of current when $t=6 \mathrm{~ms}$.

Sketch one cycle of the oscillation.
11. A triangular plot of land $A B C$ is shown in Figure RT9.1. Solve the triangle and determine its area.

\section*{Chapter 25}

\section*{Areas of common shapes}

\subsection*{25.1 Introduction}

Area is a measure of the size or extent of a plane surface. Area is measured in square units such as $\mathrm{mm}^{2}, \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$ and $\mathrm{m}^{2}$. This chapter deals with finding the areas of common shapes.

In engineering it is often important to be able to calculate simple areas of various shapes. In everyday life its important to be able to measure area to, say, lay a carpet, order sufficient paint for a decorating job or order sufficient bricks for a new wall.

On completing this chapter you will be able to recognize common shapes and be able to find the areas of rectangles, squares, parallelograms, triangles, trapeziums and circles.

\subsection*{25.2 Common shapes}

\subsection*{25.2.1 Polygons}

A polygon is a closed plane figure bounded by straight lines. A polygon which has

3 sides is called a triangle - see Figure 25.1(a)
4 sides is called a quadrilateral - see Figure 25.1(b)
5 sides is called a pentagon - see Figure 25.1(c)
6 sides is called a hexagon - see Figure 25.1(d)
7 sides is called a heptagon - see Figure 25.1(e)
8 sides is called an octagon - see Figure 25.1(f)

\subsection*{25.2.2 Quadrilaterals}

There are five types of quadrilateral, these being rectangle, square, parallelogram, rhombus and trapezium.

If the opposite corners of any quadrilateral are joined by a straight line, two triangles are produced. Since the sum of the angles of a triangle is $180^{\circ}$, the sum of the angles of a quadrilateral is $360^{\circ}$.

\section*{Rectangle}

In the rectangle $A B C D$ shown in Figure 25.2,
(a) all four angles are right angles,
(b) the opposite sides are parallel and equal in length, and
(c) diagonals $A C$ and $B D$ are equal in length and bisect one another.

\section*{Square}

In the square $P Q R S$ shown in Figure 25.3,
(a) all four angles are right angles,
(b) the opposite sides are parallel,
(c) all four sides are equal in length, and
(d) diagonals $P R$ and $Q S$ are equal in length and bisect one another at right angles.

\section*{Parallelogram}

In the parallelogram $W X Y Z$ shown in Figure 25.4,
(a) opposite angles are equal,
(b) opposite sides are parallel and equal in length, and
(c) diagonals $W Y$ and $X Z$ bisect one another.

\section*{Rhombus}

In the rhombus $A B C D$ shown in Figure 25.5,
(a) opposite angles are equal,
(b) opposite angles are bisected by a diagonal,
(c) opposite sides are parallel,
(d) all four sides are equal in length, and
(e) diagonals $A C$ and $B D$ bisect one another at right angles.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-233.jpg?height=804&width=1232&top_left_y=295&top_left_x=455)

Problem 1. State the types of quadrilateral shown in Figure 25.7 and determine the angles marked $a$ to $l$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-233.jpg?height=674&width=733&top_left_y=1358&top_left_x=1128)

Figure 25.7
(a) $A B C D$ is a square

The diagonals of a square bisect each of the right angles, hence

\section*{Trapezium}

In the trapezium $E F G H$ shown in Figure 25.6,
$$a=\frac{90^{\circ}}{2}=45^{\circ}$$
(a) only one pair of sides is parallel.
(b) $E F G H$ is a rectangle

In triangle $F G H, 40^{\circ}+90^{\circ}+b=180^{\circ}$, since the angles in a triangle add up to $180^{\circ}$, from which $\boldsymbol{b}=\mathbf{5 0}{ }^{\circ}$. Also, $\boldsymbol{c}=\mathbf{4 0}^{\circ}$ (alternate angles between parallel lines $E F$ and $H G$ ). (Alternatively, $b$ and $c$ are complementary; i.e., add up to $90^{\circ}$.)
$d=90^{\circ}+c$ (external angle of a triangle equals the sum of the interior opposite angles), hence $\boldsymbol{d}=90^{\circ}+40^{\circ}=\mathbf{1 3 0}^{\circ}$ (or $\angle E F H=50^{\circ}$ and $d=180^{\circ}-50^{\circ}=130^{\circ}$ ).
(c) JKLM is a rhombus

The diagonals of a rhombus bisect the interior angles and the opposite internal angles are equal. Thus, $\angle J K M=\angle M K L=\angle J M K=\angle L M K=30^{\circ}$, hence, $\boldsymbol{e}=\mathbf{3 0}{ }^{\circ}$.
In triangle $K L M, 30^{\circ}+\angle K L M+30^{\circ}=180^{\circ}$ (the angles in a triangle add up to $180^{\circ}$ ), hence, $\angle K L M=120^{\circ}$. The diagonal $J L$ bisects $\angle K L M$, hence, $f=\frac{120^{\circ}}{2}=\mathbf{6 0}^{\circ}$.
(d) $N O P Q$ is a parallelogram
$g=52^{\circ}$ since the opposite interior angles of a parallelogram are equal.
In triangle $N O Q, g+h+65^{\circ}=180^{\circ}$ (the angles in a triangle add up to $180^{\circ}$ ), from which $h=180^{\circ}-65^{\circ}-52^{\circ}=63^{\circ}$.
$\boldsymbol{i}=\mathbf{6 5}{ }^{\circ}$ (alternate angles between parallel lines $N Q$ and $O P$ ).
$j=52^{\circ}+i=52^{\circ}+65^{\circ}=117^{\circ}$ (the external angle of a triangle equals the sum of the interior opposite angles). (Alternatively, $\angle P Q O=h=$ $63^{\circ}$; hence, $j=180^{\circ}-63^{\circ}=117^{\circ}$.)
(e) $\boldsymbol{R S T U}$ is a trapezium
$35^{\circ}+k=75^{\circ}$ (external angle of a triangle equals the sum of the interior opposite angles), hence, $k=40^{\circ}$.
$\angle S T R=35^{\circ}$ (alternate angles between parallel lines $R U$ and $S T$ ). $l+35^{\circ}=115^{\circ}$ (external angle of a triangle equals the sum of the interior opposite angles), hence, $\boldsymbol{l}=115^{\circ}-35^{\circ}=\mathbf{8 0}$.

\subsection*{25.3 Areas of common shapes}

The formulae for the areas of common shapes are shown in Table 25.1.
Here are some worked problems to demonstrate how the formulae are used to determine the area of common shapes.

Problem 2. Calculate the area and length of the perimeter of the square shown in Figure 25.9
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-234.jpg?height=291&width=351&top_left_y=1189&top_left_x=1289)

Figure 25.9
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area of square }=x^{2}=(4.0)^{2} & =4.0 \mathrm{~cm} \times 4.0 \mathrm{~cm} \\
& =\mathbf{1 6 . 0} \mathrm{cm}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$
(Note the unit of area is $\mathrm{cm} \times \mathrm{cm}=\mathrm{cm}^{2}$; i.e., square centimetres or centimetres squared.)
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Perimeter of square }= & 4.0 \mathrm{~cm}+4.0 \mathrm{~cm}+4.0 \mathrm{~cm} \\
& +4.0 \mathrm{~cm}=\mathbf{1 6 . 0} \mathbf{~ c m ~}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 3. Calculate the area and length of the perimeter of the rectangle shown in Figure 25.10
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-234.jpg?height=318&width=513&top_left_y=2062&top_left_x=1208)

Figure 25.10

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-235.jpg?height=199&width=687&top_left_y=1018&top_left_x=801)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-235.jpg?height=239&width=735&top_left_y=1532&top_left_x=804)

Area $=\frac{\theta^{\circ}}{360}\left(\pi r^{2}\right)$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area of rectangle }=l \times b & =7.0 \times 4.5 \\
& =\mathbf{3 1 . 5} \mathbf{c m}^{2} \\
\text { Perimeter of rectangle }= & 7.0 \mathrm{~cm}+4.5 \mathrm{~cm} \\
& +7.0 \mathrm{~cm}+4.5 \mathrm{~cm} \\
= & \mathbf{2 3 . 0} \mathbf{c m}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 4. Calculate the area of the parallelogram shown in Figure 25.11
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-236.jpg?height=432&width=733&top_left_y=755&top_left_x=263)

Figure 25.11

Area of a parallelogram $=$ base $\times$ perpendicular height
The perpendicular height $h$ is not shown in Figure 25.11 but may be found using Pythagoras' theorem (see Chapter 21).
From Figure 25.12, $9^{2}=5^{2}+h^{2}$, from which $h^{2}=9^{2}-5^{2}=81-25=56$.
Hence, perpendicular height,
$$h=\sqrt{56}=7.48 \mathrm{~mm}$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-236.jpg?height=389&width=706&top_left_y=1731&top_left_x=244)

Figure 25.12
Hence, area of parallelogram $\boldsymbol{E F G H}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =16 \mathrm{~mm} \times 7.48 \mathrm{~mm} \\
& =\mathbf{1 2 0} \mathbf{m m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 5. Calculate the area of the triangle shown in Figure 25.13
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-236.jpg?height=392&width=562&top_left_y=436&top_left_x=1189)

Figure 25.13

Area of triangle $I J K=\frac{1}{2} \times$ base $\times$ perpendicular height
$$=\frac{1}{2} \times I J \times J K$$

To find $J K$, Pythagoras' theorem is used; i.e.,
$$\begin{aligned}
5.68^{2} & =1.92^{2}+J K^{2}, \text { from which } \\
J K & =\sqrt{5.68^{2}-1.92^{2}}=5.346 \mathrm{~cm}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, area of triangle $\boldsymbol{I J K}=\frac{1}{2} \times 1.92 \times 5.346$
$$=5.132 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$$

Problem 6. Calculate the area of the trapezium shown in Figure 25.14
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-236.jpg?height=159&width=438&top_left_y=1629&top_left_x=1251)

Figure 25.14

Area of a trapezium $=\frac{1}{2} \times($ sum of parallel sides $)$
$\times$ (perpendicular distance between the parallel sides)

Hence, area of trapezium LMNO
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{1}{2} \times(27.4+8.6) \times 5.5 \\
& =\frac{1}{2} \times 36 \times 5.5=99 \mathrm{~mm}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 7. A rectangular tray is 820 mm long and 400 mm wide. Find its area in (a) $\mathrm{mm}^{2}$ (b) $\mathrm{cm}^{2}$ (c) $\mathrm{m}^{2}$
(a) Area of tray $=$ length $\times$ width $=820 \times 400$
$$=328000 \mathrm{~mm}^{2}$$
(b) Since $1 \mathrm{~cm}=10 \mathrm{~mm}, 1 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}=1 \mathrm{~cm} \times 1 \mathrm{~cm}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =10 \mathrm{~mm} \times 10 \mathrm{~mm}=100 \mathrm{~mm}^{2}, \text { or } \\
1 \mathrm{~mm}^{2} & =\frac{1}{100} \mathrm{~cm}^{2}=0.01 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $\mathbf{3 2 8 0 0 0} \mathrm{mm}^{\mathbf{2}}=328000 \times 0.01 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$
$$=3280 \mathrm{~cm}^{2} .$$
(c) Since $1 \mathrm{~m}=100 \mathrm{~cm}, 1 \mathrm{~m}^{2}=1 \mathrm{~m} \times 1 \mathrm{~m}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =100 \mathrm{~cm} \times 100 \mathrm{~cm}=10000 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}, \text { or } \\
1 \mathrm{~cm}^{2} & =\frac{1}{10000} \mathrm{~m}^{2}=0.0001 \mathrm{~m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $\mathbf{3 2 8 0} \mathbf{c m}^{\mathbf{2}}=3280 \times 0.0001 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$
$$=0.3280 \mathrm{~m}^{2} .$$

Problem 8. The outside measurements of a picture frame are 100 cm by 50 cm . If the frame is 4 cm wide, find the area of the wood used to make the frame

A sketch of the frame is shown shaded in Figure 25.15.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-237.jpg?height=345&width=627&top_left_y=1720&top_left_x=343)

Figure 25.15
Area of wood $=$ area of large rectangle - area of small rectangle
$$\begin{aligned}
& =(100 \times 50)-(92 \times 42) \\
& =5000-3864 \\
& =\mathbf{1 1 3 6} \mathbf{c m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 9. Find the cross-sectional area of the girder shown in Figure 25.16
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-237.jpg?height=367&width=343&top_left_y=440&top_left_x=1317)

Figure 25.16

The girder may be divided into three separate rectangles, as shown.
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area of rectangle } A & =50 \times 5=250 \mathrm{~mm}^{2} \\
\text { Area of rectangle } B & =(75-8-5) \times 6 \\
& =62 \times 6=372 \mathrm{~mm}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Area of rectangle $C=70 \times 8=560 \mathrm{~mm}^{2}$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Total area of girder } & =250+372+560 \\
& =\mathbf{1 1 8 2} \mathbf{m m}^{\mathbf{2}} \text { or } \mathbf{1 1 . 8 2} \mathbf{c m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 10. Figure 25.17 shows the gable end of a building. Determine the area of brickwork in the gable end
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-237.jpg?height=315&width=305&top_left_y=1586&top_left_x=1339)

Figure 25.17

The shape is that of a rectangle and a triangle.
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area of rectangle } & =6 \times 8=48 \mathrm{~m}^{2} \\
\text { Area of triangle } & =\frac{1}{2} \times \text { base } \times \text { height }
\end{aligned}$$
$C D=4 \mathrm{~m}$ and $A D=5 \mathrm{~m}$, hence $A C=3 \mathrm{~m}$ (since it is a $3,4,5$ triangle - or by Pythagoras).

Hence, area of triangle $A B D=\frac{1}{2} \times 8 \times 3=12 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$.

Total area of brickwork $=48+12$
$$=60 \mathrm{~m}^{2} .$$

Here are some further worked problems on finding the areas of common shapes.

Problem 11. Find the area of a circle having a radius of 5 cm

Area of circle $=\pi r^{2}=\pi(5)^{2}=25 \pi=78.54 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$

Problem 12. Find the area of a circle having a diameter of 15 mm

Area of circle $=\frac{\pi d^{2}}{4}=\frac{\pi(15)^{2}}{4}=\frac{225 \pi}{4}=\mathbf{1 7 6 . 7} \mathbf{m m}^{2}$

Problem 13. Find the area of a circle having a circumference of 70 mm

Circumference, $c=2 \pi r$, hence
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { radius, } r & =\frac{c}{2 \pi}=\frac{70}{2 \pi}=\frac{35}{\pi} \mathrm{~mm} \\
\text { Area of circle } & =\pi r^{2}=\pi\left(\frac{35}{\pi}\right)^{2}=\frac{35^{2}}{\pi} \\
& =\mathbf{3 8 9 . 9} \mathbf{m m}^{2} \text { or } \mathbf{3 . 8 9 9} \mathbf{c m}^{\mathbf{2}}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 14. Calculate the area of the sector of a circle having radius 6 cm with angle subtended at centre $50^{\circ}$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area of sector } & =\frac{\theta^{2}}{360}\left(\pi r^{2}\right)=\frac{50}{360}\left(\pi 6^{2}\right) \\
& =\frac{50 \times \pi \times 36}{360}=\mathbf{1 5 . 7 1} \mathrm{cm}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 15. Calculate the area of the sector of a circle having diameter 80 mm with angle subtended at centre $107^{\circ} 42^{\prime}$

If diameter $=80 \mathrm{~mm}$ then radius, $r=40 \mathrm{~mm}$, and
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { area of sector } & =\frac{107^{\circ} 42^{\prime}}{360}\left(\pi 40^{2}\right)=\frac{107 \frac{42}{60}}{360}\left(\pi 40^{2}\right) \\
& =\frac{107.7}{360}\left(\pi 40^{2}\right) \\
& =\mathbf{1 5 0 4} \mathbf{m m}^{2} \text { or } \mathbf{1 5 . 0 4} \mathbf{c m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 16. A hollow shaft has an outside diameter of 5.45 cm and an inside diameter of 2.25 cm . Calculate the cross-sectional area of the shaft

The cross-sectional area of the shaft is shown by the shaded part in Figure 25.22 (often called an annulus).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-239.jpg?height=310&width=193&top_left_y=1076&top_left_x=1395)

Figure 25.22

Area of shaded part $=$ area of large circle - area of small circle
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{\pi D^{2}}{4}-\frac{\pi d^{2}}{4}=\frac{\pi}{4}\left(D^{2}-d^{2}\right) \\
& =\frac{\pi}{4}\left(5.45^{2}-2.25^{2}\right) \\
& =\mathbf{1 9 . 3 5} \mathbf{c m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 17. Calculate the area of a regular octagon if each side is 5 cm and the width across the flats is 12 cm

An octagon is an 8 -sided polygon. If radii are drawn from the centre of the polygon to the vertices then 8 equal triangles are produced, as shown in Figure 25.25.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-240.jpg?height=318&width=326&top_left_y=676&top_left_x=1307)

Figure 25.25
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area of one triangle } & =\frac{1}{2} \times \text { base } \times \text { height } \\
& =\frac{1}{2} \times 5 \times \frac{12}{2}=15 \mathrm{~cm}^{2} \\
\text { Area of octagon } & =8 \times 15=\mathbf{1 2 0} \mathbf{c m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 18. Determine the area of a regular hexagon which has sides 8 cm long

A hexagon is a 6 -sided polygon which may be divided into 6 equal triangles as shown in Figure 25.26. The angle subtended at the centre of each triangle is $360^{\circ} \div$ $6=60^{\circ}$. The other two angles in the triangle add up to $120^{\circ}$ and are equal to each other. Hence, each of the triangles is equilateral with each angle $60^{\circ}$ and each side 8 cm .
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-240.jpg?height=299&width=240&top_left_y=1865&top_left_x=1347)

Figure 25.26
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area of one triangle } & =\frac{1}{2} \times \text { base } \times \text { height } \\
& =\frac{1}{2} \times 8 \times h
\end{aligned}$$
$h$ is calculated using Pythagoras' theorem:
$$\begin{aligned}
8^{2} & =h^{2}+4^{2} \\
\text { from which } \quad h & =\sqrt{8^{2}-4^{2}}=6.928 \mathrm{~cm}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence,
Area of one triangle $=\frac{1}{2} \times 8 \times 6.928=27.71 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area of hexagon } & =6 \times 27.71 \\
& =\mathbf{1 6 6 . 3} \mathbf{c m}^{\mathbf{2}}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 19. Figure 25.27 shows a plan of a floor of a building which is to be carpeted. Calculate the area of the floor in square metres. Calculate the cost, correct to the nearest pound, of carpeting the floor with carpet costing $Â£ 16.80$ per m$^{2}$, assuming $30 \%$ extra carpet is required due to wastage in fitting
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-241.jpg?height=538&width=514&top_left_y=1106&top_left_x=394)

Figure 25.27

Area of floor plan
$$\begin{aligned}
= & \text { area of triangle } A B C+\text { area of semicircle } \\
& + \text { area of rectangle } C G L M \\
& + \text { area of rectangle } C D E F \\
& - \text { area of trapezium } H I J K
\end{aligned}$$

Triangle $A B C$ is equilateral since $A B=B C=3 \mathrm{~m}$ and, hence, angle $B^{\prime} C B=60^{\circ}$.
$$\sin B^{\prime} C B=B B^{\prime} / 3$$
$$\begin{aligned}
& \qquad \begin{aligned}
B B^{\prime} & =3 \sin 60^{\circ}=2.598 \mathrm{~m} . \\
\text { Area of triangle } A B C & =\frac{1}{2}(A C)\left(B B^{\prime}\right) \\
& =\frac{1}{2}(3)(2.598)=3.897 \mathrm{~m}^{2}
\end{aligned}
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area of semicircle } & =\frac{1}{2} \pi r^{2}=\frac{1}{2} \pi(2.5)^{2} \\
& =9.817 \mathrm{~m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area of } C G L M & =5 \times 7=35 \mathrm{~m}^{2} \\
\text { Area of } C D E F & =0.8 \times 3=2.4 \mathrm{~m}^{2} \\
\text { Area of } H I J K & =\frac{1}{2}(K H+I J)(0.8)
\end{aligned}$$

Since $M C=7 \mathrm{~m}$ then $L G=7 \mathrm{~m}$, hence $J I=7-5.2=1.8 \mathrm{~m}$. Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area of HIJK } & =\frac{1}{2}(3+1.8)(0.8)=1.92 \mathrm{~m}^{2} \\
\text { Total floor area } & =3.897+9.817+35+2.4-1.92 \\
& =49.194 \mathrm{~m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

To allow for $30 \%$ wastage, amount of carpet required $=1.3 \times 49.194=63.95 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$
Cost of carpet at $Â£ \mathbf{1 6 . 8 0}$ per m ${ }^{2}$
$=63.95 \times 16.80=Â£ \mathbf{1 0 7 4}$, correct to the nearest pound.

\subsection*{25.4 Areas of similar shapes}

Figure 25.29 shows two squares, one of which has sides three times as long as the other.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-242.jpg?height=351&width=500&top_left_y=871&top_left_x=382)

Figure 25.29
Area of Figure 25.29(a) $=(x)(x)=x^{2}$
Area of Figure 25.29(b) $=(3 x)(3 x)=9 x^{2}$
Hence, Figure 25.29(b) has an area (3) ${ }^{2}$; i.e., 9 times the area of Figure 25.29(a).
In summary, the areas of similar shapes are proportional to the squares of corresponding linear dimensions.

Problem 20. A rectangular garage is shown on a building plan having dimensions 10 mm by 20 mm . If the plan is drawn to a scale of 1 to 250 , determine the true area of the garage in square metres
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area of garage on the plan } & =10 \mathrm{~mm} \times 20 \mathrm{~mm} \\
& =200 \mathrm{~mm}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Since the areas of similar shapes are proportional to the squares of corresponding dimensions,
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { True area of garage } & =200 \times(250)^{2} \\
& =12.5 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{~mm}^{2} \\
& =\frac{12.5 \times 10^{6}}{10^{6}} \mathrm{~m}^{2} \\
& \text { since } 1 \mathrm{~m}^{2}=10^{6} \mathrm{~mm}^{2} \\
& =\mathbf{1 2 . 5} \mathbf{m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{Chapter 26}

\subsection*{26.1 Introduction}

A circle is a plain figure enclosed by a curved line, every point on which is equidistant from a point within, called the centre.

In Chapter 25, worked problems on the areas of circles and sectors were demonstrated. In this chapter, properties of circles are listed and arc lengths are calculated, together with more practical examples on the areas of sectors of circles. Finally, the equation of a circle is explained.

\subsection*{26.2 Properties of circles}
(a) The distance from the centre to the curve is called the radius, $r$, of the circle (see $O P$ in Figure 26.1).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-243.jpg?height=297&width=283&top_left_y=1663&top_left_x=515)

Figure 26.1
(b) The boundary of a circle is called the circumference, $c$.
(c) Any straight line passing through the centre and touching the circumference at each end is called the diameter, $\boldsymbol{d}$ (see $Q R$ in Figure 26.1). Thus, $d=2 r$.
(d) The ratio $\frac{\text { circumference }}{\text { diameter }}$ is a constant for any circle. This constant is denoted by the Greek letter $\pi$

\section*{The circle}
(pronounced 'pie'), where $\pi=3.14159$, correct to 5 decimal places (check with your calculator). Hence, $\frac{c}{d}=\pi$ or $\boldsymbol{c}=\boldsymbol{\pi} \boldsymbol{d}$ or $\boldsymbol{c}=\mathbf{2} \boldsymbol{\pi} \boldsymbol{r}$.
(e) A semicircle is one half of a whole circle.
(f) A quadrant is one quarter of a whole circle.
(g) A tangent to a circle is a straight line which meets the circle at one point only and does not cut the circle when produced. $A C$ in Figure 26.1 is a tangent to the circle since it touches the curve at point $B$ only. If radius $O B$ is drawn, angle $A B O$ is a right angle.
(h) The sector of a circle is the part of a circle between radii (for example, the portion $O X Y$ of Figure 26.2 is a sector). If a sector is less than a semicircle it is called a minor sector; if greater than a semicircle it is called a major sector.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-243.jpg?height=281&width=264&top_left_y=1698&top_left_x=1359)

Figure 26.2
(i) The chord of a circle is any straight line which divides the circle into two parts and is terminated at each end by the circumference. $S T$, in Figure 26.2, is a chord.
(j) Segment is the name given to the parts into which a circle is divided by a chord. If the segment is less than a semicircle it is called a minor segment (see shaded area in Figure 26.2). If the segment
is greater than a semicircle it is called a major segment (see the un-shaded area in Figure 26.2).
(k) An arc is a portion of the circumference of a circle. The distance $S R T$ in Figure 26.2 is called a minor arc and the distance $S X Y T$ is called a major arc.
(1) The angle at the centre of a circle, subtended by an arc, is double the angle at the circumference subtended by the same arc. With reference to Figure 26.3,
$$\text { Angle } A O C=2 \times \text { angle } A B C$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-244.jpg?height=218&width=226&top_left_y=892&top_left_x=522)

Figure 26.3
(m) The angle in a semicircle is a right angle (see angle $B Q P$ in Figure 26.3).

Problem 1. Find the circumference of a circle of radius 12.0 cm

Circumference, $c=2 \times \pi \times$ radius $=2 \pi r=2 \pi(12.0)$
$$=75.40 \mathrm{~cm}$$

Problem 2. If the diameter of a circle is 75 mm , find its circumference

Circumference, $c=\pi \times$ diameter $=\pi d=\pi(75)$
$$=235.6 \mathrm{~mm}$$

Problem 3. Determine the radius of a circular pond if its perimeter is 112 m

Perimeter $=$ circumference, $c=2 \pi r$
Hence, radius of pond, $r=\frac{c}{2 \pi}=\frac{112}{2 \pi}=\mathbf{1 7 . 8 3} \mathbf{~ c m}$

Problem 4. In Figure 26.4, $A B$ is a tangent to the circle at $B$. If the circle radius is 40 mm and $A B=150 \mathrm{~mm}$, calculate the length $A O$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-244.jpg?height=178&width=383&top_left_y=334&top_left_x=1273)

Figure 26.4

A tangent to a circle is at right angles to a radius drawn from the point of contact; i.e., $\boldsymbol{A B O}=\mathbf{9 0}^{\circ}$. Hence, using Pythagoras' theorem,
$$\begin{aligned}
A O^{2} & =A B^{2}+O B^{2} \\
\text { from which, } \quad \boldsymbol{A O} & =\sqrt{A B^{2}+O B^{2}} \\
& =\sqrt{150^{2}+40^{2}}=\mathbf{1 5 5 . 2} \mathbf{m m}
\end{aligned}$$

Figure 26.6

\subsection*{26.3 Radians and degrees}

One radian is defined as the angle subtended at the centre of a circle by an arc equal in length to the radius. With reference to Figure 26.7, for arc length $s$,
$$\theta \text { radians }=\frac{s}{r}$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-245.jpg?height=248&width=256&top_left_y=1492&top_left_x=528)

Figure 26.7
When $s=$ whole circumference $(=2 \pi r)$ then
$$\theta=\frac{s}{r}=\frac{2 \pi r}{r}=2 \pi$$
i.e. $\mathbf{2 \pi}$ radians $=\mathbf{3 6 0}^{\circ}$ or $\boldsymbol{\pi}$ radians $=180^{\circ}$

Thus, $1 \mathbf{r a d}=\frac{\mathbf{1 8 0}^{\circ}}{\boldsymbol{\pi}}=\mathbf{5 7 . 3 0}^{\circ}$, correct to 2 decimal places.
Since $\pi \mathrm{rad}=180^{\circ}$, then $\frac{\pi}{2}=90^{\circ}, \frac{\pi}{3}=60^{\circ}, \frac{\pi}{4}=45^{\circ}$, and so on.

Problem 5. Convert to radians: (a) $125^{\circ}$
(b) $69^{\circ} 47^{\prime}$
(a) Since $180^{\circ}=\pi \mathrm{rad}, 1^{\circ}=\frac{180}{\pi} \mathrm{rad}$, therefore
$$125^{\circ}=125\left(\frac{\pi}{180}\right) \mathrm{rad}=\mathbf{2 . 1 8 2} \text { radians. }$$
(b) $69^{\circ} 47^{\prime}=69 \frac{47^{\circ}}{60}=69.783^{\circ}$ (or, with your calculator, enter $69^{\circ} 47^{\prime}$ using ${ }^{\circ},, '$ function, press $=$ and press ${ }^{\circ}$, ,' again).
and $69.783^{\circ}=69.783\left(\frac{\pi}{180}\right) \mathrm{rad}$
$$=1.218 \text { radians }$$

Problem 6. Convert to degrees and minutes:
(a) 0.749 radians (b) $3 \pi / 4$ radians
(a) Since $\pi \mathrm{rad}=180^{\circ}, 1 \mathrm{rad}=\frac{180^{\circ}}{\pi}$
therefore $\quad 0.749 \mathrm{rad}=0.749\left(\frac{180}{\pi}\right)^{\circ}=42.915^{\circ}$ $0.915^{\circ}=(0.915 \times 60)^{\prime}=55^{\prime}$, correct to the nearest minute,
Hence, $\mathbf{0 . 7 4 9}$ radians $=\mathbf{4 2}^{\circ} \mathbf{5 5}^{\prime}$
(b) Since $1 \mathrm{rad}=\left(\frac{180}{\pi}\right)^{0}$ then
$$\frac{3 \pi}{4} \mathrm{rad}=\frac{3 \pi}{4}\left(\frac{180}{\pi}\right)^{\circ}=\frac{3}{4}(180)^{\circ}=\mathbf{1 3 5}^{\circ}$$

Problem 7. Express in radians, in terms of $\pi$,
(a) $150^{\circ}$
(b) $270^{\circ}$
(c) $37.5^{\circ}$

Since $180^{\circ}=\pi \mathrm{rad}, 1^{\circ}=\frac{\pi}{180} \mathrm{rad}$
(a) $150^{\circ}=150\left(\frac{\pi}{180}\right) \mathrm{rad}=\frac{5 \pi}{\mathbf{6}} \mathbf{r a d}$
(b) $270^{\circ}=270\left(\frac{\pi}{180}\right) \mathrm{rad}=\frac{\mathbf{3 \pi}}{\mathbf{2}} \mathbf{r a d}$
(c) $37.5^{\circ}=37.5\left(\frac{\pi}{180}\right) \mathrm{rad}=\frac{75 \pi}{360} \mathrm{rad}=\frac{\mathbf{5 \pi}}{\mathbf{2 4}} \mathbf{r a d}$
