\subsection*{26.4 Arc length and area of circles and sectors}

\subsection*{26.4.1 Arc length}

From the definition of the radian in the previous section and Figure 26.7,
arc length, $\boldsymbol{s}=\boldsymbol{r} \boldsymbol{\theta} \quad$ where $\theta$ is in radians

\subsection*{26.4.2 Area of a circle}

From Chapter 25, for any circle, area $=\pi \times(\text { radius })^{2}$ i.e.
$$\text { area }=\pi r^{2}$$

Since $r=\frac{d}{2}$,
area $=\pi r^{2}$ or $\frac{\pi d^{2}}{4}$

\subsection*{26.4.3 Area of a sector}
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area of a sector } & =\frac{\boldsymbol{\theta}}{\mathbf{3 6 0}}\left(\boldsymbol{\pi} \boldsymbol{r}^{\mathbf{2}}\right) \text { when } \theta \text { is in degrees } \\
& =\frac{\theta}{2 \pi}\left(\pi r^{2}\right) \\
& =\frac{\mathbf{1}}{\mathbf{2}} \boldsymbol{r}^{\mathbf{2}} \boldsymbol{\theta} \quad \text { when } \theta \text { is in radians }
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 8. A hockey pitch has a semicircle of radius 14.63 m around each goal net. Find the area enclosed by the semicircle, correct to the nearest square metre

Area of a semicircle $=\frac{1}{2} \pi r^{2}$
When $r=14.63 \mathrm{~m}, \quad$ area $=\frac{1}{2} \pi(14.63)^{2}$ i.e. area of semicircle $=\mathbf{3 3 6} \mathbf{m}^{\mathbf{2}}$

Problem 9. Find the area of a circular metal plate having a diameter of 35.0 mm , correct to the nearest square millimetre

Area of a circle $=\pi r^{2}=\frac{\pi d^{2}}{4}$
When $d=35.0 \mathrm{~mm}, \quad$ area $=\frac{\pi(35.0)^{2}}{4}$
i.e. area of circular plate $=962 \mathbf{~ m m}^{2}$

Problem 10. Find the area of a circle having a circumference of 60.0 mm

Circumference, $c=2 \pi r$
from which radius, $r=\frac{c}{2 \pi}=\frac{60.0}{2 \pi}=\frac{30.0}{\pi}$
Area of a circle $=\pi r^{2}$
i.e. $\quad$ area $=\pi\left(\frac{30.0}{\pi}\right)^{2}=\mathbf{2 8 6 . 5} \mathbf{m m}^{2}$

Problem 11. Find the length of the arc of a circle of radius 5.5 cm when the angle subtended at the centre is 1.20 radians

Length of arc, $s=r \theta$, where $\theta$ is in radians.
Hence, arc length, $s=(5.5)(1.20)=\mathbf{6 . 6 0} \mathbf{c m}$.

Problem 12. Determine the diameter and circumference of a circle if an arc of length 4.75 cm subtends an angle of 0.91 radians

Since arc length, $s=r \theta$ then
radius, $r=\frac{s}{\theta}=\frac{4.75}{0.91}=5.22 \mathrm{~cm}$
Diameter $=2 \times$ radius $=2 \times 5.22=\mathbf{1 0 . 4 4} \mathbf{c m}$
Circumference, $c=\pi d=\pi(10.44)=\mathbf{3 2 . 8 0} \mathbf{~ c m}$

Problem 13. If an angle of $125^{\circ}$ is subtended by an arc of a circle of radius 8.4 cm , find the length of (a) the minor arc and (b) the major arc, correct to 3 significant figures

Since $180^{\circ}=\pi \mathrm{rad}$ then $1^{\circ}=\left(\frac{\pi}{180}\right) \mathrm{rad}$ and $125^{\circ}=125\left(\frac{\pi}{180}\right) \mathrm{rad}$
(a) Length of minor arc,
$$s=r \theta=(8.4)(125)\left(\frac{\pi}{180}\right)=\mathbf{1 8 . 3} \mathbf{c m}$$
correct to 3 significant figures
(b) Length of major arc $=$ (circumference - minor $\operatorname{arc})=2 \pi(8.4)-18.3=\mathbf{3 4 . 5} \mathbf{c m}$, correct to 3 significant figures.
(Alternatively, major arc $=r \theta$
$=8.4(360-125)\left(\frac{\pi}{180}\right)=\mathbf{3 4 . 5} \mathbf{c m}$.)
Problem 14. Determine the angle, in degrees and minutes, subtended at the centre of a circle of diameter 42 mm by an arc of length 36 mm .
Calculate also the area of the minor sector formed
Since length of arc, $s=r \theta$ then $\theta=\frac{s}{r}$
Radius, $\quad r=\frac{\text { diameter }}{2}=\frac{42}{2}=21 \mathrm{~mm}$, hence $\quad \theta=\frac{s}{r}=\frac{36}{21}=1.7143$ radians.
$1.7143 \mathrm{rad}=1.7143 \times\left(\frac{180}{\pi}\right)^{\circ}=98.22^{\circ}=\mathbf{9 8}^{\circ} \mathbf{1 3}{ }^{\prime}=$ angle subtended at centre of circle.
From equation (2),
$$\text { area of sector } \begin{aligned}
=\frac{1}{2} r^{2} \theta & =\frac{1}{2}(21)^{2}(1.7143) \\
& =\mathbf{3 7 8} \mathbf{m m}^{\mathbf{2}}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 15. A football stadium floodlight can spread its illumination over an angle of $45^{\circ}$ to a distance of 55 m . Determine the maximum area that is floodlit.
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Floodlit area }=\text { area of sector } & =\frac{1}{2} r^{2} \theta \\
& =\frac{1}{2}(55)^{2}\left(45 \times \frac{\pi}{180}\right) \\
& =\mathbf{1 1 8 8} \mathbf{m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 16. An automatic garden sprayer produces spray to a distance of 1.8 m and revolves through an angle $\alpha$ which may be varied. If the desired spray catchment area is to be $2.5 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$, to what should angle $\alpha$ be set, correct to the nearest degree?

Area of sector $=\frac{1}{2} r^{2} \theta$, hence $2.5=\frac{1}{2}(1.8)^{2} \alpha$ from which, $\quad \alpha=\frac{2.5 \times 2}{1.8^{2}}=1.5432$ radians
$$1.5432 \mathrm{rad}=\left(1.5432 \times \frac{180}{\pi}\right)^{\circ}=88.42^{\circ}$$

Hence, angle $\boldsymbol{\alpha}=\mathbf{8 8}^{\circ}$, correct to the nearest degree.

Problem 17. The angle of a tapered groove is checked using a 20 mm diameter roller as shown in Figure 26.8. If the roller lies 2.12 mm below the top of the groove, determine the value of angle $\theta$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-247.jpg?height=411&width=768&top_left_y=516&top_left_x=1113)

Figure 26.8
In Figure 26.9, triangle $A B C$ is right-angled at $C$ (see property (g) in Section 26.2).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-247.jpg?height=427&width=765&top_left_y=1148&top_left_x=1109)

Figure 26.9
Length $B C=10 \mathrm{~mm}$ (i.e. the radius of the circle), and $A B=30-10-2.12=17.88 \mathrm{~mm}$, from Figure 26.9. Hence, $\sin \frac{\theta}{2}=\frac{10}{17.88}$ and $\frac{\theta}{2}=\sin ^{-1}\left(\frac{10}{17.88}\right)=34^{\circ}$ and angle $\boldsymbol{\theta}=\mathbf{6 8}^{\circ}$.

9. Find the area of the shaded portion of Figure 26.11.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-248.jpg?height=392&width=367&top_left_y=1968&top_left_x=446)

Figure 26.11
10. Find the length of an arc of a circle of radius 8.32 cm when the angle subtended at the centre is 2.14 radians. Calculate also the area of the minor sector formed.

\subsection*{26.5 The equation of a circle}

The simplest equation of a circle, centre at the origin and radius $r$, is given by
$$x^{2}+y^{2}=r^{2}$$

For example, Figure 26.15 shows a circle $x^{2}+y^{2}=9$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-249.jpg?height=394&width=484&top_left_y=2005&top_left_x=414)

Figure 26.15

More generally, the equation of a circle, centre $(a, b)$ and radius $r$, is given by
$$(x-a)^{2}+(y-b)^{2}=r^{2}$$

Figure 26.16 shows a circle $(x-2)^{2}+(y-3)^{2}=4$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-249.jpg?height=446&width=405&top_left_y=580&top_left_x=1289)

Figure 26.16
The general equation of a circle is
$$x^{2}+y^{2}+2 e x+2 f y+c=0$$

Multiplying out the bracketed terms in equation (1) gives
$$x^{2}-2 a x+a^{2}+y^{2}-2 b y+b^{2}=r^{2}$$

Comparing this with equation (2) gives
and
$$\begin{aligned}
2 e & =-2 a, \text { i.e. } \boldsymbol{a}=-\frac{\mathbf{2 e}}{\mathbf{2}} \\
2 f & =-2 b, \text { i.e. } \boldsymbol{b}=-\frac{\mathbf{2 f}}{\mathbf{2}}
\end{aligned}$$
and $\quad c=a^{2}+b^{2}-r^{2}$, i.e. $\boldsymbol{r}=\sqrt{\boldsymbol{a}^{2}+\boldsymbol{b}^{\mathbf{2}}-\boldsymbol{c}}$
Thus, for example, the equation
$$x^{2}+y^{2}-4 x-6 y+9=0$$
represents a circle with centre,
$$a=-\left(\frac{-4}{2}\right), b=-\left(\frac{-6}{2}\right) \text { i.e., at }(2,3) \text { and }$$
radius, $r=\sqrt{2^{2}+3^{2}-9}=2$
Hence, $x^{2}+y^{2}-4 x-6 y+9=0$ is the circle shown in Figure 26.16 (which may be checked by multiplying out the brackets in the equation $\left.(x-2)^{2}+(y-3)^{2}=4\right)$.

Problem 18. Determine (a) the radius and (b) the co-ordinates of the centre of the circle given by the equation $x^{2}+y^{2}+8 x-2 y+8=0$
$x^{2}+y^{2}+8 x-2 y+8=0$ is of the form shown in equation (2),
where $\quad a=-\left(\frac{8}{2}\right)=-4, b=-\left(\frac{-2}{2}\right)=1$
and
$$r=\sqrt{(-4)^{2}+1^{2}-8}=\sqrt{9}=3$$

Hence, $x^{2}+y^{2}+8 x-2 y+8=0$ represents a circle centre $(\mathbf{- 4 , 1})$ and radius 3 , as shown in Figure 26.17.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-250.jpg?height=454&width=546&top_left_y=744&top_left_x=356)

Figure 26.17
Alternatively, $x^{2}+y^{2}+8 x-2 y+8=0$ may be rearranged as
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
(x+4)^{2}+(y-1)^{2}-9 & =0 \\
(x+4)^{2}+(y-1)^{2} & =3^{2}
\end{aligned}$$
which represents a circle, centre $(-4,1)$ and radius 3 , as stated above.

Problem 19. Sketch the circle given by the equation $x^{2}+y^{2}-4 x+6 y-3=0$

The equation of a circle, centre $(a, b)$, radius $r$ is given by
$$(x-a)^{2}+(y-b)^{2}=r^{2}$$

The general equation of a circle is $x^{2}+y^{2}+2 e x+2 f y+c=0$
From above $a=-\frac{2 e}{2}, b=-\frac{2 f}{2}$ and $r=\sqrt{a^{2}+b^{2}-c}$
Hence, if $x^{2}+y^{2}-4 x+6 y-3=0$
then $\quad a=-\left(\frac{-4}{2}\right)=2, b=-\left(\frac{6}{2}\right)=-3 \quad$ and $r=\sqrt{2^{2}+(-3)^{2}-(-3)}=\sqrt{16}=4$

Thus, the circle has centre $(\mathbf{2}, \mathbf{- 3})$ and radius 4 , as shown in Figure 26.18.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-250.jpg?height=630&width=544&top_left_y=458&top_left_x=1195)

Figure 26.18

Alternatively, $x^{2}+y^{2}-4 x+6 y-3=0$ may be rearranged as
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
(x-2)^{2}+(y+3)^{2}-3-13 & =0 \\
(x-2)^{2}+(y+3)^{2} & =4^{2}
\end{aligned}$$
which represents a circle, centre $(2,-3)$ and radius 4 , as stated above.

\section*{Chapter 27}

\section*{Volumes of common solids}

\subsection*{27.1 Introduction}

The volume of any solid is a measure of the space occupied by the solid. Volume is measured in cubic units such as $\mathrm{mm}^{3}, \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$ and $\mathrm{m}^{3}$.

This chapter deals with finding volumes of common solids; in engineering it is often important to be able to calculate volume or capacity to estimate, say, the amount of liquid, such as water, oil or petrol, in different shaped containers.

A prism is a solid with a constant cross-section and with two ends parallel. The shape of the end is used to describe the prism. For example, there are rectangular prisms (called cuboids), triangular prisms and circular prisms (called cylinders).

On completing this chapter you will be able to calculate the volumes and surface areas of rectangular and other prisms, cylinders, pyramids, cones and spheres, together with frusta of pyramids and cones. Volumes of similar shapes are also considered.

\subsection*{27.2 Volumes and surface areas of common shapes}

\subsection*{27.2.1 Cuboids or rectangular prisms}

A cuboid is a solid figure bounded by six rectangular faces; all angles are right angles and opposite faces are equal. A typical cuboid is shown in Figure 27.1 with length $l$, breadth $b$ and height $h$.
$$\text { Volume of cuboid }=l \times b \times h$$
and
$$\text { surface area }=2 b h+2 h l+2 l b=2(b h+h l+l b)$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-253.jpg?height=340&width=657&top_left_y=871&top_left_x=1160)

Figure 27.1

A cube is a square prism. If all the sides of a cube are $x$ then
$$\text { Volume }=x^{3} \text { and surface area }=6 x^{2}$$

Problem 1. A cuboid has dimensions of 12 cm by 4 cm by 3 cm . Determine (a) its volume and (b) its total surface area

The cuboid is similar to that in Figure 27.1, with $l=12 \mathrm{~cm}, b=4 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $h=3 \mathrm{~cm}$.
(a) Volume of cuboid $=l \times b \times h=12 \times 4 \times 3$
$$=144 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$$
(b) Surface area $=2(b h+h l+l b)$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =2(4 \times 3+3 \times 12+12 \times 4) \\
& =2(12+36+48) \\
& =2 \times 96=\mathbf{1 9 2} \mathbf{c m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 2. An oil tank is the shape of a cube, each edge being of length 1.5 m . Determine (a) the maximum capacity of the tank in $\mathrm{m}^{3}$ and litres and (b) its total surface area ignoring input and output orifices
(a) Volume of oil tank $=$ volume of cube
$$\begin{aligned}
& =1.5 \mathrm{~m} \times 1.5 \mathrm{~m} \times 1.5 \mathrm{~m} \\
& =1.5^{3} \mathrm{~m}^{3}=\mathbf{3 . 3 7 5} \mathrm{m}^{3}
\end{aligned}$$
$1 \mathrm{~m}^{3}=100 \mathrm{~cm} \times 100 \mathrm{~cm} \times 100 \mathrm{~cm}=10^{6} \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$.
Hence,
$$\text { volume of tank }=3.375 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$$

1 litre $=1000 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$, hence oil tank capacity
$$=\frac{3.375 \times 10^{6}}{1000} \text { litres }=\mathbf{3 3 7 5} \text { litres }$$
(b) Surface area of one side $=1.5 \mathrm{~m} \times 1.5 \mathrm{~m}$
$$=2.25 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$$

A cube has six identical sides, hence
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { total surface area of oil tank } & =6 \times 2.25 \\
& =13.5 \mathrm{~m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 3. A water tank is the shape of a rectangular prism having length 2 m , breadth 75 cm and height 500 mm . Determine the capacity of the tank in (a) $\mathrm{m}^{3}$ (b) $\mathrm{cm}^{3}$ (c) litres

Capacity means volume; when dealing with liquids, the word capacity is usually used.
The water tank is similar in shape to that in Figure 27.1, with $l=2 \mathrm{~m}, b=75 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $h=500 \mathrm{~mm}$.
(a) Capacity of water tank $=l \times b \times h$. To use this formula, all dimensions must be in the same units. Thus, $l=2 \mathrm{~m}, b=0.75 \mathrm{~m}$ and $h=0.5 \mathrm{~m}$ (since $1 \mathrm{~m}=100 \mathrm{~cm}=1000 \mathrm{~mm})$. Hence,
$$\text { capacity of tank }=2 \times 0.75 \times 0.5=\mathbf{0 . 7 5} \mathbf{m}^{\mathbf{3}}$$
(b) $1 \mathrm{~m}^{3}=1 \mathrm{~m} \times 1 \mathrm{~m} \times 1 \mathrm{~m}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =100 \mathrm{~cm} \times 100 \mathrm{~cm} \times 100 \mathrm{~cm} \\
& \text { i.e., } \mathbf{1 \mathbf { m } ^ { 3 } = \mathbf { 1 0 0 0 } \mathbf { 0 0 0 } = \mathbf { 1 0 } ^ { \mathbf { 6 } } \mathbf { c m } ^ { 3 } \text { . Hence, }} \begin{aligned}
\text { capacity }=0.75 \mathrm{~m}^{3}=0.75 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{~cm}^{3} \\
=\mathbf{7 5 0} \mathbf{0 0 0} \mathbf{c m}^{\mathbf{3}}
\end{aligned}
\end{aligned}$$
(c) 1 litre $=1000 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$. Hence,
$$750000 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}=\frac{750,000}{1000}=750 \text { litres }$$

\subsection*{27.2.2 Cylinders}

A cylinder is a circular prism. A cylinder of radius $r$ and height $h$ is shown in Figure 27.2.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-254.jpg?height=321&width=286&top_left_y=303&top_left_x=1321)

Figure 27.2
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Volume } & =\pi r^{2} h \\
\text { Curved surface area } & =2 \pi r h \\
\text { Total surface area } & =2 \pi r h+2 \pi r^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Total surface area means the curved surface area plus the area of the two circular ends.

Problem 4. A solid cylinder has a base diameter of 12 cm and a perpendicular height of 20 cm . Calculate (a) the volume and (b) the total surface area
$$\text { (a) } \begin{aligned}
\text { Volume } & =\pi r^{2} h=\pi \times\left(\frac{12}{2}\right)^{2} \times 20 \\
& =720 \pi=\mathbf{2 2 6 2} \mathbf{c m}^{\mathbf{3}}
\end{aligned}$$
(b) Total surface area
$$\begin{aligned}
& =2 \pi r h+2 \pi r^{2} \\
& =(2 \times \pi \times 6 \times 20)+\left(2 \times \pi \times 6^{2}\right) \\
& =240 \pi+72 \pi=312 \pi=\mathbf{9 8 0} \mathbf{c m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 5. A copper pipe has the dimensions shown in Figure 27.3. Calculate the volume of copper in the pipe, in cubic metres.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-254.jpg?height=454&width=733&top_left_y=1926&top_left_x=1106)

Figure 27.3

Outer diameter, $D=25 \mathrm{~cm}=0.25 \mathrm{~m}$ and inner diameter, $d=12 \mathrm{~cm}=0.12 \mathrm{~m}$.

Area of cross-section of copper
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{\pi D^{2}}{4}-\frac{\pi d^{2}}{4}=\frac{\pi(0.25)^{2}}{4}-\frac{\pi(0.12)^{2}}{4} \\
& =0.0491-0.0113=0.0378 \mathrm{~m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, volume of copper
$$\begin{aligned}
& =(\text { cross-sectional area }) \times \text { length of pipe } \\
& =0.0378 \times 2.5=\mathbf{0 . 0 9 4 5} \mathbf{m}^{\mathbf{3}}
\end{aligned}$$

\subsection*{27.2.3 More prisms}

A right-angled triangular prism is shown in Figure 27.4 with dimensions $b, h$ and $l$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-255.jpg?height=587&width=435&top_left_y=1152&top_left_x=436)

Figure 27.4
$$\text { Volume }=\frac{1}{2} b h l$$
and
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { surface area }= & \text { area of each end } \\
& + \text { area of three sides }
\end{aligned}$$

Notice that the volume is given by the area of the end (i.e. area of triangle $=\frac{1}{2} b h$ ) multiplied by the length $l$. In fact, the volume of any shaped prism is given by the area of an end multiplied by the length.

Problem 6. Determine the volume (in $\mathrm{cm}^{3}$ ) of the shape shown in Figure 27.5
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-255.jpg?height=359&width=321&top_left_y=352&top_left_x=1331)

Figure 27.5

The solid shown in Figure 27.5 is a triangular prism. The volume $V$ of any prism is given by $V=A h$, where $A$ is the cross-sectional area and $h$ is the perpendicular height. Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { volume }=\frac{1}{2} \times 16 \times 12 \times 40=3840 \mathrm{~mm}^{3} \\
&= \mathbf{3 . 8 4 0} \mathrm{cm}^{3} \\
&\left(\text { since } 1 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}=1000 \mathrm{~mm}^{3}\right)
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 7. Calculate the volume of the right-angled triangular prism shown in Figure 27.6. Also, determine its total surface area
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-255.jpg?height=717&width=593&top_left_y=1418&top_left_x=1195)

Figure 27.6

Volume of right-angled triangular prism
$$=\frac{1}{2} b h l=\frac{1}{2} \times 8 \times 6 \times 40$$
i.e. $\quad$ volume $=960 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$

Total surface area $=$ area of each end + area of three sides.

In triangle $A B C, \quad A C^{2}=A B^{2}+B C^{2}$
from which,
$$\begin{aligned}
A C=\sqrt{A B^{2}+B C^{2}} & =\sqrt{6^{2}+8^{2}} \\
& =10 \mathrm{~cm}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, total surface area
$$\begin{aligned}
& =2\left(\frac{1}{2} b h\right)+(A C \times 40)+(B C \times 40)+(A B \times 40) \\
& =(8 \times 6)+(10 \times 40)+(8 \times 40)+(6 \times 40) \\
& =48+400+320+240
\end{aligned}$$
i.e. total surface area $=1008 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$

Problem 8. Calculate the volume and total surface area of the solid prism shown in Figure 27.7
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-256.jpg?height=541&width=440&top_left_y=1199&top_left_x=412)

Figure 27.7

The solid shown in Figure 27.7 is a trapezoidal prism.

Volume of prism $=$ cross-sectional area $\times$ height
$$\begin{aligned}
=\frac{1}{2}(11+5) 4 \times 15 & =32 \times 15 \\
& =\mathbf{4 8 0} \mathbf{c m}^{3}
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{Surface area of prism}
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\text { sum of two trapeziums }+4 \text { rectangles } \\
& =(2 \times 32)+(5 \times 15)+(11 \times 15)+2(5 \times 15) \\
& =64+75+165+150=\mathbf{4 5 4} \mathbf{c m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

19. A cylinder is cast from a rectangular piece of alloy 5.20 cm by 6.50 cm by 19.33 cm . If the height of the cylinder is to be 52.0 cm , determine its diameter, correct to the nearest centimetre.
20. How much concrete is required for the construction of the path shown in Figure 27.8, if the path is 12 cm thick?
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-257.jpg?height=535&width=733&top_left_y=1826&top_left_x=290)

Figure 27.8

\subsection*{27.2.4 Pyramids}

\section*{Volume of any pyramid}
$$=\frac{1}{3} \times \text { area of base } \times \text { perpendicular height }$$

A square-based pyramid is shown in Figure 27.9 with base dimension $x$ by $x$ and the perpendicular height of the pyramid $h$. For the square-base pyramid shown,
$$\text { volume }=\frac{1}{3} x^{2} h$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-257.jpg?height=354&width=551&top_left_y=913&top_left_x=1213)

Figure 27.9

Problem 9. A square pyramid has a perpendicular height of 16 cm . If a side of the base is 6 cm , determine the volume of a pyramid

\section*{Volume of pyramid}
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{1}{3} \times \text { area of base } \times \text { perpendicular height } \\
& =\frac{1}{3} \times(6 \times 6) \times 16 \\
& =\mathbf{1 9 2} \mathbf{c m}^{3}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 10. Determine the volume and the total surface area of the square pyramid shown in Figure 27.10 if its perpendicular height is 12 cm .

\section*{Volume of pyramid}
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{1}{3}(\text { area of base }) \times \text { perpendicular height } \\
& =\frac{1}{3}(5 \times 5) \times 12 \\
& =\mathbf{1 0 0} \mathbf{c m}^{\mathbf{3}}
\end{aligned}$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-258.jpg?height=533&width=288&top_left_y=330&top_left_x=488)

Figure 27.10

The total surface area consists of a square base and 4 equal triangles.

Area of triangle $A D E$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{1}{2} \times \text { base } \times \text { perpendicular height } \\
& =\frac{1}{2} \times 5 \times A C
\end{aligned}$$

The length $A C$ may be calculated using Pythagoras ${ }^{\prime}$ theorem on triangle $A B C$, where $A B=12 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $B C=\frac{1}{2} \times 5=2.5 \mathrm{~cm}$.
$$A C=\sqrt{A B^{2}+B C^{2}}=\sqrt{12^{2}+2.5^{2}}=12.26 \mathrm{~cm}$$

Hence,
$$\text { area of triangle } A D E=\frac{1}{2} \times 5 \times 12.26=30.65 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$$

Total surface area of pyramid $=(5 \times 5)+4(30.65)$
$$=147.6 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$$

Problem 11. A rectangular prism of metal having dimensions of 5 cm by 6 cm by 18 cm is melted down and recast into a pyramid having a rectangular base measuring 6 cm by 10 cm . Calculate the perpendicular height of the pyramid, assuming no waste of metal

Volume of rectangular prism $=5 \times 6 \times 18=540 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$
Volume of pyramid
$$=\frac{1}{3} \times \text { area of base } \times \text { perpendicular height }$$

Hence, $\quad 540=\frac{1}{3} \times(6 \times 10) \times h$
from which, $\quad h=\frac{3 \times 540}{6 \times 10}=27 \mathrm{~cm}$
i.e. perpendicular height of pyramid $=27 \mathrm{~cm}$

\subsection*{27.2.5 Cones}

A cone is a circular-based pyramid. A cone of base radius $r$ and perpendicular height $h$ is shown in Figure 27.11.

Volume $=\frac{1}{3} \times$ area of base $\times$ perpendicular height
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-258.jpg?height=513&width=427&top_left_y=850&top_left_x=1251)

Figure 27.11
i.e.
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Volume } & =\frac{1}{3} \pi r^{2} h \\
\text { Curved surface area } & =\pi r l \\
\text { Total surface area } & =\pi r l+\pi r^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 12. Calculate the volume, in cubic centimetres, of a cone of radius 30 mm and perpendicular height 80 mm

Volume of cone $=\frac{1}{3} \pi r^{2} h=\frac{1}{3} \times \pi \times 30^{2} \times 80$
$$=75398.2236 \ldots \mathrm{~mm}^{3}$$
$1 \mathrm{~cm}=10 \mathrm{~mm}$ and
$1 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}=10 \mathrm{~mm} \times 10 \mathrm{~mm} \times 10 \mathrm{~mm}=10^{3} \mathrm{~mm}^{3}$, or
$$1 \mathrm{~mm}^{3}=10^{-3} \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$$

Hence, $75398.2236 \ldots \mathrm{~mm}^{3}$
$=75398.2236 \ldots \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$
i.e.,
$$\text { volume }=75.40 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$$

Alternatively, from the question, $r=30 \mathrm{~mm}=3 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $h=80 \mathrm{~mm}=8 \mathrm{~cm}$. Hence,
$$\text { volume }=\frac{1}{3} \pi r^{2} h=\frac{1}{3} \times \pi \times 3^{2} \times 8=75.40 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$$

Problem 13. Determine the volume and total surface area of a cone of radius 5 cm and perpendicular height 12 cm

The cone is shown in Figure 27.12.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-259.jpg?height=370&width=264&top_left_y=805&top_left_x=524)

Figure 27.12
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Volume of cone }=\frac{1}{3} \pi r^{2} h & =\frac{1}{3} \times \pi \times 5^{2} \times 12 \\
& =\mathbf{3 1 4 . 2} \mathbf{c m}^{\mathbf{3}}
\end{aligned}$$

Total surface area $=$ curved surface area + area of base
$$=\pi r l+\pi r^{2}$$

From Figure 27.12, slant height $l$ may be calculated using Pythagoras' theorem:
$$l=\sqrt{12^{2}+5^{2}}=13 \mathrm{~cm}$$

Hence, total surface area $=(\pi \times 5 \times 13)+\left(\pi \times 5^{2}\right)$
$$=282.7 \mathrm{~cm}^{2} .$$

\subsection*{27.2.6 Spheres}

For the sphere shown in Figure 27.13:
$$\text { Volume }=\frac{4}{3} \pi r^{3} \quad \text { and } \quad \text { surface area }=4 \pi r^{2}$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-259.jpg?height=253&width=259&top_left_y=2157&top_left_x=527)

Figure 27.13

Problem 14. Find the volume and surface area of a sphere of diameter 10 cm

Since diameter $=10 \mathrm{~cm}$, radius, $r=5 \mathrm{~cm}$.

Volume of sphere $=\frac{4}{3} \pi r^{3}=\frac{4}{3} \times \pi \times 5^{3}$
$$=523.6 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$$

Surface area of sphere $=4 \pi r^{2}=4 \times \pi \times 5^{2}$
$$=314.2 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$$

Problem 15. The surface area of a sphere is $201.1 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$. Find the diameter of the sphere and hence its volume

Surface area of sphere $=4 \pi r^{2}$.
Hence, $201.1 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}=4 \times \pi \times r^{2}$,
from which $\quad r^{2}=\frac{201.1}{4 \times \pi}=16.0$
and $\quad$ radius, $r=\sqrt{16.0}=4.0 \mathrm{~cm}$
from which, diameter $=2 \times r=2 \times 4.0=8.0 \mathrm{~cm}$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Volume of sphere }=\frac{4}{3} \pi r^{3} & =\frac{4}{3} \times \pi \times(4.0)^{3} \\
& =\mathbf{2 6 8 . 1} \mathbf{c m}^{\mathbf{3}}
\end{aligned}$$

\subsection*{27.3 Summary of volumes and surface areas of common solids}

A summary of volumes and surface areas of regular solids is shown in Table 27.1.

Table 27.1 Volumes and surface areas of regular solids

\section*{Rectangular prism}
(or cuboid)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-260.jpg?height=224&width=735&top_left_y=2201&top_left_x=267)

Cylinder
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-260.jpg?height=232&width=201&top_left_y=399&top_left_x=1098)

Volume $=\pi r^{2} h$
Total surface area $=2 \pi r h+2 \pi r^{2}$

\section*{Triangular prism}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-260.jpg?height=416&width=320&top_left_y=706&top_left_x=1090)

Volume $=\frac{1}{2} b h l$
Surface area $=$ area of each end + area of three sides

\section*{Pyramid}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-260.jpg?height=215&width=318&top_left_y=1183&top_left_x=1099)

Volume $=\frac{1}{3} \times A \times h$ Total surface area $=$ sum of areas of triangles forming sides + area of base

Cone
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-260.jpg?height=229&width=191&top_left_y=1496&top_left_x=1100)

Curved surface area $=\pi r l$ Total surface area $=\pi r l+\pi r^{2}$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-260.jpg?height=185&width=199&top_left_y=1806&top_left_x=1094)

Volume $=\frac{4}{3} \pi r^{3}$
Surface area $=4 \pi r^{2}$

\subsection*{27.4 More complex volumes and surface areas}

Here are some worked problems involving more complex and composite solids.

Problem 16. A wooden section is shown in Figure 27.14. Find (a) its volume in $\mathrm{m}^{3}$ and (b) its total surface area
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-261.jpg?height=281&width=562&top_left_y=364&top_left_x=375)

Figure 27.14
(a) The section of wood is a prism whose end comprises a rectangle and a semicircle. Since the radius of the semicircle is 8 cm , the diameter is 16 cm . Hence, the rectangle has dimensions 12 cm by 16 cm .
$$\text { Area of end }=(12 \times 16)+\frac{1}{2} \pi 8^{2}=292.5 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$$

\section*{Volume of wooden section}
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\text { area of end } \times \text { perpendicular height } \\
& =292.5 \times 300=\mathbf{8 7 7 5 0} \mathrm{cm}^{3} \\
& =\frac{87750}{10^{6}} \mathrm{~m}^{3} \text {, since } 1 \mathrm{~m}^{3}=10^{6} \mathrm{~cm}^{3} \\
& =\mathbf{0 . 0 8 7 7 5} \mathbf{m}^{\mathbf{3}}
\end{aligned}$$
(b) The total surface area comprises the two ends (each of area $292.5 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$ ), three rectangles and a curved surface (which is half a cylinder). Hence,

\section*{total surface area}
$$\begin{aligned}
& =(2 \times 292.5)+2(12 \times 300) \\
& \quad+(16 \times 300)+\frac{1}{2}(2 \pi \times 8 \times 300) \\
& =585+7200+4800+2400 \pi \\
& =\mathbf{2 0} \mathbf{1 2 5} \mathbf{c m}^{2} \text { or } \mathbf{2 . 0 1 2 5} \mathbf{m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 17. A pyramid has a rectangular base 3.60 cm by 5.40 cm . Determine the volume and total surface area of the pyramid if each of its sloping edges is 15.0 cm

The pyramid is shown in Figure 27.15. To calculate the volume of the pyramid, the perpendicular height $E F$ is required. Diagonal $B D$ is calculated using Pythagoras' theorem,
i.e.
$$B D=\sqrt{\left[3.60^{2}+5.40^{2}\right]}=6.490 \mathrm{~cm}$$

Hence, $\quad E B=\frac{1}{2} B D=\frac{6.490}{2}=3.245 \mathrm{~cm}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-261.jpg?height=771&width=430&top_left_y=314&top_left_x=1282)

Figure 27.15
Using Pythagoras' theorem on triangle $B E F$ gives
$$B F^{2}=E B^{2}+E F^{2}$$
from which $\quad E F=\sqrt{\left(B F^{2}-E B^{2}\right)}$
$$=\sqrt{15.0^{2}-3.245^{2}}=14.64 \mathrm{~cm}$$

\section*{Volume of pyramid}
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{1}{3}(\text { area of base })(\text { perpendicular height }) \\
& =\frac{1}{3}(3.60 \times 5.40)(14.64)=\mathbf{9 4 . 8 7} \mathbf{c m}^{3}
\end{aligned}$$

Area of triangle $A D F$ (which equals triangle $B C F$ ) $=\frac{1}{2}(A D)(F G)$, where $G$ is the midpoint of $A D$. Using Pythagoras' theorem on triangle $F G A$ gives
$$F G=\sqrt{\left[15.0^{2}-1.80^{2}\right]}=14.89 \mathrm{~cm}$$

Hence, area of triangle $A D F=\frac{1}{2}(3.60)(14.89)$
$$=26.80 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$$

Similarly, if $H$ is the mid-point of $A B$,
$$F H=\sqrt{15.0^{2}-2.70^{2}}=14.75 \mathrm{~cm}$$

Hence, area of triangle $A B F$ (which equals triangle $C D F)=\frac{1}{2}(5.40)(14.75)=39.83 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$

\section*{Total surface area of pyramid}
$$\begin{aligned}
& =2(26.80)+2(39.83)+(3.60)(5.40) \\
& =53.60+79.66+19.44 \\
& =\mathbf{1 5 2 . 7} \mathbf{c m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 18. Calculate the volume and total surface area of a hemisphere of diameter 5.0 cm
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Volume of hemisphere }=\frac{1}{2}(\text { volume of sphere }) \\
& \\
& =\frac{2}{3} \pi r^{3}=\frac{2}{3} \pi\left(\frac{5.0}{2}\right)^{3} \\
& \\
& =\mathbf{3 2 . 7} \mathbf{c m}^{3}
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{Total surface area}
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\text { curved surface area }+ \text { area of circle } \\
& =\frac{1}{2}(\text { surface area of sphere })+\pi r^{2} \\
& =\frac{1}{2}\left(4 \pi r^{2}\right)+\pi r^{2} \\
& =2 \pi r^{2}+\pi r^{2}=3 \pi r^{2}=3 \pi\left(\frac{5.0}{2}\right)^{2} \\
& =\mathbf{5 8 . 9} \mathbf{c m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 19. A rectangular piece of metal having dimensions 4 cm by 3 cm by 12 cm is melted down and recast into a pyramid having a rectangular base measuring 2.5 cm by 5 cm . Calculate the perpendicular height of the pyramid
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Volume of rectangular prism of metal } & =4 \times 3 \times 12 \\
& =144 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}
\end{aligned}$$

Volume of pyramid
$$=\frac{1}{3}(\text { area of base })(\text { perpendicular height })$$

Assuming no waste of metal,
$$144=\frac{1}{3}(2.5 \times 5)(\text { height })$$
i.e. perpendicular height of pyramid $=\frac{144 \times 3}{2.5 \times 5}$
$$=34.56 \mathrm{~cm}$$

Problem 20. A rivet consists of a cylindrical head, of diameter 1 cm and depth 2 mm , and a shaft of diameter 2 mm and length 1.5 cm . Determine the volume of metal in 2000 such rivets

Radius of cylindrical head $=\frac{1}{2} \mathrm{~cm}=0.5 \mathrm{~cm}$ and height of cylindrical head $=2 \mathrm{~mm}=0.2 \mathrm{~cm}$.

Hence, volume of cylindrical head
$$=\pi r^{2} h=\pi(0.5)^{2}(0.2)=0.1571 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$$

Volume of cylindrical shaft
$$=\pi r^{2} h=\pi\left(\frac{0.2}{2}\right)^{2}(1.5)=0.0471 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$$

Total volume of 1 rivet $=0.1571+0.0471$
$$=0.2042 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$$

\section*{Volume of metal in 2000 such rivets}
$$=2000 \times 0.2042=408.4 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$$

Problem 21. A solid metal cylinder of radius 6 cm and height 15 cm is melted down and recast into a shape comprising a hemisphere surmounted by a cone. Assuming that $8 \%$ of the metal is wasted in the process, determine the height of the conical portion if its diameter is to be 12 cm

Volume of cylinder $=\pi r^{2} h=\pi \times 6^{2} \times 15$
$$=540 \pi \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$$

If $8 \%$ of metal is lost then $92 \%$ of $540 \pi$ gives the volume of the new shape, shown in Figure 27.16.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-262.jpg?height=462&width=177&top_left_y=1936&top_left_x=1376)

Figure 27.16

Hence, the volume of (hemisphere + cone )
$$=0.92 \times 540 \pi \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$$
i.e. $\quad \frac{1}{2}\left(\frac{4}{3} \pi r^{3}\right)+\frac{1}{3} \pi r^{2} h=0.92 \times 540 \pi$

Dividing throughout by $\pi$ gives
$$\frac{2}{3} r^{3}+\frac{1}{3} r^{2} h=0.92 \times 540$$

Since the diameter of the new shape is to be 12 cm , radius $r=6 \mathrm{~cm}$,
hence
$$\begin{aligned}
\frac{2}{3}(6)^{3}+\frac{1}{3}(6)^{2} h & =0.92 \times 540 \\
144+12 h & =496.8
\end{aligned}$$
i.e. height of conical portion,
$$\boldsymbol{h}=\frac{496.8-144}{12}=\mathbf{2 9 . 4} \mathrm{cm}$$

Problem 22. A block of copper having a mass of 50 kg is drawn out to make 500 m of wire of uniform cross-section. Given that the density of copper is $8.91 \mathrm{~g} / \mathrm{cm}^{3}$, calculate (a) the volume of copper, (b) the cross-sectional area of the wire and (c) the diameter of the cross-section of the wire
(a) A density of $8.91 \mathrm{~g} / \mathrm{cm}^{3}$ means that 8.91 g of copper has a volume of $1 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$, or 1 g of copper has a volume of $(1 \div 8.91) \mathrm{cm}^{3}$.
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Density } & =\frac{\text { mass }}{\text { volume }} \\
\text { from which } & \text { volume }
\end{aligned}=\frac{\text { mass }}{\text { density }}$$

Hence, 50 kg , i.e. 50000 g , has a
$$\text { volume }=\frac{\text { mass }}{\text { density }}=\frac{50000}{8.91} \mathrm{~cm}^{3}=\mathbf{5 6 1 2} \mathrm{cm}^{3}$$
(b) Volume of wire $=$ area of circular cross-section
$$\times \text { length of wire }$$

Hence, $5612 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}=$ area $\times(500 \times 100 \mathrm{~cm})$
$$\text { from which, } \begin{aligned}
\text { area } & =\frac{5612}{500 \times 100} \mathrm{~cm}^{2} \\
& =\mathbf{0 . 1 1 2 2} \mathrm{cm}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$
(c) Area of circle $=\pi r^{2}$ or $\frac{\pi d^{2}}{4}$ hence, $0.1122=\frac{\pi d^{2}}{4}$ from which, $d=\sqrt{\left(\frac{4 \times 0.1122}{\pi}\right)}=0.3780 \mathrm{~cm}$ i.e. diameter of cross-section is $\mathbf{3 . 7 8 0} \mathbf{~ m m}$.

Problem 23. A boiler consists of a cylindrical section of length 8 m and diameter 6 m , on one end of which is surmounted a hemispherical section of diameter 6 m and on the other end a conical section of height 4 m and base diameter 6 m . Calculate the volume of the boiler and the total surface area

The boiler is shown in Figure 27.17.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-263.jpg?height=497&width=281&top_left_y=1113&top_left_x=1351)

Figure 27.17
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Volume of hemisphere, } \begin{aligned}
P= & \frac{2}{3} \pi r^{3} \\
= & \frac{2}{3} \times \pi \times 3^{3}=18 \pi \mathrm{~m}^{3}
\end{aligned} \\
& \text { Volume of cylinder, } \begin{aligned}
Q=\pi r^{2} h & =\pi \times 3^{2} \times 8 \\
& =72 \pi \mathrm{~m}^{3}
\end{aligned} \\
& \text { Volume of cone, } \quad \begin{aligned}
R=\frac{1}{3} \pi r^{2} h & =\frac{1}{3} \times \pi \times 3^{2} \times 4 \\
& =12 \pi \mathrm{~m}^{3}
\end{aligned}
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Total volume of boiler } & =18 \pi+72 \pi+12 \pi \\
& =102 \pi=\mathbf{3 2 0 . 4} \mathbf{m}^{\mathbf{3}}
\end{aligned}$$

Surface area of hemisphere, $P=\frac{1}{2}\left(4 \pi r^{2}\right)$
$$=2 \times \pi \times 3^{2}=18 \pi \mathrm{~m}^{2}$$

Curved surface area of cylinder,
$$\begin{aligned}
Q & =2 \pi r h \\
& =2 \times \pi \times 3 \times 8 \\
& =48 \pi \mathrm{~m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

The slant height of the cone, $l$, is obtained by Pythagoras' theorem on triangle $A B C$, i.e.
$$l=\sqrt{\left(4^{2}+3^{2}\right)}=5$$

Curved surface area of cone,
$$R=\pi r l=\pi \times 3 \times 5=15 \pi \mathrm{~m}^{2}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Total surface area of boiler } & =18 \pi+48 \pi+15 \pi \\
& =81 \pi=\mathbf{2 5 4 . 5} \mathbf{m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

(a) the diameter of the metal sphere.
(b) the perpendicular height of the cone, assuming that $15 \%$ of the metal is lost in the process.

10. A petrol container is in the form of a central cylindrical portion 5.0 m long with a hemispherical section surmounted on each end. If the diameters of the hemisphere and cylinder are both 1.2 m , determine the capacity of the tank in litres ( 1 litre $=1000 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$ ).
11. Figure 27.18 shows a metal rod section. Determine its volume and total surface area.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-264.jpg?height=397&width=397&top_left_y=1963&top_left_x=1269)

Figure 27.18
12. Find the volume (in $\mathrm{cm}^{3}$ ) of the die-casting shown in Figure 27.19. The dimensions are in millimetres.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-265.jpg?height=487&width=492&top_left_y=494&top_left_x=410)

Figure 27.19
13. The cross-section of part of a circular ventilation shaft is shown in Figure 27.20, ends $A B$ and $C D$ being open. Calculate
(a) the volume of the air, correct to the nearest litre, contained in the part of the system shown, neglecting the sheet metal thickness (given 1litre $=1000 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$ ).
(b) the cross-sectional area of the sheet metal used to make the system, in square metres.
(c) the cost of the sheet metal if the material costs $Â£ 11.50$ per square metre, assuming that $25 \%$ extra metal is required due to wastage.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-265.jpg?height=616&width=582&top_left_y=1753&top_left_x=368)

\subsection*{27.5 Volumes and surface areas of frusta of pyramids and cones}

The frustum of a pyramid or cone is the portion remaining when a part containing the vertex is cut off by a plane parallel to the base.
The volume of a frustum of a pyramid or cone is given by the volume of the whole pyramid or cone minus the volume of the small pyramid or cone cut off.
The surface area of the sides of a frustum of a pyramid or cone is given by the surface area of the whole pyramid or cone minus the surface area of the small pyramid or cone cut off. This gives the lateral surface area of the frustum. If the total surface area of the frustum is required then the surface area of the two parallel ends are added to the lateral surface area.
There is an alternative method for finding the volume and surface area of a frustum of a cone. With reference to Figure 27.21,
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-265.jpg?height=259&width=285&top_left_y=1183&top_left_x=1349)

Figure 27.21
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Volume } & =\frac{1}{3} \pi h\left(R^{2}+R r+r^{2}\right) \\
\text { Curved surface area } & =\pi l(R+r) \\
\text { Total surface area } & =\pi l(R+r)+\pi r^{2}+\pi R^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 24. Determine the volume of a frustum of a cone if the diameter of the ends are 6.0 cm and 4.0 cm and its perpendicular height is 3.6 cm
(i) Method 1

A section through the vertex of a complete cone is shown in Figure 27.22.
Using similar triangles, $\quad \frac{A P}{D P}=\frac{D R}{B R}$

Hence,
$$\frac{A P}{2.0}=\frac{3.6}{1.0}$$
from which
$$\begin{aligned}
A P & =\frac{(2.0)(3.6)}{1.0} \\
& =7.2 \mathrm{~cm}
\end{aligned}$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-266.jpg?height=1026&width=657&top_left_y=314&top_left_x=303)

Figure 27.22
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { The height of the large cone } & =3.6+7.2 \\
& =10.8 \mathrm{~cm}
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{Volume of frustum of cone}
$$\begin{aligned}
= & \text { volume of large cone } \\
& - \text { volume of small cone cut off } \\
= & \frac{1}{3} \pi(3.0)^{2}(10.8)-\frac{1}{3} \pi(2.0)^{2}(7.2) \\
= & 101.79-30.16=71.6 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}
\end{aligned}$$
(ii) Method 2

From above, volume of the frustum of a cone
where
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{1}{3} \pi h\left(R^{2}+R r+r^{2}\right) \\
R & =3.0 \mathrm{~cm}, \\
r & =2.0 \mathrm{~cm} \text { and } h=3.6 \mathrm{~cm}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, volume of frustum
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{1}{3} \pi(3.6)\left[(3.0)^{2}+(3.0)(2.0)+(2.0)^{2}\right] \\
& =\frac{1}{3} \pi(3.6)(19.0)=71.6 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 25. Find the total surface area of the frustum of the cone in Problem 24.
(i) Method 1

Curved surface area of frustum = curved surface area of large cone - curved surface area of small cone cut off.
From Figure 27.22, using Pythagoras' theorem,
$$A B^{2}=A Q^{2}+B Q^{2}$$
from which $A B=\sqrt{\left[10.8^{2}+3.0^{2}\right]}=11.21 \mathrm{~cm}$ and
$$A D^{2}=A P^{2}+D P^{2}$$
from which $A D=\sqrt{\left[7.2^{2}+2.0^{2}\right]}=7.47 \mathrm{~cm}$
Curved surface area of large cone $=\pi r l$
$$\begin{aligned}
=\pi(B Q)(A B) & =\pi(3.0)(11.21) \\
& =105.65 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$
and curved surface area of small cone
$$=\pi(D P)(A D)=\pi(2.0)(7.47)=46.94 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$$

Hence, curved surface area of frustum
$$\begin{aligned}
& =105.65-46.94 \\
& =58.71 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{Total surface area of frustum}
$$\begin{aligned}
= & \text { curved surface area } \\
& + \text { area of two circular ends } \\
= & 58.71+\pi(2.0)^{2}+\pi(3.0)^{2} \\
= & 58.71+12.57+28.27=\mathbf{9 9 . 6} \mathbf{c m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$
(ii) Method 2

From page 252, total surface area of frustum
$$=\pi l(R+r)+\pi r^{2}+\pi R^{2}$$
where $l=B D=11.21-7.47=3.74 \mathrm{~cm}$, $R=3.0 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $r=2.0 \mathrm{~cm}$. Hence, total surface area of frustum
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\pi(3.74)(3.0+2.0)+\pi(2.0)^{2}+\pi(3.0)^{2} \\
& =99.6 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 26. A storage hopper is in the shape of a frustum of a pyramid. Determine its volume if the ends of the frustum are squares of sides 8.0 m and 4.6 m , respectively, and the perpendicular height between its ends is 3.6 m

The frustum is shown shaded in Figure 27.23(a) as part of a complete pyramid. A section perpendicular to the base through the vertex is shown in Figure 27.23(b).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-267.jpg?height=430&width=353&top_left_y=764&top_left_x=287)
(a)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-267.jpg?height=373&width=373&top_left_y=817&top_left_x=654)
(b)

Figure 27.23

By similar triangles $\frac{C G}{B G}=\frac{B H}{A H}$
From which, height
$$C G=B G\left(\frac{B H}{A H}\right)=\frac{(2.3)(3.6)}{1.7}=4.87 \mathrm{~m}$$

Height of complete pyramid $=3.6+4.87=8.47 \mathrm{~m}$
Volume of large pyramid $=\frac{1}{3}(8.0)^{2}(8.47)$
$$=180.69 \mathrm{~m}^{3}$$

Volume of small pyramid cut off $=\frac{1}{3}(4.6)^{2}(4.87)$
$$=34.35 \mathrm{~m}^{3}$$

Hence, volume of storage hopper $=180.69-34.35$
$$=146.3 \mathrm{~m}^{3}$$

Problem 27. Determine the lateral surface area of the storage hopper in Problem 26

The lateral surface area of the storage hopper consists of four equal trapeziums. From Figure 27.24,
$$\text { Area of trapezium } P R S U=\frac{1}{2}(P R+S U)(Q T)$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-267.jpg?height=416&width=593&top_left_y=313&top_left_x=1195)

Figure 27.24
$O T=1.7 \mathrm{~m}$ (same as $A H$ in Figure 27.23(b) and $O Q=3.6 \mathrm{~m}$. By Pythagoras' theorem,
$$Q T=\sqrt{\left(O Q^{2}+O T^{2}\right)}=\sqrt{\left[3.6^{2}+1.7^{2}\right]}=3.98 \mathrm{~m}$$

Area of trapezium PRSU
$$=\frac{1}{2}(4.6+8.0)(3.98)=25.07 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$$

Lateral surface area of hopper $=4(25.07)$
$$=100.3 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$$

Problem 28. A lampshade is in the shape of a frustum of a cone. The vertical height of the shade is 25.0 cm and the diameters of the ends are 20.0 cm and 10.0 cm , respectively. Determine the area of the material needed to form the lampshade, correct to 3 significant figures

The curved surface area of a frustum of a cone $=\pi l(R+r)$ from page 252 . Since the diameters of the ends of the frustum are 20.0 cm and 10.0 cm , from Figure 27.25,
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-267.jpg?height=513&width=391&top_left_y=1886&top_left_x=1293)

Figure 27.25
and
$$\begin{aligned}
r & =5.0 \mathrm{~cm}, R=10.0 \mathrm{~cm} \\
l & =\sqrt{\left[25.0^{2}+5.0^{2}\right]}=25.50 \mathrm{~cm}
\end{aligned}$$
from Pythagoras' theorem.
Hence, curved surface area
$$=\pi(25.50)(10.0+5.0)=1201.7 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$$
i.e., the area of material needed to form the lampshade is $\mathbf{1 2 0 0} \mathbf{~ c m}^{2}$, correct to 3 significant figures.

Problem 29. A cooling tower is in the form of a cylinder surmounted by a frustum of a cone, as shown in Figure 27.26. Determine the volume of air space in the tower if $40 \%$ of the space is used for pipes and other structures
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-268.jpg?height=457&width=481&top_left_y=981&top_left_x=383)

Figure 27.26

Volume of cylindrical portion $=\pi r^{2} h$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\pi\left(\frac{25.0}{2}\right)^{2} \\
& =5890 \mathrm{~m}^{3}
\end{aligned}$$

Volume of frustum of cone $=\frac{1}{3} \pi h\left(R^{2}+R r+r^{2}\right)$
where
$$\begin{aligned}
& h=30.0-12.0=18.0 \mathrm{~m}, \\
& R=25.0 \div 2=12.5 \mathrm{~m}
\end{aligned}$$
and
$$r=12.0 \div 2=6.0 \mathrm{~m} .$$

Hence, volume of frustum of cone
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{1}{3} \pi(18.0)\left[(12.5)^{2}+(12.5)(6.0)+(6.0)^{2}\right] \\
& =5038 \mathrm{~m}^{3}
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Total volume of cooling tower } & =5890+5038 \\
& =10928 \mathrm{~m}^{3}
\end{aligned}$$

If $40 \%$ of space is occupied then
volume of air space $=0.6 \times 10928=\mathbf{6 5 5 7} \mathbf{m}^{\mathbf{3}}$

\subsection*{27.6 Volumes of similar shapes}

Figure 27.27 shows two cubes, one of which has sides three times as long as those of the other.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-269.jpg?height=486&width=497&top_left_y=592&top_left_x=527)

Figure 27.27

Volume of Figure 27.27(a) $=(x)(x)(x)=x^{3}$
Volume of Figure 27.27(b) $=(3 x)(3 x)(3 x)=27 x^{3}$
Hence, Figure 27.27(b) has a volume (3) ${ }^{3}$, i.e. 27, times the volume of Figure 27.27(a).
Summarizing, the volumes of similar bodies are proportional to the cubes of corresponding linear dimensions.

Problem 30. A car has a mass of 1000 kg . A model of the car is made to a scale of 1 to 50 . Determine the mass of the model if the car and its model are made of the same material
$$\frac{\text { Volume of model }}{\text { Volume of car }}=\left(\frac{1}{50}\right)^{3}$$
since the volume of similar bodies are proportional to the cube of corresponding dimensions.
Mass $=$ density $\times$ volume and, since both car and model are made of the same material,
$$\frac{\text { Mass of model }}{\text { Mass of car }}=\left(\frac{1}{50}\right)^{3}$$

Hence, mass of model
$$=(\text { mass of car })\left(\frac{1}{50}\right)^{3}=\frac{1000}{50^{3}}=\mathbf{0 . 0 0 8} \mathbf{~ k g} \text { or } \mathbf{8 g}$$

\section*{Chapter 28}

\section*{Irregular areas and volumes, and mean values}

\subsection*{28.1 Areas of irregular figures}

Areas of irregular plane surfaces may be approximately determined by using
(a) a planimeter,
(b) the trapezoidal rule,
(c) the mid-ordinate rule, or
(d) Simpson's rule.

Such methods may be used by, for example, engineers estimating areas of indicator diagrams of steam engines, surveyors estimating areas of plots of land or naval architects estimating areas of water planes or transverse sections of ships.
(a) A planimeter is an instrument for directly measuring small areas bounded by an irregular curve. There are many different kinds of planimeters but all operate in a similar way. A pointer on the planimeter is used to trace around the boundary of the shape. This induces a movement in another part of the instrument and a reading of this is used to establish the area of the shape.
(b) Trapezoidal rule

To determine the area $P Q R S$ in Figure 28.1,
(i) Divide base $P S$ into any number of equal intervals, each of width $d$ (the greater the number of intervals, the greater the accuracy).
(ii) Accurately measure ordinates $y_{1}, y_{2}, y_{3}$, etc.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-270.jpg?height=272&width=421&top_left_y=1046&top_left_x=1259)

Figure 28.1
(iii) Area $P Q R S$
$$=d\left[\frac{y_{1}+y_{7}}{2}+y_{2}+y_{3}+y_{4}+y_{5}+y_{6}\right] .$$

In general, the trapezoidal rule states
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area }= & \binom{\text { width of }}{\text { interval }}\left[\frac{1}{2}\binom{\text { first }+ \text { last }}{\text { ordinate }}\right. \\
& \left.+\left(\begin{array}{c}
\text { sum of } \\
\text { remaining } \\
\text { ordinates }
\end{array}\right)\right]
\end{aligned}$$
(c) Mid-ordinate rule
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-270.jpg?height=283&width=430&top_left_y=2020&top_left_x=1252)

Figure 28.2

To determine the area $A B C D$ of Figure 28.2,
(i) Divide base $A D$ into any number of equal intervals, each of width $d$ (the greater the number of intervals, the greater the accuracy).
(ii) Erect ordinates in the middle of each interval (shown by broken lines in Figure 28.2).
(iii) Accurately measure ordinates $y_{1}, y_{2}, y_{3}$, etc.
(iv) Area $A B C D$
$$=d\left(y_{1}+y_{2}+y_{3}+y_{4}+y_{5}+y_{6}\right) .$$

In general, the mid-ordinate rule states

\section*{Area $=($ width of interval $)($ sum of mid-ordinates)}
(d) Simpson's rule

To determine the area PQRS of Figure 28.1,
(i) Divide base $P S$ into an even number of intervals, each of width $d$ (the greater the number of intervals, the greater the accuracy).
(ii) Accurately measure ordinates $y_{1}, y_{2}, y_{3}$, etc.
(iii) Area $P Q R S=\frac{d}{3}\left[\left(y_{1}+y_{7}\right)+4\left(y_{2}+y_{4}+y_{6}\right)\right.$ $\left.+2\left(y_{3}+y_{5}\right)\right]$
In general, Simpson's rule states
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { Area }=\frac{1}{3}\binom{\text { width of }}{\text { interval }}\left[\binom{\text { first }+ \text { last }}{\text { ordinate }}\right. \\
& \left.+4\binom{\text { sum of even }}{\text { ordinates }}+2\binom{\text { sum of remaining }}{\text { odd ordinates }}\right]
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 1. A car starts from rest and its speed is measured every second for 6 s .
\begin{tabular}{|lllllccc|} 
Time $t(\mathrm{~s})$ & 0 & 1 & 2 & 3 & 4 & 5 & 6 \\
\hline Speed $v(\mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s})$ & 0 & 2.5 & 5.5 & 8.75 & 12.5 & 17.5 & 24.0
\end{tabular}

Determine the distance travelled in 6 seconds (i.e. the area under the $v / t$ graph), using (a) the trapezoidal rule (b) the mid-ordinate rule
(c) Simpson's rule

A graph of speed/time is shown in Figure 28.3.
(a) Trapezoidal rule (see (b) above)

The time base is divided into 6 strips, each of width 1 s , and the length of the ordinates measured. Thus,
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { area }=(1) & {\left[\left(\frac{0+24.0}{2}\right)+2.5+5.5\right.} \\
& +8.75+12.5+17.5]=\mathbf{5 8 . 7 5} \mathbf{m}
\end{aligned}$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-271.jpg?height=627&width=717&top_left_y=302&top_left_x=1136)

Figure 28.3
(b) Mid-ordinate rule (see (c) above)

The time base is divided into 6 strips each of width 1 s . Mid-ordinates are erected as shown in Figure 28.3 by the broken lines. The length of each mid-ordinate is measured. Thus,
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { area }=(1)[1.25+4.0+7.0 & +10.75+15.0 \\
& +20.25]=\mathbf{5 8 . 2 5} \mathbf{m}
\end{aligned}$$
(c) Simpson's rule (see (d) above)

The time base is divided into 6 strips each of width 1 s and the length of the ordinates measured. Thus,
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { area }=\frac{1}{3}(1) & {[(0+24.0)+4(2.5+8.75} \\
& \quad+17.5)+2(5.5+12.5)]=\mathbf{5 8 . 3 3} \mathbf{m}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 2. A river is 15 m wide. Soundings of the depth are made at equal intervals of 3 m across the river and are as shown below.
$$\text { Depth (m) } \begin{array}{lllllllll}
0 & 2.2 & 3.3 & 4.5 & 4.2 & 2.4 & 0
\end{array}$$

Calculate the cross-sectional area of the flow of water at this point using Simpson's rule

From (d) above,
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area } & =\frac{1}{3}(3)[(0+0)+4(2.2+4.5+2.4)+2(3.3+4.2)] \\
& =(1)[0+36.4+15]=\mathbf{5 1 . 4} \mathbf{m}^{\mathbf{2}}
\end{aligned}$$

\subsection*{28.2 Volumes of irregular solids}

If the cross-sectional areas $A_{1}, A_{2}, A_{3}, \ldots$ of an irregular solid bounded by two parallel planes are known at equal intervals of width $d$ (as shown in Figure 28.5), by Simpson's rule

Volume, $V=\frac{d}{3}\left[\left(A_{1}+A_{7}\right)+4\left(A_{2}+A_{4}+A_{6}\right)\right.$
$$\left.+2\left(A_{3}+A_{5}\right)\right]$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-272.jpg?height=235&width=492&top_left_y=837&top_left_x=1218)

Figure 28.5

Problem 3. A tree trunk is 12 m in length and has a varying cross-section. The cross-sectional areas at intervals of 2 m measured from one end are $0.52,0.55,0.59,0.63,0.72,0.84$ and $0.97 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$.
Estimate the volume of the tree trunk

A sketch of the tree trunk is similar to that shown in Figure 28.5 above, where $d=2 \mathrm{~m}, A_{1}=0.52 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$, $A_{2}=0.55 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$, and so on.
Using Simpson's rule for volumes gives
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Volume }= & \frac{2}{3}[(0.52+0.97)+4(0.55+0.63+0.84) \\
& +2(0.59+0.72)] \\
= & \frac{2}{3}[1.49+8.08+2.62]=\mathbf{8 . 1 3} \mathbf{m}^{\mathbf{3}}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 4. The areas of seven horizontal cross-sections of a water reservoir at intervals of 10 m are $210,250,320,350,290,230$ and $170 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$.
Calculate the capacity of the reservoir in litres

Using Simpson's rule for volumes gives
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Volume }=\frac{10}{3}[(210+170)+4(250 & +350+230) \\
& +2(320+290)] \\
=\frac{10}{3}[380+3320+1220]= & \mathbf{1 6} \mathbf{4 0 0} \mathbf{m}^{3}
\end{aligned}$$
$16400 \mathrm{~m}^{3}=16400 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$.
Since 1 litre $=1000 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$,
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { capacity of reservoir } & =\frac{16400 \times 10^{6}}{1000} \text { litres } \\
& =16400000=\mathbf{1 6 . 4} \times \mathbf{1 0}^{\mathbf{6}} \text { litres }
\end{aligned}$$

one end $(\mathrm{m})$
\end{tabular} & 8 & 10 & 12 \\
\hline Circumference $(\mathrm{m})$ & 5.16 & 5.82 & 6.36 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

\subsection*{28.3 Mean or average values of waveforms}

The mean or average value, $y$, of the waveform shown in Figure 28.6 is given by
$$y=\frac{\text { area under curve }}{\text { length of base, } b}$$

If the mid-ordinate rule is used to find the area under the curve, then
$$\begin{gathered}
y=\frac{\text { sum of mid-ordinates }}{\text { number of mid-ordinates }} \\
\left(=\frac{y_{1}+y_{2}+y_{3}+y_{4}+y_{5}+y_{6}+y_{7}}{7}\right. \text { for Figure 28.6) }
\end{gathered}$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-273.jpg?height=337&width=525&top_left_y=718&top_left_x=1229)

Figure 28.6

For a sine wave, the mean or average value
(a) over one complete cycle is zero (see Figure 28.7(a)),
(b) over half a cycle is $\mathbf{0 . 6 3 7} \times$ maximum value or $\frac{2}{\pi} \times$ maximum value,
(c) of a full-wave rectified waveform (see Figure 28.7(b)) is $\mathbf{0 . 6 3 7} \times$ maximum value,
(d) of a half-wave rectified waveform (see Figure 28.7(c)) is $\mathbf{0 . 3 1 8} \mathbf{x}$ maximum value or $\frac{\mathbf{1}}{\pi} \times$ maximum value.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-273.jpg?height=565&width=660&top_left_y=1838&top_left_x=1161)

Figure 28.7

Problem 5. Determine the average values over half a cycle of the periodic waveforms shown in Figure 28.8
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-274.jpg?height=248&width=354&top_left_y=462&top_left_x=265)
(a)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-274.jpg?height=251&width=402&top_left_y=455&top_left_x=615)
(b)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-274.jpg?height=245&width=340&top_left_y=759&top_left_x=508)
(c)

Figure 28.8
(a) Area under triangular waveform (a) for a half cycle is given by
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area } & =\frac{1}{2}(\text { base })(\text { perpendicular height }) \\
& =\frac{1}{2}\left(2 \times 10^{-3}\right)(20)=20 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{Vs}
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{Average value of waveform}
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{\text { area under curve }}{\text { length of base }} \\
& =\frac{20 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{Vs}}{2 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{~s}}=\mathbf{1 0} \mathrm{V}
\end{aligned}$$
(b) Area under waveform (b) for a half cycle $=(1 \times 1)+(3 \times 2)=7 \mathrm{As}$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Average value of waveform } & =\frac{\text { area under curve }}{\text { length of base }} \\
& =\frac{7 \mathrm{As}}{3 \mathrm{~s}}=\mathbf{2 . 3 3 A}
\end{aligned}$$
(c) A half cycle of the voltage waveform (c) is completed in 4 ms .
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area under curve } & =\frac{1}{2}\left\{(3-1) 10^{-3}\right\}(10) \\
& =10 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{Vs}
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Average value of waveform } & =\frac{\text { area under curve }}{\text { length of base }} \\
& =\frac{10 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{Vs}}{4 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{~s}}=\mathbf{2 . 5} \mathbf{V}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 6. Determine the mean value of current over one complete cycle of the periodic waveforms shown in Figure 28.9
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-274.jpg?height=226&width=486&top_left_y=473&top_left_x=1227)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-274.jpg?height=199&width=459&top_left_y=719&top_left_x=1224)

Figure 28.9
(a) One cycle of the trapezoidal waveform (a) is completed in 10 ms (i.e. the periodic time is 10 ms ).

Area under curve $=$ area of trapezium
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{1}{2}(\text { sum of parallel sides })(\text { perpendicular } \\
& \text { distance between parallel sides) }
\end{aligned} \quad \begin{aligned}
&= \frac{1}{2}\left\{(4+8) \times 10^{-3}\right\}\left(5 \times 10^{-3}\right) \\
&=30 \times 10^{-6} \mathrm{As}
\end{aligned}$$
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Mean value over one cycle } & =\frac{\text { area under curve }}{\text { length of base }} \\
& =\frac{30 \times 10^{-6} \mathrm{As}}{10 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{~s}} \\
& =\mathbf{3 m A}
\end{aligned}$$
(b) One cycle of the saw-tooth waveform (b) is completed in 5 ms .
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { Area under curve } & =\frac{1}{2}\left(3 \times 10^{-3}\right)(2) \\
& =3 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{As} \\
\text { Mean value over one cycle } & =\frac{\text { area under curve }}{\text { length of base }} \\
& =\frac{3 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{As}}{5 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{~s}} \\
& =\mathbf{0 . 6} \mathbf{A}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 7. The power used in a manufacturing process during a 6 hour period is recorded at intervals of 1 hour as shown below.
\begin{tabular}{llllllrrr} 
Time (h) & 0 & 1 & 2 & 3 & 4 & 5 & 6 \\
Power (kW) & 0 & 14 & 29 & 51 & 45 & 23 & 0
\end{tabular}

Plot a graph of power against time and, by using the mid-ordinate rule, determine (a) the area under the curve and (b) the average value of the power

The graph of power/time is shown in Figure 28.10.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-275.jpg?height=586&width=701&top_left_y=892&top_left_x=306)

Figure 28.10
(a) The time base is divided into 6 equal intervals, each of width 1 hour. Mid-ordinates are erected (shown by broken lines in Figure 28.10) and measured. The values are shown in Figure 28.10.

\section*{Area under curve}
$$\begin{aligned}
& =(\text { width of interval }) \text { (sum of mid-ordinates }) \\
& =(1)[7.0+21.5+42.0+49.5+37.0+10.0] \\
& =\mathbf{1 6 7} \mathbf{k W h} \text { (i.e. a measure of electrical energy) }
\end{aligned}$$
(b) Average value of waveform $=\frac{\text { area under curve }}{\text { length of base }}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{167 \mathrm{kWh}}{6 \mathrm{~h}} \\
& =27.83 \mathrm{~kW}
\end{aligned}$$

Alternatively, average value
$$=\frac{\text { sum of mid-ordinates }}{\text { number of mid-ordinates }}$$

Problem 8. Figure 28.11 shows a sinusoidal output voltage of a full-wave rectifier. Determine, using the mid-ordinate rule with 6 intervals, the mean output voltage
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-275.jpg?height=291&width=662&top_left_y=527&top_left_x=1163)

Figure 28.11

One cycle of the output voltage is completed in $\pi$ radians or $180^{\circ}$. The base is divided into 6 intervals, each of width $30^{\circ}$. The mid-ordinate of each interval will lie at $15^{\circ}, 45^{\circ}, 75^{\circ}$, etc.
At $15^{\circ}$ the height of the mid-ordinate is $10 \sin 15^{\circ}=2.588 \mathrm{~V}$,
At $45^{\circ}$ the height of the mid-ordinate is $10 \sin 45^{\circ}=7.071 \mathrm{~V}$, and so on.

The results are tabulated below.
\begin{tabular}{|l|l|}
\hline Mid-ordinate & Height of mid-ordinate \\
\hline $15^{\circ}$ & $10 \sin 15^{\circ}=2.588 \mathrm{~V}$ \\
\hline $45^{\circ}$ & $10 \sin 45^{\circ}=7.071 \mathrm{~V}$ \\
\hline $75^{\circ}$ & $10 \sin 75^{\circ}=9.659 \mathrm{~V}$ \\
\hline $105^{\circ}$ & $10 \sin 105^{\circ}=9.659 \mathrm{~V}$ \\
\hline $135^{\circ}$ & $10 \sin 135^{\circ}=7.071 \mathrm{~V}$ \\
\hline $165^{\circ}$ & $10 \sin 165^{\circ}=2.588 \mathrm{~V}$ \\
\hline \multicolumn{2}{|r|}{Sum of mid-ordinates $=38.636 \mathrm{~V}$} \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

\section*{Mean or average value of output voltage}
$$=\frac{\text { sum of mid-ordinates }}{\text { number of mid-ordinates }}=\frac{38.636}{6}=\mathbf{6 . 4 3 9 V}$$
(With a larger number of intervals a more accurate answer may be obtained.)
For a sine wave the actual mean value is $0.637 \times$ maximum value, which in this problem gives 6.37 V .

Problem 9. An indicator diagram for a steam engine is shown in Figure 28.12. The base line has
been divided into 6 equally spaced intervals and the lengths of the 7 ordinates measured with the results shown in centimetres. Determine
(a) the area of the indicator diagram using Simpson's rule
(b) the mean pressure in the cylinder given that 1 cm represents 100 kPa .
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-276.jpg?height=223&width=487&top_left_y=694&top_left_x=383)

Figure 28.12
(a) The width of each interval is $\frac{12.0}{6} \mathrm{~cm}$. Using Simpson's rule,
$$\begin{aligned}
\text { area }= & \frac{1}{3}(2.0)[(3.6+1.6)+4(4.0+2.9+1.7) \\
& +2(3.5+2.2)] \\
= & \frac{2}{3}[5.2+34.4+11.4]=\mathbf{3 4} \mathbf{c m}^{2}
\end{aligned}$$
(b) Mean height of ordinates $=\frac{\text { area of diagram }}{\text { length of base }}$
$$=\frac{34}{12}=2.83 \mathrm{~cm}$$

Since 1 cm represents 100 kPa , mean pressure in the cylinder $=2.83 \mathrm{~cm} \times 100 \mathrm{kPa} / \mathrm{cm}=\mathbf{2 8 3} \mathbf{k P a}$
