\section*{Chapter 29}

\section*{Vectors}

\subsection*{29.1 Introduction}

This chapter initially explains the difference between scalar and vector quantities and shows how a vector is drawn and represented.

Any object that is acted upon by an external force will respond to that force by moving in the line of the force. However, if two or more forces act simultaneously, the result is more difficult to predict; the ability to add two or more vectors then becomes important.

This chapter thus shows how vectors are added and subtracted, both by drawing and by calculation, and how finding the resultant of two or more vectors has many uses in engineering. (Resultant means the single vector which would have the same effect as the individual vectors.) Relative velocities and vector $\boldsymbol{i}, \boldsymbol{j}, \boldsymbol{k}$ notation are also briefly explained.

\subsection*{29.2 Scalars and vectors}

The time taken to fill a water tank may be measured as, say, 50 s . Similarly, the temperature in a room may be measured as, say, $16^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ or the mass of a bearing may be measured as, say, 3 kg . Quantities such as time, temperature and mass are entirely defined by a numerical value and are called scalars or scalar quantities.

Not all quantities are like this. Some are defined by more than just size; some also have direction. For example, the velocity of a car may be $90 \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{h}$ due west, a force of 20 N may act vertically downwards, or an acceleration of $10 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$ may act at $50^{\circ}$ to the horizontal.

Quantities such as velocity, force and acceleration, which have both a magnitude and a direction, are called vectors.

\section*{Now try the following Practice Exercise}

\section*{Practice Exercise 113 Scalar and vector quantities (answers on page 352)}
1. State the difference between scalar and vector quantities.

In problems 2 to 9 , state whether the quantities given are scalar or vector.
2. A temperature of $70^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$
3. $5 \mathrm{~m}^{3}$ volume
4. A downward force of 20 N
5. 500 J of work
6. $30 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$ area
7. A south-westerly wind of 10 knots
8. 50 m distance
9. An acceleration of $15 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$ at $60^{\circ}$ to the horizontal

\subsection*{29.3 Drawing a vector}

A vector quantity can be represented graphically by a line, drawn so that
(a) the length of the line denotes the magnitude of the quantity, and
(b) the direction of the line denotes the direction in which the vector quantity acts.

An arrow is used to denote the sense, or direction, of the vector.
The arrow end of a vector is called the 'nose' and the other end the 'tail'. For example, a force of 9 N acting
at $45^{\circ}$ to the horizontal is shown in Figure 29.1. Note that an angle of $\mathbf{+ 4 5 ^ { \circ }}$ is drawn from the horizontal and moves anticlockwise.

Figure 29.1

A velocity of $20 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ at $-60^{\circ}$ is shown in Figure 29.2. Note that an angle of $\mathbf{- 6 0 ^ { \circ }}$ is drawn from the horizontal and moves clockwise.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-280.jpg?height=269&width=332&top_left_y=1048&top_left_x=463)

Figure 29.2

\subsection*{29.3.1 Representing a vector}

There are a number of ways of representing vector quantities. These include
(a) Using bold print.
(b) $\overrightarrow{A B}$ where an arrow above two capital letters denotes the sense of direction, where $A$ is the starting point and $B$ the end point of the vector.
(c) $\overline{A B}$ or $\bar{a}$; i.e., a line over the top of letter.
(d) $\underline{a}$; i.e., underlined letter.

The force of 9 N at $45^{\circ}$ shown in Figure 29.1 may be represented as
$$\mathbf{0} \boldsymbol{a} \text { or } \overline{0} \vec{a} \text { or } \overline{0 a}$$

The magnitude of the force is $0 a$.
Similarly, the velocity of $20 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ at $-60^{\circ}$ shown in Figure 29.2 may be represented as
$$\mathbf{0} \boldsymbol{b} \text { or } \overline{0} \vec{b} \text { or } \overline{0 b}$$

The magnitude of the velocity is $0 b$.

In this chapter a vector quantity is denoted by bold print.

\subsection*{29.4 Addition of vectors by drawing}

Adding two or more vectors by drawing assumes that a ruler, pencil and protractor are available. Results obtained by drawing are naturally not as accurate as those obtained by calculation.
(a) Nose-to-tail method

Two force vectors, $F_{1}$ and $F_{2}$, are shown in Figure 29.3. When an object is subjected to more than one force, the resultant of the forces is found by the addition of vectors.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-280.jpg?height=210&width=299&top_left_y=969&top_left_x=1315)

Figure 29.3
To add forces $F_{1}$ and $F_{2}$,
(i) Force $F_{1}$ is drawn to scale horizontally, shown as $\mathbf{0} \boldsymbol{a}$ in Figure 29.4.
(ii) From the nose of $F_{1}$, force $F_{2}$ is drawn at angle $\theta$ to the horizontal, shown as $\boldsymbol{a b}$.
(iii) The resultant force is given by length $\mathbf{0 b}$, which may be measured.
This procedure is called the 'nose-to-tail' or 'triangle' method.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-280.jpg?height=210&width=576&top_left_y=1685&top_left_x=1176)

Figure 29.4
(b) Parallelogram method

To add the two force vectors, $F_{1}$ and $F_{2}$ of Figure 29.3,
(i) A line $c b$ is constructed which is parallel to and equal in length to $\mathbf{0} \boldsymbol{a}$ (see Figure 29.5).
(ii) A line $a b$ is constructed which is parallel to and equal in length to $\mathbf{0 c}$.
(iii) The resultant force is given by the diagonal of the parallelogram; i.e., length $\mathbf{0 b}$.
This procedure is called the 'parallelogram' method.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-281.jpg?height=197&width=530&top_left_y=311&top_left_x=391)

Figure 29.5

Problem 1. A force of 5 N is inclined at an angle of $45^{\circ}$ to a second force of 8 N , both forces acting at a point. Find the magnitude of the resultant of these two forces and the direction of the resultant with respect to the 8 N force by (a) the nose-to-tail method and (b) the parallelogram method

The two forces are shown in Figure 29.6. (Although the 8 N force is shown horizontal, it could have been drawn in any direction.)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-281.jpg?height=199&width=367&top_left_y=1142&top_left_x=473)

Figure 29.6
(a) Nose-to-tail method
(i) The 8 N force is drawn horizontally 8 units long, shown as $\mathbf{0} \boldsymbol{a}$ in Figure 29.7.
(ii) From the nose of the 8 N force, the 5 N force is drawn 5 units long at an angle of $45^{\circ}$ to the horizontal, shown as $\boldsymbol{a b}$.
(iii) The resultant force is given by length $0 b$ and is measured as $\mathbf{1 2} \mathbf{N}$ and angle $\theta$ is measured as $17^{\circ}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-281.jpg?height=193&width=541&top_left_y=1894&top_left_x=386)

Figure 29.7
(b) Parallelogram method
(i) In Figure 29.8, a line is constructed which is parallel to and equal in length to the 8 N force.
(ii) A line is constructed which is parallel to and equal in length to the 5 N force.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-281.jpg?height=226&width=633&top_left_y=310&top_left_x=1175)

Figure 29.8
(iii) The resultant force is given by the diagonal of the parallelogram, i.e. length $0 b$, and is measured as $\mathbf{1 2} \mathbf{N}$ and angle $\theta$ is measured as $17^{\circ}$.

Thus, the resultant of the two force vectors in Figure 29.6 is $\mathbf{1 2} \mathbf{N}$ at $17^{\circ}$ to the 8 N force.

Problem 2. Forces of 15 and 10 N are at an angle of $90^{\circ}$ to each other as shown in Figure 29.9. Find, by drawing, the magnitude of the resultant of these two forces and the direction of the resultant with respect to the 15 N force
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-281.jpg?height=324&width=351&top_left_y=1221&top_left_x=1316)

Figure 29.9

Using the nose-to-tail method,
(i) The 15 N force is drawn horizontally 15 units long, as shown in Figure 29.10.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-281.jpg?height=345&width=375&top_left_y=1861&top_left_x=1304)

Figure 29.10
(ii) From the nose of the 15 N force, the 10 N force is drawn 10 units long at an angle of $90^{\circ}$ to the horizontal as shown.
(iii) The resultant force is shown as $R$ and is measured as $\mathbf{1 8} \mathbf{N}$ and angle $\theta$ is measured as $\mathbf{3 4}$.

Thus, the resultant of the two force vectors is 18 N at $34^{\circ}$ to the $\mathbf{1 5 N}$ force.

Problem 3. Velocities of $10 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}, 20 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ and $15 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ act as shown in Figure 29.11. Determine, by drawing, the magnitude of the resultant velocity and its direction relative to the horizontal
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-282.jpg?height=491&width=603&top_left_y=747&top_left_x=328)

Figure 29.11
When more than 2 vectors are being added the nose-totail method is used. The order in which the vectors are added does not matter. In this case the order taken is $\nu_{1}$, then $\nu_{2}$, then $\nu_{3}$. However, if a different order is taken the same result will occur.
(i) $v_{1}$ is drawn 10 units long at an angle of $30^{\circ}$ to the horizontal, shown as $\mathbf{0} \boldsymbol{a}$ in Figure 29.12.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-282.jpg?height=535&width=416&top_left_y=1707&top_left_x=421)

Figure 29.12
(ii) From the nose of $\nu_{1}, \nu_{2}$ is drawn 20 units long at an angle of $90^{\circ}$ to the horizontal, shown as $\boldsymbol{a b}$.
(iii) From the nose of $\nu_{2}, \nu_{3}$ is drawn 15 units long at an angle of $195^{\circ}$ to the horizontal, shown as $\boldsymbol{b r}$.
(iv) The resultant velocity is given by length $\mathbf{0 r}$ and is measured as $\mathbf{2 2} \mathbf{~ m} / \mathbf{s}$ and the angle measured to the horizontal is $\mathbf{1 0 5}^{\circ}$.

Thus, the resultant of the three velocities is $22 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ at $105^{\circ}$ to the horizontal.

Worked Examples 1 to 3 have demonstrated how vectors are added to determine their resultant and their direction. However, drawing to scale is time-consuming and not highly accurate. The following sections demonstrate how to determine resultant vectors by calculation using horizontal and vertical components and, where possible, by Pythagoras' theorem.

\subsection*{29.5 Resolving vectors into horizontal and vertical components}

A force vector $\boldsymbol{F}$ is shown in Figure 29.13 at angle $\theta$ to the horizontal. Such a vector can be resolved into two components such that the vector addition of the components is equal to the original vector.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-282.jpg?height=218&width=365&top_left_y=1361&top_left_x=1282)

Figure 29.13
The two components usually taken are a horizontal component and a vertical component. If a right-angled triangle is constructed as shown in Figure 29.14, 0a is called the horizontal component of $F$ and $a b$ is called the vertical component of $F$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-282.jpg?height=277&width=383&top_left_y=1909&top_left_x=1273)

Figure 29.14
From trigonometry (see Chapter 21 and remember SOH CAH TOA),
$$\cos \theta=\frac{0 a}{0 b}, \text { from which } 0 a=0 b \cos \theta=F \cos \theta$$
i.e. the horizontal component of $\boldsymbol{F}=\boldsymbol{F} \cos \boldsymbol{\theta}$, and
$$\sin \theta=\frac{a b}{0 b} \text { from which, } a b=0 b \sin \theta=F \sin \theta$$
i.e. the vertical component of $F=F \sin \theta$.

Problem 4. Resolve the force vector of 50 N at an angle of $35^{\circ}$ to the horizontal into its horizontal and vertical components

The horizontal component of the 50 N force, $0 a=50 \cos 35^{\circ}=40.96 \mathrm{~N}$.
The vertical component of the 50 N force, $a b=50 \sin 35^{\circ}=\mathbf{2 8 . 6 8} \mathbf{N}$.
The horizontal and vertical components are shown in Figure 29.15.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-283.jpg?height=272&width=443&top_left_y=1071&top_left_x=435)

Figure 29.15
(To check: by Pythagoras,
and
$$\begin{aligned}
0 b & =\sqrt{40.96^{2}+28.68^{2}}=50 \mathrm{~N} \\
\theta & =\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{28.68}{40.96}\right)=35^{\circ}
\end{aligned}$$

Thus, the vector addition of components 40.96 N and 28.68 N is 50 N at $35^{\circ}$.)

Problem 5. Resolve the velocity vector of $20 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ at an angle of $-30^{\circ}$ to the horizontal into horizontal and vertical components

The horizontal component of the $20 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ velocity, $0 a=20 \cos \left(-30^{\circ}\right)=\mathbf{1 7 . 3 2} \mathbf{m} / \mathbf{s}$.
The vertical component of the $20 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ velocity, $a b=20 \sin \left(-30^{\circ}\right)=-\mathbf{1 0 m} / \mathbf{s}$.
The horizontal and vertical components are shown in Figure 29.16.

Problem 6. Resolve the displacement vector of 40 m at an angle of $120^{\circ}$ into horizontal and vertical components
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-283.jpg?height=291&width=433&top_left_y=299&top_left_x=1275)

Figure 29.16

The horizontal component of the 40 m displacement, $0 a=40 \cos 120^{\circ}=\mathbf{- 2 0 . 0} \mathbf{m}$.
The vertical component of the 40 m displacement, $a b=40 \sin 120^{\circ}=\mathbf{3 4 . 6 4} \mathbf{m}$.
The horizontal and vertical components are shown in Figure 29.17.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-283.jpg?height=264&width=383&top_left_y=1007&top_left_x=1300)

Figure 29.17

\subsection*{29.6 Addition of vectors by calculation}

Two force vectors, $F_{1}$ and $F_{2}$, are shown in Figure 29.18, $F_{1}$ being at an angle of $\theta_{1}$ and $F_{2}$ at an angle of $\theta_{2}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-283.jpg?height=486&width=544&top_left_y=1715&top_left_x=1222)

Figure 29.18
A method of adding two vectors together is to use horizontal and vertical components.
The horizontal component of force $F_{1}$ is $F_{1} \cos \theta_{1}$ and the horizontal component of force $F_{2}$ is $F_{2} \cos \theta_{2}$. The
total horizontal component of the two forces,
$$H=F_{1} \cos \theta_{1}+F_{2} \cos \theta_{2}$$

The vertical component of force $F_{1}$ is $F_{1} \sin \theta_{1}$ and the vertical component of force $F_{2}$ is $F_{2} \sin \theta_{2}$. The total vertical component of the two forces,
$$V=F_{1} \sin \theta_{1}+F_{2} \sin \theta_{2}$$

Since we have $H$ and $V$, the resultant of $F_{1}$ and $F_{2}$ is obtained by using the theorem of Pythagoras. From Figure 29.19,
$$0 b^{2}=H^{2}+V^{2}$$
i.e. $\quad$ resultant $=\sqrt{\boldsymbol{H}^{2}+V^{2}}$ at an angle
$$\text { given by } \theta=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{V}{H}\right)$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-284.jpg?height=237&width=326&top_left_y=1145&top_left_x=466)

Figure 29.19

Problem 7. A force of 5 N is inclined at an angle of $45^{\circ}$ to a second force of 8 N , both forces acting at a point. Calculate the magnitude of the resultant of these two forces and the direction of the resultant with respect to the 8 N force

The two forces are shown in Figure 29.20.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-284.jpg?height=235&width=416&top_left_y=1881&top_left_x=421)

Figure 29.20

The horizontal component of the 8 N force is $8 \cos 0^{\circ}$ and the horizontal component of the 5 N force is $5 \cos 45^{\circ}$. The total horizontal component of the two forces,
$$\boldsymbol{H}=8 \cos 0^{\circ}+5 \cos 45^{\circ}=8+3.5355=\mathbf{1 1 . 5 3 5 5}$$

The vertical component of the 8 N force is $8 \sin 0^{\circ}$ and the vertical component of the 5 N force is $5 \sin 45^{\circ}$. The total vertical component of the two forces,
$$\boldsymbol{V}=8 \sin 0^{\circ}+5 \sin 45^{\circ}=0+3.5355=\mathbf{3 . 5 3 5 5}$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-284.jpg?height=259&width=487&top_left_y=581&top_left_x=1221)

Figure 29.21

From Figure 29.21, magnitude of resultant vector
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\sqrt{H^{2}+V^{2}} \\
& =\sqrt{11.5355^{2}+3.5355^{2}}=\mathbf{1 2 . 0 7} \mathbf{N}
\end{aligned}$$

The direction of the resultant vector,
$$\begin{aligned}
\theta=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{V}{H}\right) & =\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{3.5355}{11.5355}\right) \\
& =\tan ^{-1} 0.30648866 \ldots=\mathbf{1 7 . 0 4}
\end{aligned}$$

Thus, the resultant of the two forces is a single vector of 12.07 N at $17.04^{\circ}$ to the 8 N vector.

Problem 8. Forces of 15 N and 10 N are at an angle of $90^{\circ}$ to each other as shown in Figure 29.22. Calculate the magnitude of the resultant of these two forces and its direction with respect to the 15 N force
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-284.jpg?height=318&width=354&top_left_y=1815&top_left_x=1290)

Figure 29.22

The horizontal component of the 15 N force is $15 \cos 0^{\circ}$ and the horizontal component of the 10 N force is $10 \cos 90^{\circ}$. The total horizontal component of the two velocities,
$$\boldsymbol{H}=15 \cos 0^{\circ}+10 \cos 90^{\circ}=15+0=\mathbf{1 5}$$

The vertical component of the 15 N force is $15 \sin 0^{\circ}$ and the vertical component of the 10 N force is $10 \sin 90^{\circ}$. The total vertical component of the two velocities,
$$\boldsymbol{V}=15 \sin 0^{\circ}+10 \sin 90^{\circ}=0+10=\mathbf{1 0}$$

Magnitude of resultant vector
$=\sqrt{H^{2}+V^{2}}=\sqrt{15^{2}+10^{2}}=\mathbf{1 8 . 0 3} \mathbf{N}$
The direction of the resultant vector,
$\theta=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{V}{H}\right)=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{10}{15}\right)=33.69^{\circ}$
Thus, the resultant of the two forces is a single vector of 18.03 N at $33.69^{\circ}$ to the 15 N vector.

There is an alternative method of calculating the resultant vector in this case. If we used the triangle method, the diagram would be as shown in Figure 29.23.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-285.jpg?height=340&width=375&top_left_y=1018&top_left_x=466)

Figure 29.23
Since a right-angled triangle results, we could use Pythagoras' theorem without needing to go through the procedure for horizontal and vertical components. In fact, the horizontal and vertical components are 15 N and 10 N respectively.
This is, of course, a special case. Pythagoras can only be used when there is an angle of $90^{\circ}$ between vectors. This is demonstrated in worked Problem 9.

Problem 9. Calculate the magnitude and direction of the resultant of the two acceleration vectors shown in Figure 29.24.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-285.jpg?height=378&width=313&top_left_y=2018&top_left_x=500)

Figure 29.24

The $15 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$ acceleration is drawn horizontally, shown as $\mathbf{0} \boldsymbol{a}$ in Figure 29.25.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-285.jpg?height=411&width=405&top_left_y=418&top_left_x=1289)

Figure 29.25
From the nose of the $15 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$ acceleration, the $28 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$ acceleration is drawn at an angle of $90^{\circ}$ to the horizontal, shown as $\boldsymbol{a} \boldsymbol{b}$.
The resultant acceleration, $R$, is given by length $\mathbf{0 b}$.
Since a right-angled triangle results, the theorem of Pythagoras may be used.
and
$$\begin{aligned}
0 b & =\sqrt{15^{2}+28^{2}}=\mathbf{3 1 . 7 6 m} / \mathbf{s}^{2} \\
\alpha & =\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{28}{15}\right)=\mathbf{6 1 . 8 2}
\end{aligned}$$

Measuring from the horizontal,
$\boldsymbol{\theta}=180^{\circ}-61.82^{\circ}=\mathbf{1 1 8 . 1 8}^{\circ}$
Thus, the resultant of the two accelerations is a single vector of $31.76 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{\mathbf{2}}$ at $118.18^{\circ}$ to the horizontal.

Problem 10. Velocities of $10 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}, 20 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ and $15 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ act as shown in Figure 29.26. Calculate the magnitude of the resultant velocity and its direction relative to the horizontal
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-285.jpg?height=503&width=617&top_left_y=1788&top_left_x=1183)

Figure 29.26

The horizontal component of the $10 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ velocity $=10 \cos 30^{\circ}=8.660 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$,
the horizontal component of the $20 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ velocity is $20 \cos 90^{\circ}=0 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$
and the horizontal component of the $15 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ velocity is $15 \cos 195^{\circ}=-14.489 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$.
The total horizontal component of the three velocities,
$$\boldsymbol{H}=8.660+0-14.489=-\mathbf{5 . 8 2 9} \mathbf{m} / \mathbf{s}$$

The vertical component of the $10 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ velocity $=10 \sin 30^{\circ}=5 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$,
the vertical component of the $20 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ velocity is $20 \sin 90^{\circ}=20 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$
and the vertical component of the $15 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ velocity is $15 \sin 195^{\circ}=-3.882 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$.
The total vertical component of the three forces,
$$\boldsymbol{V}=5+20-3.882=\mathbf{2 1 . 1 1 8} \mathbf{m} / \mathbf{s}$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-286.jpg?height=348&width=340&top_left_y=935&top_left_x=459)

Figure 29.27
From Figure 29.27, magnitude of resultant vector,
$R=\sqrt{H^{2}+V^{2}}=\sqrt{5.829^{2}+21.118^{2}}=\mathbf{2 1 . 9 1 \mathbf { m } / \mathbf { s }}$
The direction of the resultant vector,
$$\alpha=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{V}{H}\right)=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{21.118}{5.829}\right)=74.57^{\circ}$$

Measuring from the horizontal,
$\boldsymbol{\theta}=180^{\circ}-74.57^{\circ}=105.43^{\circ}$.
Thus, the resultant of the three velocities is a single vector of $21.91 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ at $105.43^{\circ}$ to the horizontal.

\subsection*{29.7 Vector subtraction}

In Figure 29.33, a force vector $\boldsymbol{F}$ is represented by $\boldsymbol{o a}$. The vector $(-\boldsymbol{o a})$ can be obtained by drawing a vector from $o$ in the opposite sense to oa but having the same magnitude, shown as $\boldsymbol{o b}$ in Figure 29.33; i.e., $o b=(-o a)$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-287.jpg?height=275&width=609&top_left_y=676&top_left_x=1187)

Figure 29.33
For two vectors acting at a point, as shown in Figure 29.34(a), the resultant of vector addition is
$$o s=o a+o b$$

Figure 29.33(b) shows vectors $\boldsymbol{o b}+(-\boldsymbol{o} \boldsymbol{a})$ that is, $\boldsymbol{o b}-\boldsymbol{o a}$ and the vector equation is $\boldsymbol{o b}-\boldsymbol{o a}=\boldsymbol{o d}$. Comparing $\boldsymbol{o d}$ in Figure 29.34(b) with the broken line $a b$ in Figure 29.34(a) shows that the second diagonal of the parallelogram method of vector addition gives the magnitude and direction of vector subtraction of $\boldsymbol{o a}$ from $o b$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-287.jpg?height=210&width=782&top_left_y=1560&top_left_x=1100)

Figure 29.34

Problem 11. Accelerations of $a_{1}=1.5 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$ at $90^{\circ}$ and $a_{2}=2.6 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$ at $145^{\circ}$ act at a point. Find $\boldsymbol{a}_{1}+\boldsymbol{a}_{\mathbf{2}}$ and $\boldsymbol{a}_{1}-\boldsymbol{a}_{\mathbf{2}}$ by (a) drawing a scale vector diagram and (b) calculation
(a) The scale vector diagram is shown in Figure 29.35. By measurement,
$$\begin{aligned}
& a_{1}+a_{2}=3.7 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2} \text { at } 126^{\circ} \\
& a_{1}-a_{2}=2.1 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2} \text { at } 0^{\circ}
\end{aligned}$$
(b) Resolving horizontally and vertically gives Horizontal component of $a_{1}+a_{2}$, $\boldsymbol{H}=1.5 \cos 90^{\circ}+2.6 \cos 145^{\circ}=\mathbf{- 2 . 1 3}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-288.jpg?height=557&width=598&top_left_y=307&top_left_x=330)

Figure 29.35

Vertical component of $\boldsymbol{a}_{\mathbf{1}}+\boldsymbol{a}_{\mathbf{2}}$, $\boldsymbol{V}=1.5 \sin 90^{\circ}+2.6 \sin 145^{\circ}=\mathbf{2 . 9 9}$
From Figure 29.36, the magnitude of $a_{1}+a_{2}$, $\boldsymbol{R}=\sqrt{(-2.13)^{2}+2.99^{2}}=\mathbf{3 . 6 7 m} / \mathbf{s}^{\mathbf{2}}$
In Figure 29.36, $\alpha=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{2.99}{2.13}\right)=54.53^{\circ}$ and $\theta=180^{\circ}-54.53^{\circ}=125.47^{\circ}$
Thus, $a_{1}+a_{2}=3.67 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$ at $125.47^{\circ}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-288.jpg?height=522&width=535&top_left_y=1393&top_left_x=362)

Figure 29.36
Horizontal component of $\boldsymbol{a}_{\mathbf{1}} \mathbf{- a} \boldsymbol{a}_{\mathbf{2}}$
$=1.5 \cos 90^{\circ}-2.6 \cos 145^{\circ}=\mathbf{2 . 1 3}$
Vertical component of $\boldsymbol{a}_{\mathbf{1}}-\boldsymbol{a}_{\mathbf{2}}$
$=1.5 \sin 90^{\circ}-2.6 \sin 145^{\circ}=0$
Magnitude of $\boldsymbol{a}_{\mathbf{1}}-\boldsymbol{a}_{\mathbf{2}}=\sqrt{2.13^{2}+0^{2}}$
$=2.13 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$
Direction of $\boldsymbol{a}_{\mathbf{1}}-\boldsymbol{a}_{\mathbf{2}}=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{0}{2.13}\right)=\mathbf{0}^{\circ}$
Thus, $a_{1}-a_{2}=2.13 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$ at $0^{\circ}$

Problem 12. Calculate the resultant of (a) $v_{1}-v_{2}+v_{3}$ and (b) $v_{2}-v_{1}-v_{3}$ when $\boldsymbol{v}_{1}=22$ units at $140^{\circ}, \boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{2}}=40$ units at $190^{\circ}$ and $v_{3}=15$ units at $290^{\circ}$
(a) The vectors are shown in Figure 29.37.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-288.jpg?height=741&width=782&top_left_y=649&top_left_x=1079)

Figure 29.37
The horizontal component of $\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{1}}-\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{2}}+\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{3}}$ $=\left(22 \cos 140^{\circ}\right)-\left(40 \cos 190^{\circ}\right)+\left(15 \cos 290^{\circ}\right)$ $=(-16.85)-(-39.39)+(5.13)=\mathbf{2 7 . 6 7}$ units
The vertical component of $\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{1}} \boldsymbol{- \boldsymbol { v } _ { \mathbf { 2 } } + \boldsymbol { v } _ { \mathbf { 3 } }}$ $=\left(22 \sin 140^{\circ}\right)-\left(40 \sin 190^{\circ}\right)+\left(15 \sin 290^{\circ}\right)$ $=(14.14)-(-6.95)+(-14.10)=\mathbf{6 . 9 9}$ units
The magnitude of the resultant,
$\boldsymbol{R}=\sqrt{27.67^{2}+6.99^{2}}=\mathbf{2 8 . 5 4}$ units
The direction of the resultant
$R=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{6.99}{27.67}\right)=14.18^{\circ}$
Thus, $v_{1}-v_{2}+v_{3}=28.54$ units at $\mathbf{1 4 . 1 8}^{\circ}$
(b) The horizontal component of $v_{2}-v_{1}-v_{3}$
$=\left(40 \cos 190^{\circ}\right)-\left(22 \cos 140^{\circ}\right)-\left(15 \cos 290^{\circ}\right)$
$=(-39.39)-(-16.85)-(5.13)=-27.67$ units
The vertical component of $\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{2}}-\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{1}}-\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{3}}$
$=\left(40 \sin 190^{\circ}\right)-\left(22 \sin 140^{\circ}\right)-\left(15 \sin 290^{\circ}\right)$
$=(-6.95)-(14.14)-(-14.10)=\mathbf{- 6 . 9 9}$ units
From Figure 29.38, the magnitude of the resultant, $\boldsymbol{R}=\sqrt{(-27.67)^{2}+(-6.99)^{2}}=\mathbf{2 8 . 5 4}$ units and $\alpha=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{6.99}{27.67}\right)=14.18^{\circ}$, from which, $\theta=180^{\circ}+14.18^{\circ}=194.18^{\circ}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-289.jpg?height=464&width=568&top_left_y=305&top_left_x=372)

Figure 29.38

Thus, $\boldsymbol{v}_{2}-v_{1}-v_{3}=\mathbf{2 8 . 5 4}$ units at $\mathbf{1 9 4 . 1 8}{ }^{\circ}$
This result is as expected, since $\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{2}}-\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{1}}-\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{3}}$ $=-\left(v_{1}-v_{2}+v_{3}\right)$ and the vector 28.54 units at $194.18^{\circ}$ is minus times (i.e. is $180^{\circ}$ out of phase with) the vector 28.54 units at $14.18^{\circ}$

Practice Exercise 115 Vector subtraction (answers on page 352)
1. Forces of $F_{1}=40 \mathrm{~N}$ at $45^{\circ}$ and $F_{2}=30 \mathrm{~N}$ at $125^{\circ}$ act at a point. Determine by drawing and by calculation (a) $\boldsymbol{F}_{\mathbf{1}}+\boldsymbol{F}_{\mathbf{2}}$ (b) $\boldsymbol{F}_{\mathbf{1}}-\boldsymbol{F}_{\mathbf{2}}$
2. Calculate the resultant of (a) $\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{1}}+\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{2}}-\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{3}}$ (b) $\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{3}}-\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{2}}+\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{1}}$ when $\boldsymbol{v}_{\mathbf{1}}=15 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ at $85^{\circ}$, $\boldsymbol{v}_{2}=25 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ at $175^{\circ}$ and $\boldsymbol{v}_{3}=12 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ at $235^{\circ}$.

\subsection*{29.8 Relative velocity}

For relative velocity problems, some fixed datum point needs to be selected. This is often a fixed point on the earth's surface. In any vector equation, only the start and finish points affect the resultant vector of a system. Two different systems are shown in Figure 29.39, but, in each of the systems, the resultant vector is $\boldsymbol{a d}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-289.jpg?height=256&width=635&top_left_y=2155&top_left_x=336)

Figure 29.39

The vector equation of the system shown in Figure 29.39(a) is
$$a d=a b+b d$$
and that for the system shown in Figure 29.39(b) is
$$a d=a b+b c+c d$$

Thus, in vector equations of this form, only the first and last letters, $a$ and $d$, respectively, fix the magnitude and direction of the resultant vector. This principle is used in relative velocity problems.

Problem 13. Two cars, $P$ and $Q$, are travelling towards the junction of two roads which are at right angles to one another. Car $P$ has a velocity of $45 \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{h}$ due east and car $Q$ a velocity of $55 \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{h}$ due south. Calculate (a) the velocity of car $P$ relative to car $Q$ and (b) the velocity of car $Q$ relative to car $P$
(a) The directions of the cars are shown in Figure 29.40(a), which is called a space diagram. The velocity diagram is shown in Figure 29.40(b), in which $\boldsymbol{p e}$ is taken as the velocity of car $P$ relative to point $e$ on the earth's surface. The velocity of $P$ relative to $Q$ is vector $\boldsymbol{p q}$ and the vector equation is $\boldsymbol{p q}=\boldsymbol{p e} \boldsymbol{e} \boldsymbol{e q}$. Hence, the vector directions are as shown, $\boldsymbol{e q}$ being in the opposite direction to $q \boldsymbol{e}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-289.jpg?height=381&width=679&top_left_y=1564&top_left_x=1155)

Figure 29.40
From the geometry of the vector triangle, the magnitude of $p q=\sqrt{45^{2}+55^{2}}=71.06 \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{h}$ and the direction of $p q=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{55}{45}\right)=50.71^{\circ}$ That is, the velocity of car $P$ relative to car $Q$ is $71.06 \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{h}$ at $50.71^{\circ}$
(b) The velocity of car $Q$ relative to car $P$ is given by the vector equation $\boldsymbol{q} \boldsymbol{p}=\boldsymbol{q} \boldsymbol{e}+\boldsymbol{e} \boldsymbol{p}$ and the vector diagram is as shown in Figure 29.40(c), having $\boldsymbol{e p}$ opposite in direction to $\boldsymbol{p e}$.

From the geometry of this vector triangle, the magnitude of $\boldsymbol{q} \boldsymbol{p}=\sqrt{45^{2}+55^{2}}=71.06 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ and the direction of $\boldsymbol{q} \boldsymbol{p}=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{55}{45}\right)=50.71^{\circ}$ but must lie in the third quadrant; i.e., the required angle is $180^{\circ}+50.71^{\circ}=230.71^{\circ}$
That is, the velocity of car $\boldsymbol{Q}$ relative to car $P$ is $71.06 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{s}$ at $230.71^{\circ}$

\section*{$29.9 \quad i, j$ and $k$ notation}

A method of completely specifying the direction of a vector in space relative to some reference point is to use three unit vectors, $\boldsymbol{i}, \boldsymbol{j}$ and $\boldsymbol{k}$, mutually at right angles to each other, as shown in Figure 29.41.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-290.jpg?height=460&width=524&top_left_y=1942&top_left_x=367)

Figure 29.41

Calculations involving vectors given in $\boldsymbol{i}, \boldsymbol{j}, \boldsymbol{k}$ notation are carried out in exactly the same way as standard algebraic calculations, as shown in the worked examples below.

Problem 14. Determine
$(3 \boldsymbol{i}+2 \boldsymbol{j}+2 \boldsymbol{k})-(4 \boldsymbol{i}-3 \boldsymbol{j}+2 \boldsymbol{k})$
$$\begin{aligned}
(3 \boldsymbol{i}+2 \boldsymbol{j}+2 \boldsymbol{k})- & (4 \boldsymbol{i}-3 \boldsymbol{j}+2 \boldsymbol{k}) \\
& =3 \boldsymbol{i}+2 \boldsymbol{j}+2 \boldsymbol{k}-4 \boldsymbol{i}+3 \boldsymbol{j}-2 \boldsymbol{k} \\
& =-\boldsymbol{i}+\mathbf{5} \boldsymbol{j}
\end{aligned}$$

Problem 15. Given $p=3 i+2 k$, $\boldsymbol{q}=4 \boldsymbol{i}-2 \boldsymbol{j}+3 \boldsymbol{k}$ and $r=-3 \boldsymbol{i}+5 \boldsymbol{j}-4 \boldsymbol{k}$, determine
(a) $-\boldsymbol{r}$ (b) $3 \boldsymbol{p}$ (c) $2 \boldsymbol{p}+3 \boldsymbol{q}$ (d) $-\boldsymbol{p}+2 \boldsymbol{r}$
(e) $0.2 \boldsymbol{p}+0.6 \boldsymbol{q}-3.2 \boldsymbol{r}$
(a) $-r=-(-3 i+5 j-4 k)=+3 i-5 j+4 k$
(b) $3 p=3(3 i+2 k)=9 \boldsymbol{i}+\mathbf{6 k}$
(c) $2 \boldsymbol{p}+3 \boldsymbol{q}=2(3 \boldsymbol{i}+2 \boldsymbol{k})+3(4 \boldsymbol{i}-2 \boldsymbol{j}+3 \boldsymbol{k})$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =6 \boldsymbol{i}+4 \boldsymbol{k}+12 \boldsymbol{i}-6 \boldsymbol{j}+9 \boldsymbol{k} \\
& =\mathbf{1 8} \boldsymbol{i}-\mathbf{6} \boldsymbol{j}+\mathbf{1 3} \boldsymbol{k}
\end{aligned}$$
(d) $-\boldsymbol{p}+2 \boldsymbol{r}=-(3 \boldsymbol{i}+2 \boldsymbol{k})+2(-3 \boldsymbol{i}+5 \boldsymbol{j}-4 \boldsymbol{k})$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =-3 \boldsymbol{i}-2 \boldsymbol{k}+(-6 \boldsymbol{i}+10 \boldsymbol{j}-8 \boldsymbol{k}) \\
& =-3 \boldsymbol{i}-2 \boldsymbol{k}-6 \boldsymbol{i}+10 \boldsymbol{j}-8 \boldsymbol{k} \\
& =-\mathbf{i} \boldsymbol{i}+\mathbf{1 0} \boldsymbol{j}-\mathbf{1 0} \boldsymbol{k}
\end{aligned}$$
(e) $0.2 \boldsymbol{p}+0.6 \boldsymbol{q}-3.2 \boldsymbol{r}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =0.2(3 \boldsymbol{i}+2 \boldsymbol{k})+0.6(4 \boldsymbol{i}-2 \boldsymbol{j}+3 \boldsymbol{k}) \\
& =0.6 \boldsymbol{i}+0.4 \boldsymbol{k}+2.4 \boldsymbol{i}-1.2 \boldsymbol{j}+1.8 \boldsymbol{i}+5+9.6 \boldsymbol{i}-4 \boldsymbol{k}) \\
& =\mathbf{1 2 . 6} \boldsymbol{i}-\mathbf{1 7 . 2} \boldsymbol{j}+\mathbf{1 5} \boldsymbol{k} \quad-16 \boldsymbol{j}+12.8 \boldsymbol{k}
\end{aligned}$$

\section*{Chapter 30}

\title{
Methods of adding alternating waveforms
}

\subsection*{30.1 Combining two periodic functions}

There are a number of instances in engineering and science where waveforms have to be combined and where it is required to determine the single phasor (called the resultant) that could replace two or more separate phasors. Uses are found in electrical alternating current theory, in mechanical vibrations, in the addition of forces and with sound waves.

There are a number of methods of determining the resultant waveform. These include
(a) Drawing the waveforms and adding graphically.
(b) Drawing the phasors and measuring the resultant.
(c) Using the cosine and sine rules.
(d) Using horizontal and vertical components.

\subsection*{30.2 Plotting periodic functions}

This may be achieved by sketching the separate functions on the same axes and then adding (or subtracting) ordinates at regular intervals. This is demonstrated in the following worked problems.

> Problem 1. Plot the graph of $y_{1}=3 \sin A$ from $A=0^{\circ}$ to $A=360^{\circ}$. On the same axes plot $y_{2}=2 \cos A$. By adding ordinates, plot $y_{R}=3 \sin A+2 \cos A$ and obtain a sinusoidal expression for this resultant waveform
$y_{1}=3 \sin A$ and $y_{2}=2 \cos A$ are shown plotted in Figure 30.1. Ordinates may be added at, say, $15^{\circ}$ intervals. For example,
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { at } 0^{\circ}, y_{1}+y_{2}=0+2=2 \\
& \text { at } 15^{\circ}, y_{1}+y_{2}=0.78+1.93=2.71 \\
& \text { at } 120^{\circ}, y_{1}+y_{2}=2.60+-1=1.6
\end{aligned}$$
at $210^{\circ}, y_{1}+y_{2}=-1.50-1.73=-3.23$, and so on.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-291.jpg?height=598&width=753&top_left_y=1575&top_left_x=1115)

Figure 30.1

The resultant waveform, shown by the broken line, has the same period, i.e. $360^{\circ}$, and thus the same frequency as the single phasors. The maximum value, or amplitude, of the resultant is 3.6. The resultant
waveform leads $y_{1}=3 \sin A$ by $34^{\circ}$ or $34 \times \frac{\pi}{180} \mathrm{rad}=$ 0.593 rad .

The sinusoidal expression for the resultant waveform is
$$y_{R}=3.6 \sin \left(A+34^{\circ}\right) \text { or } y_{R}=3.6 \sin (A+0.593)$$

Problem 2. Plot the graphs of $y_{1}=4 \sin \omega t$ and $y_{2}=3 \sin (\omega t-\pi / 3)$ on the same axes, over one cycle. By adding ordinates at intervals plot $y_{R}=y_{1}+y_{2}$ and obtain a sinusoidal expression for the resultant waveform
$y_{1}=4 \sin \omega t$ and $y_{2}=3 \sin (\omega t-\pi / 3)$ are shown plotted in Figure 30.2.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-292.jpg?height=464&width=679&top_left_y=991&top_left_x=290)

Figure 30.2

Ordinates are added at $15^{\circ}$ intervals and the resultant is shown by the broken line. The amplitude of the resultant is 6.1 and it lags $y_{1}$ by $25^{\circ}$ or 0.436 rad .
Hence, the sinusoidal expression for the resultant waveform is
$$y_{R}=6.1 \sin (\omega t-0.436)$$

Problem 3. Determine a sinusoidal expression for $y_{1}-y_{2}$ when $y_{1}=4 \sin \omega t$ and
$y_{2}=3 \sin (\omega t-\pi / 3)$
$y_{1}$ and $y_{2}$ are shown plotted in Figure 30.3. At $15^{\circ}$ intervals $y_{2}$ is subtracted from $y_{1}$. For example,
$$\begin{aligned}
& \text { at } 0^{\circ}, y_{1}-y_{2}=0-(-2.6)=+2.6 \\
& \text { at } 30^{\circ}, y_{1}-y_{2}=2-(-1.5)=+3.5 \\
& \text { at } 150^{\circ}, y_{1}-y_{2}=2-3=-1, \text { and so on. }
\end{aligned}$$

The amplitude, or peak, value of the resultant (shown by the broken line) is 3.6 and it leads $y_{1}$ by $45^{\circ}$ or 0.79 rad .
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-292.jpg?height=427&width=674&top_left_y=307&top_left_x=1130)

Figure 30.3

Hence,
$$y_{1}-y_{2}=3.6 \sin (\omega t+0.79)$$

Problem 4. Two alternating currents are given by $i_{1}=20 \sin \omega t$ amperes and $i_{2}=10 \sin \left(\omega t+\frac{\pi}{3}\right)$ amperes. By drawing the waveforms on the same axes and adding, determine the sinusoidal expression for the resultant $i_{1}+i_{2}$
$i_{1}$ and $i_{2}$ are shown plotted in Figure 30.4. The resultant waveform for $i_{1}+i_{2}$ is shown by the broken line. It has the same period, and hence frequency, as $i_{1}$ and $i_{2}$. The amplitude or peak value is 26.5 A . The resultant waveform leads the waveform of $i_{1}=20 \sin \omega t$ by $19^{\circ}$ or 0.33 rad .
Hence, the sinusoidal expression for the resultant $i_{1}+i_{2}$ is given by
$$i_{R}=i_{1}+i_{2}=26.5 \sin (\omega t+0.33) \mathrm{A}$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-293.jpg?height=682&width=864&top_left_y=302&top_left_x=642)

\subsection*{30.3 Determining resultant phasors by drawing}

The resultant of two periodic functions may be found from their relative positions when the time is zero. For example, if $y_{1}=4 \sin \omega t$ and $y_{2}=3 \sin (\omega t-\pi / 3)$ then each may be represented as phasors as shown in Figure $30.5, y_{1}$ being 4 units long and drawn horizontally and $y_{2}$ being 3 units long, lagging $y_{1}$ by $\pi / 3$ radians or $60^{\circ}$. To determine the resultant of $y_{1}+y_{2}, y_{1}$ is drawn horizontally as shown in Figure 30.6 and $y_{2}$ is joined to the end of $y_{1}$ at $60^{\circ}$ to the horizontal. The resultant is given by $y_{R}$. This is the same as the diagonal of a parallelogram that is shown completed in Figure 30.7.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-293.jpg?height=242&width=232&top_left_y=1994&top_left_x=540)

Figure 30.5
Resultant $y_{R}$, in Figures 30.6 and 30.7, may be determined by drawing the phasors and their directions to scale and measuring using a ruler and protractor. In this
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-293.jpg?height=226&width=335&top_left_y=1113&top_left_x=1324)

Figure 30.6
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-293.jpg?height=253&width=421&top_left_y=1446&top_left_x=1281)

Figure 30.7
example, $y_{R}$ is measured as 6 units long and angle $\phi$ is measured as $25^{\circ}$.
$$25^{\circ}=25 \times \frac{\pi}{180} \text { radians }=0.44 \mathrm{rad}$$

Hence, summarizing, by drawing, $y_{R}=y_{1}+y_{2}=4 \sin \omega t+3 \sin (\omega t-\pi / 3)$ $=6 \sin (\omega t-0.44)$.
If the resultant phasor, $y_{R}=y_{1}-y_{2}$ is required then $y_{2}$ is still 3 units long but is drawn in the opposite direction, as shown in Figure 30.8.

Problem 5. Two alternating currents are given by $i_{1}=20 \sin \omega t$ amperes and $i_{2}=10 \sin \left(\omega t+\frac{\pi}{3}\right)$ amperes. Determine $i_{1}+i_{2}$ by drawing phasors
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-294.jpg?height=445&width=438&top_left_y=309&top_left_x=410)

Figure 30.8

The relative positions of $i_{1}$ and $i_{2}$ at time $t=0$ are shown as phasors in Figure 30.9, where $\frac{\pi}{3} \mathrm{rad}=60^{\circ}$. The phasor diagram in Figure 30.10 is drawn to scale with a ruler and protractor.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-294.jpg?height=185&width=356&top_left_y=1133&top_left_x=451)

Figure 30.9
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-294.jpg?height=242&width=557&top_left_y=1479&top_left_x=351)

Figure 30.10

The resultant $i_{R}$ is shown and is measured as 26 A and angle $\phi$ as $19^{\circ}$ or 0.33 rad leading $i_{1}$. Hence, by drawing and measuring,
$$i_{R}=i_{1}+i_{2}=26 \sin (\omega t+0.33) \mathrm{A}$$

Problem 6. For the currents in Problem 5, determine $i_{1}-i_{2}$ by drawing phasors

At time $t=0$, current $i_{1}$ is drawn 20 units long horizontally as shown by $0 a$ in Figure 30.11. Current $i_{2}$ is shown, drawn 10 units long in a broken line and leading by $60^{\circ}$. The current $-i_{2}$ is drawn in the opposite direction to the broken line of $i_{2}$, shown as $a b$ in Figure 30.11. The resultant $i_{R}$ is given by $0 b$ lagging by angle $\phi$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-294.jpg?height=362&width=609&top_left_y=302&top_left_x=1160)

Figure 30.11
By measurement, $i_{R}=17 \mathrm{~A}$ and $\phi=30^{\circ}$ or 0.52 rad . Hence, by drawing phasors,
$$i_{R}=i_{1}-i_{2}=17 \sin (\omega t-0.52) \mathrm{A}$$

\subsection*{30.4 Determining resultant phasors by the sine and cosine rules}

As stated earlier, the resultant of two periodic functions may be found from their relative positions when the time is zero. For example, if $y_{1}=5 \sin \omega t$ and $y_{2}=4 \sin (\omega t-\pi / 6)$ then each may be represented by phasors as shown in Figure 30.12, $y_{1}$ being 5 units long and drawn horizontally and $y_{2}$ being 4 units long, lagging $y_{1}$ by $\pi / 6$ radians or $30^{\circ}$. To determine the resultant of $y_{1}+y_{2}, y_{1}$ is drawn horizontally as shown in Figure 30.13 and $y_{2}$ is joined to the end of $y_{1}$ at $\pi / 6$ radians; i.e., $30^{\circ}$ to the horizontal. The resultant is given by $y_{R}$.
Using the cosine rule on triangle $0 a b$ of Figure 30.13 gives
$$\begin{aligned}
y_{R}^{2} & =5^{2}+4^{2}-\left[2(5)(4) \cos 150^{\circ}\right] \\
& =25+16-(-34.641)=75.641
\end{aligned}$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-295.jpg?height=191&width=297&top_left_y=303&top_left_x=508)

Figure 30.12
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-295.jpg?height=264&width=584&top_left_y=687&top_left_x=362)

Figure 30.13
from which
$$y_{R}=\sqrt{75.641}=8.697$$

Using the sine rule, $\frac{8.697}{\sin 150^{\circ}}=\frac{4}{\sin \phi}$
from which
$$\sin \phi=\frac{4 \sin 150^{\circ}}{8.697}=0.22996$$
and
$$\begin{aligned}
\phi & =\sin ^{-1} 0.22996 \\
& =13.29^{\circ} \text { or } 0.232 \mathrm{rad}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $\boldsymbol{y}_{\boldsymbol{R}}=\boldsymbol{y}_{\mathbf{1}}+\boldsymbol{y}_{\mathbf{2}}=5 \sin \omega t+4 \sin (\omega t-\pi / 6)$
$$=8.697 \sin (\omega t-0.232)$$

Problem 7. Given $y_{1}=2 \sin \omega t$ and $y_{2}=3 \sin (\omega t+\pi / 4)$, obtain an expression, by calculation, for the resultant, $y_{R}=y_{1}+y_{2}$

When time $t=0$, the position of phasors $y_{1}$ and $y_{2}$ are as shown in Figure 30.14(a). To obtain the resultant, $y_{1}$ is drawn horizontally, 2 units long, and $y_{2}$ is drawn 3 units long at an angle of $\pi / 4 \mathrm{rad}$ or $45^{\circ}$ and joined to the end of $y_{1}$ as shown in Figure 30.14(b).
From Figure 30.14(b), and using the cosine rule,
$$\begin{aligned}
y_{R}^{2} & =2^{2}+3^{2}-\left[2(2)(3) \cos 135^{\circ}\right] \\
& =4+9-[-8.485]=21.485
\end{aligned}$$

Hence,
$$y_{R}=\sqrt{21.485}=4.6352$$

Using the sine rule
$$\frac{3}{\sin \phi}=\frac{4.6352}{\sin 135^{\circ}}$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-295.jpg?height=733&width=570&top_left_y=295&top_left_x=1204)

Figure 30.14
from which
$$\sin \phi=\frac{3 \sin 135^{\circ}}{4.6352}=0.45765$$

Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
\phi & =\sin ^{-1} 0.45765 \\
& =27.24^{\circ} \text { or } 0.475 \mathrm{rad}
\end{aligned}$$

Thus, by calculation, $\quad y_{R}=4.635 \sin (\omega t+0.475)$

Problem 8. Determine
$$20 \sin \omega t+10 \sin \left(\omega t+\frac{\pi}{3}\right)$$
using the cosine and sine rules

From the phasor diagram of Figure 30.15 and using the cosine rule,
$$i_{R}^{2}=20^{2}+10^{2}-\left[2(20)(10) \cos 120^{\circ}\right]=700$$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-295.jpg?height=218&width=538&top_left_y=1968&top_left_x=1222)

Figure 30.15

Hence,
$$i_{R}=\sqrt{700}=26.46 \mathrm{~A}$$

Using the sine rule gives
$$\frac{10}{\sin \phi}=\frac{26.46}{\sin 120^{\circ}}$$
from which
$$\begin{aligned}
\sin \phi & =\frac{10 \sin 120^{\circ}}{26.46} \\
& =0.327296
\end{aligned}$$
and
$$\begin{aligned}
\phi & =\sin ^{-1} 0.327296=\mathbf{1 9 . 1 0} \\
& =19.10 \times \frac{\pi}{180}=\mathbf{0 . 3 3 3} \mathbf{r a d}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, by cosine and sine rules, $i_{R}=i_{1}+i_{2}=26.46 \sin (\omega t+0.333) \mathrm{A}$

\subsection*{30.5 Determining resultant phasors by horizontal and vertical components}

If a right-angled triangle is constructed as shown in Figure 30.16, 0a is called the horizontal component of $F$ and $a b$ is called the vertical component of $F$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-296.jpg?height=319&width=462&top_left_y=2075&top_left_x=398)

Figure 30.16

From trigonometry (see Chapter 21 and remember SOH CAH TOA),
$$\cos \theta=\frac{0 a}{0 b}, \text { from which } 0 a=0 b \cos \theta=F \cos \theta$$
i.e. the horizontal component of $\boldsymbol{F}, \boldsymbol{H}=\boldsymbol{F} \cos \boldsymbol{\theta}$, and
$$\sin \theta=\frac{a b}{0 b}, \text { from which } a b=0 b \sin \theta=F \sin \theta$$
i.e. the vertical component of $F, V=F \sin \theta$.

Determining resultant phasors by horizontal and vertical components is demonstrated in the following worked problems.

Problem 9. Two alternating voltages are given by $v_{1}=15 \sin \omega t$ volts and $v_{2}=25 \sin (\omega t-\pi / 6)$ volts. Determine a sinusoidal expression for the resultant $v_{R}=v_{1}+v_{2}$ by finding horizontal and vertical components

The relative positions of $v_{1}$ and $v_{2}$ at time $t=0$ are shown in Figure 30.17(a) and the phasor diagram is shown in Figure 30.17(b).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-296.jpg?height=625&width=565&top_left_y=1534&top_left_x=1185)

Figure 30.17

The horizontal component of $v_{R}$, $\boldsymbol{H}=15 \cos 0^{\circ}+25 \cos \left(-30^{\circ}\right)=\mathbf{3 6 . 6 5 V}$

The vertical component of $v_{R}$, $\boldsymbol{V}=15 \sin 0^{\circ}+25 \sin \left(-30^{\circ}\right)=-\mathbf{1 2 . 5 0} \mathbf{V}$

Hence,
$$\begin{aligned}
v_{R} & =\sqrt{36.65^{2}+(-12.50)^{2}} \\
& \text { by Pythagoras' theorem } \\
& =\mathbf{3 8 . 7 2} \text { volts }
\end{aligned}$$
$$\tan \phi=\frac{V}{H}=\frac{-12.50}{36.65}=-0.3411$$
from which
$$\begin{aligned}
\phi & =\tan ^{-1}(-0.3411) \\
& =\mathbf{- 1 8 . 8 3}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence, $v_{R}=v_{1}+v_{2}=38.72 \sin (\omega t-0.329) \mathrm{V}$

Problem 10. For the voltages in Problem 9, determine the resultant $v_{R}=v_{1}-v_{2}$ using horizontal and vertical components

The horizontal component of $v_{R}$,
$\boldsymbol{H}=15 \cos 0^{\circ}-25 \cos \left(-30^{\circ}\right)=\mathbf{- 6 . 6 5} \mathbf{V}$
The vertical component of $v_{R}$,
$\boldsymbol{V}=15 \sin 0^{\circ}-25 \sin \left(-30^{\circ}\right)=\mathbf{1 2 . 5 0} \mathrm{V}$
Hence,
$$v_{R}=\sqrt{(-6.65)^{2}+(12.50)^{2}}$$
by Pythagoras' theorem
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\mathbf{1 4 . 1 6} \text { volts } \\
\tan \phi & =\frac{V}{H}=\frac{12.50}{-6.65}=-1.8797
\end{aligned}$$
from which
$$\begin{aligned}
\phi & =\tan ^{-1}(-1.8797) \\
& =\mathbf{1 1 8 . 0 1}
\end{aligned}$$

Hence,
$$v_{R}=v_{1}-v_{2}=14.16 \sin (\omega t+2.06) \mathrm{V}$$

The phasor diagram is shown in Figure 30.18.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-297.jpg?height=367&width=622&top_left_y=1796&top_left_x=345)

Figure 30.18

$$20 \sin \omega t+10 \sin \left(\omega t+\frac{\pi}{3}\right)$$
using horizontal and vertical components

From the phasors shown in Figure 30.19,
Total horizontal component,
$\boldsymbol{H}=20 \cos 0^{\circ}+10 \cos 60^{\circ}=\mathbf{2 5 . 0}$
Total vertical component, $\boldsymbol{V}=20 \sin 0^{\circ}+10 \sin 60^{\circ}=\mathbf{8 . 6 6}$

By Pythagoras, the resultant,
$i_{R}=\sqrt{\left[25.0^{2}+8.66^{2}\right]}=\mathbf{2 6 . 4 6} \mathrm{A}$
Phase angle, $\phi=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{8.66}{25.0}\right)$
$$=19.11^{\circ} \text { or } \mathbf{0 . 3 3 3} \mathrm{rad}$$

Hence, by using horizontal and vertical components,
$\underline{20 \sin \omega t+10 \sin \left(\omega t+\frac{\pi}{3}\right)=26.46 \sin (\omega t+0.333)}$

\section*{Chapter 31}

\section*{Presentation of statistical data}

\subsection*{31.1 Some statistical terminology}

\subsection*{31.1.1 Discrete and continuous data}

Data are obtained largely by two methods:
(a) By counting - for example, the number of stamps sold by a post office in equal periods of time.
(b) By measurement - for example, the heights of a group of people.

When data are obtained by counting and only whole numbers are possible, the data are called discrete. Measured data can have any value within certain limits and are called continuous.

Problem 1. Data are obtained on the topics given below. State whether they are discrete or continuous data.
(a) The number of days on which rain falls in a month for each month of the year.
(b) The mileage travelled by each of a number of salesmen.
(c) The time that each of a batch of similar batteries lasts.
(d) The amount of money spent by each of several families on food.
(a) The number of days on which rain falls in a given month must be an integer value and is obtained by counting the number of days. Hence, these data are discrete.
(b) A salesman can travel any number of miles (and parts of a mile) between certain limits and these data are measured. Hence, the data are continuous.
(c) The time that a battery lasts is measured and can have any value between certain limits. Hence, these data are continuous.
(d) The amount of money spent on food can only be expressed correct to the nearest pence, the amount being counted. Hence, these data are discrete.

(d) The number of defective items produced in each of 10 one-hour periods by a machine.

\subsection*{31.1.2 Further statistical terminology}

A set is a group of data and an individual value within the set is called a member of the set. Thus, if the masses of five people are measured correct to the nearest 0.1 kilogram and are found to be $53.1 \mathrm{~kg}, 59.4 \mathrm{~kg}$, $62.1 \mathrm{~kg}, 77.8 \mathrm{~kg}$ and 64.4 kg then the set of masses in kilograms for these five people is
$$\{53.1,59.4,62.1,77.8,64.4\}$$
and one of the members of the set is 59.4
A set containing all the members is called a population. Some members selected at random from a population are called a sample. Thus, all car registration numbers form a population but the registration numbers of, say, 20 cars taken at random throughout the country are a sample drawn from that population.

The number of times that the value of a member occurs in a set is called the frequency of that member. Thus, in the set $\{2,3,4,5,4,2,4,7,9\}$, member 4 has a frequency of three, member 2 has a frequency of 2 and the other members have a frequency of one.

The relative frequency with which any member of a set occurs is given by the ratio
$$\frac{\text { frequency of member }}{\text { total frequency of all members }}$$

For the set $\{2,3,5,4,7,5,6,2,8\}$, the relative frequency of member 5 is $\frac{2}{9}$. Often, relative frequency is expressed as a percentage and the percentage relative frequency is
$$(\text { relative frequency } \times 100) \%$$

\subsection*{31.2 Presentation of ungrouped data}

Ungrouped data can be presented diagrammatically in several ways and these include
(a) pictograms, in which pictorial symbols are used to represent quantities (see Problem 2),
(b) horizontal bar charts, having data represented by equally spaced horizontal rectangles (see Problem 3), and
(c) vertical bar charts, in which data are represented by equally spaced vertical rectangles (see Problem 4).

Trends in ungrouped data over equal periods of time can be presented diagrammatically by a percentage component bar chart. In such a chart, equally spaced rectangles of any width, but whose height corresponds to $100 \%$, are constructed. The rectangles are then subdivided into values corresponding to the percentage relative frequencies of the members (see Problem 5).

A pie diagram is used to show diagrammatically the parts making up the whole. In a pie diagram, the area of a circle represents the whole and the areas of the sectors of the circle are made proportional to the parts which make up the whole (see Problem 6).

\begin{tabular}{|lccc|}

\hline Month & April & May & June \\

\end{tabular}

Each symbol shown in Figure 31.1 represents two television sets repaired. Thus, in January, $5 \frac{1}{2}$ symbols are used to represent the 11 sets repaired; in February, 3 symbols are used to represent the 6 sets repaired, and so on.

Problem 5. The numbers of various types of dwellings sold by a company annually over a three-year period are as shown below. Draw percentage component bar charts to present these data
\begin{tabular}{|l|ccc|}
\hline & Year 1 & Year 2 & Year 3 \\
\hline 4-roomed bungalows & 24 & 17 & 7 \\
\hline 5-roomed bungalows & 38 & 71 & 118 \\
\hline 4-roomed houses & 44 & 50 & 53 \\
\hline 5-roomed houses & 64 & 82 & 147 \\
\hline 6-roomed houses & 30 & 30 & 25 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

A table of percentage relative frequency values, correct to the nearest $1 \%$, is the first requirement. Since
percentage relative frequency
$$=\frac{\text { frequency of member } \times 100}{\text { total frequency }}$$
then for 4-roomed bungalows in year 1 percentage relative frequency
$$=\frac{24 \times 100}{24+38+44+64+30}=12 \%$$

The percentage relative frequencies of the other types of dwellings for each of the three years are similarly calculated and the results are as shown in the table below.
\begin{tabular}{|lccc|}
\hline & Year 1 & Year 2 & Year 3 \\
\hline 4-roomed bungalows & $12 \%$ & $7 \%$ & $2 \%$ \\
\hline 5-roomed bungalows & $19 \%$ & $28 \%$ & $34 \%$ \\
\hline 4-roomed houses & $22 \%$ & $20 \%$ & $15 \%$ \\
\hline 5-roomed houses & $32 \%$ & $33 \%$ & $42 \%$ \\
\hline 6-roomed houses & $15 \%$ & $12 \%$ & $7 \%$ \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

The percentage component bar chart is produced by constructing three equally spaced rectangles of any width, corresponding to the three years. The heights of the rectangles correspond to $100 \%$ relative frequency and are
subdivided into the values in the table of percentages shown above. A key is used (different types of shading or different colour schemes) to indicate corresponding percentage values in the rows of the table of percentages. The percentage component bar chart is shown in Figure 31.4.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-304.jpg?height=603&width=717&top_left_y=585&top_left_x=273)

Problem 6. The retail price of a product costing $Â£ 2$ is made up as follows: materials 10 p , labour 20 p , research and development 40 p , overheads 70 p , profit 60 p . Present these data on a pie diagram

A circle of any radius is drawn. The area of the circle represents the whole, which in this case is $Â£ 2$. The circle is subdivided into sectors so that the areas of the sectors are proportional to the parts; i.e., the parts which make up the total retail price. For the area of a sector to be proportional to a part, the angle at the centre of the circle must be proportional to that part. The whole, $Â£ 2$ or 200 p, corresponds to $360^{\circ}$. Therefore,
$$\begin{aligned}
& 10 \mathrm{p} \text { corresponds to } 360 \times \frac{10}{200} \text { degrees, i.e. } 18^{\circ} \\
& 20 \mathrm{p} \text { corresponds to } 360 \times \frac{20}{200} \text { degrees, i.e. } 36^{\circ}
\end{aligned}$$
and so on, giving the angles at the centre of the circle for the parts of the retail price as $18^{\circ}, 36^{\circ}, 72^{\circ}, 126^{\circ}$ and $108^{\circ}$, respectively.
The pie diagram is shown in Figure 31.5.

The profit when selling 700 units of the product is
$$Â£ \frac{700 \times 60}{100} \text { i.e. } Â£ 420$$

\subsection*{31.3 Presentation of grouped data}

When the number of members in a set is small, say ten or less, the data can be represented diagrammatically without further analysis, by means of pictograms, bar charts, percentage components bar charts or pie diagrams (as shown in Section 31.2).

For sets having more than ten members, those members having similar values are grouped together in
classes to form a frequency distribution. To assist in accurately counting members in the various classes, a tally diagram is used (see Problems 8 and 12).

A frequency distribution is merely a table showing classes and their corresponding frequencies (see Problems 8 and 12). The new set of values obtained by forming a frequency distribution is called grouped data. The terms used in connection with grouped data are shown in Figure 31.6(a). The size or range of a class is given by the upper class boundary value minus the lower class boundary value and in Figure 31.6(b) is 7.65-7.35; i.e., 0.30 . The class interval for the class shown in Figure 31.6(b) is 7.4 to 7.6 and the class mid-point value is given by
$$\frac{(\text { upper class boundary value })+(\text { lower class boundary value })}{2}$$
and in Figure 31.6(b) is
$$\frac{7.65+7.35}{2} \text { i.e. } 7.5$$
(a)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-306.jpg?height=394&width=465&top_left_y=1246&top_left_x=421)

Figure 31.6

One of the principal ways of presenting grouped data diagrammatically is to use a histogram, in which the areas of vertical, adjacent rectangles are made proportional to frequencies of the classes (see Problem 9). When class intervals are equal, the heights of the rectangles of a histogram are equal to the frequencies of the classes. For histograms having unequal class intervals, the area must be proportional to the frequency. Hence, if the class interval of class $A$ is twice the class interval of class $B$, then for equal frequencies the height of the rectangle representing $A$ is half that of $B$ (see Problem 11).

Another method of presenting grouped data diagrammatically is to use a frequency polygon, which is the graph produced by plotting frequency against class midpoint values and joining the co-ordinates with straight lines (see Problem 12).

A cumulative frequency distribution is a table showing the cumulative frequency for each value of upper class boundary. The cumulative frequency for a particular value of upper class boundary is obtained by adding the frequency of the class to the sum of the previous frequencies. A cumulative frequency distribution is formed in Problem 13.

The curve obtained by joining the co-ordinates of cumulative frequency (vertically) against upper class boundary (horizontally) is called an ogive or a cumulative frequency distribution curve (see Problem 13).

Problem 8. The data given below refer to the gain of each of a batch of 40 transistors, expressed correct to the nearest whole number. Form a frequency distribution for these data having seven classes
\begin{tabular}{|llllllll|}
\hline 81 & 83 & 87 & 74 & 76 & 89 & 82 & 84 \\
\hline 86 & 76 & 77 & 71 & 86 & 85 & 87 & 88 \\
\hline 84 & 81 & 80 & 81 & 73 & 89 & 82 & 79 \\
\hline 81 & 79 & 78 & 80 & 85 & 77 & 84 & 78 \\
\hline 83 & 79 & 80 & 83 & 82 & 79 & 80 & 77 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

The range of the data is the value obtained by taking the value of the smallest member from that of the largest member. Inspection of the set of data shows that range $=89-71=18$. The size of each class is given approximately by the range divided by the number of classes. Since 7 classes are required, the size of each class is $18 \div 7$; that is, approximately 3 . To achieve seven equal classes spanning a range of values from 71 to 89 , the class intervals are selected as $70-72,73-75$, and so on.

To assist with accurately determining the number in each class, a tally diagram is produced, as shown in Table 31.1(a). This is obtained by listing the classes in the left-hand column and then inspecting each of the 40 members of the set in turn and allocating them to the appropriate classes by putting ' 1 's in the appropriate rows. Every fifth ' 1 ' allocated to a particular row is shown as an oblique line crossing the four previous ' 1 's, to help with final counting.

A frequency distribution for the data is shown in Table 31.1(b) and lists classes and their corresponding frequencies, obtained from the tally diagram. (Class mid-point values are also shown in the table, since they are used for constructing the histogram for these data (see Problem 9).)

\section*{Chapter 32}

\section*{Mean, median, mode and standard deviation}

\subsection*{32.1 Measures of central tendency}

A single value, which is representative of a set of values, may be used to give an indication of the general size of the members in a set, the word 'average' often being used to indicate the single value. The statistical term used for 'average' is the 'arithmetic mean' or just the 'mean'.
Other measures of central tendency may be used and these include the median and the modal values.

\subsection*{32.2 Mean, median and mode for discrete data}

\subsection*{32.2.1 Mean}

The arithmetic mean value is found by adding together the values of the members of a set and dividing by the number of members in the set. Thus, the mean of the set of numbers $\{4,5,6,9\}$ is
$$\frac{4+5+6+9}{4} \text { i.e. } 6$$

In general, the mean of the set $\left\{x_{1}, x_{2}, x_{3}, \ldots x_{n}\right\}$ is
$$\bar{x}=\frac{x_{1}+x_{2}+x_{3}+\cdots+x_{n}}{n} \text { written as } \frac{\sum x}{n}$$
where $\sum$ is the Greek letter 'sigma' and means 'the sum of ' and $\bar{x}$ (called $x$-bar) is used to signify a mean value.

\subsection*{32.2.2 Median}

The median value often gives a better indication of the general size of a set containing extreme values. The set $\{7,5,74,10\}$ has a mean value of 24 , which is not really representative of any of the values of the members of the set. The median value is obtained by
(a) ranking the set in ascending order of magnitude, and
(b) selecting the value of the middle member for sets containing an odd number of members or finding the value of the mean of the two middle members for sets containing an even number of members.

For example, the set $\{7,5,74,10\}$ is ranked as $\{5,7,10,74\}$ and, since it contains an even number of members (four in this case), the mean of 7 and 10 is taken, giving a median value of 8.5 . Similarly, the set $\{3,81,15,7,14\}$ is ranked as $\{3,7,14,15,81\}$ and the median value is the value of the middle member, i.e. 14 .

\subsection*{32.2.3 Mode}

The modal value, or mode, is the most commonly occurring value in a set. If two values occur with the same frequency, the set is 'bi-modal'. The set $\{5,6,8,2,5,4,6,5,3\}$ has a modal value of 5 , since the member having a value of 5 occurs the most, i.e. three times.

Problem 1. Determine the mean, median and mode for the set $\{2,3,7,5,5,13,1,7,4,8,3,4,3\}$

The mean value is obtained by adding together the values of the members of the set and dividing by the number of members in the set. Thus,
mean value, $\bar{x}$
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{2+3+7+5+5+13+1+7+4+8+3+4+3}{13} \\
& =\frac{65}{13}=\mathbf{5}
\end{aligned}$$

To obtain the median value the set is ranked, that is, placed in ascending order of magnitude, and since the set contains an odd number of members the value of the middle member is the median value. Ranking the set gives $\{1,2,3,3,3,4,4,5,5,7,7,8,13\}$. The middle term is the seventh member; i.e., 4 . Thus, the median value is 4 .
The modal value is the value of the most commonly occurring member and is $\mathbf{3}$, which occurs three times, all other members only occurring once or twice.

Problem 2. The following set of data refers to the amount of money in $Â£$ s taken by a news vendor for 6 days. Determine the mean, median and modal values of the set $\{27.90,34.70,54.40,18.92,47.60,39.68\}$

\section*{Mean value}
$$\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{27.90+34.70+54.40+18.92+47.60+39.68}{6} \\
& =Â£ \mathbf{3 7 . 2 0}
\end{aligned}$$

The ranked set is $\{18.92,27.90,34.70,39.68,47.60,54.40\}$.
Since the set has an even number of members, the mean of the middle two members is taken to give the median value; i.e.,
$$\text { median value }=\frac{34.70+39.68}{2}=Â£ \mathbf{3 7 . 1 9}$$

Since no two members have the same value, this set has no mode.

\section*{Now try the following Practice Exercise}

\section*{Practice Exercise 125 Mean, median and mode for discrete data (answers on page 353)}

In problems 1 to 4, determine the mean, median and modal values for the sets given.
1. $\{3,8,10,7,5,14,2,9,8\}$
2. $\{26,31,21,29,32,26,25,28\}$
3. $\{4.72,4.71,4.74,4.73,4.72,4.71,4.73,4.72\}$
4. $\{73.8,126.4,40.7,141.7,28.5,237.4,157.9\}$

\subsection*{32.3 Mean, median and mode for grouped data}

The mean value for a set of grouped data is found by determining the sum of the (frequency $\times$ class mid-point values) and dividing by the sum of the frequencies; i.e.,
$$\text { mean value, } \bar{x}=\frac{f_{1} x_{1}+f_{2} x_{2}+\cdots f_{n} x_{n}}{f_{1}+f_{2}+\cdots+f_{n}}=\frac{\sum(f x)}{\sum f}$$
where $f$ is the frequency of the class having a mid-point value of $x$, and so on.

Problem 3. The frequency distribution for the value of resistance in ohms of 48 resistors is as shown. Determine the mean value of resistance
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$20.5-20.9$ & 3 \\
$21.0-21.4$ & 10 \\
$21.5-21.9$ & 11 \\
$22.0-22.4$ & 13 \\
$22.5-22.9$ & 9 \\
$23.0-23.4$ & 2
\end{tabular}

The class mid-point/frequency values are 20.7 3, 21.2 10, 21.7 11, $22.213,22.79$ and 23.22.

For grouped data, the mean value is given by
$$\bar{x}=\frac{\sum(f x)}{\sum f}$$
where $f$ is the class frequency and $x$ is the class midpoint value. Hence mean value,
$$\begin{aligned}
& \bar{x}=\frac{(3 \times 20.7)+(10 \times 21.2)+(11 \times 21.7)}{+(13 \times 22.2)+(9 \times 22.7)+(2 \times 23.2)} \\
& 48 \\
&=\frac{1052.1}{48}=21.919 \ldots
\end{aligned}$$
i.e. the mean value is $\mathbf{2 1 . 9}$ ohms, correct to 3 significant figures.

\subsection*{32.3.1 Histograms}

The mean, median and modal values for grouped data may be determined from a histogram. In a histogram,
frequency values are represented vertically and variable values horizontally. The mean value is given by the value of the variable corresponding to a vertical line drawn through the centroid of the histogram. The median value is obtained by selecting a variable value such that the area of the histogram to the left of a vertical line drawn through the selected variable value is equal to the area of the histogram on the right of the line. The modal value is the variable value obtained by dividing the width of the highest rectangle in the histogram in proportion to the heights of the adjacent rectangles. The method of determining the mean, median and modal values from a histogram is shown in Problem 4.

Problem 4. The time taken in minutes to assemble a device is measured 50 times and the results are as shown. Draw a histogram depicting the data and hence determine the mean, median and modal values of the distribution
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$14.5-15.5$ & 5 \\
$16.5-17.5$ & 8 \\
$18.5-19.5$ & 16 \\
$20.5-21.5$ & 12 \\
$22.5-23.5$ & 6 \\
$24.5-25.5$ & 3
\end{tabular}

The histogram is shown in Figure 32.1. The mean value lies at the centroid of the histogram. With reference to any arbitrary axis, say $Y Y$ shown at a time of 14 minutes, the position of the horizontal value of the centroid can be obtained from the relationship $A M=\sum(a m)$, where $A$ is the area of the histogram, $M$ is the horizontal distance of the centroid from the axis $Y Y, a$ is the area of a rectangle of the histogram and $m$ is the distance of the centroid of the rectangle from $Y Y$. The areas of the individual rectangles are shown circled
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_06_6087c43bc92deffd8bd0g-314.jpg?height=549&width=671&top_left_y=1854&top_left_x=294)

Figure 32.1
on the histogram giving a total area of 100 square units. The positions, $m$, of the centroids of the individual rectangles are $1,3,5, \ldots$ units from $Y Y$. Thus
$$\begin{aligned}
100 M=(10 \times 1)+(16 \times 3)+ & (32 \times 5)+(24 \times 7) \\
& +(12 \times 9)+(6 \times 11)
\end{aligned}$$
i.e. $M=\frac{560}{100}=5.6$ units from $Y Y$

Thus, the position of the mean with reference to the time scale is $14+5.6$, i.e. $\mathbf{1 9 . 6}$ minutes.

The median is the value of time corresponding to a vertical line dividing the total area of the histogram into two equal parts. The total area is 100 square units, hence the vertical line must be drawn to give 50 units of area on each side. To achieve this with reference to Figure 32.1, rectangle $A B F E$ must be split so that $50-(10+16)$ units of area lie on one side and $50-(24+12+6)$ units of area lie on the other. This shows that the area of $A B F E$ is split so that 24 units of area lie to the left of the line and 8 units of area lie to the right; i.e., the vertical line must pass through 19.5 minutes. Thus, the median value of the distribution is $\mathbf{1 9 . 5}$ minutes.

The mode is obtained by dividing the line $A B$, which is the height of the highest rectangle, proportionally to the heights of the adjacent rectangles. With reference to Figure 32.1, this is achieved by joining $A C$ and $B D$ and drawing a vertical line through the point of intersection of these two lines. This gives the mode of the distribution, which is $\mathbf{1 9 . 3}$ minutes.

$89.5-91.5$ & 27 \\
$92.5-94.5$ & 15 \\
$95.5-97.5$ & 3
\end{tabular}
4. The diameters, in centimetres, of 60 holes bored in engine castings are measured and the results are as shown. Draw a histogram depicting these results and hence determine the mean, median and modal values of the distribution.
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$2.011-2.014$ & 7 \\
$2.016-2.019$ & 16 \\
$2.021-2.024$ & 23 \\
$2.026-2.029$ & 9 \\
$2.031-2.034$ & 5
\end{tabular}

\subsection*{32.4 Standard deviation}

\subsection*{32.4.1 Discrete data}

The standard deviation of a set of data gives an indication of the amount of dispersion, or the scatter, of members of the set from the measure of central tendency. Its value is the root-mean-square value of the members of the set and for discrete data is obtained as follows.
(i) Determine the measure of central tendency, usually the mean value, (occasionally the median or modal values are specified).
(ii) Calculate the deviation of each member of the set from the mean, giving
$$\left(x_{1}-\bar{x}\right),\left(x_{2}-\bar{x}\right),\left(x_{3}-\bar{x}\right), \ldots$$
(iii) Determine the squares of these deviations; i.e., $\left(x_{1}-\bar{x}\right)^{2},\left(x_{2}-\bar{x}\right)^{2},\left(x_{3}-\bar{x}\right)^{2}, \ldots$
(iv) Find the sum of the squares of the deviations, i.e.
$$\left(x_{1}-\bar{x}\right)^{2}+\left(x_{2}-\bar{x}\right)^{2}+\left(x_{3}-\bar{x}\right)^{2}, \ldots$$
(v) Divide by the number of members in the set, $n$, giving
$$\frac{\left(x_{1}-\bar{x}\right)^{2}+\left(x_{2}-\bar{x}\right)^{2}+\left(x^{3}-\bar{x}\right)^{2}+\cdots}{n}$$
(vi) Determine the square root of (v).

The standard deviation is indicated by $\sigma$ (the Greek letter small 'sigma') and is written mathematically as
$$\text { standard deviation, } \sigma=\sqrt{\left\{\frac{\sum(x-\bar{x})^{2}}{n}\right\}}$$
where $x$ is a member of the set, $\bar{x}$ is the mean value of the set and $n$ is the number of members in the set. The value of standard deviation gives an indication of the distance of the members of a set from the mean value. The set $\{1,4,7,10,13\}$ has a mean value of 7 and a standard deviation of about 4.2. The set $\{5,6,7,8,9\}$ also has a mean value of 7 but the standard deviation is about 1.4. This shows that the members of the second set are mainly much closer to the mean value than the members of the first set. The method of determining the standard deviation for a set of discrete data is shown in Problem 5.

Problem 5. Determine the standard deviation from the mean of the set of numbers $\{5,6,8,4,10,3\}$, correct to 4 significant figures

The arithmetic mean, $\bar{x}=\frac{\sum x}{n}$
$$=\frac{5+6+8+4+10+3}{6}=6$$

Standard deviation, $\sigma=\sqrt{\left\{\frac{\sum(x-\bar{x})^{2}}{n}\right\}}$
The $(x-\bar{x})^{2}$ values are $(5-6)^{2},(6-6)^{2},(8-6)^{2}$, $(4-6)^{2},(10-6)^{2}$ and $(3-6)^{2}$.

The sum of the $(x-\bar{x})^{2}$ values, i.e. $\sum(x-\bar{x})^{2}$, is $1+0+4+4+16+9=34$
and $\frac{\sum(x-\bar{x})^{2}}{n}=\frac{34}{6}=5 . \dot{6}$ since there are 6 members in the set.

Hence, standard deviation,
$$\sigma=\sqrt{\left\{\frac{\sum(x-\bar{x})^{2}}{n}\right\}}=\sqrt{5 . \dot{6}}=\mathbf{2 . 3 8 0}$$
correct to 4 significant figures.

\subsection*{32.4.2 Grouped data}

For grouped data,
$$\text { standard deviation, } \sigma=\sqrt{\left\{\frac{\sum\left\{f(x-\bar{x})^{2}\right\}}{\sum f}\right\}}$$
where $f$ is the class frequency value, $x$ is the class midpoint value and $\bar{x}$ is the mean value of the grouped data. The method of determining the standard deviation for a set of grouped data is shown in Problem 6.

Problem 6. The frequency distribution for the values of resistance in ohms of 48 resistors is as shown. Calculate the standard deviation from the mean of the resistors, correct to 3 significant figures
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$20.5-20.9$ & 3 \\
$21.0-21.4$ & 10 \\
$21.5-21.9$ & 11 \\
$22.0-22.4$ & 13 \\
$22.5-22.9$ & 9 \\
$23.0-23.4$ & 2
\end{tabular}

The standard deviation for grouped data is given by
$$\sigma=\sqrt{\left\{\frac{\sum\left\{f(x-\bar{x})^{2}\right\}}{\sum f}\right\}}$$

From Problem 3, the distribution mean value is $\bar{x}=21.92$, correct to 2 significant figures.

The ' $x$-values' are the class mid-point values, i.e. 20.7, 21.2, 21.7, ...

Thus, the $(x-\bar{x})^{2}$ values are $(20.7-21.92)^{2}$, $(21.2-21.92)^{2},(21.7-21.92)^{2}, \ldots$
and the $f(x-\bar{x})^{2}$ values are $3(20.7-21.92)^{2}$, $10(21.2-21.92)^{2}, 11(21.7-21.92)^{2}, \ldots$
The $\sum f(x-\bar{x})^{2}$ values are
$$\begin{aligned}
4.4652+5.1840+0.5324 & +1.0192 \\
+5.4756+3.2768 & =19.9532 \\
\frac{\sum\left\{f(x-\bar{x})^{2}\right\}}{\sum f}=\frac{19.9532}{48} & =0.41569
\end{aligned}$$
and standard deviation,
$$\begin{aligned}
\sigma & =\sqrt{\left\{\frac{\sum\left\{f(x-\bar{x})^{2}\right\}}{\sum f}\right\}}=\sqrt{0.41569} \\
& =\mathbf{0 . 6 4 5}, \text { correct to } 3 \text { significant figures. }
\end{aligned}$$
